Yonge\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     How the children leave us, and no traces\r\n       Linger of that smiling angel-band,\r\n     Gone, for ever gone, and in their places\r\n       Weary men and anxious women stand.\r\n                          ADELAIDE A. PROCTOR\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nPREFACE\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIf a book by an author who must call herself a veteran should be taken\r\nup by readers of a younger generation, they are begged to consider the\r\nfirst few chapters as a sort of prologue, introduced for the sake of\r\nthose of elder years, who were kind enough to be interested in the\r\ndomestic politics of the Mohuns and the Underwoods.\r\n\r\nContinuations are proverbially failures, and yet it is perhaps a\r\nconsequence of the writer\'s realization of characters that some seem as\r\nif they could not be parted with, and must be carried on in the mind,\r\nand not only have their after-fates described, but their minds and\r\nopinions under the modifications of advancing years and altered\r\ncircumstances.\r\n\r\nTurner and other artists have been known literally to see colours in\r\nabsolutely different hues as they grew older, and so no doubt it is\r\nwith thinkers. The outlines may be the same, the tints are insensibly\r\nmodified and altered, and the effect thus far changed.\r\n\r\nThus it is with the writers of fiction. The young write in full sympathy\r\nwith, as well as for, the young, they have a pensive satisfaction in\r\nfeeling and depicting the full pathos of a tragedy, and on the other\r\nhand they delight in their own mirth, and fully share it with the\r\nbeings of their imagination, or they work out great questions with the\r\nunhesitating decision of their youth.\r\n\r\nBut those who write in elder years look on at their young people,\r\nnot with inner sympathy but from the outside. Their affections and\r\ncomprehension are with the fathers, mothers, and aunts; they dread,\r\nrather than seek, piteous scenes, and they have learnt that there are\r\ntwo sides to a question, that there are many stages in human life, and\r\nthat the success or failure of early enthusiasm leaves a good deal more\r\nyet to come.\r\n\r\nThus the vivid fancy passes away, which the young are carried along\r\nwith, and the older feel refreshed by; there is still a sense of\r\nexperience, and a pleasure in tracing the perspective from another\r\npoint of sight, where what was once distant has become near at hand, the\r\nearnest of many a day-dream has been gained, and more than one ideal has\r\nbeen tried, and merits and demerits have become apparent.\r\n\r\nAnd thus it is hoped that the Long Vacation may not be devoid of\r\ninterest for readers who have sympathized in early days with Beechcroft,\r\nStoneborough, and Vale Leston, when they were peopled with the outcome\r\nof a youthful mind, and that they may be ready to look with interest on\r\nthe perplexities and successes attending on the matured characters in\r\nafter years.\r\n\r\nIf they will feel as if they were on a visit to friends grown older,\r\nwith their children about them, and if the young will forgive the seeing\r\nwith elder eyes, and observing instead of participating, that is all the\r\nveteran author would ask.\r\n\r\n\r\nC. M. YONGE.\r\n\r\nElderfield,\r\n\r\nJanuary 31, 1895.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nLIST OF CONTENTS\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nI. A CHAPTER OF RETROSPECT\r\n\r\nII. A CHAPTER OF TWADDLE\r\n\r\nIII. RIGHTED\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE LONG VACATION\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER I. -- A CHAPTER OF RETROSPECT\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     Sorrow He gives and pain, good store;\r\n     Toil to bear, for the neck which bore;\r\n     For duties rendered, a duty more;\r\n     And lessons spelled in the painful lore\r\n       Of a war which is waged eternally.--ANON.\r\n\r\n\r\n"Ah! my Gerald boy! There you are! Quite well?"\r\n\r\nGerald Underwood, of slight delicate mould, with refined,\r\ntransparent-looking features, and with hair and budding moustache too\r\nfair for his large dark eyes, came bounding up the broad stair, to the\r\nembrace of the aunt who stood at the top, a little lame lady supported\r\nby an ivory-headed staff. Her deep blue eyes, dark eyebrows, and sweet\r\nthough piquant face were framed by the straight crape line of widowhood,\r\nwhence a soft white veil hung on her shoulders.\r\n\r\n"Cherie sweet! You are well? And the Vicar?"\r\n\r\n"Getting on. How are they all at Vale Leston?"\r\n\r\n"All right. Your mother got to church on Easter-day." This was to Anna\r\nVanderkist, a young person of the plump partridge order, and fair, rosy\r\ncountenance ever ready for smiles and laughter.\r\n\r\n"Here are no end of flowers," as the butler brought a hamper.\r\n\r\n"Daffodils! Oh!--and anemones! How delicious! I must take Clement a\r\nbunch of those dear white violets. I know where they came from," and she\r\nheld them to her lips. "Some primroses too, I hope."\r\n\r\n"A few; but the main body, tied up in tight bunches like cauliflowers, I\r\ndropped at Kensington Palace Gardens."\r\n\r\n"A yellow primrose is much more than a yellow primrose at present,"\r\nsaid Mrs. Grinstead, picking out the few spared from political purposes.\r\n"Clement will want his button-hole, to greet Lance."\r\n\r\n"So he is advanced to button-holes! And Lance?"\r\n\r\n"He is coming up for the Press dinner, and will sleep here, to be ready\r\nfor Primrose-day."\r\n\r\n"That\'s prime, whatever brings him."\r\n\r\n"There, children, go and _do_ the flowers, and drink tea. I am going to\r\nread to your uncle to keep him fresh for Lance."\r\n\r\n"How bright she looks," said Gerald, as Anna began collecting vases from\r\nthe tables in a drawing-room not professionally artistic, but entirely\r\ndomestic, and full of grace and charm of taste, looking over a suburban\r\ngarden fresh with budding spring to a church spire.\r\n\r\n"The thought of Uncle Lance has cheered them both very much."\r\n\r\n"So the Vicar is really recovering?"\r\n\r\n"Since Cousin Marilda flew at the curates, and told them that if they\r\ncame near him with their worries, they should never see a farthing of\r\nhers! And they are all well at home? Is anything going on?"\r\n\r\n"Chiefly defence of the copses from primrose marauders. You know the\r\ngreat agitation. They want to set up a china clay factory on Penbeacon,\r\nand turn the Ewe, not to say the Leston, into milk and water."\r\n\r\n"The wretches! But they can\'t. It is yours."\r\n\r\n"Not the western quarry; but they cannot get the stream without a\r\npiece of the land which belongs to Hodnet\'s farm, for which they make\r\nastounding bids; but, any way, nothing can be done till I am of age,\r\nwhen the lease to Hodnet is out, except by Act of Parliament, which is\r\nhardly worth while, considering--"\r\n\r\n"That you are near twenty. But surely you won\'t consent?"\r\n\r\n"Well, I don\'t want to break all your hearts, Cherie\'s especially, but\r\nwhy should all that space be nothing but a playground for us Underwoods,\r\ninstead of making work for the million?"\r\n\r\n"And a horrid, nasty million it would be," retorted Anna. "You born\r\nYankee! Don\'t worry Aunt Cherry about profaning the Ewe, just to spoil\r\ngood calico with nasty yellow dust."\r\n\r\n"I don\'t want to worry her, but there never were such groovy people as\r\nyou are! I shall think it over, and make up my mind by the time I have\r\nthe power."\r\n\r\n"I wish you had to wait till five-and-twenty, so as to get more time and\r\nsense."\r\n\r\nGerald laughed, and sauntered away. He was not Yankee, except that he\r\nhad been born at Boston. His father was English, his mother a Hungarian\r\nsinger, who had divorced and deserted his father, the ne\'er-do-weel\r\nsecond son of an old family. When Gerald was five years old his father\r\nwas killed, and he himself severely injured, in a raid of the Indians\r\nfar west, and he was brought home by an old friend of the family. His\r\neldest uncle\'s death made him heir to the estate, but his life was a\r\nvery frail one till his thirteenth year, when he seemed to have outgrown\r\nthe shock to spine and nerves.\r\n\r\nMuch had befallen the house of Underwood since the days when we took\r\nleave of them, still sorrowing under the loss of the main pillar of\r\ntheir house, but sending forth the new founders with good hope.\r\n\r\nGeraldine had made her home at St. Matthew\'s with her brother Clement\r\nand the little delicate orphan Gerald; but after three years she had\r\nyielded to the persevering constancy of Mr. Grinstead, a sculptor of\r\nconsiderable genius and repute, much older than herself, who was ready\r\nand willing to be a kind uncle to her little charge, and who introduced\r\nher to all at home or abroad that was refined, intellectual, or\r\nbeautiful.\r\n\r\nIt was in the first summer after their marriage that he was charmed with\r\nthe vivacity and musical talent of her young sister Angela, now upon the\r\nworld again. Angela had grown up as the pet and plaything of the Sisters\r\nof St. Faith\'s at Dearport, which she regarded as another home, and when\r\ncrushed by grief at her eldest brother\'s death had hurried thither for\r\nsolace. Her family thought her safe there, not realizing how far life\r\nis from having its final crisis over at one-and-twenty. New Sisters came\r\nin, old ones went to found fresh branches; stricter rules grew, up,\r\nand were enforced by a Superior out of sympathy with the girl, who had\r\nalways rebelled against what she thought dictation. It was decided that\r\nshe could stay there no longer, and her brother Lancelot and his wife\r\nreceived her at Marshlands with indignant sympathy for her wrongs; but\r\nneither she nor her sister-in-law were made to suit one another.\r\nWith liberty her spirit and audacity revived, and she showed so much\r\nattraction towards the Salvation Army, that her brother declared their\r\nmusic to have been the chief deterrent from her becoming a "Hallelujah\r\nlass." However, in a brief visit to London, she so much pleased Mr.\r\nGrinstead that he invited her to partake in the winter\'s journey to\r\nItaly. Poor man, he little knew what he undertook. Music, art, Roman\r\nCatholic services, and novelty conspired to intoxicate her, and her\r\nsister was thankful to carry her off northward before she had pledged\r\nherself to enter a convent.\r\n\r\nMountain air and scenery, however, proved equally dangerous. Her\r\nenterprises inspired the two quiet people with constant fears for her\r\nneck; but it was worse when they fell in with a party of very Bohemian\r\nartists, whom Mr. Grinstead knew just well enough not to be able to\r\nshake them off. The climax came when she started off with them in\r\ncostume at daybreak on an expedition to play the zither and sing at a\r\nvillage fete. She came back safe and sound, but Geraldine was already\r\npacked up to take her to Munich, where Charles Audley and Stella now\r\nwere, and to leave her under their charge before she had driven Mr.\r\nGrinstead distracted.\r\n\r\nThere was a worse trouble at home. Since the death of his good old\r\nmother and of Felix Underwood, Sir Adrian Vanderkist had been rapidly\r\ngoing downhill; as though he had thrown off all restraint, and as if\r\nthe yearly birth of a daughter left him the more free to waste his\r\npatrimony. Little or nothing had been heard direct from poor Alda till\r\nClement was summoned by a telegram from Ironbeam Park to find his sister\r\nin the utmost danger, with a new-born son by her side, and her husband\r\nin the paroxysms of the terrible Nemesis of indulgence in alcohol.\r\n\r\nSir Adrian had quarrelled with all the family in turn except Clement,\r\nand this fact, or else that gentleness towards a sufferer that had won\r\non old Fulbert Underwood, led him in a lucid interval to direct and sign\r\na hurried will, drawn up by his steward, leaving the Reverend Edward\r\nClement Underwood sole guardian to his children, and executor, together\r\nwith his lawyer. It was done without Clement\'s knowledge, or he would\r\nhave remonstrated, for never was there a more trying bequest than the\r\ncharge which in a few days he found laid on him.\r\n\r\nHe had of course already made acquaintance with the little girls. Poor\r\nchildren, they had hitherto led a life as dreary as was possible to\r\nchildren who had each other, and fresh air and open grounds. Their\r\nmother was more and more of an invalid, and dreaded that their father\r\nshould take umbrage at the least expense that they caused; so that they\r\nwere scrupulously kept out of his way, fed, dressed, and even educated\r\nas plainly as possible by a governess, cheap because she was passe, and\r\nmade up for her deficiencies by strictness amounting to harshness,\r\nwhile they learnt to regard each new little sister\'s sex as a proof of\r\nnaughtiness on her part or theirs.\r\n\r\nThe first time they ever heard a man\'s step in the school-room passage\r\nwas in those days of undefined sorrow, alarm, and silence after the\r\ngoverness had despatched the message to the only relation whose address\r\nshe knew. The step came nearer; there was a knock, the sweet, strong\r\nvoice asked,\r\n\r\n"Are the poor little girls here?" and the tall figure was on one knee\r\namong them, gathering as many as he could within his loving arms.\r\nPerhaps he recollected Sister Constance among the forlorn flock at\r\nBexley; but these were even more desolate, for they had no past of love\r\nand loyalty. But with that embrace it seemed to the four elders that\r\ntheir worst days were over. What mattered it to them that they all eight\r\nof them--were almost destitute? the birth of the poor little male heir\r\npreventing the sale of the property, so terribly encumbered; and the\r\nonly available maintenance being the L5000 that Mr. Thomas Underwood had\r\nsettled securely upon their mother.\r\n\r\nThey began to know what love and kindness meant. Kind uncles and aunts\r\ngathered round them. Their mother seemed to be able to live when her\r\ntwin-sister hung over her, and as soon as she could be moved, the whole\r\nparty left the gloom of Ironbeam for Vale Leston, where a house was\r\narranged for them. Lady Vanderkist continued a chronic invalid, watched\r\nover by her sister Wilmet and her excellent young daughter Mary. Robina,\r\nwho had only one girl, and had not forgotten her training as a teacher,\r\nundertook, with the assistance of Sophia, the second daughter, the\r\neducation of the little ones; and the third and fourth, Emilia and Anna,\r\nwere adopted into the childless homes of Mrs. Travis Underwood and Mrs.\r\nGrinstead, and lived there as daughters. Business cares of the most\r\nperplexing kind fell, however, on Clement Underwood\'s devoted and\r\nunaccustomed head, and in the midst arrived a telegram from Charles\r\nAudley, summoning him instantly to Munich.\r\n\r\nAngela was in danger of fulfilling her childish design of marrying a\r\nDuke, or at least a Graf. Diplomates could not choose their society, and\r\nshe had utterly disdained all restraints from "the babies," as she chose\r\nto call Mr. and Mrs. Audley, and thus the wunderschones madchen had\r\nfascinated the Count, an unbelieving Roman Catholic of evil repute, and\r\nhad derided their remonstrances.\r\n\r\nClement hurried off, but to find the bird flown. She had come down in\r\nthe morning, white and tear-stained, and had told Stella that she could\r\nstay no longer, kissed her, and was gone out of the house before even\r\nCharles could be called. Stella\'s anxiety, almost despair, had however\r\nbeen relieved just before her brother\'s arrival by an electric message\r\nfrom Vale Leston with the words, "Angela safe at home."\r\n\r\nLetters followed, and told how Robina had found her sobbing upon her\r\nbrother Felix\'s grave. Her explanation was, that on the very night\r\nbefore her proposed betrothal, she had dreamt that she was drifting\r\ndown the Ewe in the little boat Miss Ullin, and saw Felix under the\r\nwillow-tree holding out his bared arms to her. She said, "Is that the\r\nscar of the scald?" and his only answer was the call "Angela! Angela!"\r\nand with the voice still sounding in her ears, she awoke, and determined\r\ninstantly to obey the call, coming to her, as she felt, from another\r\nworld. If it were only from her own conscience, still it was a cause\r\nof great thankfulness to her family, and she soon made herself very\r\nvaluable at Vale Leston in a course of epidemics which ran through the\r\nvillage, and were in some cases very severe. The doctors declared that\r\ntwo of the little Vanderkists owed their lives to her unremitting care.\r\n\r\nHer destiny seemed to be fixed, and she went off radiant to be trained\r\nat a London hospital as a nurse. Her faculty in that line was undoubted.\r\nAll the men in her ward were devoted to her, and so were almost all the\r\nyoung doctors; but the matron did not like her, and at the end of the\r\nthree years, an act of independent treatment of a patient caused a\r\ntremendous commotion, all the greater because many outsiders declared\r\nthat she was right. But it almost led to a general expulsion of lady\r\nnurses.\r\n\r\nOf course she had to retire, and happily for her, Mother Constance was\r\njust at that time sentenced by her rheumatism to spend the winter in a\r\nwarm climate. She eagerly claimed Angela\'s tendance, and just at the end\r\nof the year there came an urgent request for a Sister from England to\r\nform a foundation in one of the new cities of Australia on the model of\r\nSt. Faith\'s; and thither Mother Constance proceeded, with one Sister and\r\nAngela, who had thenceforth gone on so well and quietly that her family\r\nhoped the time for Angela\'s periodical breaking out had passed.\r\n\r\nThe ensuing years had been tranquil as to family events, though the\r\nvarious troubles and perplexities that fell on Clement were endless,\r\nboth those parochial and ritualistic, and those connected with the\r\nVanderkist affairs, where his sister did not spare him her murmurs.\r\nFulbert\'s death in Australia was a blow both to Lancelot and to him,\r\nthough they had never had much hope of seeing this brother again. He\r\nhad left the proceeds of his sheep-farm between Lancelot, Bernard, and\r\nAngela.\r\n\r\nThus had passed about fourteen years since the death of Felix, when kind\r\nold Mr. Grinstead died suddenly at a public meeting, leaving his widow\r\nwell endowed, and the possessor of her pretty home at Brompton. When,\r\nsoon after the blow, her sisters took her to the home at Vale Leston,\r\nshe had seemed oppressed by the full tide of young life overflowing\r\nthere, and as if she again felt the full force of the early sorrow in\r\nthe loss that she had once said made Vale Leston to her a desolation. On\r\nher return to Brompton, she had still been in a passive state, as though\r\nthe taste of life had gone from her, and there was nothing to call\r\nforth her interest or energy. The first thing that roused her was the\r\ndangerous illness of her brother Clement, the result of blood-poisoning\r\nduring a mission week in a pestilential locality, after a long course\r\nof family worries and overwork in his parish. Low, lingering fever had\r\nthreatened every organ in turn, till in the early days of January,\r\na fatal time in the family, he was almost despaired of. However, Dr.\r\nBrownlow and Lancelot Underwood had strength of mind to run the risk,\r\nwith the earnest co-operation of Professor Tom May, of a removal to\r\nBrompton, where he immediately began to mend, so that he was in April\r\ndecidedly convalescent, though with doubts as to a return to real\r\nhealth, nor had he yet gone beyond his dressing-room, since any exertion\r\nwas liable to cause fainting.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER II. -- A CHAPTER OF TWADDLE\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     The blessing of my later years\r\n     Was with me when a boy.--WORDSWORTH.\r\n\r\n\r\nWhen Mrs. Grinstead, on her nephew\'s arm, came into her drawing-room\r\nafter dinner, she was almost as much dismayed as pleased to find a long\r\nblack figure in a capacious arm-chair by the fire.\r\n\r\n"You adventurous person," she said, "how came you here?"\r\n\r\n"I could not help it, with the prospect of Lancey boy," he said in\r\nsmiling excuse, holding out a hand in greeting to Gerald, and thanking\r\nAnna, who brought a cushion.\r\n\r\n"Hark! there he is!" and Gerald and Anna sprang forward, but were only\r\nin time to open the room door, when there was a double cry of greeting,\r\nnot only of the slender, bright-eyed, still youthful-looking uncle, but\r\nof the pleasant face of his wife. She exclaimed as Lancelot hung over\r\nhis brother--\r\n\r\n"Indeed, I would not have come but that I thought he was still in his\r\nroom."\r\n\r\n"That\'s a very bad compliment, Gertrude, when I have just made my\r\nescape."\r\n\r\n"I shall be too much for you," said Gertrude. "Here, children, take me\r\noff somewhere."\r\n\r\n"To have some dinner," said Geraldine, her hand on the bell.\r\n\r\n"No, no, Marilda feasted me."\r\n\r\n"Then don\'t go," entreated Clement. "It is a treat to look at you two\r\nsunny people."\r\n\r\n"Let us efface ourselves, and be seen and not heard," returned Gertrude,\r\nsitting down between Gerald and Anna on a distant couch, whence she\r\ncontemplated the trio--Clement, of course, with the extreme pallor,\r\nlanguor, and emaciation of long illness, with a brow gaining in dignity\r\nand expression by the loss of hair, and with a look of weary, placid\r\nenjoyment as he listened to the talk of the other two; Lance with\r\nbright, sweet animation and cheeriness, still young-looking, though\r\nhis hair too was scantier and his musical tones subdued; and Geraldine,\r\npensive in eye and lip, but often sparkling up with flashes of her\r\ninborn playfulness, and, like Clement, resting in the sunshine diffused\r\nby Lance. This last was the editor and proprietor of the \'Pursuivant\',\r\nan important local paper, and had come up on journalistic business as\r\nwell as for the fete. Gertrude meantime had been choosing carpets and\r\ncurtains.\r\n\r\n"For," said Lance, with a smack of exultation, "we are actually going\r\nback to our old quarters over the shop."\r\n\r\n"Oh!" A responsive sound of satisfaction from Geraldine.\r\n\r\n"Nothing amiss?" asked Clement.\r\n\r\n"Far from it. We let Marshlands to great advantage, and there are many\r\nreasons for the flitting. I ought to be at head-quarters, and besides\r\nthere are the Sundays. We are too many now for picnicking in the\r\nclass-room, or sponging on the rectory."\r\n\r\n"And," said Gertrude, "I dare not put his small family in competition\r\nwith his organ."\r\n\r\n"Besides," said Lance, "the \'Pursuivant\' is more exacting, and the\r\nprinting Will Harewood\'s books has brought in more business--"\r\n\r\n"But how about space? We could squeeze, but can you?"\r\n\r\n"We have devoured our two next-door neighbours. There\'s for you! You\r\nknow Pratt the dentist had a swell hall-door and staircase, which we\r\nabsorb, so we shall not eat in the back drawing-room, nor come up the\r\nflight which used to be so severe on you, Cherry."\r\n\r\n"I can only remember the arms that helped me up. I have never left off\r\ndreaming of the dear old step springing up the stair after the day\'s\r\nwork, and the whistle to Theodore."\r\n\r\n"Ah, those were the jolly old days!" returned Lance, con amore.\r\n\r\n"Unbroken," added Clement, in the same tone.\r\n\r\n"Better than Vale Leston?" asked Gertrude.\r\n\r\n"The five years there were, as Felix called those last hours of delight,\r\nhalcyon days," said Geraldine; "but the real home was in the rough and\r\nthe smooth, the contrivances, the achievements, the exultation at each\r\nstep on the ladder, the flashes of Edgar, the crowded holiday times--all\r\nhappier than we knew! I hope your children will care as much."\r\n\r\n"Vale Leston is their present paradise," said Gertrude. "You should\r\nsee Master Felix\'s face at the least hope of a visit, and even little\r\nFulbert talks about boat and fish."\r\n\r\n"What have you done with the Lambs?" demanded Clement.\r\n\r\n"They have outgrown the old place in every direction, and have got a\r\nspick-and-span chess-board of a villa out on the Minsterham road."\r\n\r\n"They have not more children than you have."\r\n\r\n"Five Lambkins to our four, besides Gussy and Killy," said Lance;\r\n"though A--which is all that appears of the great Achilles\' unlucky\r\nname--is articled to Shapcote, and as for Gussy, or rather Mr. Tanneguy,\r\nhe is my right hand."\r\n\r\n"We thought him a nice sort of youth when he was improving himself in\r\nLondon," said Clement.\r\n\r\n"You both were very good to him," said Lance, "and those three years\r\nwere not wasted. He is a far better sub-editor and reporter than I was\r\nat his age, with his French wit and cleverness. The only fault I find\r\nwith him is that he longs for plate-glass and flummery instead of old\r\nFroggatt\'s respectable panes."\r\n\r\n"He has become the London assistant, who was our bugbear," said\r\nGeraldine.\r\n\r\n"I don\'t know how we should get on without him since we made \'Pur\'\r\ndaily," said Lance.\r\n\r\n"How old ambitions get realized!" said Geraldine.\r\n\r\n"Does his mother endure the retail work, or has she not higher views for\r\nhim?" asked Clement.\r\n\r\n"In fact, ever since the first Lambkin came on the stage any one would\r\nhave thought those poor boys were her steps, not good old Lamb\'s;\r\nwhereas Felix always made a point of noticing them. Gus was nine years\r\nold that last time he was there, while I was ill, and he left such an\r\nimpression as to make him the hero model.--Aye, Gus is first-rate."\r\n\r\n"I am glad you have not altered the old shop and office."\r\n\r\n"Catch me! But we are enlarging the reading-room, and the new press\r\ndemands space. Then there\'s a dining-room for the young men, and what\r\ndo you think I\'ve got? We (not Froggatt, Underwood, and Lamb, but the\r\nChurch Committee) have bought St. Oswald\'s buildings for a coffee hotel\r\nand young men\'s lodging-house."\r\n\r\n"Our own, old house. Oh! is Edgar\'s Great Achilles there still?"\r\n\r\n"I rushed up to see. Alas! the barbarians have papered him out. But what\r\ndo you think I\'ve got? The old cupboard door where all our heights were\r\nmarked on our birthdays."\r\n\r\n"He set it up in his office," said Gertrude. "I think he danced round\r\nit. I know he brought me and all the children to adore it, and showed\r\nus, just like a weather record, where every one shot up after the\r\nmeasles, and where Clement got above you, Cherry, and Lance remained a\r\nbonny shrimp."\r\n\r\n"A great move, but it sounds comfortable," said Clement.\r\n\r\n"Yes; for now Lance will get a proper luncheon, as he never has done\r\nsince dear old Mrs. Froggatt died," said Gertrude, "and he is an animal\r\nthat needs to be made to eat! Then the children want schooling of the\r\nnew-fashioned kinds."\r\n\r\nAll this had become possible through Fulbert\'s legacy between his\r\nbrothers and unmarried sister, resulting in about L4000 apiece; besides\r\nwhich the firm had gone on prospering. Clement asked what was the\r\npresent circulation of the \'Pursuivant\', and as Lance named it,\r\nexclaimed--\r\n\r\n"What would old Froggatt have said, or even Felix?"\r\n\r\n"It is his doing," said Lance, "the lines he traced out."\r\n\r\n"My father says it is the writing with a conscience," said Gertrude.\r\n\r\n"Yes, with life, faculty, and point, so as to hinder the conscience from\r\nbeing a dead weight," added Geraldine.\r\n\r\n"No wonder," said Lance, "with such contributors as the Harewoods, and\r\nsuch a war-correspondent as Aubrey May."\r\n\r\nJust then the door began to open, and a black silk personage\r\ndisconsolately exclaimed--\r\n\r\n"Master Clement! Master Clem! Wherever is the boy gone, when he ought to\r\nbe in his bed?"\r\n\r\n"Ha, Sibby!" cried Lance, catching both hands, and kissing the cheery,\r\nwithered-apple cheeks of the old nurse. "You see your baby has begun to\r\nrun alone."\r\n\r\n"Ah, Master Lance, \'twas your doing. You always was the mischief."\r\n\r\n"No indeed, Sibby, the long boy did it all by himself, before ever I was\r\nin the house; but I\'ll bring him back again."\r\n\r\n"May I not stay a little longer, Sibby," said Clement, rather piteously,\r\n"to hear Lance sing? I have been looking forward to it all day."\r\n\r\n"If ye\'ll take yer jelly, sir," said Sibby, "as it\'s fainting ye\'ll be,\r\nand bringing our hearts into our mouths."\r\n\r\nSo Sibby administered her jelly, and heard histories of Lance\'s\r\nchildren, then, after exacting a promise that Master Lance should only\r\nsing once, she withdrew, as peremptory and almost as happy as in her\r\nonce crowded nursery.\r\n\r\n"What shall that once be, Clem?" asked Lance.\r\n\r\n"\'Lead, kindly Light.\'"\r\n\r\n"Is it not too much?" he inquired, glancing towards his widowed sister.\r\n\r\n"I want it as much as he does," she answered fervently.\r\n\r\nAt thirty-eight Lance\'s voice was, if possible, more perfect in\r\nsweetness, purity, and expression than it had been at twenty, and never\r\nhad the poem, connected with all the crises of their joint lives, come\r\nmore home to their hearts, filling them with aspiration as well as\r\nmemory.\r\n\r\nThen Lance helped his brother up, and was surprised, after those\r\ncheerful tones, to feel the weight so prone and feeble, that Gerald\'s\r\nsupport on the other side was welcome. Mrs. Grinstead followed to take\r\nGertrude to her room and find her children\'s photographs.\r\n\r\nThe two young people began to smile as soon as they were left alone.\r\n\r\n"Did you ever see Bexley?" asked Anna.\r\n\r\n"Yes--an awful hole," and both indulged in a merry laugh.\r\n\r\n"My mother mentions it with pious horror," said Anna.\r\n\r\n"Life is much more interesting when it is from hand to mouth," said\r\nGerald, with a yawn. "If I went in for sentiment, which I don\'t, it\r\nwould be for Fiddler\'s Ranch; though it is now a great city called\r\nViolinia, with everything like everything else everywhere."\r\n\r\n"Not Uncle Lance."\r\n\r\n"Certainly not. For a man with that splendid talent to bury it behind a\r\ncounter, mitigated by a common church organ, is as remarkable as absurd;\r\nthough he seems to thrive on it. It is a treat to see such innocent\r\nrapture, all genuine too!"\r\n\r\n"You worn-out old man!" laughed Anna. "Aunt Cherry has always said that\r\nself-abnegation is the secret of Uncle Lance\'s charm."\r\n\r\n"All very well in that generation--ces bons jours quand nous etions\r\nsi miserables," said Gerald, in his low, maundering voice. "Prosperity\r\nmeans the lack of object."\r\n\r\n"Does it?"\r\n\r\n"In these days when everything is used up."\r\n\r\n"Not to those two--"\r\n\r\n"Happy folk, never to lose the sense of achievement!"\r\n\r\n"Poor old man! You talk as if you were twenty years older than Uncle\r\nLance."\r\n\r\n"I sometimes think I am, and that I left my youth at Fiddler\'s Ranch."\r\n\r\nWherewith he strolled to the piano, and began to improvise something so\r\nyearning and melancholy that Anna was not sorry when her uncle came back\r\nand mentioned the tune the old cow died of.\r\n\r\nWas Gerald, the orphan of Fiddler\'s Ranch, to be always the spoilt child\r\nof prosperity and the creature of modern life, with more aspirations\r\nthan he saw how to fulfil, hampered as he was by duties, scruples, and\r\naffections?\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER III. -- DARBY AND JOAN\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                        My reason haply more\r\n     To bandy word for word and frown for frown;\r\n     But now I see our lances are but straws!\r\n                                        SHAKESPEARE.\r\n\r\n\r\nLancelot saw his brother\'s doctors the next morning, and communicated\r\nto his wife the upshot of the interview when they were driving to their\r\nmeeting in Mrs. Grinstead\'s victoria, each adorned with a big bunch of\r\nprimroses.\r\n\r\n"Two doctors! and not Tom," said Gertrude.\r\n\r\n"Both Brownlows. Tom knows them well, and wrote. One lives at the\r\nEast-end, and is sheet anchor to Whittingtonia. He began with Clement,\r\nbut made the case over to the cousin, the fashionable one, when we made\r\nthe great removal."\r\n\r\n"So they consulted?"\r\n\r\n"And fairly see the way out of the wood, though not by any means quit of\r\nit, poor Tina; but there\'s a great deal to be thankful for," said Lance,\r\nwith a long breath.\r\n\r\n"Indeed there is!" said the wife, with a squeeze of the hand. "But is\r\nthere any more to be feared?"\r\n\r\n"Everything," Lance answered; "heart chiefly, but the lungs are not\r\nsafe. He has been whirling his unfortunate machine faster and faster,\r\ntill no wonder the mainspring has all but broken down. His ideal always\r\nwas working himself to death, and only Felix could withhold him, so\r\nnow he has fairly run himself down. No rest from that tremendous parish\r\nwork, with the bothers about curates, school boards and board schools,\r\nand the threatened ritual prosecution, which came to nothing, but\r\nworried him almost as much as if it had gone on, besides all the trouble\r\nabout poor Alda, and the loss of Fulbert took a great deal out of him.\r\nWhen Somers got a living, there was no one to look after him, and he\r\nnever took warning. So when in that Stinksmeech Mission he breathed\r\npestiferous air and drank pestiferous water, he was finished up. They\'ve\r\ngot typhus down there--a very good thing too," he added vindictively.\r\n\r\n"I put it further back than Mr. Somers\' going," said Gertrude. "He never\r\nwas properly looked after since Cherry married. What is he to do now?"\r\n\r\n"Just nothing. If he wishes to live or have a chance of working again,\r\nhe must go to the seaside and vegetate, attempt nothing for the next six\r\nmonths, nor even think about St. Matthew\'s for a year, and, as they told\r\nme afterwards, be only able to go on cautiously even then."\r\n\r\n"How did he take it?"\r\n\r\n"He laid his head against Cherry, who was standing by his chair, put\r\nan arm round her, and said, \'There!\' and she gave him such a smile as\r\nI would not have missed seeing on any account. \'Mine now,\' she said.\r\n\'Best!\' he said. He is too much tired and worn out to vex himself about\r\nanything."\r\n\r\n"Where are they to go? Not to Ewmouth, or all the family worries would\r\ncome upon them. Alda would give him no peace."\r\n\r\n"Certainly not there. Brownlow advises Rockquay. His delicate brother is\r\na curate there, and it agrees with him better than any other place. So\r\nI am to go and see for a house for them. It is the very best thing for\r\nCherry."\r\n\r\n"Indeed it is. Was not she like herself last night? Anna says she has\r\nnever brightened up so much before! I do believe that if Clement goes on\r\nmending, the dear person will have a good time yet; nay, all the better\r\nnow that she is free to be a thorough-going Underwood again."\r\n\r\n"You Underwooder than Underwood!"\r\n\r\n"Exactly! I never did like--Yes, Lance, I am going to have it out. I do\r\nthink Clement would have done better to let her alone."\r\n\r\n"He did let her alone. He told me so."\r\n\r\n"Yes, but she let out to me the difference between that time and the one\r\nof the first offer when dear Felix could not keep back his delight at\r\nkeeping her; whereas she could not help seeing that she was a burthen on\r\nClement\'s soul, between fear of neglecting her and that whirl of parish\r\nwork, and that St. Wulstan\'s Hall was wanted for the girls\' school.\r\nBesides, Wilmet persuaded her."\r\n\r\n"She did. But it turned out well. The old man worshipped her, and she\r\nwas very fond of him."\r\n\r\n"Oh! very well in a way, but you know better, Lance."\r\n\r\n"Well, perhaps he did not begin young enough. He was a good, religious\r\nman, but Pro Ecclesia Dei had not been his war-cry from his youth, and\r\nhe did not understand, and thought it clerical; good, but outside his\r\nlife. Still, she was happy."\r\n\r\n"Petting, Society, Art, travels! I had rather have had our two first\r\nyears of tiffs than all that sort of happiness."\r\n\r\n"Tiffs! I thought we might have gone in for the Dunmow flitch."\r\n\r\n"You might! Do you mean that you forget how fractious and nasty and\r\nabominable I was, and how many headaches I gave you?"\r\n\r\n"Only what you had to put up with."\r\n\r\n"You don\'t recollect that first visit of my father\'s, when I was so\r\nfrightfully cross because you said we must ask the Lambs and Bruces to\r\ndinner? You came down in the morning white as a ghost, an owl in its\r\nblinkers, and though I know you would rather have died than have uttered\r\na word, no sooner were you off than he fell upon me with, \'Mrs. Daisy,\r\nI give you to understand that you haven\'t a husband made of such tough\r\ncommodity as you are used to at home, and if you worry him you will have\r\nto rue it.\'"\r\n\r\n"What an ass I must have looked! Did I really go playing the martyr?"\r\n\r\n"A very smiling martyr, pretending to be awfully jolly. I believe I\r\nrequited papa by being very cross."\r\n\r\n"At his interfering, eh? No wonder."\r\n\r\n"Chiefly to conceal my fright, but I did begin trying not to fly out as\r\nI used to do, and I was frightened whenever I did so."\r\n\r\n"Poor Daisy! That is why you always seemed to think every headache your\r\nfault."\r\n\r\n"The final effect--I won\'t say cure--was from that book on education\r\nwhich said that a child should never know a cross word or look between\r\nfather and mother. So you really have forgotten how horrid I could be?"\r\n\r\n"Or never felt it! But to return to our muttons. I can\'t believe\r\notherwise than that Cherry liked her old man, and if their parallel\r\nlines did not meet, she never found it out."\r\n\r\n"That is true. She liked him and leant on him, and was constantly\r\npleased and amused as well as idolized, but I don\'t think the deep\r\nplaces in her heart were stirred. Then there were constraints. He could\r\nnot stand Angela\'s freaks. And his politics--"\r\n\r\n"He was not so very much advanced."\r\n\r\n"Enough not to like the \'Pursuivant\' to lie about, nor her writing for\r\nit, even about art or books; nor did his old bones enjoy the rivers at\r\nVale Leston. Now you will see a rebound."\r\n\r\n"Or will she be too tender of him to do what he disliked?"\r\n\r\n"That will be the test. Now she has Clement, I expect an article will\r\ncome on the first book they read together."\r\n\r\nLance laughed, but returned to defend his sister.\r\n\r\n"Indeed she was attached to him. She was altogether drooping and crushed\r\nat Vale Leston in the autumn."\r\n\r\n"It was too soon. She was overdone with the multitudes, and in fact it\r\nwas more the renewal of the old sorrow than the new one. Anna tells me\r\nthat when they returned there was the same objectless depression. She\r\nwould not take up her painting again, she said it was of no use, there\r\nwas no one to care. I remember her being asked once to do something for\r\nthe Kyrle Society, and Mr. Grinstead did not like it, but now Clement\'s\r\nillness has made a break, and in a new place, with him to occupy her\r\ninstead of only that dawdling boy, you will see what you shall see!"\r\n\r\n"Ah! Gerald!" was the answer, in a doubtful, wistful tone, just as they\r\narrived.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IV. -- SLUM, SEA, OR SEASON\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     For in spite of all her mother had taught her,\r\n     She was really remarkably fond of the water.\r\n                                            JANE TAYLOR.\r\n\r\n\r\nMr. and Mrs. Lancelot Underwood had not long been gone to their meeting\r\nwhen there ran into the drawing-room a girl a year older than Anna, with\r\na taller, better figure, but a less clear complexion, namely Emilia, the\r\nadopted child of Mr. Travis Underwood. She found Anna freshening up the\r\nflowers, and Gerald in an arm-chair reading a weekly paper.\r\n\r\n"I knew I should find you," she cried, kissing Anna, while Gerald held\r\nout a finger or two without rising. "I thought you would not be gone\r\nprimrosing."\r\n\r\n"A perspicacity that does you credit," said Gerald, still behind his\r\npaper.\r\n\r\n"Are the cousins gone?" asked Anna.\r\n\r\n"Of course they are; Cousin Marilda, in a bonnet like a primrose bank,\r\nis to pick up Fernan somewhere, but I told her I was too true to my\r\nprinciples to let wild horses drag me there."\r\n\r\n"Let alone fat tame ones," ejaculated Gerald.\r\n\r\n"What did she say?" asked Anna.\r\n\r\n"Oh, she opened her eyes, and said she never should ask any one to\r\nact against principles, but principles in her time were for Church and\r\nState. Is Aunt Cherry in the vortex?"\r\n\r\n"No, she is reading to Uncle Clem, or about the house somewhere. I don\'t\r\nthink she would go now at least."\r\n\r\n"Uncle Grin\'s memory would forbid," muttered Gerald. "He saw a good many\r\nthings, though he was a regular old-fashioned Whig, an Edinburgh Review\r\nman."\r\n\r\n"You\'ve got the \'Censor\' there! Oh, let me see it. My respected cousins\r\ndon\'t think it good for little girls. What are you going to do?"\r\n\r\n"I believe the doctors want Uncle Clem to get a long leave of absence,\r\nand that we shall go to the seaside," replied Anna.\r\n\r\n"Oh! then you will come to us for the season! We reckon on it."\r\n\r\n"No, indeed, Emmie, I don\'t see how I can. Those two are not in the\r\nleast fit to go without some one."\r\n\r\n"But then mother is reckoning on our having a season together. You lost\r\nthe last."\r\n\r\nGerald laughed a little and hummed--\r\n\r\n\r\n          "If I were na to marry a rich sodger lad\r\n           My friends would be dismal, my minnie be mad."\r\n\r\n\r\n"Don\'t be so disgusting, Gerald! My friends have too much sense," cried\r\nAnna.\r\n\r\n"But it is true enough as regards \'my minnie,\'" said Emilia.\r\n\r\n"Well, eight daughters _are_ serious--baronet\'s daughters!" observed\r\nGerald in his teasing voice.\r\n\r\n"Tocherless lasses without even the long pedigree," laughed Anna. "Poor\r\nmother."\r\n\r\n"The pedigree is long enough to make her keep poor Vale Leston suitors\r\nat arm\'s length," mumbled Gerald; but the sisters did not hear him, for\r\nEmilia was exclaiming--\r\n\r\n"I mean to be a worker. I shall make Marilda let me have hospital\r\ntraining, and either go out to Aunt Angela or have a hospital here. Come\r\nand help me, Annie."\r\n\r\n"I have a hospital here," laughed Anna.\r\n\r\n"But, Nan dear, do come! You know such lots of swells. You would get one\r\ninto real society if one is to have it; Lady Rotherwood, Lady Caergwent,\r\nbesides all your delightful artist friends; and that would pacify\r\nmother, and make it so much pleasanter for me. Oh, if you knew what the\r\nevenings are!"\r\n\r\n"What an inducement!"\r\n\r\n"It would not be so if Annie were there. We should go out, and miss the\r\nhorrid aldermanic kind of dinners; and at home, when we had played the\r\ntwo old dears to sleep, as I have to do every night, while they nod over\r\ntheir piquet or backgammon, we could have some fun together! Now, Annie,\r\nyou would like it. You do care for good society, now don\'t you?"\r\n\r\n"I did enjoy it very much when Aunt Cherry went with me, but--"\r\n\r\n"No buts, no buts. You would come to the laundry girls, and the\r\ncooking-class, and all the rest with me, and we should not have a dreary\r\nmoment. Have you done fiddling over those flowers?"\r\n\r\n"Not yet; Vale Leston flowers, you know. Besides, Aunt Cherry can\'t bear\r\nthem not artistic."\r\n\r\n"Tidy is enough for Marilda. She does them herself, or the housekeeper;\r\nI can\'t waste time worrying over them."\r\n\r\n"That\'s the reason they always look like a gardener\'s prize bouquet at a\r\ncountry horticultural show," said Gerald.\r\n\r\n"What does it signify? They are only a testimony to Sir Gorgias Midas\'\r\nriches. I do hate orchids."\r\n\r\n"I wish them on their native rocks, poor things," said Gerald. "But poor\r\nFernan, you do him an injustice."\r\n\r\n"Oh, yes, he does quantities of good works, and so does Marilda, till I\r\nam quite sick of hearing of them! The piles of begging letters they get!\r\nAnd then they want them read and explained, and answered sometimes."\r\n\r\n"A means of good works," observed Gerald.\r\n\r\n"How would you like it? Docketing the crumbs from Dives\' table,"\r\nexclaimed Emilia.\r\n\r\n"A clerk or secretary could do it," said Anna.\r\n\r\n"Of course. Now if you have finished those flowers, do come out with me.\r\nI want to go into Ponter\'s Court, and Fernan won\'t let me go alone."\r\n\r\n"Have you any special object?" said Gerald lazily, "or is it to refresh\r\nyourself with the atmosphere?"\r\n\r\n"That dear boy--that Silky--has been taken up, and they\'ve sent him to a\r\nreformatory."\r\n\r\n"What a good thing!"\r\n\r\n"Yes, only I don\'t believe he did it! It was that nasty little Bill\r\nNosey. I am sure that he got hold of the lady\'s parcel, and stuffed it\r\ninto Silky\'s cap."\r\n\r\nEmilia spoke with a vehemence that made them both laugh, and Gerald\r\nsaid--\r\n\r\n"But if he is in a reformatory, what then? Are we to condole with his\r\nafflicted family, or bring Bill Nosey to confess?"\r\n\r\n"I thought I would see about it," said Emilia vaguely.\r\n\r\n"Well, I decline to walk in the steps of the police as an amateur! How\r\nabout the Dicksons?"\r\n\r\n"Drifted away no one knows where. That\'s the worst of it. Those poor\r\nthings do shift about so."\r\n\r\n"Yes. I thought we had got hold of those boys with the gymnasium. But\r\nwork wants regulating."\r\n\r\n"Oh, Gerald, I am glad you are coming. Now I am free! Just fancy, they\r\nhad a horrid, stupid, slow dinner-party on Easter Monday, of all the\r\nburgomasters and great One-eyers, and would not let me go down and sing\r\nto the match-girls!"\r\n\r\n"You had the pleasure of a study of the follies of wealth instead of the\r\nfollies of poverty."\r\n\r\n"Oh, to hear Mrs. Brown discourse on her troubles with her first,\r\nsecond, and third coachman!"\r\n\r\n"Was the irresistible Ferdinand Brown there?"\r\n\r\n"Yes, indeed, with diamond beetle studs and a fresh twist to his\r\nmoustache. It has grown long enough to be waxed."\r\n\r\n"How happy that fellow would be if he were obliged to dig! I should like\r\nto scatter his wardrobe over Ponter\'s Court."\r\n\r\n"There, Nan, have you finished?" as Anna swept the scattered leaves into\r\na basket. "Are you coming?"\r\n\r\n"I don\'t think I shall. You would only talk treason--well--social\r\ntreason all the way, and you don\'t want me, and Aunt Cherry would have\r\nto lunch alone, unless you wait till after."\r\n\r\n"Oh no, I know a scrumptious place for lunch," said Gerald. "You are\r\nright, Annie, one lady is quite enough on one\'s hands in such regions.\r\nYou have no jewellery, Emmie?"\r\n\r\n"Do you see any verdure about me?" she retorted.\r\n\r\nSo when Gerald\'s tardy movements had been overcome, off they started\r\nto their beloved slum, Emilia looking as if she were setting forth for\r\nElysium, and they were seen no more, even when five o\'clock tea was\r\nspread, and Anna making it for her Uncle Lance and his wife, who had\r\njust returned, full of political news; and likewise Lance said that he\r\nhad picked up some intelligence for his sister. He had met General Mohun\r\nand Sir Jasper Merrifield, both connections of the Underwoods.\r\n\r\nGeneral Mohun lived with his sister at Rockstone, Sir Jasper, his\r\nbrother-in-law, at Clipstone, not far off, and they both recommended\r\nRockquay and its bay "with as much praise," said Lance, "as the\r\ninhabitants ever give of a sea place."\r\n\r\n"Very good, except for the visitors," said Geraldine.\r\n\r\n"Exactly so. Over-built, over-everythinged, but still tolerable. The\r\nGeneral lives there with his sister, and promises to write to me about\r\nhouses, and Sir Jasper in a house a few miles off."\r\n\r\n"He is Bernard\'s father-in-law?"\r\n\r\n"Yes," said Gertrude; "and my brother Harry married a sister of Lady\r\nMerrifield, a most delightful person as ever I saw. We tell my father\r\nthat if she were not out in New Zealand we should all begin to be\r\njealous, he is so enthusiastic about Phyllis."\r\n\r\n"You have never told us how Dr. May is."\r\n\r\n"It is not easy to persuade him that he is not as young as he was," said\r\nGertrude.\r\n\r\n"I should say he was," observed Lance.\r\n\r\n"In heart--that\'s true," said Gertrude; "but he does get tired, and goes\r\nto sleep a good deal, but he likes to go and see his old patients, as\r\nmuch as they like to have him, and Ethel is always looking after him. It\r\nis just her life now that Cocksmoor has grown so big and wants her less.\r\nThings do settle themselves. If any one had told her twenty years ago\r\nthat Richard would have a great woollen factory living, and Cocksmoor\r\nand Stoneborough meet, and a separate parish be made, with a disgusting\r\npaper-mill, two churches, and a clergyman\'s wife--(what\'s the female of\r\nwhipper-snapper, Lance?)--who treats her as--"\r\n\r\n"As an extinct volcano," murmured Lance.\r\n\r\n"She would have thought her heart would be broken," pursued Gertrude.\r\n"Whereas now she owns that it is the best thing, and a great relief,\r\nfor she could not attend to Cocksmoor and my father both. We want her to\r\ntake a holiday, but she never will. Once she did when Blanche and Hector\r\ncame to stay, but he was not happy, hardly well, and I don\'t think she\r\nwill ever leave him again."\r\n\r\n"Mrs. Rivers is working still in London?"\r\n\r\n"Oh yes; I don\'t know what the charities of all kinds and descriptions\r\nwould do without her."\r\n\r\n"No," said Clement from his easy-chair. "She is a most valuable person.\r\nShe has such good judgment."\r\n\r\n"It has been her whole life ever since poor George Rivers\' fatal\r\naccident," said Gertrude. "I hardly remember her before she was married,\r\nexcept a sense that I was naughty with her, and then she was terribly\r\nsad. But since she gave up Abbotstoke to young Dickie May she has\r\nbeen much brighter, and she can do more than any one at Cocksmoor. She\r\nmanages Cocksmoor and London affairs in her own way, and has two houses\r\nand young Mrs. Dickie on her hands to boot."\r\n\r\n"How many societies is she chairwoman of?" said Lance. "I counted\r\ntwenty-four pigeon-holes in her cabinet one day, and I believe there was\r\na society for each of them; but I must say she is quiet about them."\r\n\r\n"It is fine to see the little hen-of-the-walk of Cocksmoor lower her\r\ncrest to her!" said Gertrude, "when Ethel has not thought it worth while\r\nto assert herself, being conscious of being an old fogey."\r\n\r\n"And your Bishop?"\r\n\r\n"Norman? I do believe he is coming home next year. I think he really\r\nwould if papa begged him, but that he--my father, I mean--said he would\r\nnever do so; though I believe nothing would be such happiness to him\r\nas to have Norman and Meta at home again. You know they came home on\r\nGeorge\'s death, but then those New Somersetas went and chose him Bishop,\r\nand there he is for good."\r\n\r\n"For good indeed," said Clement; "he is a great power there."\r\n\r\n"So are his books," added Geraldine. "Will Harewood sets great store by\r\nthem. Ah! I hear our young folks--or is that a carriage?"\r\n\r\nEmilia and Gerald came in simultaneously with Marilda, expanded into a\r\nportly matron, as good-humoured as ever, and better-looking than long\r\nago.\r\n\r\nShe was already insisting on Gerald\'s coming to a party of hers and\r\nbringing his violin, and only interrupted her persuasions to greet and\r\ncongratulate Clement.\r\n\r\nGerald, lying back on a sofa, and looking tired, only replied in a\r\nbantering, lazy manner.\r\n\r\n"Ah! if I asked you to play to the chimney-sweeps," she said, "you would\r\ncome fast enough, you idle boy. And you, Annie, do you know you are\r\ncoming to me for the season when your uncle and aunt go out of town?"\r\n\r\n"Indeed, Cousin Marilda, thank you, I don\'t know it, and I don\'t believe\r\nit."\r\n\r\n"Ah, we\'ll see! You haven\'t thought of the dresses you two are to have\r\nfor the Drawing-Room from Worth\'s, and Lady Caergwent to present you."\r\n\r\nAnna shook her head laughingly, while Gerald muttered--\r\n\r\n"Salmon are caught with gay flies."\r\n\r\nThey closed round the tea-table while Marilda sighed--\r\n\r\n"Alda\'s daughters are not like herself."\r\n\r\n"A different generation," said Geraldine.\r\n\r\n"See the Beggars Opera," said Lance--\r\n\r\n\r\n    "\'I wonder any man alive will ever rear a daughter,\r\n      For when she\'s drest with care and cost, and made all neat and gay,\r\n      As men should serve a cucumber, she throws herself away.\'"\r\n\r\n\r\n"Ah! your time has not come yet, Lance. Your little girls are at a\r\ncomfortable age."\r\n\r\n"There are different ways of throwing oneself away," said Clement.\r\n"Perhaps each generation says it of the next."\r\n\r\n"Emmie is not throwing herself away, except her chances," said Marilda.\r\n"If she would only think of poor Ferdy Brown, who is as good a fellow as\r\never lived!"\r\n\r\n"Not much chance of that," said Geraldine.\r\n\r\nTheir eyes all met as each had glanced at the tea-table, where Emilia\r\nand Gerald were looking over a report together, but Geraldine shook her\r\nhead. She was sure that Gerald did not think of his cousins otherwise\r\nthan as sisters, but she was by no means equally sure of Emilia, to whom\r\nhe was certainly a hero.\r\n\r\nAnna had not heard the last of the season. Her mother wrote to her, and\r\nalso to Geraldine, whom she piteously entreated not to let Anna lose\r\nanother chance, in the midst of her bloom, when she could get good\r\nintroductions, and Marilda would do all she could for her.\r\n\r\nBut Anna was obdurate. She should never see any one in society like\r\nUncle Clem. She had had a taste two years ago, and she wished for no\r\nmore. She should see the best pictures at the studios before leaving\r\ntown, and she neither could nor would leave her uncle and aunt to\r\nthemselves. So the matter remained in abeyance till the place of sojourn\r\nhad been selected and tried; and meantime Gerald spent what remained of\r\nthe Easter vacation in a little of exhibitions with Anna, a little of\r\nslumming with Emilia, a little of society impartially with swells and\r\nartists, and a good deal of amiable lounging and of modern reading of\r\nall kinds. His aunt watched, enjoyed, yet could not understand, his\r\nuncle said, that he was an undeveloped creature.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER V. -- A HAPPY SPRITE\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     Such trifles will their hearts engage,\r\n       A shell, a flower, a feather;\r\n     If none of these, a cup of joy\r\n       It is to be together.--ISAAC WILLIAMS.\r\n\r\n\r\nA retired soldier, living with his sister in a watering-place, is apt to\r\nform to himself regular habits, of which one of the most regular is the\r\nwalking to the station in quest of his newspaper. Here, then, it was\r\nthat the tall, grey-haired, white-moustached General Mohun beheld,\r\nemerging on the platform, a slight figure in a grey suit, bag in hand,\r\naccompanied by a pretty pink-cheeked, fair-haired, knicker-bockered\r\nlittle boy, whose air of content and elation at being father\'s companion\r\nmade his sapphire eyes goodly to behold.\r\n\r\n"Mr. Underwood! I am glad to see you."\r\n\r\n"I thought I would run down and look at the house you were so good as to\r\nmention for my sister, and let this chap have a smell of the sea."\r\n\r\nThere was a contention between General Mohun\'s hospitality and\r\nLancelot\'s intention of leaving his bag at the railway hotel, but the\r\nformer gained the day, the more easily because there was an assurance\r\nthat the nephew who slept at Miss Mohun\'s for the sake of his day-school\r\nwould take little Felix Underwood under his protection, and show him his\r\ncuriosities. The boy\'s eyes grew round, and he exclaimed--\r\n\r\n"Foolish guillemots\' eggs?"\r\n\r\n"He is in the egg stage," said his father, smiling.\r\n\r\n"I won\'t answer for guillemots," said the General, "but nothing seems to\r\ncome amiss to Fergus, though his chief turn is for stones."\r\n\r\nThere was a connection between the families, Bernard Underwood, the\r\nyoungest brother of Lance, having married the elder sister of the\r\naforesaid Fergus Merrifield. Miss Mohun, the sister who made a home for\r\nthe General, had looked out the house that Lance had come to inspect.\r\nAs it was nearly half-past twelve o\'clock, the party went round by the\r\nschool, where, in the rear of the other rushing boys, came Fergus, in\r\nall the dignity of the senior form.\r\n\r\n"Look at him," said the General, "those are honours one only gets once\r\nor twice in one\'s life, before beginning at the bottom again."\r\n\r\nFergus graciously received the introduction; and the next sound that\r\nwas heard was, "Have you any good fossils about you?" in a tone as if\r\nhe doubted whether so small a boy knew what a fossil meant; but little\r\nFelix was equal to the occasion.\r\n\r\n"I once found a shepherd\'s crown, and father said it was a fossil\r\nsea-urchin, and that they are alive sometimes."\r\n\r\n"Echini. Oh yes--recent, you mean. There are lots of them here. I don\'t\r\ngo in for those mere recent things," said Fergus, in a pre-Adamite tone,\r\n"but my sister does. I can take you down to a fisherman who has always\r\ngot some."\r\n\r\n"Father, may I? I\'ve got my eighteenpence," asked the boy, turning up\r\nhis animated face, while Fergus, with an air of patronage, vouched for\r\nthe honesty of Jacob Green, and undertook to bring his charge back in\r\ntime for luncheon.\r\n\r\nLancelot Underwood had entirely got over that sense of being in a false\r\nposition which had once rendered society distasteful to him. Many more\r\nmen of family were in the like position with himself than had been\r\nthe case when his brother had begun life; moreover, he had personally\r\nachieved some standing and distinction through the \'Pursuivant\'.\r\n\r\nGeneral Mohun was delighted with his companion, whom he presented to his\r\nsister as the speedy consequence of her recommendation. She was rather\r\nsurprised at the choice of an emissary, but her heart was won when she\r\nfound Mr. Underwood as deep in the voluntary school struggle as she\r\ncould be. Her brother held up his hands, and warned her that it was\r\nquite enough to be in the fray without going over it again, and that the\r\nbreath of parish troubles would frighten away the invalid.\r\n\r\n"I\'ll promise not to molest him," she said.\r\n\r\n"Besides," said Lance, "one can look at other people\'s parishes more\r\nphilosophically than at one\'s own."\r\n\r\nHe had begun to grow a little anxious about his boy, but presently from\r\nthe garden, up from the cliff-path, the two bounded in--little Felix\r\nwith the brightest of eyes and rosiest of cheeks, and a great ruddy,\r\nwhite-beaded sea-urchin held in triumph in his hands.\r\n\r\n"Oh, please," he cried, "my hands are too dirty to shake; we\'ve been\r\ndigging in the sand. It\'s too splendid! And they ought to have spines.\r\nWhen they are alive they walk on them. There\'s a bay! Oh, do come down\r\nand look for them."\r\n\r\n"And pray what would become of Aunt Cherry\'s house, sir? Miss Mohun, may\r\nI take him to make his paws presentable?"\r\n\r\n"A jolly little kid," pronounced Fergus, lingering before performing the\r\nsame operation, "but he has not got his mind opened to stratification,\r\nand only cares for recent rubbish. I wish it was a half-holiday, I would\r\nshow him something!"\r\n\r\nThe General, who had a great turn for children, and for the chase in\r\nany form, was sufficiently pleased with little Felix\'s good manners\r\nand bright intelligence about bird, beast, and fish, as to volunteer\r\nto conduct him to the region most favourable to spouting razor-fish and\r\nambulatory sea-urchins. The boy turned crimson and gasped--\r\n\r\n"Oh, thank you!"\r\n\r\n"Thank you indeed," said his father, when he had been carried off to\r\ninspect Fergus\'s museum in the lumber-room. "\'To see a real General out\r\nof the wars\' was one great delight in coming here, though I believe he\r\nwould have been no more surprised to hear that you had been at Agincourt\r\nthan in Afghanistan. \'It\'s in history,\' he said with an awe-stricken\r\nvoice."\r\n\r\nWhen Fergus, after some shouting, was torn from his beloved museum,\r\nFelix came down in suppressed ecstasy, declaring it the loveliest and\r\nmost delicious of places, all bones and stones, where his father must\r\ncome and see what Fergus thought was a megatherium\'s tooth. The long\r\nword was pronounced with a triumphant delicacy of utterance, amid\r\ndancing bounds of the dainty, tightly-hosed little legs.\r\n\r\nThe General and his companion went their way, while the other two had a\r\nmore weary search, resulting in the choice of not the most inviting of\r\nthe houses, but the one soonest available within convenient distance of\r\nchurch and sea. When it came to practical details, Miss Mohun was struck\r\nby the contrast between her companion\'s business promptness and the\r\nrapt, musing look she had seen when she came on him listening to the\r\nmeasured cadence of the waves upon the cliffs, and the reverberations\r\nin the hollows beneath. And when he went to hire a piano she, albeit\r\nunmusical, was struck by what her ears told her, yet far more by the\r\nlook of reverent admiration and wonder that his touch and his technical\r\nremarks brought out on the dealer\'s face.\r\n\r\n"Has that man, a bookseller and journalist, missed his vocation?" she\r\nsaid to herself. "Yet he looks too strong and happy for that. Has he\r\nconquered something, and been the better for it?"\r\n\r\nHe made so many inquiries about Fergus and his school, that she began\r\nto think it must be with a view to his own pretty boy, who came back\r\nall sea-water and ecstasy, with a store of limpets, sea-weeds, scales,\r\npurses, and cuttle-fish\'s backbones for the delectation of his sisters.\r\nAbove all, he was eloquent on the shell of a lacemaker crab, all\r\nover prickles, which he had seen hanging in the window of a little\r\ntobacconist. He had been so much fascinated by it that General Mohun\r\nregretted not having taken him to buy it, though it appeared to be\r\ndisplayed more for ornament than for sale.\r\n\r\n"It is a disgusting den," added the General, "with \'Ici on parle\r\nFrancais\' in the window, and people hanging about among whom I did not\r\nfancy taking the boy."\r\n\r\n"I know the place," said Miss Mohun. "Strange to say, it produces rather\r\na nice girl, under the compulsion of the school officer. She is plainly\r\nhalf a foreigner, and when Mr. Flight got up those theatricals last\r\nwinter she sung most sweetly, and showed such talent that I thought it\r\nquite dangerous."\r\n\r\n"I remember," said her brother. "She was a fairy among the clods."\r\n\r\nThe next morning, to the amazement of Miss Mohun, who thought herself\r\none of the earliest of risers, she not only met the father and son at\r\nearly matins, but found that they had been out for two hours enjoying\r\nsea-side felicity, watching the boats come in, and delighting in the\r\nbeauty of the fresh mackerel.\r\n\r\n"If they had not all been dead!" sighed the tender-hearted little\r\nfellow. "But I\'ve got my lacemaker for Audrey."\r\n\r\n"\'The carapace of a pagurus,\' as Fergus translated it." Adding, "I don\'t\r\nknow the species."\r\n\r\n"I can find out when father has time to let us look at the big natural\r\nhistory book in the shop," said Felix. "We must not look at it unless he\r\nturns it over, so Pearl and I are saving up to buy it."\r\n\r\n"For instance!" said his father, laughing.\r\n\r\n"Oh, I could not help getting something for them all," pleaded the boy,\r\n"and pagurus was not dear. At least he is, in the other way."\r\n\r\n"Take care, Fely--he won\'t stand caresses. I should think he was the\r\nfirst crab ever so embraced."\r\n\r\n"I wonder you got entrance so early in the day," said Miss Mohun.\r\n\r\n"The girl was sweeping out the shop, and singing the morning hymn,\r\nso sweetly and truly, that it would have attracted me anyway," said\r\nLancelot. "No doubt the seafaring men want \'baccy at all hours. She\r\nwas much amazed at our request, and called her mother, who came out in\r\nremarkable dishabille, and is plainly foreign. I can\'t think where\r\nI have seen such a pair of eyes--most likely in the head of some\r\nchorus-singer, indeed the voice had something of the quality. Anyway,\r\nshe stared at me to the full extent of them."\r\n\r\nSo Lancelot departed, having put in hand negotiations for a tolerable\r\nhouse not far from St. Andrew\'s Church, and studied the accommodation\r\navailable for horses, and the powers of the pianos on hire.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VI. -- ST. ANDREW\'S ROCK\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     Helpmates and hearthmates, gladdeners of gone years,\r\n       Tender companions of our serious days,\r\n     Who colour with your kisses, smiles, and tears,\r\n       Life\'s worn web woven over wonted ways.--LYTTON.\r\n\r\n\r\n"How does he seem now?" said Geraldine, as Lancelot came into the\r\ndrawing-room of St. Andrew\'s Rock at Rockquay, in the full glare of a\r\ncold east windy May evening.\r\n\r\n"Pretty well fagged out, but that does not greatly matter. I say,\r\nCherry, how will you stand this? Till I saw you in this den, I had no\r\nnotion how shabby, and dull, and ugly it is."\r\n\r\n"My dear Lance, if you did but know how refreshing it is to see anything\r\nshabby, and dull, and ugly," Mrs. Grinstead answered with imitative\r\ninflections, which set Anna Vanderkist off into a fit of laughter,\r\ninfecting both her uncle and aunt. The former gravely said--\r\n\r\n"If you had only mentioned it in time, I could have gratified you more\r\neffectually."\r\n\r\n"I suppose it is Aunt Cherry\'s charity," said Anna, recovering. "The\r\nreflection that but for her the poor natives would never have been able\r\nto go to their German baths."\r\n\r\n"Oh, no such philanthropy, my dear. It is homeliness, or rather\r\nhomeyness, that is dear to my bourgeoise mind. I was afraid of\r\nspick-and-span, sap-green aestheticism, but those curtains have done\r\ntheir own fading in pleasing shades, that good old sofa can be lain\r\nupon, and there\'s a real comfortable crack on that frame; while as to\r\nthe chiffonier, is not it the marrow of the one Mrs. Froggatt left us,\r\nwhere Wilmet kept all the things in want of mending?"\r\n\r\n"Ah! didn\'t you shudder when she turned the key?" said Lance.\r\n\r\n"Not knowing what was good for me."\r\n\r\n"But you will send for some of our things and make it nice," entreated\r\nAnna, "or Gerald will never stay here."\r\n\r\n"Never fear; we\'ll have it presentable by the vacation. As for Uncle\r\nClement, he would never see whether he was in a hermit\'s cell, if he\r\nonly had one arm-chair and one print from Raffaelle."\r\n\r\nThere was a certain arch ring in her voice that had long been absent,\r\nand Anna looked joyous as she waited on them both.\r\n\r\n"I am glad you brought her," said Lance, as she set off with Uncle\r\nClement\'s tea.\r\n\r\n"Yes, she would not hear of the charms of the season."\r\n\r\n"So much the better for her. She is a good girl, and will be all\r\nthe happier down here, as well as better. There\'s a whole hive of\r\nMerrifields to make merry with her; and, by the bye, Cherry, what should\r\nyou think of housing a little chap for the school here where Fergus\r\nMerrifield is?"\r\n\r\n"Your dear little Felix? Delightful!"\r\n\r\n"_Ouf_! No, he is booked for our grammar school."\r\n\r\n"The grammar school was not good for any of you, except the one whom\r\nnothing hurt."\r\n\r\n"It is very different now. I have full confidence in the head, and the\r\ntone is improved throughout. Till my boys are ready for a public school\r\nI had rather they were among our own people. No, Cherry, I can\'t do it,\r\nI can\'t give up the delight of him yet; no, I can\'t, nor lose his little\r\nvoice out of the choir, and have his music spoilt."\r\n\r\n"I don\'t wonder."\r\n\r\n"I don\'t think I spoil him. I really have flogged him once," said Lance,\r\nhalf wistfully, half playfully.\r\n\r\n"How proud you are of it."\r\n\r\n"It was for maltreating little Joan Vanderkist, though if it had only\r\nbeen her brother, I should have said, \'Go it, boys.\' It was not till\r\nafterwards that it turned out that Joan was too loyal not to bear the\r\npenalty of having tied our little Audrey into a chair to be pelted with\r\nhorse-chestnuts."\r\n\r\n"At Adrian\'s bidding?"\r\n\r\n"Of course. I fancy the Harewood boys set him on. And what I thought of\r\nwas sending Adrian here to be schooled at Mrs. Edgar\'s, boarded by\r\nyou, mothered by Anna, and altogether saved from being made utterly\r\ndetestable, as he will inevitably be if he remains to tyrannize over\r\nVale Leston."\r\n\r\n"Would his mother consent?"\r\n\r\n"You know he is entirely in Clement\'s power."\r\n\r\n"It would only be another worry for Clement."\r\n\r\n"He need not have much of him, and I believe he would prefer to have him\r\nunder his own eye; and Anna will think it bliss to have him, though what\r\nit may prove is another question. She will keep you from being too much\r\nbothered."\r\n\r\n"My dear Lance, will you never understand, that as furze and thistles\r\nare to a donkey, so are shabbiness and bother to me--a native element?"\r\n\r\nIn the morning Clement, raised on his pillows in bed, showed himself\r\nhighly grateful for the proposal about his youngest ward.\r\n\r\n"It is very good of you, Cherry," he said. "That poor boy has been very\r\nmuch on my mind. This is the way to profit by my enforced leisure."\r\n\r\n"That\'s the way to make me dread him. You were to lie fallow."\r\n\r\n"Not exactly. I have thirteen or fourteen years\' reading and thinking to\r\nmake up. I have done no more than get up a thing cursorily since I left\r\nVale Leston."\r\n\r\n"You are welcome to read and think, provided it is nothing more recent\r\nthan St. Chrysostom."\r\n\r\n"So here is the letter to Alda," giving it to her open.\r\n\r\n"Short and to the purpose," she said.\r\n\r\n"Alda submits to the inevitable," he said. "Don\'t appear as if she had a\r\nchoice."\r\n\r\n"Only mention the alleviations. No, you are not to get up yet. There\'s\r\nno place for you to sit in, and the east wind is not greatly mitigated\r\nby the sea air. Shall I send Anna to read to you?"\r\n\r\n"In half-an-hour, if she is ready then; meantime, those two books, if\r\nyou please."\r\n\r\nShe handed him his Greek Testament and Bishop Andrews, and repaired\r\nto the drawing-room, where she found Anna exulting in the decorations\r\nbrought from home, and the flowers brought in from an itinerant barrow.\r\n\r\n"I have been setting down what they must send us from home--your own\r\nchair and table, and the Liberty rugs, and the casts of St. Cecilia and\r\nlittle St. Cyrillus for those bare corners, and I am going out for a\r\nterra-cotta vase."\r\n\r\n"Oh, my dear, the room is charming; but don\'t let us get too dependent\r\non pretty things. They demoralize as much or more than ugly ones."\r\n\r\n"Do you mean that they are a luxury? Is it not right to try to have\r\neverything beautiful?"\r\n\r\n"I don\'t know, my dear."\r\n\r\n"Don\'t know!" exclaimed Anna.\r\n\r\n"Yes, my dear, I really get confused sometimes as to what is mere lust\r\nof the eye, and what is regard to whatever things are lovely. I believe\r\nthe principle is really in each case to try whether the high object or\r\nthe gratification of the senses should stand first."\r\n\r\n"Well," said Anna, laughing, "I suppose it is a high object not to\r\nalienate Gerald, as would certainly be done by the culture of the\r\nugly--"\r\n\r\n"Or rather of that which pretends to be the reverse, and is only\r\nfashion," said her aunt, who meantime was moving about, adding nameless\r\ngrace by her touch to all Anna\'s arrangements.\r\n\r\n"May I send for the things then?" said Anna demurely.\r\n\r\n"Oh yes, certainly; and you had better get the study arm-chair for your\r\nuncle. There is nothing so comfortable here. But I have news for you.\r\nWhat do you say to having little Adrian here, to go to school with the\r\nMerrifield boy?"\r\n\r\n"What fun! what fun! How delicious!" cried the sister, springing about\r\nlike a child.\r\n\r\n"I suspected that the person to whom he would give most trouble would\r\nfeel it most pleasure."\r\n\r\n"You don\'t know what a funny, delightful child he is! You didn\'t see him\r\ndriving all the little girls in a team four-in-hand."\r\n\r\nIt would be much to say that Mrs. Grinstead was enchanted by this\r\nproof of his charms; but they were interrupted by Marshall, the polite,\r\npatronizing butler, bringing in a card. Miss Mohun would be glad to know\r\nhow Mr. Underwood was, and whether there was anything that she could do\r\nfor Mrs. Grinstead.\r\n\r\nOf course she was asked to come in, and thus they met, the quick, slim,\r\nactive little spinster, whose whole life had been work, and the far\r\nyounger widow, whose vocation had been chiefly home-making. Their first\r\nsilent impressions were--\r\n\r\n"I hope she is not going to be pathetic," and--\r\n\r\n"She is enough to take one\'s breath away. But I think she has tact."\r\n\r\nAfter a few exchanges of inquiry and answer, Miss Mohun said--\r\n\r\n"My niece Gillian is burning to see you, after all your kindness to\r\nher."\r\n\r\n"I shall be very glad. This is not quite a land of strangers."\r\n\r\n"I told her I was sure you would not want her to-day."\r\n\r\n"Thank you. My brother is hardly up to afternoon visitors yet, and we\r\nhave not been able to arrange his refuge."\r\n\r\n"You have transformed this room."\r\n\r\n"Or Anna has."\r\n\r\nOn which Miss Mohun begged for Miss Vanderkist to meet her nieces by and\r\nby at tea. Gillian would call for her at four o\'clock, and show her the\r\nway that it was hoped might soon be quite natural to her.\r\n\r\n"Gillian\'s \'Aunt Jane,\'" said Anna, when the visitor had tripped out.\r\n"I never quite understood her way of talking of her. I think she worried\r\nher."\r\n\r\n"Your pronouns are confused, Annie. Which worried which? Or was it\r\nmutual?"\r\n\r\n"On the whole," laughed Anna back, "I prefer an aunt to be waited on to\r\none who pokes me up."\r\n\r\n"Aunt Log to Aunt Stork? To be poked will be wholesome."\r\n\r\nIn due time there was a ring at the front door; Gillian Merrifield was\r\nindulged with a kiss and smile from the heroine of her worship, and\r\nAnna found herself in the midst of a garland of bright girls. She was\r\na contrast to them, with her fair Underwood complexion, her short plump\r\nVanderkist figure, and the mourning she still wore for the fatherly\r\nUncle Grinstead; while the Merrifield party were all in different shades\r\nof the brunette, and wore bright spring raiment.\r\n\r\nThey had only just come down the steps when they were greeted by a young\r\nclergyman, who said he was on his way to inquire for Mr. Underwood, and\r\nas he looked as if he expected a reply from Miss Vanderkist, she said\r\nher uncle was better, and would be glad to see Mr. Brownlow when he had\r\nrested after his journey.\r\n\r\n"I hope he will not bother him," she added; "I know who he is now. He\r\nwas at Whittingtonia for a little while, but broke down. There\'s no\r\nremembering all the curates there. My aunt likes his mother. Does he\r\nbelong to this St. Andrew\'s Church?"\r\n\r\n"No, to the old one. You begin to see the tower."\r\n\r\n"Is that where you go?"\r\n\r\n"To the old one in the morning, but we have a dear little old chapel at\r\nClipstone, where Mr. Brownlow comes for the afternoon. It is all a good\r\ndeal mixed up together."\r\n\r\nThen another voice--\r\n\r\n"Do you think Mr. Underwood would preach to us? Mr. Brownlow says he\r\nnever heard any one like him."\r\n\r\nAnna stood still.\r\n\r\n"Nobody is to dare to mention preaching to Uncle Clement for the next\r\nsix months, or they will deserve never to hear another sermon in their\r\nlives."\r\n\r\n"What an awful penalty!"\r\n\r\n"For shame, Dolores! Now," as the short remainder of a steep street was\r\nsurmounted, "here, as you may see, is the great hotel, and next beyond\r\nis Aunt Jane\'s, Beechcroft. On beyond, where you see that queer tower,\r\nis Cliff House, Mr. White\'s, who married our Aunt Adeline, only they are\r\nin Italy; and then comes Carrara, Captain Henderson\'s--"\r\n\r\n"You are expected to rave about Mrs. Henderson\'s beauty," said the\r\ncousin, Dolores Mohun, as she opened Miss Mohun\'s gate, between two\r\ncopper beeches, while Anna listened to the merry tongues, almost\r\nbewildered by the chatter, so unlike the seclusion and silent watching\r\nof the last month; but when Mysie Merrifield asked, "Is it not quite\r\noverwhelming?" she said--\r\n\r\n"Oh no! it is like being among them all at Vale Leston. My sisters\r\nalways tell me my tongue wants greasing when I come down."\r\n\r\nHer tongue was to have exercise enough among the bevy of damsels who\r\nsurrounded her in Miss Mohun\'s drawing-room--four Merrifields, ranging\r\nfrom twenty-two to twelve years old, and one cousin, Dolores Mohun, with\r\na father in New Zealand.\r\n\r\n"Won\'t you be in the Mouse-trap?" presently asked number three, by name\r\nValetta.\r\n\r\n"If I did not know that she would drag it in!" cried Dolores.\r\n\r\n"What may it be?" asked Anna.\r\n\r\n"An essay society and not an essay society," was the lucid answer.\r\n"Gillian said you would be sure to belong to it."\r\n\r\n"I am afraid I can\'t if it takes much time," said Anna in a pleading\r\ntone. "My uncle is very far from well, and I have a good deal to do\r\nin the way of reading to him, and my little brother is coming to go to\r\nschool with yours."\r\n\r\n"Mr. Underwood brought his little boy," said Gillian. "Fergus said he\r\nwas one of the jolliest little chaps he had ever seen."\r\n\r\n"Uncle Reginald quite lost his heart to him," said Mysie, "and Aunt Jane\r\nsays he is a charming little fellow."\r\n\r\n"Oh, Felix Underwood!" said Anna. "Adrian is much more manly. You should\r\nsee him ride and climb trees."\r\n\r\nThe comparative value of brothers and cousins was very apparent.\r\nHowever, it was fixed that Anna should attend the Mouse-trap, and hear\r\nand contribute as she could find time.\r\n\r\n"I did the Erl King," said Valetta.\r\n\r\n\r\n          "\'Who rideth so late in the forest so wild?\r\n            It is the fond father and his loving child.\'"\r\n\r\n\r\n"Oh, spare us, Val," cried her sister Gillian.
