"Every one has done\r\nthat."\r\n\r\n"Gerald parodied mine," said Anna.\r\n\r\n\r\n          "\'Who trampeth so late in a shocking bad hat?\r\n           \'Tis the tipsy old father a-hugging his brat."\r\n\r\n\r\n"Oh, go on."\r\n\r\n"I can\'t recollect any more, but the Erl King\'s daughter is a\r\nbeggar-woman, and it ends with--\r\n\r\n\r\n          "I\'ll give thee a tanner and make him a bait,\r\n           So in the gin palace was settled his fate."\r\n\r\n\r\nSome of the party were scandalized, others laughed as much or more than\r\nthe effusion deserved.\r\n\r\n"We accept drawings," added another voice, "and if any one does anything\r\nextraordinarily good in that way, or in writing, it makes a little\r\nbook."\r\n\r\n"We have higher designs than that," said Gillian. "We want to print the\r\ncream."\r\n\r\n"For the benefit of the school board--no, the board school."\r\n\r\n"Oh! oh! Valetta!" cried the general voice.\r\n\r\n"The thing is," explained Gillian, "that we must build a new school for\r\nthe out-liers of St. Kenelm\'s, or \'my lords\' will be down on us, and we\r\nshall be swamped by board schools."\r\n\r\n"Aunt Jane is frantic about it," said Dolores Mohun.\r\n\r\n"There\'s no escape from school board worries!" exclaimed Anna. "They\r\nhelped to demolish Uncle Clement."\r\n\r\n"There is to be a sale of work, and a concert, and all sorts of jolly\r\nlarks," added Valetta.\r\n\r\n"Larks! Oh, Val!"\r\n\r\n"Larks aren\'t slang. They are in the dictionary," declared Valetta.\r\n\r\n"By the bye, she has not heard the rules of the Mice," put in Mysie.\r\n\r\n"I\'ll say them," volunteered Valetta the irrepressible. "Members of the\r\nMouse-trap never utter slang expressions, never wear live birds--I mean\r\ndead ones--in their hats."\r\n\r\n"Is an ostrich feather a live bird or a dead?" demanded Anna.\r\n\r\n"And," said Dolores, "what of the feather screens that the old Miss\r\nSmiths have been making all the winter-circles of pheasants\' feathers\r\nand peacocks\' eyes outside a border of drakes\' curls?"\r\n\r\n"Oh, like ostriches they don\'t count, since peacocks don\'t die, and\r\ndrakes and pheasants _must_," said Gillian.\r\n\r\n"We have been getting ready for this sale ever so long," said Mysie.\r\n"Aunt Jane has a working party every Friday for it."\r\n\r\n"The fit day," said Dolores, "for she is a perfect victim to other\r\npeople\'s bad work, and spends the evening in stitching up and making\r\npresentable the wretched garments they turn out."\r\n\r\n"The next rule--" began Valetta, but Gillian mercilessly cut her short.\r\n\r\n"You know clever people, Anna. Do you know how to manage about our\r\nMouse-trap book? Our bookseller here is a school-board man, all on\r\nthe wrong side, and when I tried to feel our way, he made out that the\r\nprinting and getting it up would cost a great deal more than we could\r\nrisk."\r\n\r\n"It is a pity that Uncle Lance is gone home," said Anna. "He could tell\r\nyou all about it."\r\n\r\n"Could you not write to him?"\r\n\r\n"Oh, yes, but I know he will want to see a specimen before he can make\r\nany estimate."\r\n\r\nIt was agreed that the specimen should be forthcoming on the next\r\noccasion, and Miss Mohun coming home, and tea coming in, the conference\r\nwas ended. Anna began to unravel the relationships.\r\n\r\nDolores Mohun was a niece of Lady Merrifield. She had lost her own\r\nmother early, and after living with the Merrifields for a year, had been\r\ntaken by her father to New Zealand, where he had an appointment. He\r\nwas a man of science, and she had been with him at Rotaruna during the\r\nterrible volcanic eruption, when there had been danger and terror enough\r\nto bring out her real character, and at the same time to cause an amount\r\nof intimacy with a young lady visitor little older than herself, which\r\nhad suddenly developed into a second marriage of her father. In this\r\nstate of things she had gladly availed herself of the home offered her\r\nat Clipstone, and had gone home under the escort of her Aunt Phyllis\r\n(Mrs. Harry May), who was going with her husband to spend a year in\r\nEngland. Dolores had greatly improved in all ways during her two years\'\r\nabsence, and had become an affectionate, companionable, and thoughtful\r\nmember of the Merrifield household, though still taking a line of her\r\nown.\r\n\r\nThe Kalliope whom Gillian had befriended, to her own detriment, was now\r\nthe very beautiful Mrs. Henderson, wife to the managing partner in the\r\nmarble works. She continued to take a great interest in the young women\r\nemployed in designing and mosaics, and had a class of them for reading\r\nand working. Dolores had been asked to tell first Aunt Jane\'s G. F. S.\r\n(Girl\'s Friendly Society) girls, and afterwards Mrs. Henderson\'s, about\r\nher New Zealand experiences and the earthquake, and this developed into\r\nregular weekly lectures on volcanoes and on colonies. She did these\r\nso well, that she was begged to repeat them for the girls at the High\r\nSchool, and she had begun to get them up very carefully, studying the\r\nbest scientific books she could get, and thinking she saw her vocation.\r\n\r\nMrs. Henderson was quite a power in the place. Her brother Alexis was\r\nan undergraduate, but had been promised a tutorship for the vacation.\r\nHe seldom appeared at Carrara, shrinking from what recalled the pain and\r\nshame that he had suffered; while Petros worked under Captain Henderson,\r\nand Theodore was still in the choir at St. Matthew\'s. Maura had become\r\nthe darling of Mr. White, and was much beloved by Mrs. White, though\r\nthere had been a little alarm the previous year, when Lord Rotherwood\r\nand his son came down to open a public park or garden on the top of the\r\ncliffs, where Lord Rotherwood\'s accident had occurred. Lord Ivinghoe,\r\na young Guardsman, had shown himself enough disposed to flirt with the\r\npretty little Greek to make the prudent very glad that her home was on\r\nthe Italian mountains.\r\n\r\nGillian was always Mrs. Henderson\'s friend, but Gillian\'s mind was full\r\nof other things. For her father had reluctantly promised, that if one\r\nof her little brothers got a scholarship at one of the public schools,\r\nGillian might fulfil her ardent desire of going to a ladies\' college.\r\nWilfred was a hopeless subject. It might be doubted if he could have\r\nsucceeded. He had apparently less brain power than some of the family,\r\nand he certainly would not exert what he had. His mother had dragged him\r\nthrough holiday tasks; but nobody else could attempt to make him work\r\nwhen at home, and Gillian\'s offers had been received with mockery or\r\nviolence. So all her hopes centred on Fergus, who, thanks to Aunt Jane\'s\r\nevening influence over his lessons, stood foremost in Mrs. Edgar\'s\r\nschool, and was to go up to try for election at Winchester College at\r\nthe end of the term. Were Gillian\'s hopes to be ruined by his devotion\r\nto the underground world?\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VII. -- THE HOPE OF VANDERKIST\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     A breath of air,\r\n     A bullock\'s low,\r\n     A bunch of flowers,\r\n     Hath power to call from everywhere\r\n     The spirit of forgotten hours--\r\n     Hours when the heart was fresh and young,\r\n     When every string in freedom sung,\r\n     Ere life had shed one leaf of green.\r\n                              JAMES RUSSELL LOWELL.\r\n\r\n\r\nThere had been some curiosity as to who would be thought worthy to bring\r\nthe precious little baronet to Rockquay, and there was some diversion,\r\nas well as joy, when it proved that no one was to be entrusted with\r\nhim but his eldest aunt, Mrs. Harewood, who was to bring him in Whitsun\r\nweek, so that he might begin with a half-term.\r\n\r\nThe arrival was a pretty sight, as the aunt rejoiced at seeing both her\r\nhosts at the front door to greet her, and as Anna held out her glad arms\r\nto the little brother who was the pride of the family.\r\n\r\n"Ha, Adrian, boy!" said the Vicar, only greeting with the hand, at sight\r\nof the impatient wriggle out of the embrace.\r\n\r\nIt was an open, sunburnt, ruddy face, and wide, fearless grey eyes that\r\nlooked up to him, the bullet head in stiff, curly flaxen hair held aloft\r\nwith an air of "I am monarch of all I survey," and there was a tone\r\nof equality in the "Holloa, Uncle Clement," to the tall clergyman who\r\ntowered so far above the sturdy little figure.\r\n\r\nPresently on the family inquiries there broke--\r\n\r\n"I say, Annie, where\'s the school?"\r\n\r\n"At the foot of this hill."\r\n\r\n"I want to see it" (imperiously).\r\n\r\n"You must have some tea first."\r\n\r\n"Then you are glad to come, Adrian?" said Mrs. Grinstead.\r\n\r\n"Yes, Aunt Cherry. It is high time I was away from such a lot of\r\nwomen-folk," he replied, with his hands in his pockets, and his legs set\r\nlike a little colossus.\r\n\r\nAnna had no peace till, after the boy had swallowed a tolerable amount\r\nof bread-and-butter and cake, she took him out, and then Mrs. Harewood\r\nhad to explain his mother\'s urgent entreaties that the regime at Vale\r\nLeston should be followed up, and the boy see only such habits as would\r\nbe those of total abstainers.\r\n\r\nPoor woman! as her brother and sisters knew, there was reason to believe\r\nthat the vice which had been fatal to her happiness and her husband\'s\r\nlife, had descended to him from Dutch forefathers, and there was the\r\nless cause for wonder at the passionate desire to guard her son from it.\r\nAlmost all her family had been water-drinkers from infancy, and though\r\nMajor Harewood called teetotalism a superstitious contempt of Heaven\'s\r\ngood gifts, and disapproved of supplementing the baptismal vow, his\r\nbrother the Rector had found it expedient, for the sake of the parish,\r\nto embrace formally the temperance movement, and thus there had\r\nbeen little difficulty in giving way to Alda\'s desire that, at the\r\nluncheon-table, Adrian should never see wine or beer, and she insisted\r\nthat the same rule should prevail at Rockquay.\r\n\r\nClement had taken the pledge when a lad of sixteen, and there were those\r\nwho thought that, save for his persistence under warnings of failing\r\nstrength, much of his present illness might have been averted, with all\r\nthe consequent treatment. He believed in total abstinence as safer for\r\nhis ward, but he thought that the time had come for training, in seeing\r\nwithout partaking. Wilmet agreed, and said she had tried to persuade\r\nher sister; but she had only caused an hysterical agitation, so that\r\nweakness as usual gained the victory, and she had all but promised to\r\nbring the boy home again unless she could exact an engagement.\r\n\r\n"To follow the Vale Leston practice at his early dinner," said\r\nGeraldine.\r\n\r\n"That may be," said Clement; "but I do not engage not to have the matter\r\nout with him if I see that it is expedient."\r\n\r\n"I am only doubtful how Gerald will take it," said his sister.\r\n\r\n"Gerald has always been used to it at Vale Leston," said Wilmet.\r\n\r\n"True, but there he is your guest. Here he will regard himself as at\r\nhome. However, he is a good boy, and will only grumble a little for\r\nappearance sake."\r\n\r\n"I should hope so," said Wilmet severely.\r\n\r\n"How is the Penbeacon affair going on?" asked Clement.\r\n\r\n"Oh, Clem, I did not think you had heard of it."\r\n\r\n"I had a letter in the middle of the mission, but I could not answer it\r\nthen, and it seems to have been lost."\r\n\r\nGeraldine pronounced it the straw that broke the camel\'s back, when she\r\nheard of the company that only waited to dig china clay out of Penbeacon\r\nand wash it in the Ewe till they could purchase a slice of the hill\r\npertaining to the Vale Leston estate. Major Harewood had replied that\r\nhis fellow-trustee was too ill to attend to business, and that the\r\nmatter had better be let alone till the heir attained his majority.\r\n\r\n"Shelved for the present," said Mrs. Grinstead. "Fancy Ewe and Leston\r\ncontaminated!"\r\n\r\n"John talks to the young engineer, Mr. Bramshaw, and thinks that may be\r\nprevented; but that is not the worst," said Wilmet; "it would change the\r\nwhole face of the parish, and bring an influx of new people."\r\n\r\n"Break up Penbeacon and cover it with horrible little new houses. Men\r\nlike Walsh never see a beautiful place but they begin to think how to\r\ndestroy it."\r\n\r\n"Well, Cherry, you have the most influence with Gerald, but he talks to\r\nthe girls of our having no right to keep the treasures of the hills for\r\nour exclusive pleasure."\r\n\r\n"It is not exclusive. Half the country disports itself there. It is the\r\ngreat place for excursions."\r\n\r\n"Then he declares that it is a grave matter to hinder an industry that\r\nwould put bread into so many mouths, and that fresh outlets would be\r\ngood for the place; something too about being an obstruction, and the\r\nrights of labour."\r\n\r\n"Oh, I know what that means. It is only teasing the cousinhood when they\r\nfall on him open-mouthed," said Geraldine, with a laugh, though with\r\na qualm of misgiving at her heart, while Clement sat listening and\r\nthinking.\r\n\r\nMrs. Harewood farther explained, that she hoped either that Gerald would\r\nmarry, or that her sister would make a home for him at the Priory. It\r\nthen appeared that Major Harewood thought it would be wise to leave the\r\nyoung man to manage the property for himself without interference; and\r\nthat the uncle to whom the Major had become heir was anxious to have the\r\nfamily at hand, even offering to arrange a house for Lady Vanderkist.\r\n\r\n"A year of changes," sighed Geraldine; "but this waiting time seems\r\nintended to let one gather one\'s breath."\r\n\r\nBut Wilmet looked careworn, partly, no doubt, with the harass of\r\ncontinual attention to her sister Alda, who, though subdued and improved\r\nin many important ways, was unavoidably fretful from ill-health, and\r\ndisposed to be very miserable over her straitened means, and the future\r\nlot of her eight daughters, especially as the two of the most favourable\r\nage seemed to resign their immediate chances of marrying. Moreover,\r\nthough all began life as pretty little girls, they had a propensity to\r\nturn into Dutchwomen as they grew up, and Franceska, the fifth in age,\r\nwas the only one who renewed the beauty of the twin sisters.\r\n\r\nAlda was not, however, Wilmet\'s chief care, though of that she did\r\nnot speak. She was not happy at heart about her two boys. Kester was a\r\nsoldier in India, not actually unsteady, but not what her own brothers\r\nhad been, and Edward was a midshipman, too much of the careless,\r\nwild sailor. Easy-going John Harewood\'s lax discipline had not been\r\nsuccessful with them in early youth, and still less had later severity\r\nand indignation been effectual.\r\n\r\n"I am glad you kept Anna," said Mrs. Harewood, "though Alda is very much\r\ndisappointed that she is not having a season in London."\r\n\r\n"She will not take it," said Geraldine. "She insists that she prefers\r\nUncle Clem to all the fine folk she might meet; and after all, poor\r\nMarilda\'s acquaintance are not exactly the upper ten thousand."\r\n\r\n"Poor Marilda! You know that she is greatly vexed that Emilia is bent on\r\nbeing a hospital nurse, or something like it, and only half yields to go\r\nout with her this summer in very unwilling obedience."\r\n\r\n"Yes, I know. She wants to come here, and I mean to have her before the\r\nlong vacation for a little while. We heard various outpourings, and I\r\ncannot quite think Miss Emilia a grateful person, though I can believe\r\nthat she does not find it lively at home."\r\n\r\n"She seems to be allowed plenty of slum work, as it is the fashion to\r\ncall it, and no one can be more good and useful than Fernan and Marilda,\r\nso that I call it sheer discontent and ingratitude not to put up with\r\nthem!"\r\n\r\n"Only modernishness, my dear Wilmet. It is the spirit of the times, and\r\nthe young things can\'t help it."\r\n\r\n"You don\'t seem to suffer in that way--at least with Anna."\r\n\r\n"No; Anna is a dear good girl, and Uncle Clem is her hero, but I am very\r\nglad she has nice young companions in the Merrifields, and an excitement\r\nin prospect in this bazaar."\r\n\r\n"I thought a bazaar quite out of your line."\r\n\r\n"There seems to be no other chance of saving this place from board\r\nschools. Two thousand pounds have to be raised, and though Lord\r\nRotherwood and Mr. White, the chief owners of property, have done,\r\nand will do, much, there still remains greater need than a fleeting\r\npopulation like this can be expected to supply, and Clement thinks that\r\na bazaar is quite justifiable in such a case."\r\n\r\n"If there is nothing undesirable," said Mrs. Harewood, in her original\r\n"what it may lead to" voice.\r\n\r\n"Trust Lady Merrifield and Jane Mohun for that! I am going to take you\r\nto call upon Lilias Merrifield."\r\n\r\n"Yea; I shall wish to see the mother of Bernard\'s wife."\r\n\r\nClement, who went with them, explained to his somewhat wondering elder\r\nsister that he thought safeguards to Christian education so needful,\r\nthat he was quite willing that, even in this brief stay, all the aid\r\nin their power should be given to the cause at Rockquay. Nay, as\r\nhe afterwards added to Wilmet, he was very glad to see how much it\r\ninterested Geraldine, and that the work for the Church and the congenial\r\nfriends were rousing her from her listless state of dejection.\r\n\r\nLady Merrifield and Mrs. Harewood were mutually charmed, perhaps all the\r\nmore because the former was not impassioned about the bazaar. She said\r\nshe had been importuned on such subjects wherever she had gone, and had\r\nlearnt to be passive; but her sister Jane was all eagerness, and her\r\nyounger young people, as she called the present half of her family, were\r\nin the greatest excitement over their first experience of the kind.\r\n\r\n"Well is it for all undertakings that there should always be somebody to\r\nwhom all is new, and who can be zealous and full of delight."\r\n\r\n"By no means surtout point de zele," returned Geraldine.\r\n\r\n"As well say no fermentation," said Lady Merrifield.\r\n\r\n"A dangerous thing," said Clement.\r\n\r\n"But sourness comes without it, or at least deadness," returned his\r\nsister.\r\n\r\nWherewith they returned to talk of their common relations.\r\n\r\nIt was like a joke to the brother and sisters, that their Bernard should\r\nbe a responsible husband and father, whereas Lady Merrifield\'s notion of\r\nhim was as a grave, grand-looking man with a splendid beard.\r\n\r\nFergus Merrifield was asked to become the protector of Adrian, whereat\r\nhe looked sheepish; but after the round of pets had been made he\r\ninformed his two youngest sisters, Valetta and Primrose, that it was the\r\ncheekiest little fellow he had ever seen, who would never know if he was\r\nbullied within an inch of his life; not that he (Fergus) should let the\r\nfellows do it.\r\n\r\nSo though until Monday morning Anna was the slave of her brother, doing\r\nher best to supply the place of the six devoted sisters at home, the\r\nyoung gentleman ungratefully announced at breakfast--\r\n\r\n"I don\'t want gy-arls after me," with a peculiarly contemptuous twirl at\r\nthe beginning of the word; "Merrifield is to call for me."\r\n\r\nAnna, who had brought down her hat, looked mortified.\r\n\r\n"Never mind, Annie," said her uncle, "he will know better one of these\r\ndays."\r\n\r\n"No, I shan\'t," said Adrian, turning round defiantly. "If she comes\r\nbothering after me at dinner-time I shall throw my books at her--that\'s\r\nall! There\'s Merrifield," and he banged out of the room.\r\n\r\n"Never mind," again said his uncle, "he has had a large dose of the\r\nfeminine element, and this is his swing out of it."\r\n\r\nHopes, which Anna thought cruel, were entertained by her elders that\r\nthe varlet would return somewhat crestfallen, but there were no such\r\nsymptoms; the boy re-appeared in high spirits, having been placed well\r\nfor his years, but not too well for popularity, and in the playground\r\nhe had found himself in his natural element. The boys were mostly of his\r\nown size, or a little bigger, and bullying was not the fashion. He had\r\nheard enough school stories to be wary of boasting of his title, and\r\nas long as he did not flaunt it before their eyes, it was regarded as\r\nrather a credit to the school.\r\n\r\nMerrifield was elated at the success of his protege, and patronized\r\nhim more than he knew, accepting his devotion in a droll, contemptuous\r\nmanner, so that the pair were never willingly apart. As Fergus slept at\r\nhis aunt\'s during the week, the long summer evenings afforded splendid\r\nopportunities for what Fergus called scientific researches in the\r\nquarries and cliffs. It was as well for Lady Vanderkist\'s peace of mind\r\nthat she did not realize them, though Fergus was certified by his family\r\nto be cautious and experienced enough to be a safe guide. Perhaps people\r\nwere less nervous about sixth sons than only ones.\r\n\r\nThere was, indeed, a certain undeveloped idea held out that some of the\r\nduplicates of Fergus\'s precious collection might be arranged as a\r\nsample of the specimens of minerals and fossils of Rockquay at the\r\nlong-talked-of sale of work.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VIII. -- THE MOUSE-TRAP\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n  If a talent be a claw, look how he claws him with a talent.\r\n                                        Love\'s Labour\'s Lost.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe young ladies were truly in an intense state of excitement about the\r\nsale of work, especially about the authorship; and Uncle Lancelot having\r\npromised to send an estimate, a meeting of the Mouse-trap was convened\r\nto consider of the materials, and certainly the mass of manuscript\r\ncontributed at different times to the Mouse-trap magazine was appalling\r\nto all but Anna, who knew what was the shrinkage in the press.\r\n\r\nShe, however, held herself bound not to inflict on her busy uncle the\r\nreading of anything entirely impracticable, so she sat with a stern and\r\ncritical eye as the party mustered in Miss Mohun\'s drawing-room, and\r\nGillian took the chair.\r\n\r\n"The great design," said she impressively, "is that the Mouse-trap\r\nshould collect and print and publish a selection for the benefit of the\r\nschool."\r\n\r\nThe Mice vehemently applauded, only Miss Norton, the oldest of the\r\nparty, asked humbly--\r\n\r\n"Would any one think it worth buying?"\r\n\r\n"Oh, yes," cried Valetta. "Lots of translations!"\r\n\r\n"The Erl King, for instance," put in Dolores Mohun.\r\n\r\n"If Anna would append the parody," suggested Gillian.\r\n\r\n"Oh, parodies are--are horrid," said Mysie.\r\n\r\n"Many people feel them so," said Gillian, "but to others I think they\r\nare almost a proof of love, that they can make sport with what they\r\nadmire so much."\r\n\r\n"Then," said Mysie, "there\'s Dolores\' Eruption!"\r\n\r\n"What a nice subject," laughed Gillian. "However, it will do\r\nbeautifully, being the description of the pink terraces of that place\r\nwith the tremendous name in New Zealand."\r\n\r\n"Were you there?" cried Anna.\r\n\r\n"Yes. I always wonder how she can look the same after such adventures,"\r\nsaid Mysie.\r\n\r\n"You know it is much the same as my father\'s paper in the Scientific\r\nWorld," said Dolores.\r\n\r\n"Nobody over reads that, so it won\'t signify," was the uncomplimentary\r\nverdict.\r\n\r\n"And," added Mysie, "Mr. Brownlow would do a history of Rockquay, and\r\nthat would be worth having."\r\n\r\n"Oh yes, the dear ghost and all!" cried Valetta.\r\n\r\nThe acclamation was general, for the Reverend Armine Brownlow was the\r\ncynosure curate of the lady Church-helpers, and Mysie produced as a\r\nprecious loan, to show what could be done, the volume containing the\r\nchoicest morceaux of the family magazine of his youth, the Traveller\'s\r\nJoy, in white parchment binding adorned with clematis, and emblazoned\r\nwith the Evelyn arms on one side, the Brownlow on the other, and full of\r\nphotographs and reproductions of drawings.\r\n\r\n"Much too costly," said the prudent.\r\n\r\n"It was not for sale," said Mysie, obviously uneasy while it was being\r\nhanded round.\r\n\r\n"Half-a-crown should be our outside price," said Gillian.\r\n\r\n"Or a shilling without photographs, half-a-crown with," was added.\r\n\r\n"Shall I ask Uncle Lance what can be done for how much?" asked Anna, and\r\nthis was accepted with acclamation, but, as Gillian observed, they had\r\nyet got no further than Dolores\' Eruption and the unwritten history.\r\n\r\n"There are lots of stories," said Kitty Varley; "the one about Bayard\r\nand all the knights in Italy."\r\n\r\n"The one," said Gillian, "where Padua got into the kingdom of Naples,\r\nand the lady of the house lighted a lucifer match, besides the horse who\r\ndrained a goblet of red wine."\r\n\r\n"You know that was only the pronouns," suggested the author.\r\n\r\n"Then there\'s another," added Valetta, "called Monrepos--such a beauty,\r\nwhen the husband was wounded, and died at his wife\'s feet just as the\r\nsun gilded the tops of the pines, and she died when the moon set, and\r\nthe little daughter went in and was found dead at their feet."\r\n\r\n"No, no, Val," said Gillian. "Here is a story that Bessie has sent\r\nus--really worth having."\r\n\r\n"Mesa! Oh, of course," was the acclamation.\r\n\r\n"And here\'s a little thing of mine," Gillian added modestly, "about the\r\ndevelopment of the brain."\r\n\r\nAt this there was a shout.\r\n\r\n"A little thing! Isn\'t it on the differential calculus?"\r\n\r\n"Really, I don\'t see why Rockquay should not have a little rational\r\nstudy!"\r\n\r\n"Ah! but the present question is what Rockquay will buy; to further\r\nfuture development it may be, but I am afraid their brains are not yet\r\ndeveloped enough," said Emma Norton.\r\n\r\n"Well then, here is the comparison between Euripides and Shakespeare."\r\n\r\n"That\'s what you read papa and everybody to sleep with," said Valetta\r\npertly.\r\n\r\n"Except Aunt Lily, and she said she had read something very like it in\r\nSchlegel," added Dolores.\r\n\r\n"You must not be too deep for ordinary intellects, Gillian," said Emma\r\nNorton good-naturedly. "Surely there is that pretty history you made out\r\nof Count Baldwin the Pretender."\r\n\r\n"That! Oh, that is a childish concern."\r\n\r\n"The better fitted for our understandings," said Emma, disinterring it,\r\nand handing it over to Anna, while Mysie breathed out--\r\n\r\n"Oh! I did like it! And, Gill, where is Phyllis\'s account of the Jubilee\r\ngaieties and procession last year?"\r\n\r\n"That would make the fortune of any paper," said Anna.\r\n\r\n"Yes, if Lady Rotherwood will let it be used," said Gillian. "It is\r\nreally delightful and full of fun, but I am quite sure that her name\r\ncould not appear, and I do not expect leave to use it."\r\n\r\n"Shall I write and ask?" said Mysie.\r\n\r\n"Oh yes, do; if Cousin Rotherwood is always gracious, it is specially to\r\nyou."\r\n\r\n"I wrote to my cousin, Gerald Underwood," said Anna, "to ask if he had\r\nanything to spare us, though I knew he would laugh at the whole concern,\r\nand he has sent down this. I don\'t quite know whether he was in earnest\r\nor in mischief."\r\n\r\nAnd she read aloud--\r\n\r\n\r\n          "Dreaming of her laurels green,\r\n           The learned Girton girl is seen,\r\n           Or under the trapeze neat\r\n           Figuring as an athlete.\r\n\r\n           Never at the kitchen door\r\n           Will she scrub or polish more;\r\n           No metaphoric dirt she eats,\r\n           Literal dirt may form her treats.\r\n\r\n           Mary never idle sits,\r\n           Home lessons can\'t be learnt by fits;\r\n           Hard she studies all the week,\r\n           Answers with undaunted cheek.\r\n\r\n           When to exam Mary goes,\r\n           Smartly dressed in stunning clothes,\r\n           Expert in algebraic rule,\r\n           Best pupil-teacher of her school.\r\n\r\n           Oh, how clever we are found\r\n           Who live on England\'s happy ground,\r\n           Where rich and poor and wretched may\r\n           Be drilled in Whitehall\'s favoured way."\r\n\r\n\r\nThere was a good deal of laughter at this parody of Jane Taylor\'s\r\nVillage Girl, though Mysie was inclined to be shocked as at something\r\nprofane.\r\n\r\n"Then what will you think of this?" said Anna, beginning gravely to read\r\naloud The Inspector\'s Tour.\r\n\r\nIt was very clever, so clever that Valetta and Kitty Varley both\r\nlistened as in sober earnest, never discovering, or only in flashes\r\nlike Mysie, that it was really a satire on all the social state of the\r\ndifferent European nations, under the denomination of schools. One being\r\ndepicted as highly orthodox, but much given to sentence insubordination\r\nto dark cold closets; another as given to severe drill, but neglecting\r\nmanners; a third as repudiating religious teaching, and now and\r\nthen preparing explosions for the masters--no, teachers. The various\r\nconversations were exceedingly bright and comical; and there were\r\nbrilliant hits at existing circumstances, all a little in a socialistic\r\nspirit, which made Anna pause as she read. She really had not perceived\r\ntill she heard it in her own voice and with other ears how audacious it\r\nwas, especially for a school bazaar.\r\n\r\nDolores applauded with her whole heart, but owned that it might be\r\ntoo good for the Mouse-trap, it would be too like catching a monkey!\r\nGillian, more doubtfully, questioned whether it would "quite do"; and\r\nMysie, when she understood the allusions, thought it would not. Emma\r\nNorton was more decided, and it ended by deciding that the paper should\r\nbe read to the elders at Clipstone, and their decision taken before\r\nsending it to Uncle Lance.\r\n\r\nThe spirits of the Muscipula party rose as they discussed the remaining\r\nMSS., but these were not of the highest order of merit; and Anna thought\r\nthat the really good would be sufficient; and all the Underwood kith and\r\nkin had sufficient knowledge of the Press through their connection with\r\nthe \'Pursuivant\' to be authorities on the subject.\r\n\r\n"Fergus has some splendid duplicate ammonites for me and bits of\r\ncrystal," said Mysie.\r\n\r\n"Oh, do let Fergus alone," entreated Gillian. "He is almost a\r\npetrifaction already, and you know what depends on it."\r\n\r\n"My sister is coming next week for a few days," said Anna. "She is very\r\nclever, and may help us."\r\n\r\nEmilia was accordingly introduced to the Mice, but she was not very\r\ntolerant of them. Essay societies, she said, were out of date, and she\r\nthought the Rockquay young ladies a very country-town set.\r\n\r\n"You don\'t know them, Emmie," said Anna. "Gillian and Dolores are very\r\nremarkable girls, only--"\r\n\r\n"Only they are kept down by their mothers, I suppose. Is that the\r\nreason they don\'t do anything but potter after essay societies and\r\nSunday-schools like our little girls at Vale Leston? Why, I asked\r\nGillian, as you call her, what they were doing about the Penitents\'\r\nHome, and she said her mother and Aunt Jane went to look after it, but\r\nnever talked about it."\r\n\r\n"You know they are all very young."\r\n\r\n"Young indeed! How is one ever to be of any use if mothers and people\r\nare always fussing about one\'s being young?"\r\n\r\n"One won\'t always be so--"\r\n\r\n"They would think so, like the woman of a hundred years old, who said\r\non her daughter\'s death at eighty, \'Ah, poor girl, I knew I never should\r\nrear her!\' How shall I get to see the Infirmary here?"\r\n\r\n"Miss Mohun would take you."\r\n\r\n"Can\'t I go without a fidgety old maid after me?"\r\n\r\n"I\'ll tell you what I wish you would do, Emmie. Write an account of one\r\nof your hospital visits, or of the match-girls, for the Mouse-trap. Do!\r\nYou know Gerald has written something for it."\r\n\r\n"He! Why he has too much sense to write for your voluntary schools. Or\r\nit would be too clever and incisive for you. Ah! I see it was so by your\r\nface! What did he send you? Have you got it still?"\r\n\r\n"We have really a parody of his which is going in--The Girton Girl.\r\nNow, Emmie, won\'t you? You have told me such funny things about your\r\nmatch-girls."\r\n\r\n"I do not mean to let them be turned into ridicule by your prim,\r\ndecorous swells. Why, I unfortunately told Fernan Brown one story--about\r\ntheir mocking old Miss Bruce with putting on imitation spectacles--and\r\nit has served him for a cheval de bataille ever since! Oh, my dear Anna,\r\nhe gets more hateful than ever. I wish you would come back and divert\r\nhis attention."\r\n\r\n"Thank you."\r\n\r\n"Don\'t you think we could change? You could go and let Marilda fuss with\r\nyou, now that Uncle Clem and Aunt Cherry are so well, and I could look\r\nafter Adrian, and go to the Infirmary, and the penitents, and all that\r\nthese people neglect; maybe I would write for the Mouse-trap, if Gerald\r\ndoes when he comes home."\r\n\r\nAnna did not like the proposal, but she pitied Emilia, and cared for her\r\nenough to carry the scheme to her aunt. But Geraldine shook her head.\r\nThe one thing she did not wish was to have Emmie riding, walking,\r\nsinging, and expanding into philanthropy with Gerald, and besides, she\r\nknew that Emilia would never have patience to read to her uncle, or help\r\nAdrian in his preparation.\r\n\r\n"Do you really wish this, my dear?" she asked.\r\n\r\n"N--no, not at all; but Emmie does. Could you not try her?"\r\n\r\n"Annie dear, if you wish to have a fortnight or more in town--"\r\n\r\n"Oh no, no, auntie, indeed!"\r\n\r\n"We could get on now without you. Or we would keep Emmie till the room\r\nis wanted; but I had far rather be alone than have the responsibility of\r\nEmmie."\r\n\r\n"No, no, indeed; I don\'t think Adrian would be good long with her. I had\r\nmuch rather stay--only Emmie did wish, and she hates the--"\r\n\r\n"Oh, my dear, you need not tell me; I only know that I cannot have her\r\nafter next week; the room will be wanted for Gerald."\r\n\r\n"She could sleep with me."\r\n\r\n"No, Annie, I must disappoint you. There is not room for her, and her\r\nflights when Gerald comes would never do for your uncle. You know it\r\nyourself."\r\n\r\nAnna could not but own the wisdom of the decision, and Emmie, after\r\ngrumbling at Aunt Cherry, took herself off. She had visited the\r\nInfirmary and the Convalescent Home, and even persuaded Mrs. Hablot to\r\nshow her the Union Workhouse, but she never sent her contribution to the\r\nMouse-trap.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IX. -- OUT BEYOND\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     Do the work that\'s nearest,\r\n     Though it\'s dull at whiles,\r\n     Helping, when we meet them,\r\n     Lame dogs over stiles.\r\n     See in every hedgerow\r\n     Marks of angels\' feet;\r\n     Epics in each pebble\r\n     Underneath our feet.--C. KINGSLEY.\r\n\r\n\r\n"Drawing? Well done, Cherie! That\'s a jolly little beggar; quite\r\nmasterly, as old Renville would say," exclaimed Gerald Underwood,\r\nlooking at a charming water-colour of a little fisher-boy, which Mrs.\r\nGrinstead was just completing.\r\n\r\n"\'The Faithful Henchman,\' it ought to be called," said Anna. "That\r\nlittle being has attached himself to Fergus Merrifield, and follows him\r\nand Adrian everywhere on what they are pleased to call their scientific\r\nexpeditions."\r\n\r\n"The science of larks?"\r\n\r\n"Oh dear, no. Fergus is wild after fossils, and has made Adrian the\r\nsame, and he really knows an immense deal. They are always after fossils\r\nand stones when they are out of school."\r\n\r\n"The precious darling!"\r\n\r\n"Miss Mohun says Fergus is quite to be trusted not to take him into\r\ndangerous places."\r\n\r\n"An unlooked-for blessing. Ha!" as he turned over his aunt\'s portfolio,\r\n"that\'s a stunner! You should work it up for the Academy."\r\n\r\n"This kind of thing is better for the purpose," Mrs. Grinstead said.\r\n\r\n"Throw away such work upon a twopenny halfpenny bazaar! Heaven\r\nforefend!"\r\n\r\n"Don\'t be tiresome, Gerald," entreated Anna. "You are going to do all\r\nsorts of things for it, and we shall have no end of fun."\r\n\r\n"For the sake of stopping the course of the current," returned\r\nGerald, proceeding to demonstrate in true nineteenth-century style the\r\nhopelessness of subjecting education to what he was pleased to call\r\nclericalism. "You\'ll never reach the masses while you insist on using an\r\nApostle spoon."\r\n\r\n"Masses are made up of atoms," replied his aunt.\r\n\r\n"And we shall be lost if you don\'t help," added Anna.\r\n\r\n"I would help readily enough if it were free dinners, or anything to\r\nequalize the existence of the classes, instead of feeding the artificial\r\nwants of the one at the expense of the toil and wretchedness of the\r\nother."\r\n\r\nHe proceeded to mention some of the miseries that he had learnt through\r\nthe Oxford House--dilating on them with much enthusiasm--till presently\r\nhis uncle came in, and ere long a parlour-maid announced luncheon, just\r\nas there was a rush into the house. Adrian was caught by his sister,\r\nand submitted, without more than a "Bother!" to be made respectable, and\r\nonly communicating in spasmodic gasps facts about Merrifield and hockey.\r\n\r\n"Where\'s Marshall?" asked Gerald at the first opportunity, on the maid\r\nleaving the room.\r\n\r\n"Marshall could not stand it," said his aunt. "He can\'t exist without\r\nLondon, and doing the honours of a studio."\r\n\r\n"Left you!"\r\n\r\n"Most politely he informed me that this place does not agree with his\r\nhealth; and there did not seem sufficient scope for his services since\r\nthe Reverend Underwood had become so much more independent. So we were\r\nthankful to dispose of him to Lord de Vigny."\r\n\r\n"He was a great plague," interpolated Adrian, "always jawing about the\r\nhall-door."\r\n\r\n"Are you really without a man-servant?" demanded Gerald.\r\n\r\n"In the house. Lomax comes up from the stables to take some of the work.\r\nSome lemonade, Gerald?"\r\n\r\nGerald gazed round in search of unutterable requirements; but only\r\nmet imploring eyes from aunt and sister, and restraining ones from his\r\nuncle. He subsided and submitted to the lemonade, while Anna diverted\r\nattention by recurring rather nervously to the former subject.\r\n\r\n"And I have got rid of Porter, she kept me in far too good order."\r\n\r\n"As if Sibby did not," said Clement.\r\n\r\n"Aye, and you too! But that comes naturally, and began in babyhood!"\r\n\r\n"What have you done with the house at Brompton?"\r\n\r\n"Martha is taking care of it--Mrs. Lightfoot, don\'t you know? One of our\r\nold interminable little Lightfoots, who went to be a printer in London,\r\nmarried, and lost his wife; then in our break-up actually married Martha\r\nto take care of his children! Now he is dead, and I am thankful to have\r\nher in the house."\r\n\r\n"To frighten loafers with her awful squint."\r\n\r\n"You forgive the rejection of \'The Inspector\'s Tour\'? Indeed I think you\r\nexpected it."\r\n\r\n"I wanted to see whether the young ladies would find it out."\r\n\r\n"No compliment to our genius," said his aunt.\r\n\r\n"I assure you, like Mrs. Bennet, \'there is plenty of that sort of\r\nthing,\'" said Anna. "Some of them were mystified, but Gillian and\r\nDolores Mohun were in ecstasies."\r\n\r\n"Ecstasies from that cheerful name?"\r\n\r\n"She is the New Zealand niece--Mr. Maurice Mohun\'s daughter. They\r\ncarried it home to their seniors, and of course the verdict was \'too\r\nstrong for Rockquay atmosphere,\'" said his aunt.\r\n\r\n"So it did not even go to Uncle Lance," said Anna. "Shall you try the\r\n\'Pursuivant\'?"\r\n\r\n"On the contrary, I shall put in the pepper and salt I regretted, and\r\ntry the \'Censor\'."\r\n\r\n"Indeed?" observed his uncle, in a tone of surprise.\r\n\r\n"Oh," said Gerald coolly, "I have sent little things to the \'Censor\'\r\nbefore, which they seem to regard in the light of pickles and laver."\r\n\r\nThe \'Censor\' was an able paper on the side of philosophical politics,\r\nand success in that quarter was a feather in the young man\'s cap, though\r\nnot quite the kind of feather his elders might have desired.\r\n\r\n"Journalism is a kind of native air to us," said Mrs. Grinstead, "but\r\nfrom \'Pur.\'"\r\n\r\n"\'Pur\' is the element of your dear old world, Cherie," said Gerald, "and\r\nhere am I come to do your bidding in its precincts, for a whole long\r\nvacation."\r\n\r\nHe spoke lightly, and with a pretty little graceful bow to his aunt, but\r\nthere was something in his eyes and smile that conveyed to her a dread\r\nthat he meant that he only resigned himself for the time and looked\r\nbeyond.\r\n\r\n"Uncle Lance is coming," volunteered Adrian.\r\n\r\n"Yes," said Geraldine. "Chorister that he was, and champion of\r\nChurch teaching that he is, he makes the cause of Christian education\r\neverywhere his own, and is coming down to see what he can do\r\ninexpensively with native talent for concert, or masque, or\r\nsomething--\'Robin Hood\' perhaps."\r\n\r\n"Ending in character with a rush on the audience?" said Gerald.\r\n"Otherwise \'Robin Hood\' is stale."\r\n\r\n"Tennyson has spoilt that for public use," said Mrs. Grinstead. "But was\r\nnot something else in hand?"\r\n\r\n"Only rehearsed. It never came off," said Gerald.\r\n\r\n"The most awful rot," said Adrian. "I would have nothing to do with it."\r\n\r\n"In consequence it was a failure," laughed Gerald.\r\n\r\n"It was \'The Tempest\', wasn\'t it?" said Anna.\r\n\r\n"Not really!" exclaimed Mrs. Grinstead.\r\n\r\n"About as like as a wren to an eagle," said Gerald.\r\n\r\n"We had it at the festival last winter. The authors adapted the plot,\r\nthat was all."\r\n\r\n"The authors being--\r\n\r\n"The present company," said Gerald, "and Uncle Bill, with Uncle Lance\r\nsupplying or adapting music, for we were not original, I assure you."\r\n\r\n"It was when Uncle Clem was ill," put in Anna, "and somehow I don\'t\r\nthink we took in the accounts of it."\r\n\r\n"No," said Gerald, "and nobody did it con amore, though we could not put\r\nit off. I should like to see it better done."\r\n\r\n"Such rot!" exclaimed Adrian. "There\'s an old man, he was Uncle Lance\r\nwith the great white beard made out of Kit\'s white bear\'s skin, and he\r\nlived in a desert island, where there was a shipwreck--very jolly if you\r\ncould see it, only you can\'t--and the savages--no, the wreckers all came\r\ndown."\r\n\r\n"What, in a desert island?"\r\n\r\n"It was not exactly desert. Gerald, I say, do let there be savages. It\r\nwould be such a lark to have them all black, and then I\'d act."\r\n\r\n"What an inducement!"\r\n\r\n"Then somebody turned out to be somebody\'s enemy, and the old chap\r\nfrightened them all with squibs and crackers and fog-horns, till\r\nsomebody turned out to be somebody else\'s son, and married the\r\ndaughter."\r\n\r\n"If you trace \'The Tempest\' through that version you are clever," said\r\nGerald.\r\n\r\n"I told you it was awful rot," said Adrian.\r\n\r\n"There\'s Merrifield! Excuse me, Cherie." And off he went.\r\n\r\n"The sentiments of the actors somewhat resembled Adrian\'s. It was too\r\nnew, and needed more learning and more pains, so they beg to revert to\r\n\'Robin Hood\'. However, I should like to see it well got up for once,\r\nif only by amateurs. Miranda has a capital song by Uncle Bill, made for\r\nFrancie\'s soprano. She cuts you all out, Anna."\r\n\r\n"That she does, in looks and voice, but she could not act here in\r\npublic. However, we will lay it before the Mouse-trap. Was it printed?"\r\n\r\n"Lance had enough for the performers struck off. Francie could send some\r\nup."\r\n\r\n"After all," said Cherie, "the desert island full of savages and\r\nwreckers is not more remarkable than the \'still-vex\'d Bermoothes\'\r\ngetting between Argiers and Sicily."\r\n\r\n"It _really was_ one of the Outer Hebrides," said Gerald, with the\r\neagerness that belonged to authorship, "so that there could be any\r\namount of Scottish songs. Prospero is an old Highland chief, who has\r\nbeen set adrift with his daughter--Francie Vanderkist to wit--and\r\nfloated up there, obtaining control over the local elves and brownies.\r\nLittle Fely was a most dainty sprite."\r\n\r\n"I am glad you did not make Ariel an electric telegraph," said his aunt.\r\n\r\n"Tempting, but such profanity in the face of Vale Leston was forbidden,\r\nand so was the comic element, as bad for the teetotallers."\r\n\r\n"But who were the wreckers?" asked Anna.\r\n\r\n"Buccaneers, my dear, singing songs out of the \'Pirate\'--schoolmaster,\r\norganist, and choir generally. They had captured Prospero\'s supplanter\r\n(he was a Highland chief in league with the Whigs) by the leg, while the\r\nexiled fellow was Jacobite, so as to have the songs dear to the feminine\r\nmind. They get wrecked on the island, and are terrified by the elves\r\ninto releasing Alonso, etc. Meantime Ferdinand carries logs, forgathers\r\nwith Miranda and Prospero--and ends--" He flourished his hands.\r\n\r\n"And it wasn\'t acted!"\r\n\r\n"No, we were getting it up before Christmas," said Gerald, "and then--"\r\n\r\nHe looked towards Clement, whose illness had then been at the crisis.\r\n\r\n"Very inconsiderate of me," said Clement, smiling, "as the old woman\r\nsaid when her husband did not die before the funeral cakes were stale.\r\nBut could it not come off at the festival?"\r\n\r\n"Now," said Gerald, "that the boy is gone, I may be allowed a glass of\r\nbeer. Is that absurdity to last on here?"\r\n\r\n"Adrian\'s mother would not let him come on any other terms," said Mrs.\r\nGrinstead.\r\n\r\n"Did she also stipulate that he was never to see a horse? Quite as fatal\r\nto his father."\r\n\r\n"You need not point the unreason, but consider how she has suffered."\r\n\r\n"You go the way to make him indulge on the sly."\r\n\r\n"True, perhaps," said Clement, "but I mean to take the matter up when I\r\nknow the poor little fellow better."\r\n\r\nGerald gave a little shrug, a relic of his foreign ancestry, and Anna\r\nproposed a ride to Clipstone to tell Gillian Merrifield of the idea.\r\n\r\n"Eh, the dogmatic damsel that came with you the year we had \'Midsummer\r\nNight\'s Dream\'?"\r\n\r\n"Yes, sister to Uncle Bernard\'s wife. Do you know Jasper Merrifield?\r\nClever man. Always photographing."\r\n\r\nSo off they went, Gerald apparently in a resigned state of mind, and\r\ncame upon dogs and girls in an old quarry, where Mysie had dragged them\r\nto look for pretty stones and young ferns to make little rockeries\r\nfor the sale of work. \'The Tempest\' was propounded, and received with\r\nacclamation, though the Merrifields declared that they could not sing,\r\nand their father would not allow them to do so in public if they could!\r\n\r\nDolores looked on in a sort of silent scorn at a young man who could\r\ntalk so eagerly about "a trumpery raree-show," especially for an object\r\nthat she did not care about. None of them knew how far it was the pride\r\nof authorship and the desire of pastime. Only Jasper said when he heard\r\ntheir report--\r\n\r\n"Underwood is a queer fellow! One never knows where to have him.\r\nSocialist one minute, old Tory the next."\r\n\r\n"A dreamer?" asked Dolores.\r\n\r\n"If you like to call him so. I believe he will dawdle and dream all his\r\nlife, and never do any good!"\r\n\r\n"Perhaps he is waiting."\r\n\r\n"I don\'t believe in waiting," said Jasper, wiping the dust off his\r\nphotographic glasses. "Why, he has a lovely moor of his own, and does\r\nnot know how to use it!"\r\n\r\n"Conclusive," said Gillian.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER X. -- NOBLESSE OBLIGE\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     The other won\'t agree thereto,\r\n       So here they fall to strife;\r\n     With one another they did fight\r\n       About the children\'s life.\r\n                             Babes in the Wood.\r\n\r\n\r\n"I say, Aunt Cherry," said Adrian, "the fossil forest is to be uncovered\r\nto-morrow, and Merrifield is going to stay for it, and I\'m going down\r\nwith him."\r\n\r\n"Fossil forest? What, in the Museum?"\r\n\r\n"No, indeed. In Anscombe Cove, they call it. There\'s a forest buried\r\nthere, and bits come up sometimes. To-morrow there\'s to be a tremendous\r\nlow tide that will leave a lot of it uncovered, and Merrifield and I\r\nmean to dig it out, and if there are some duplicate bits they may be had\r\nfor the bazaar."\r\n\r\n"Yes, they have been begging Fergus\'s duplicates for a collection of\r\nfossils," said Anna. "But can it be safe? A low tide means a high tide,\r\nyou know."\r\n\r\n"Bosh!" returned Adrian.\r\n\r\n"Miss Mohun is sure to know all about the tides, I suppose," said\r\nClement; "if her nephew goes with her consent I suppose it is safe."\r\n\r\n"If--" said Mrs. Grinstead.\r\n\r\nAdrian looked contemptuous, and muttered something, on which Anna\r\nundertook to see Miss Mohun betimes, and judge how the land, or rather\r\nthe sea, lay, and whether Fergus was to be trusted.\r\n\r\nIt would be a Saturday, a whole holiday, on which he generally went home\r\nfor Sunday, and Adrian spent the day with him, but the boys\' present\r\nscheme was, to take their luncheon with them and spend the whole day in\r\nAnscombe Cove. This was on the further side of the bay from the marble\r\nworks, shut in by big cliffs, which ran out into long chains of rocks on\r\neither side, but retreated in the midst, where a little stream from the\r\nvillage of Anscombe, or rather from the moorland beyond, made its way to\r\nthe sea.\r\n\r\nThe almanacks avouched that on this Saturday there would be an unusually\r\nlow tide, soon after twelve o\'clock, and Fergus had set his heart on\r\ninvestigating the buried forest that there was no doubt had been choked\r\nby the combined forces of river and sea. So Anna found that notice had\r\nbeen sent to Clipstone of his intention of devoting himself to the cove\r\nand not coming home till the evening, and that his uncle and aunt did\r\nnot think there was any danger, especially as his constant henchman,\r\nDavie Blake, was going with him, and all the fisher-boys of the place\r\nwere endowed with a certain instinct for their own tides. The only\r\naccident Jane Mohun had ever known was with a stranger.\r\n\r\nAnna had no choice but to subside, and the boys started as soon as the\r\nmorning\'s tide would have gone down sufficiently, carrying baskets for\r\ntheir treasures containing their luncheon, and apparently expecting\r\nto find the forest growing upright under the mud, like a wood full of\r\nbushes.\r\n\r\nThe cove for which they were bound was on the further side of the chain\r\nof rocks, nearly two miles from Rockquay, and one of the roads ran along\r\nthe top of the red cliffs that shut it in, with no opening except where\r\nthe stream emerged, and even that a very scanty bank of shingle.\r\n\r\nIn spite of all assurances, Anna could not be easy about her darling,\r\nand when afternoon came, and the horses were brought to the door, she\r\ncoaxed Gerald into riding along the cliffs in the Anscombe direction,\r\nwhere there was a good road, from whence they could turn down a steep\r\nhill into the village, and thence go up a wild moor beyond, or else\r\ncontinue along the coast for a considerable distance.\r\n\r\nAs they went out she could see nothing of the boys, only rocks rising\r\nthrough an expanse of mud, and the sea breaking beyond. She would have\r\npreferred continuing the cliff road, but Gerald had a turn for the moor,\r\nand carried her off through the village of Anscombe, up and up, till\r\nthey had had a lively canter on the moor, and looked far out at sea.\r\nWhen they turned back and had reached the cliff road, what had been\r\na sheet of mud before had been almost entirely covered with sparkling\r\nwaves, and there was white foam beating against some of the rocks.\r\n\r\n"I hope Adrian is gone home," sighed Anna.\r\n\r\n"Long ago, depend on it," returned Gerald carelessly; but the next\r\nmoment his tone changed. "By Jove!" he exclaimed, and pointed with his\r\nwhip to a rock, or island, at the end of the range of rocks.\r\n\r\nHe was much the more long-sighted of the two, and she could only first\r\ndiscern that there was something alive upon the rock.\r\n\r\n"Oh!" she cried, "is it the boys--I can\'t see?"\r\n\r\n"I can\'t tell. It is boys, maybe fishers. I must get out to them," he\r\nreplied. "Now, Anna, be quiet--use your senses. It is somebody, anyway.\r\nI saw the opening of a path down the rock just now," and he threw\r\nhimself off his horse, and threw her the bridle. "You ride to the first\r\nhouse; find where there is a Coast-guard station, or any fisherman to\r\nput out a boat. No time to be lost."\r\n\r\n"Oh, is it, is it--" cried the bewildered girl, with no hand to feel for\r\nher eyeglass. "Where shall I go?"\r\n\r\n"I tell you I can\'t tell," he shouted in answer to both questions, half\r\nangrily, already on his way. "Don\'t dawdle," and he disappeared.\r\n\r\nPoor Anna, she had no inclination to dawdle, but the two horses were\r\na sore impediment, and she went on some way without seeing any houses.\r\nShould she turn back to the little road leading down from Anscombe? but\r\nthat was rough and difficult, and could not be undertaken quickly with a\r\nled horse; or should she make the best of her way to the nearest villas,\r\noutskirts of Rockquay? However, after a moment the swish of bicycles\r\nwas heard, and up came two young men, clerks apparently, let loose by\r\nSaturday. They halted, and in answer to her agitated question where\r\nthere was a house, pointed to a path which they said led down to the\r\nPreventive station, and asked whether there had been an accident, and\r\nwhether they could be of use. They were more able to decide what was\r\nbest to be done than she could be, and they grew more keenly interested\r\nwhen they understood for whom she feared. Petros White, brother to Mrs.\r\nHenderson, and nephew to Aunt Adeline\'s husband, was one of them, the\r\nother, a youth also employed at the marble works. This latter took the\r\nhorses off her hands, while Petros showed her the way to the Coast-guard\r\nstation by a steep path, leading to a sort of ledge in the side of the\r\ncliff, scooped out partly by nature and partly by art, where stood the\r\nlittle houses covered with slate.\r\n\r\nThere the mistress was looking out anxiously with a glass; while below,\r\nthe Preventive man was unlocking the boat-house, having already observed\r\nthe peril of the boys, but lamenting the absence of his mate. Petros ran\r\ndown at speed to offer his help, and Anna could only borrow the glass,\r\nthrough which she plainly saw the three boys, bare-legged, sitting\r\nhuddled up on the top of the rock, but with the waves still a good way\r\nfrom them, and their faces all turned hopefully towards the promontory\r\nof rock along which she could see Gerald picking his way; but there was\r\nevidently a terrible and fast-diminishing space between its final point\r\nand the rock of refuge.\r\n\r\nAnna was about to rush down, and give her help with an oar; but the\r\nwoman withheld her, saying that she would only crowd the boat and retard\r\nthe rescue, for which the two were quite sufficient, only the danger was\r\nthat the current of the stream might make the tide rise rapidly in the\r\nbay. There were besides so many rocks and shoals, that it was impossible\r\nto proceed straight across, but it was needful absolutely to pass the\r\nrock and then turn back on it from the open sea. It was agonizing for\r\nthe sister to watch the devious course, and she turned the glass upon\r\nthe poor boys, plainly making out Adrian\'s scared, restless look, as he\r\nclung to the fisher-lad, and Fergus nursing his bag of specimens with\r\nhis knees drawn up. By and by Gerald was wading, and with difficulty\r\npreventing himself from being washed off the rocks. He paused, saw her,\r\nand waved encouragement. Then he plunged along, not off his feet, and\r\nreached the island where the boys were holding out their arms to him.\r\nThere ensued a few moments of apparently hot debate, and she saw, to her\r\nhorror and amazement, that he was thrusting back one boy, who struggled\r\nand almost fell off the rock in his passion, as Gerald lifted down the\r\nlittle fisher-boy. Of course she could not hear the words, "Come, boy.\r\nNo, Adrian. Noblesse oblige. I will come back, never fear. I can take\r\nbut one, don\'t I tell you. I will come back."\r\n\r\nThose were Gerald\'s words, while Adrian threw himself on the rock,\r\nsobbing and screaming, while Fergus sat still, hugging his bag. Anna\r\ncould have screamed with her brother, for the boat seemed to have\r\novershot the mark, and to be going quite aloof, when all depended upon\r\na few minutes. She could hardly hear the words of the Preventive woman,\r\nwho had found a second glass: "Never you fear, miss, the boat will be up\r\nin time."\r\n\r\nShe could not speak. Her heart was in wild rebellion as she thought of\r\nthe comparative value of her widowed mother\'s only son with that of the\r\nfisher-boy, or even of Fergus, one of so large a family. She could not\r\nor would not look to see what Gerald was doing with the wretched little\r\ncoast boy; but she heard her companion say that the gentleman had put\r\nthe boy down to scramble among the rocks, and he himself was going back\r\nto the pair on the rock, quite swimming now.\r\n\r\nShe durst look again, and saw that he had scrambled up to the boys\'\r\nperch, and had lifted Adrian up, but there was white spray dashing round\r\nnow. She could not see the boat.\r\n\r\n"They have to keep to the other side," explained the woman. "God keep\r\nthem! It will be a near shave. The gentleman is taking off his coat!"\r\n\r\nAgain there was a leap of foam--over! over! Then all was blotted out,\r\nbut the woman exclaimed--\r\n\r\n"There they are!"\r\n\r\n"Oh! where?"\r\n\r\n"One swimming! He is floating the other."\r\n\r\nAnna could see no longer. She dashed aside the telescope, then begged\r\nto be told, then looked again. No prayer would come but "Save him! save\r\nhim!"\r\n\r\nThere was a call quite close.\r\n\r\n"Mr. Norris, sir, put off your boat! Master Fergus--Oh! is he off?" and,\r\ndrenched and breathless, Davy sank down on the ground at their feet,\r\nquite spent, unable at first to get out a word after those panting ones;\r\nbut in a minute he spoke in answer to the agonized "Which? Who?"\r\n\r\n"Master Fergus is swimming. The young sir couldn\'t."\r\n\r\nAnna recollected how her mother\'s fears and entreaties had prevented Mr.\r\nHarewood from teaching Adrian to swim.\r\n\r\n"Gent is floating him," added the boy. "He took me first, because I\r\ncould get over the rocks and get help soonest. He is a real gentleman,\r\nhe is."\r\n\r\nAnna could not listen to anything but "The boat is coming!"\r\n\r\n"Oh, but they don\'t see! They are going away from it!"\r\n\r\n"That\'s the current," said Mrs. Norris. "My man knows what he is about,\r\nand so does the gentleman, never fear."\r\n\r\nThere was another terrible interval, and then boat and swimmers began to\r\napproach, though in what condition could not be made out. A dark little\r\nhead, no doubt that of Fergus, was lifted in, then another figure was\r\nraised and taken into the boat; Gerald swam with a hand on it for a\r\nshort distance, then was helped in, and almost at once took an oar.\r\n\r\n"That\'s right," said Mrs. Norris. "It will keep out the cold."\r\n\r\n"They are not coming here," exclaimed Anna. "They are going round the\r\npoint."\r\n\r\n"All right," was the answer. "\'Tis more direct, you see, no shoals,\r\nand the young gentlemen will get to their own homes and beds all the\r\nquicker. Now, miss, you will come in and take a cup of tea, I am sure\r\nyou want it, and I had just made it when Norris saw the little lads."\r\n\r\n"Oh, thank you, I must get back at once. My little brother--"\r\n\r\n"Yes, yes, miss, but you\'ll be able to ride the faster for a bit of\r\nbread and cup of tea! You are all of a tremble."\r\n\r\nIt was true, and to pacify her, Mrs. Norris sent a child up to bid\r\nPetros have the horses ready, and Anna was persuaded to swallow a little\r\ntoo, which happily had cooled enough for her haste, but she hurried off,\r\nleaving Mrs. Norris to expend her hospitality on Davy, who endured\r\nhis drenching like a fish, and could hardly wait even to swallow thick\r\nbread-and-butter till he could rush off to hear of his dear Master\r\nFergus.\r\n\r\nThe horses were ready. Petros had been joined by other spectators,\r\nand was able to entrust the bicycles to one of them, while he himself\r\nundertook to lead Mr. Underwood\'s horse to the stable. Anna rode off at\r\nas much speed or more than was safe downhill among the stones. She had\r\nto cross the broad parade above the quay, and indeed she believed she\r\nhad come faster than the boat, which had to skirt round the side of the\r\npromontory between Anscombe Cove and Rockquay. In fact, when she came\r\nabove the town she could see a crowd on the quay and pier, all looking\r\nout to sea, and she now beheld two boats making for the harbour.\r\n\r\nThen she had to ride between walls and villas, and lost sight of all\r\ntill she emerged on the parade, and thought she saw Uncle Clement\'s hat\r\nabove the crowd as she looked over their heads.\r\n\r\nShe gave her horse to a bystander, who evidently knew her, for a murmur\r\nwent through the crowd of "Little chap\'s sister," and way was made\r\nfor her to get forward, while several rough voices said, "All right";\r\n"Coast-guard boat"; "Not this one."\r\n\r\nHer uncle and Miss Mohun wore standing together. General Mohun could be\r\nseen in the foremost boat, and they could hear him call out, with a wave\r\nof his arm--\r\n\r\n"All right! All safe!"\r\n\r\n"You hero! Where\'s Gerald?" Miss Mohun exclaimed, as Anna came up to\r\nher.\r\n\r\n"There!" and she pointed to the Coast-guard boat. "We saw the boys from\r\nAnscombe Cliff, and he went out to them."\r\n\r\n"Gerald," exclaimed his uncle, with a ring of gladness in his voice,\r\nall the more that it was plain that the rower was indeed Gerald, and\r\nhe began to hail those on shore, while Fergus\'s head rose up from the\r\nbottom of the boat.\r\n\r\nIn a few moments they were close to the quay, and the little sodden mass\r\nthat purported to be Fergus was calling out--\r\n\r\n"Aunt Jane! Oh, I\'ve lost such a bit of aralia. Where\'s Davy?"\r\n\r\n"Here, take care. He is all right," were Gerald\'s words.\r\n\r\n_He_ meant Adrian, whom his cousin lifted out, with eyes open and\r\nconscious, but with limp hands and white exhausted looks, to be carried\r\nto the fly that stood in waiting.\r\n\r\n"Is the other boy safe?" asked Gerald anxiously.\r\n\r\n"Oh yes; but how could you?" were the first words that came to Anna;\r\nbut she felt rebuked by a strange look of utter surprise, and instead of\r\nanswering her he replied to General Mohun--\r\n\r\n"Thanks, no, I\'ll walk up!" as a rough coat was thrown over his dripping\r\nand scanty garments.\r\n\r\n"The wisest way," said the General. "Can you, Fergus?"\r\n\r\n"Yes, quite well. Oh, my aralia!"\r\n\r\n"He has been half crying all the way home about his fossils," said\r\nGerald. "Never mind, Fergus; look out for the next spring-tide. Uncle\r\nClem, you ought to drive up."\r\n\r\nClement submitted, clearly unable to resist, and sat down by Anna,\r\nwho had her brother in her arms, rubbing his hands and warming them,\r\ncaressing him, and asking him how he felt, to which the only answer she\r\ngot was--\r\n\r\n"It was beastly. I have my mouth awfully full of water still."\r\n\r\nClement made a low murmur of thanksgiving, and Anna, looking up, was\r\nstartled to see how white and helpless he was. The way was happily very\r\nshort, but he had so nearly fainted that Gerald, hurrying on faster\r\nuphill than the horse to reassure his aunt, lifted him out, not far from\r\ninsensible, and carried him with Sibby\'s help to his bed in the room on\r\nthe ground-floor, where the remedies were close at hand, Geraldine and\r\nnurse anxiously administering them; when the first sign of revival he\r\ngave was pointing to Gerald\'s dripping condition, and signing to him to\r\ngo and take care of himself.\r\n\r\n"All right, yes, boys and all! All right Cherie."\r\n\r\nAnd he went, swallowing down the glass of stimulant which his aunt\r\nturned from her other patient for a moment to administer, but she was\r\nmuch too anxious about Clement to have thought for any one else, for\r\ntruly it did seem likely that he would be the chief sufferer from the\r\ncatastrophe.\r\n\r\nLittle Davy\'s adventure, as he had lost no clothes, made no more\r\nimpression on his parents than if he had been an amphibious animal or\r\na water dog, and when Fergus came out of Beechwood Cottage after having\r\nchanged the few clothes he had retained, and had a good meal, to be\r\ndriven home with his uncle in the dog-cart, his constant henchman was\r\nfound watching for news of him at the gate.\r\n\r\n"Please, sir, I think we\'ll find your aralia next spring-tide."\r\n\r\nWhereupon General Mohun told him he was a good little chap, and\r\npresented him with a half-crown, the largest sum he had ever possessed\r\nin his life.\r\n\r\nFergus did not come off quite so well, for when the story had been told,\r\nthough his mother had trembled and shed tears of thankfulness as she\r\nkissed him, and his sisters sprang at him and devoured him, while all\r\nthe time he bemoaned his piece of the stump of an aralia, and a bit of\r\ncone of a pinus, and other treasures to which imaginative regret lent\r\nsuch an aid, that no doubt he would believe the lost contents of his bag\r\nto have been the most precious articles that he had ever collected; his\r\nfather, however, took him into his study.\r\n\r\n"Fergus," he said gravely, "this is the second time your ardour upon\r\nyour pursuits has caused danger and inconvenience to other people, this\r\ntime to yourself too."\r\n\r\nFergus hung his head, and faltered something about--"Never saw."\r\n\r\n"No, that is the point. Now I say nothing about your pursuits. I am very\r\nglad you should have them, and be an intelligent lad; but they must not\r\nbe taken up exclusively, so as to drive out all heed to anything else.\r\nRemember, there is a great difference between courage and foolhardiness,\r\nand that you are especially warned to be careful if your venturesomeness\r\nendangers other people\'s lives."\r\n\r\nSo Fergus went off under a sense of his father\'s displeasure, while\r\nAdrian lay in his bed, kicking about, admired and petted by his sister,\r\nwho thought every one very unkind and indifferent to him; and when\r\nhe went to sleep, began a letter to her eldest sister describing the\r\nadventure and his heroism in naming terms, such as on second thoughts\r\nshe suppressed, as likely to frighten her mother, and lead to his\r\nimmediate recall.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XI. -- HEROES AND HERO-WORSHIP\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     Nothing of him that doth fade,\r\n     But doth suffer a sea-change\r\n     Into something rich and strange.--Tempest.\r\n\r\n\r\nSunday morning found Anna in a different frame of mind from that of the\r\nevening before. Uncle Clement had been very ill all night, and the\r\nhouse was to be kept as quiet as possible. When Anna came in from\r\nearly Celebration, Aunt Cherry came out looking like a ghost, and very\r\nanxious, and gave a sigh of relief on Adrian being reported still\r\nsound asleep. Gerald presently came down, pale and languid, but calling\r\nhimself all right, and loitering over his breakfast till after the boy\r\nappeared, so rosy and ravenous as to cause no apprehension, except that\r\nhe should devour too much apricot jam, and use his new boots too noisily\r\non the stairs.\r\n\r\nAnna devised walking him to Beechcroft to hear if there were any news\r\nof Fergus, and though he observed, with a certain sound of contemptuous\r\nrivalship, that there was no need, for "Merrifield was as right as a\r\ntrivet," he was glad enough to get out of doors a little sooner, and\r\nthough he affected to be bored by the kind inquiries of the people they\r\nmet, he carried his head all the higher for them.\r\n\r\nNobody was at home except General Mohun, but he verified Adrian\'s\r\nimpression of his nephew\'s soundness, whatever the mysterious comparison\r\nmight mean; and asked rather solicitously not only after Mr. Underwood\r\nbut after Gerald, who, he said, was a delicate subject to have made such\r\nexertions.\r\n\r\n"It really was very gallant and very sensible behaviour," he said, as\r\nhe took his hat to walk to St. Andrew\'s with the brother and sister,\r\nbut Anna was conscious of a little pouting in Adrian\'s expression, and\r\ndispleasure in his stumping steps.\r\n\r\nGerald came to church, but went to sleep in the sermon, and had\r\naltogether such a worn-out look that no one could help remembering that\r\nhe had never been very strong, and had gone through much exertion the\r\nday before, nor could he eat much of the mid-day meal. Mrs. Grinstead,\r\nwho was more at ease about her brother, looked anxiously at him, and\r\nwith a kind of smile the word "Apres" passed between them. The Sunday\r\ncustom was for Clement to take Adrian to say his Catechism, and have a\r\nlittle instruction before going out walking, but as this could not be on\r\nthis day, Anna and he were to go out for a longer walk than usual, so\r\nas to remove disturbance from the household. Gerald declined, of course,\r\nand was left extended on the sofa; but just as Anna and Adrian had made\r\na few steps along the street, and the boy had prevailed not to walk to\r\nClipstone, as she wished, but to go to the cliffs, that she might hear\r\nthe adventure related in sight of the scene of action, he discovered\r\nthat he had left a glove. He was very particular about Sunday walking\r\nin gloves in any public place, and rushed back to find it, leaving his\r\nsister waiting. Presently he came tearing back and laughing.\r\n\r\n"Did you find it?"\r\n\r\n"Oh yes; it was in the drawing-room. And what else do you think I found?\r\nWhy, Cherie administering"--and he pointed down his throat, and made a\r\ngulp with a wild grimace of triumph. "On the sly! Ha! ha!"\r\n\r\nAnna felt as if the ground had opened under her feet, but she answered\r\ngravely--\r\n\r\n"Poor Gerald went through a great deal yesterday, and is quite knocked\r\nup, so no wonder he needs some strengthening medicine."\r\n\r\n"Strengthening grandmother! Don\'t you think I know better than that?" he\r\ncried, with a caper and a grin.\r\n\r\n"Of course you had to have some cordial when you were taken out of the\r\nwater."\r\n\r\n"And don\'t you know what it was?"\r\n\r\n"I know the fisher-people carry stuff about with them in case of\r\naccidents."\r\n\r\n"That\'s the way with girls--just to think one knows nothing at all."\r\n\r\n"What do you know, Adrian?"\r\n\r\n"Know? Why, I haven\'t been about with Kit and Ted Harewood for nothing!\r\nJolly good larks it is to see how all of you take for granted that a\r\nfellow never knew the taste of anything but tea and milk-and-water."\r\n\r\n"But what do you know the taste of?" she asked, with an earnestness\r\nthat provoked the boy to tease and put on a boasting manner, so that she\r\ncould not tell how much he was pretending for the sake of amazing and\r\ntormenting her, in which he certainly succeeded.\r\n\r\nHowever, his attention was diverted by coming round the corner to where\r\nthere was a view of Anscombe Bay, when he immediately began to fight his\r\nbattles o\'er again, and show where they had been groping in the mud and\r\nseaweed in pursuit of sea-urchins, and stranded star-fish, and crabs.\r\n\r\n"And it wasn\'t a forest after all, it was just a sell--nothing but mud\r\nand weed, only Fergus would go and poke in it, and there were horrid\r\ngreat rough stones and rocks too, and I tumbled over one."\r\n\r\nAnna here became conscious that the whole place was the resort of the\r\nafternoon promenaders of Rockquay, great and small, of all ranks and\r\ndegrees, belonging to the "middle class" or below it, and that they\r\nmight themselves become the object of attention; and she begged\r\nher brother to turn back and wait till they could have the place to\r\nthemselves.\r\n\r\n"These are a disgusting lot of cads," he agreed, "but there won\'t be\r\nsuch a jolly tide another time. I declare I see the very rock where I\r\nsaw the sea-mouse--out there! red and shiny at the top."\r\n\r\nHere a well-dressed man, who had just come up the Coast-guard path, put\r\naside his pipe, and taking off his hat, deferentially asked--\r\n\r\n"Have I the honour of addressing Sir Adrian Vanderkist?"\r\n\r\nAdrian replied with a gracious nod and gesture towards his straw hat,\r\nand in another moment Anna found him answering questions, and giving his\r\nown account of the adventure to the inquirer, who, she had little\r\ndoubt, was a reporter, and carrying his head, if possible, higher in\r\nconsequence as he told how Fergus Merrifield had lingered over his\r\nstones, and all the rest after his own version. She did not hear the\r\nwhole, having had to answer the inquiries of one of the bicycle friends\r\nof the previous day, but when her attention was free she heard--\r\n\r\n"And the young lady, Sir Adrian?"\r\n\r\n"Young lady! Thank goodness, we were not bothered with any of that\r\nsort."\r\n\r\n"Indeed, Sir Adrian, I understood that there was a young lady, Miss\r\nAurelia, that Master Merrifield was lamenting, as if she had met with a\r\nwatery grave."\r\n\r\n"Ha! ha! Aralia was only the name of a bit of fossil kind of a stick\r\nthat Merrifield had us down there to find in the fossil forest. I\'m sure\r\nI saw no forest, only bits of mud and stuff! But he found a bit, sure\r\nenough, and was ready to break his heart when he had to leave his bag\r\nbehind him on the rock. Aralia a young lady! That\'s a good one."\r\n\r\nHe forgathered with a school-fellow on the way home, and Anna heard\r\nlittle more.\r\n\r\nThe next day, however, there arrived the daily local paper, addressed\r\nto Sir Adrian Vanderkist, Bart., and it was opened by him at\r\nbreakfast-time.\r\n\r\n"I say! Look here! \'Dangerous Accident in Anscombe. A Youthful Baronet\r\nin peril!\' What asses people are!" he added, with an odd access of the\r\ngratified shame of seeing himself for the first time in print. But he\r\ndid not proceed to read aloud; there evidently was something he did\r\nnot like, and he was very near pocketing it and rushing off headlong to\r\nschool with it, if his aunt and Anna had not entreated or commanded for\r\nit, when he threw it over with an uncomplimentary epithet.\r\n\r\n"Just what I was afraid of when I saw the man talking to him!" exclaimed\r\nAnna. "Oh, listen!\r\n\r\n"\'The young Sir Adrian Vanderkist, at present residing at St. Andrew\'s\r\nRock with his aunt, Mrs. Grinstead, and the Rev. E. C. Underwood, and\r\nwho is a pupil at Mrs. Edgar\'s academy for young gentlemen, was, we are\r\ninformed, involved in the most imminent danger, together with a son\r\nof General Sir Jasper Merrifield, K.G.C., a young gentleman whose\r\nremarkable scientific talent and taste appear to have occasioned the\r\nperil of the youthful party, from whence they were rescued by Gerald F.\r\nUnderwood, Esq., of Vale Leston.\'"\r\n\r\n"What\'s all that?" said Gerald F. Underwood, Esquire, sauntering in and\r\nkissing his aunt. "Good-morning. How is Uncle Clement this morning?"\r\n\r\n"Much better; I think he will be up by and by," answered Mrs. Grinstead.\r\n\r\n"What bosh have you got there? The reporters seized on their prey, eh?"\r\n\r\n"There\'s Sir Jasper!" exclaimed Anna, who could see through the blinds\r\nfrom where she sat.\r\n\r\nSir Jasper had driven over with his little son, and, after leaving him\r\nat school, had come to inquire for Mr. Underwood, and to obtain a fuller\r\naccount of the accident, having already picked up a paper and glanced at\r\nit.\r\n\r\n"I am afraid my little scamp led them into the danger," he said.\r\n"Scientific taste forsooth! Science is as good a reason as anything else\r\nfor getting into scrapes."\r\n\r\n"Really," said Gerald, "I can\'t say I think your boy came out the worst\r\nin it, though I must own the Rockquay Advertiser bestows most of the\r\nhonours of the affair on the youthful baronet! You say he blew his own\r\ntrumpet," added Gerald, turning to Anna.\r\n\r\n"The reporter came and beset us," said Anna, in a displeased voice. "I\r\ndid not hear all that passed, but of course Adrian told him what he told\r\nme, only those people make things sound ridiculous."\r\n\r\n"To begin with," said Gerald, "I don\'t think Fergus, or at any rate\r\nDavy Blake, was in fault. They tried to go home in good time, having\r\nan instinct for tides, but Adrian was chasing a sea-mouse or some such\r\ngame, and could not be brought back, and then he fell over a slippery\r\nrock, and had to be dragged out of a hole, and by that time the channel\r\nof the Anscombe stream was too deep, at least for him, who has been only\r\ntoo carefully guarded from being amphibious."\r\n\r\n"Oh! that did not transpire at home," said Sir Jasper. "Boys are so\r\nreserved."\r\n\r\nMrs. Grinstead and Anna looked rather surprised. Anna even ventured--\r\n\r\n"I thought Fergus got too absorbed."\r\n\r\n"So did I," said his father dryly. "And he did not justify himself."\r\n\r\n"M--m--m," went on Gerald, skimming the article.\r\n\r\n"Read it," cried Anna. "You know none of us have seen it."\r\n\r\nGerald continued--\r\n\r\n"\'Their perilous position having been observed from Anscombe cliffs, Mr.\r\nG. F. Underwood of Vale Leston heroically\' (i.e. humbugically) \'made\r\nhis way out to their assistance, while a boat was put off by the\r\nCoast-guard, and that of Mr. Carter, fisherman, from Rockquay was\r\nlaunched somewhat later.\' We could not see either of them, you know. My\r\neye, this is coming it strong! \'The young baronet generously insisted\r\nthat the little fisher-boy, David Blake, who had accompanied them,\r\nshould first be placed in safety--\'"\r\n\r\n"Didn\'t he?" exclaimed Anna. "I saw, and I wondered, but I thought it\r\nwas his doing."\r\n\r\n"You saw?"\r\n\r\n"Yes, in the Coast-guard\'s telescope."\r\n\r\n"Oh! That is a new feature in the case!"\r\n\r\n"Then he did not insist?" said Mrs. Grinstead.\r\n\r\n"It was with the wrong side of his mouth."\r\n\r\n"But why did you send the fisher-boy first, when after all his life was\r\nless important?" exclaimed Anna, breaking forth at last.\r\n\r\n"First, for the reason that I strove to impress on \'the youthful\r\nbaronet,\' Noblesse oblige. Secondly, that Davy knew how to make his way\r\nalong the rocks, and also knew where to find the Preventive station. I\r\ncould leave him to get on, as I could not have done with the precious\r\nAdrian, and that gave a much better chance for us all.
