It was swimming\r\nwork by the time I got back, and by that time I thought the best\r\nalternative for any of us was to keep hold as long as we could, and then\r\nkeep afloat as best we might till we were picked up. Your boy was the\r\nhero of it all. Adrian was so angry with me for my disrespect that I\r\ncould hardly have got him to listen to me if Fergus had not made him\r\nunderstand, that to let himself be passive and be floated by me till the\r\nboats came up was the only thing to be done. There was one howl when he\r\nhad to let go his beloved aralia, but he showed his soldier blood, and\r\nbehaved most manfully."\r\n\r\n"I am most thankful to hear it," said his father, "and especially\r\nthankful to you."\r\n\r\n"Oh! there was not much real danger," said Gerald lightly, "to any one\r\nwho could swim."\r\n\r\n"But Adrian could not," said Anna. "Oh! Gerald, what do we not owe to\r\nyou?"\r\n\r\n"I must be off," said Sir Jasper; "I must see about a new jacket for my\r\nboy. By the bye, do you know how the little Davy fared in the matter of\r\nclothes?"\r\n\r\n"Better than any of us," said Gerald. "He was far too sharp to go\r\nmud-larking in anything that would be damaged, and had his boots safe\r\nlaid up in a corner. I wish mine were equally safe."\r\n\r\nSir Jasper\'s purchases were not confined to boots and jacket, but as\r\ncompensation for his hard words included a certain cabinet full of\r\ndrawers that had long been Fergus\'s cynosure.\r\n\r\nAnna and her aunt were much concerned at what was said of Adrian, and\r\nstill more at the boastful account that he seemed to have given; but\r\nthen something, as Mrs. Grinstead observed, must be allowed for the\r\nreporter\'s satisfaction in having interviewed a live baronet. Each of\r\nthe parties concerned had one hero, and if the Merrifields\' was Fergus,\r\nto their own great surprise and satisfaction, Aunt Cherry was very happy\r\nover her own especial boy, Gerald, and certainly it was an easier task\r\nthan to accept "the youthful baronet" at his own valuation or that of\r\nthe reporter.\r\n\r\nMrs. Grinstead considered whether to try to make him less conceited\r\nabout it, and show him his want of truth. She consulted his uncle about\r\nit, showing the newspaper, and telling, and causing Gerald to tell, the\r\nhistory of the accident, which Clement had not been fit to hear all the\r\nday before.\r\n\r\nHe was still in bed, but quite ready to attend to anything, and he\r\nlaughed over the account, which she illustrated by the discoveries she\r\nhad made from the united witnesses.\r\n\r\n"And is it not delightful to see for once what Gerald really is?" she\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\n"Yes, he seems to have behaved gallantly," said his uncle; "and I won\'t\r\nsay just what might have been expected."\r\n\r\n"One does expect something of an Underwood," she said.\r\n\r\n"Little Merrifield too, who saw the danger coming, deserves more honour\r\nthan he seems to have taken to himself."\r\n\r\n"Yes, he accepted severity from that stern father of his, who seems very\r\nsorry for it now. It is curious how those boys\' blood comes out in the\r\nmatter--chasser de race."\r\n\r\n"You must allow something for breeding. Fergus had not been the idol of\r\na mother and sisters, and Gerald remembered his father in danger."\r\n\r\n"Oh, I can never be glad enough that he has that remembrance of him! How\r\nlike him he grows! That unconscious imitation is so curious."\r\n\r\n"Yes, the other day, when I had been dozing, I caught myself calling out\r\nthat he was whistling \'Johnny Cope\' so loud that he would be heard in\r\nthe shop."\r\n\r\n"He seems to be settling down more happily here than I expected. I\r\nsometimes wonder if there is any attraction at Clipstone."\r\n\r\n"No harm if there were, except--"\r\n\r\n"Except what? Early marriage might be the very best thing."\r\n\r\n"Perhaps, though sometimes I doubt whether it is well for a man to have\r\ngone through the chief hopes and crises of life so soon. He looks out\r\nfor fresh excitement."\r\n\r\n"There are so many stages in life," said Geraldine, sighing. "And with\r\nall his likenesses, Gerald is quite different from any of you."\r\n\r\n"So I suppose each generation feels with those who succeed it. Nor do\r\nI feel as if I understood the Universities to-day as I did Cambridge\r\nthought of old. We can do nothing but wait and pray, and put out a hand\r\nwhere we see cause."\r\n\r\n"Where we see! It is the not seeing that is so trying. The being sure\r\nthat there is more going on within than is allowed to meet one\'s eye,\r\nand that one is only patronized as an old grandmother--quite out of it."\r\n\r\n"I think the conditions of life and thought are less simple than in our\r\nday."\r\n\r\n"And to come to the present. What is to be done about Adrian--the one\r\nwho was not a hero, though he made himself out so?"\r\n\r\n"Probably he really thought so. He is a mere child, you know, and it was\r\nhis first adventure, before he has outgrown the days of cowardice."\r\n\r\n"He need not have told stories."\r\n\r\n"Depend upon it, he hardly knew that he did so."\r\n\r\n"He had the reporter to help him certainly, and the \'Rockquay\r\nAdvertiser\' may not keep to the stern veracity and simplicity of the\r\n\'Pursuivant\'."\r\n\r\n"And was proud to interview a live baronet."\r\n\r\n"Then what shall we do--Anna and I, I mean?"\r\n\r\n"Write the simple facts to Vale Leston, and then let it alone."\r\n\r\n"To him?"\r\n\r\n"Certainly. He would think your speaking mere nagging. Preserve an\r\nominous silence if he speaks. His school-fellows will be his best cure."\r\n\r\n"Well, he did seem ashamed!"\r\n\r\nClement was right. The boy\'s only mention of the paragraph was once as\r\n"that beastly thing"; and Anna discovered from Valetta Merrifield, that\r\nwhatever satisfaction he might have derived from it had been effectually\r\ndriven out of him by the "fellows" at Mrs. Edgar\'s, who had beset him\r\nwith all their force of derision, called him nothing but the "youthful\r\nBart.," and made him ashamed as none of the opposite sex or of maturer\r\nyears could ever have succeeded in doing. Valetta said Fergus had tried\r\nto stop it, but there had certainly been one effect, namely, that Adrian\r\nwas less disposed to be "Merry\'s" shadow than heretofore, and seemed\r\ninclined instead to take up with the other seniors.\r\n\r\nOne thing, however, was certain. Gerald enjoyed a good deal more\r\nconsideration among the Clipstone damsels than before. True, as Jasper\r\nsaid, it was only what any one would have done; but he had done it, and\r\nproved himself by no means inferior to "any one," and Fergus regarded\r\nhim as a true hero, which had a considerable effect on his sisters, the\r\nmore perhaps because Jasper derided their admiration.\r\n\r\nThey were doubly bent on securing him for a contributor to the\r\nMouse-trap. They almost thought of inviting him to their Browning\r\nafternoons, but decided that he would not appreciate the feminine\r\ncompany, though he did so often have a number of the \'Censor\' to discuss\r\nit with Dolores, whenever they met him.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XII. -- THE LITTLE BUTTERFLY\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n   The best actors in the world, either for tragedy, comedy,\r\n   history, pastoral, pastoral-comical, historical-pastoral.\r\n   --Hamlet.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe Matrons, otherwise denominated lady patronesses, met in committee,\r\nMiss Mohun being of course the soul and spirit of all, though Mrs.\r\nEllesmere, as the wife of the rector of old Rockstone Church, was the\r\npresident, Lady Flight, one of the most interested, was there, also\r\nLady Merrifield, dragged in to secure that there was nothing decided on\r\ncontrary to old-world instincts, Mrs. Grinstead, in right of the musical\r\nelement that her brother promised, the beautiful Mrs. Henderson, to\r\nrepresent the marble works, Mrs. Simmonds of the Cliff Hotel, the\r\nMayoress, and other notables.\r\n\r\nThe time was fixed for the first week in August, the only one when\r\nengagements would permit the Rotherwood family to be present for the\r\nopening, and when the regatta was apt to fill Rockquay with visitors.\r\nThe place was to be the top of the cliffs of Rockstone, where the\r\ngardens of the Cliff Hotel, of Beechcroft Cottage, Rocca Marina, and\r\nCarrara, belonging respectively to Miss Mohun, Mr. White, and Captain\r\nHenderson, lay close together separated by low walls, and each with a\r\nprivate door opening on a path along the top of the cliffs. They\r\ncould easily be made to communicate together, by planks laid over the\r\nboundaries, and they had lawns adapted for tents, etc., and Rocca\r\nMarina rejoiced in a shrubbery and conservatories that were a show\r\nin themselves, and would be kindly lent by Mr. and Mrs. White, though\r\nhealth compelled them to be absent and to resort to Gastein. The hotel\r\nlikewise had a large well-kept garden, where what Mrs. Simmonds called\r\na pavilion, "quite mediaeval," was in course of erection, and could be\r\nthrown open on the great day.\r\n\r\nIt was rather "tea-gardenish," but it could be made available for the\r\nrepresentation of The Outlaw\'s Isle. Lancelot made a hurried visit\r\nto study the place, and review the forces, and decided that it was\r\npracticable. There could be a gallery at one end for the spectators,\r\nand the outer end toward the bay could be transformed into a stage, with\r\nroom for the orchestra, and if the weather were favourable the real sea\r\ncould be shown in the background. The scenes had been painted by the\r\nclever fingers at Vale Leston. It remained to cast the parts. Lancelot\r\nhimself would be Prospero, otherwise Alaster Maclan, and likewise\r\nconductor, bringing with him the school-master of Vale Leston, who could\r\nsupply his part as conductor when he was on the stage. His little\r\nboy Felix would be Ariel, the other elves could be selected from the\r\nschool-children, and the local Choral Society would supply the wreckers\r\nand the wrecked. But the demur was over Briggs, a retired purser, who\r\nhad always had a monopoly of sea-songs, and who looked on the boatswain\r\nas his right, and was likely to roar every one down. Ferdinand would\r\nbe Gerald, under the name of Angus, but the difficulty was his\r\nMiranda--Mona as she was called. The Vanderkists could not be asked to\r\nperform in public, nor would Sir Jasper Merrifield have consented to\r\nhis daughters doing so, even if they could have sung, and it had been\r\nprivately agreed that none of the other young ladies of Rockquay could\r\nbe brought forward, especially as there was no other grown-up female\r\ncharacter.\r\n\r\n"My wife might undertake it," said Lancelot, "but her voice is not her\r\nstrong point, and she would be rather substantial for a Miranda."\r\n\r\n"It would be rather like finding a mother instead of a wife--with all\r\nrespect to my Aunt Daisy," laughed Gerald.\r\n\r\n"By the bye, I\'m sure I once heard a voice, somewhere down by the sea,\r\nthat would be perfect," exclaimed Lance. "Sweet and powerful, fresh and\r\nyoung, just what is essential. I heard it when I was in quest of crabs\r\nwith my boy."\r\n\r\n"I know!" exclaimed Gerald, "the Little Butterfly, as they call her!"\r\n\r\n"At a cigar-shop," said Lance.\r\n\r\n"Mrs. Schnetterling\'s. Not very respectable," put in Lady Flight.\r\n\r\n"Decidedly attractive to the little boys, though," said Gerald. "Sweets,\r\nfishing-tackle, foreign stamps, cigars. I went in once to see whether\r\nAdrian was up to mischief there, and the Mother Butterfly looked at me\r\nas if I had seven heads; but I just got a glimpse of the girl, and, as\r\nmy uncle says, she would make an ideal Mona, or Miranda."\r\n\r\n"Lydia Schnetterling," exclaimed Mr. Flight. "She is a very pretty girl\r\nwith a nice voice. You remember her, Miss Mohun, at our concerts? A\r\nlovely fairy."\r\n\r\n"I remember her well. I thought she was foreign, and a Roman Catholic."\r\n\r\n"So her mother professes--a Hungarian. The school officer sent her to\r\nschool, and she did very well there, Sunday-school and all, and was a\r\nmonitor. She was even confirmed. Her name is really Ludmilla, and Lida\r\nis the correct contraction. But when I wanted her to be apprenticed as\r\na pupil-teacher, the mother suddenly objected that she is a Roman\r\nCatholic, but I very much doubt the woman\'s having any religion at all.\r\nI wrote to the priest about her, but I believe he could make nothing\r\nof her. Still, Lydia is a very nice girl--comes to church, and has not\r\ngiven up the Choral Society."\r\n\r\n"She is a remarkably nice good girl," added Mrs. Henderson. "She came\r\nto me, and entreated that I would speak for her to be taken on at the\r\nmarble works."\r\n\r\n"You have her there?"\r\n\r\n"Yes; but I am much afraid that her talents do not lie in the way of\r\nhigh promotion, and I think if she does not get wages enough to satisfy\r\nher mother, she is in dread of being made to sing at public-houses and\r\nmusic-halls."\r\n\r\n"That nice refined girl!"\r\n\r\n"Yes; I am sure the idea is dreadful to her."\r\n\r\n"Could you not put her in the way of getting trained?" asked Gerald of\r\nhis uncle.\r\n\r\n"I must hear her first."\r\n\r\n"I will bring her up to the Choral Society tonight," said Mr. Flight.\r\n\r\n"What did you call her?" said Geraldine.\r\n\r\n"Some German or foreign name, Schnetterling, and the school calls her\r\nLydia."\r\n\r\nAt that moment the council was invaded, as it sat in Miss Mohun\'s\r\ndrawing-room, upon rugs and wicker chairs, to be refreshed with tea. In\r\nburst a whole army of Merrifields, headed by little Primrose, now a tall\r\ngirl of twelve years old, more the pet of the family than any of her\r\nelders had been allowed to be. Her cry was--\r\n\r\n"Oh, mamma, mamma, here\'s the very one for the captain of the\r\nbuccaneers!"\r\n\r\nThe startling announcement was followed by the appearance of a tall,\r\nstalwart, handsome young man of a certain naval aspect, whom Lady\r\nMerrifield introduced as Captain Armytage.\r\n\r\n"We must congratulate him, Gillian," she said. "I see you are gazetted\r\nas commander."\r\n\r\nPrimrose, who had something of the licence of the youngest, observed--\r\n\r\n"We have been telling him all about it. He used to be Oliver Cromwell in\r\n\'How Do You Like It?\' and now he will be a buccaneer!"\r\n\r\n"Oliver Cromwell, you silly child!" burst out Gillian, with a little\r\nshake, while the rest fell into fits of laughing.\r\n\r\n"I fear it was a less distinguished part," said Captain Armytage.\r\n\r\n"May I understand that you will help us?" said Lancelot. "I heard of you\r\nat Devereux Castle."\r\n\r\n"I don\'t think you heard much of my capabilities, especially musical\r\nones. I was the stick of the party," said Captain Armytage.\r\n\r\nIt was explained that Captain Armytage had actually arrived that\r\nafternoon at the Cliff Hotel, and had walked over to call at Clipstone,\r\nwhence he found the young ladies setting out to walk to Rockstone.\r\nHe could not deny that he had acted and sung, though, as he said,\r\nhis performance in both cases was vile. Little Miss Primrose had most\r\ncomically taken upon her to patronize him, and to offer him as buccaneer\r\ncaptain had been a freak of her own, hardly to be accounted for, except\r\nthat Purser Briggs\'s unsuitableness had been discussed in her presence.\r\n\r\n"Primrose is getting to be a horrid little forward thing," observed\r\nGillian to her aunt.\r\n\r\n"A child of the present," said Miss Mohun. "Infant England! But her\r\nsuggestion seems to be highly opportune."\r\n\r\n"I don\'t believe he can sing," growled Gillian, "and it will be just an\r\nexcuse for his hanging about here."\r\n\r\nThere was something in Gillian\'s "savagery" which gave Aunt Jane a\r\ncurious impression, but she kept it to herself.\r\n\r\nLate in the evening Lance appeared in his sister\'s drawing-room with--\r\n\r\n"I have more hopes of it. I did not think it was feasible when Anna\r\nwrote to me, but I see my way better now. That parson, Flight, has a\r\ngood notion of drilling, and that recruit of the little Merrifield girl,\r\nCaptain Armytage, is worth having."\r\n\r\n"If he roared like a sucking dove we would have him, only to silence\r\nthat awful boatswain," said Gerald; "and as to the little Cigaretta, she\r\nis a born prima donna."\r\n\r\n"Your Miranda? Are you content with her?" said his aunt.\r\n\r\n"She is to the manner born. Lovely voice, acts like a dragon, and has an\r\ninstinct how to stand and how to hold her hands."\r\n\r\n"Coming in drolly with her prim dress and bearing. Though she was\r\ndreadfully frightened," said Lance. "Being half-foreign accounts for\r\nsomething, I suppose, but it is odd how she reminds me of some one.\r\nNo doubt it is of some singer at a concert. What did they say was her\r\nname?"\r\n\r\n"Ludmilla Schnetterling, the Little Butterfly they call her. Foreign\r\non both sides apparently," said Gerald. "Those dainty ankles never were\r\nbred on English clods."\r\n\r\n"I wonder what her mother is," said Mrs. Grinstead.\r\n\r\n"By the bye, I think it must have been her mother that I saw that\r\nmorning when little Felix dragged me to a cigar-shop in quest of an\r\nornamental crab--a handsome, slatternly hag sort of woman, who might\r\nhave been on the stage," said Lance.\r\n\r\n"Sells fishing-tackle, twine, all sorts," came from Adrian.\r\n\r\n"Have you been there?" asked his sister, rather disturbed.\r\n\r\n"Of course! All the fellows go! It is the jolliest place for"--he paused\r\na moment--"candies and ginger-beer."\r\n\r\n"I should have thought there were nicer places!" sighed Anna.\r\n\r\n"You have yet to learn that there is a period of life when it is a joy\r\nto slip out of as much civilization as possible," said Lance, putting\r\nhis sentence in involved form so as to be the less understood by the\r\nboys.\r\n\r\n"Did you say that Flight had got hold of them?" asked Clement.\r\n\r\n"Hardly. They are R.C.\'s, it seems; and as to the Mother Butterfly,\r\nI should think there was not much to get hold of in her; but Mrs.\r\nHenderson takes interest in her marble-workers, and the girl is the sort\r\nof refined, impressible creature that one longs to save, if possible.\r\nTo-morrow I am going to put you all through your parts, Master Gerald,\r\nso don\'t you be out of the way."\r\n\r\n"One submits to one\'s fate," said Gerald, "hoping that virtue may be its\r\nown reward, as it is in the matter of \'The Inspector\'s Tour\', which\r\nthe \'Censor\' accepts, really enthusiastically for a paper, though the\r\nMouse-trap would have found it--what shall I say?--a weasel in their\r\nsnare."\r\n\r\n"Does it indeed?" cried Anna, delighted. "I saw there was a letter by\r\nthis last post."\r\n\r\n"Aye--invites more from the same pen," he replied lazily.\r\n\r\n"Too much of weasel for the \'Pursuivant\' even?" said Geraldine.\r\n\r\n"Yes," said Lance; "these young things are apt to tear our old traps and\r\nflags to pieces. By the bye, who is this Captain Armytage, who happily\r\nwill limit Purser Briggs to \'We split, we split, we split,\' or something\r\nanalogous?"\r\n\r\n"I believe," said Gerald, "that he joined the Wills-of-the-Wisp, that\r\ncompany which was got up by Sir Lewis Willingham, and played at Devereux\r\nCastle a year or two ago. Some one told me they were wonderfully\r\neffective for amateurs."\r\n\r\n"That explains the acquaintance with Lady Merrifield," said Mrs.\r\nGrinstead.\r\n\r\n"Oh, yes," said Anna. "Mysie told me all about it; and how Mr. David\r\nMerrifield married the nicest of them all, and how much they liked this\r\nCaptain Armytage."\r\n\r\n"Was not Mysie there when he arrived?"\r\n\r\n"No, she was gone to see the Henderson children, but Gillian looked a\r\nwhole sheaf of daggers at him. You know what black brows Gillian has,\r\nand she drew them down like thunder," and Anna imitated as well as her\r\nfair open brows would permit, "turning as red as fire all the time."\r\n\r\n"That certainly means something," said Geraldine, laughing.\r\n\r\n"I should like to see Gillian in love," laughed Anna; "and I really\r\nthink she is afraid of it, she looked so fierce."\r\n\r\nThe next evening there was time for a grand review in the parish\r\nschool-room of all possible performers on the spot. In the midst,\r\nhowever, a sudden fancy flashed across Lancelot that there was something\r\ncuriously similar between those two young people who occupied the\r\nstage, or what was meant to be such. Their gestures corresponded to one\r\nanother, their voices had the same ring, and their eyes wore almost of\r\nthe same dark colour. Now Gerald\'s eyes had always been the only part of\r\nhim that was not Underwood, and had never quite accorded with his fair\r\ncomplexion.\r\n\r\n"Hungarian, I suppose," said Lance to himself, but he was not quite\r\nsatisfied.\r\n\r\nWhat struck him as strange was that though dreadfully shy and frightened\r\nwhen off the stage, as soon as she appeared upon it, though not yet in\r\ncostume, she seemed to lose all consciousness that she was not Mona.\r\n\r\nPerhaps Mrs. Henderson could have told him. Her husband being manager\r\nand partner at Mr. White\'s marble works, she had always taken great\r\ninterest in the young women employed, had actually attended to their\r\ninstruction, assisted in judging of their designs, and used these\r\nbusiness relations to bring them into inner contact with her, so that\r\nher influence had become very valuable. She was at the little room which\r\nshe still kept at the office, when there was a knock at the door, and\r\n"Miss Schnetterling" begged to speak to her. She felt particularly\r\ntender towards the girl, who was evidently doing her best in a trying\r\nand dangerous position, and after the first words it came out--\r\n\r\n"Oh, Mrs. Henderson, do you think I must be Mona?"\r\n\r\n"Have you any real objection, Lydia? Mr. Flight and all of them seem to\r\nwish it."\r\n\r\n"Yes, and I can\'t bear not to oblige Mr. Flight, who has been so good,\r\nso good!" cried Lydia, with a foreign gesture, clasping her hands.\r\n"Indeed, perhaps my mother would not let me off. That is what frightens\r\nme. But if you or some real lady could put me aside they could not\r\nobject."\r\n\r\n"I do not understand you, my dear. You would meet with no unpleasantness\r\nfrom any one concerned, and you can be with the fairy children. Are you\r\nshy? You were not so in the fairy scenes last winter--you acted very\r\nnicely."\r\n\r\n"Oh yes, I liked it then. It carries me away; but--oh! I am afraid!"\r\n\r\n"Please tell me, my dear."\r\n\r\nLydia lowered her voice.\r\n\r\n"I must tell you, Mrs. Henderson, mother was a singer in public once,\r\nand a dancer; and oh! they were so cruel to her, beat her, and starved\r\nher, and ill-used her. She used to tell me about it when I was very\r\nlittle, but now I have grown older, and the people like my voice, she\r\nis quite changed. She wants me to go and sing at the Herring-and-a-Half,\r\nbut I won\'t, I won\'t--among all the tipsy men. That was why she would\r\nnot let me be a pupil-teacher, and why she will not see a priest. And\r\nnow--now I am sure she has a plan in her head. If I do well at this\r\noperetta, and people like me, I am sure she will get the man at the\r\ncircus to take me, by force perhaps, and then it would be all her life\r\nover again, and I know that was terrible."\r\n\r\nPoor Ludmilla burst into tears.\r\n\r\n"Nay, if she suffered so much she would not wish to expose you to the\r\nsame."\r\n\r\n"I don\'t know. She is in trouble about the shop--the cigars. Oh! I\r\nshould not have told! You won\'t--you won\'t--Mrs. Henderson?"\r\n\r\n"No, you need not fear, I have nothing to do with that."\r\n\r\n"I don\'t think," Lydia whispered again, "that she cares for me as she\r\nused to do when I was a little thing. Now that I care for my duty, and\r\nall that you and Mr. Flight have taught me, she is angry, and laughs\r\nat English notions. I was in hopes when I came to work here that my\r\nearnings would have satisfied her, but they don\'t, and I don\'t seem to\r\nget on."\r\n\r\nMrs. Henderson could not say that her success was great, but she\r\nventured as much as to tell her that Captain Henderson could prevent any\r\nattempt to send her away without her consent.\r\n\r\n"Oh! but if my mother went too you could not hinder it."\r\n\r\n"Are you sixteen, my dear? Then you could not be taken against your\r\nwill."\r\n\r\n"Not till December. And oh! that gentleman, the conductor, he knew all\r\nabout it, I could see, and by and by I saw him lingering about the shop,\r\nas if he wanted to watch me."\r\n\r\n"Mr. Lancelot Underwood! Oh, my dear, you need not be afraid of him,\r\nhe is a brother of Mrs. Grinstead\'s, a connection of Miss Mohun\'s; and\r\nthough he is such a musician, it is quite as an amateur. But, Lydia, I\r\ndo think that if you sing your best, he may very likely be able to put\r\nyou in a way to make your talent available so as to satisfy your mother,\r\nwithout leading to anything so undesirable and dangerous as a circus."\r\n\r\n"Then you think I ought--"\r\n\r\n"It is a dangerous thing to give advice, but really, my dear, I do think\r\nmore good is likely to come of this than harm."\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIII. -- TWO SIDES OF A SHIELD AGAIN\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     The wisest aunt telling the saddest tale.\r\n                               Midsummer Night\'s Dream.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe earlier proofs of the Mouse-trap were brought by Lance, who had\r\nspent more time in getting them into shape than his wife approved, and\r\nthey were hailed with rapture by the young ladies on seeing themselves\r\nfor the first time in print. As to Gerald, he had so long been bred--as\r\nit were--to journalism that, young as he was, he had caught the trick,\r\nand \'The Inspector\'s Tour\' had not only been welcomed by the \'Censor\',\r\nbut portions had been copied into other papers, and there was a proposal\r\nof publishing it in a separate brochure. It would have made the fortune\r\nof the Mouse-trap, if it had not been so contrary to its principles, and\r\nit had really been sent to them in mischief, together with The \'Girton\r\nGirl\', of which some were proud, though when she saw it in print, with a\r\nlyre and wreath on the page, sober Mysie looked grave.\r\n\r\n"Do you think it profane to parody Jane Taylor?" said Gerald.\r\n\r\n"No, but I thought it might hurt some people\'s feelings, and discourage\r\nthem, if we laugh at the High School."\r\n\r\n"Why, Dolores goes to give lectures there," exclaimed Valetta.\r\n\r\n"Nobody is discouraged by a little good-humoured banter," said Gillian.\r\n"Nobody with any stuff in them."\r\n\r\n"There must be some training in chaff though," said Gerald, "or they\r\ndon\'t know how to take it."\r\n\r\n"And in point of fact," said Dolores, "the upper tradesmen\'s daughters\r\ncome off with greater honours in the High School than do the young\r\ngentlewomen."\r\n\r\n"Very wholesome for the young Philistines," said Gerald. "The daughters\r\nof self-made men may well surpass in energy those settled on their\r\nlees."\r\n\r\nGerald and Dolores were standing with their backs to the wall of\r\nAnscombe Church, which Jasper Merrifield and Mysie were zealously\r\nphotographing, the others helping--or hindering.\r\n\r\n"I thought upper tradesfolk were the essence of Philistines," returned\r\nDolores.\r\n\r\n"The elder generation--especially if he is the son of the energetic man.\r\nThe younger are more open to ideas."\r\n\r\n"The stolid Conservative is the one who has grown up while his father\r\nwas making his fortune, the third generation used to be the gentleman,\r\nnow he is the man who is tired of it."\r\n\r\n"Tired of it, aye!" with a sigh.\r\n\r\n"Why you are a man with a pedigree!" she returned.\r\n\r\n"Pedigrees don\'t hinder--what shall I call it?--the sense of being\r\nfettered."\r\n\r\n"One lives in fetters," she exclaimed. "And the better one likes one\'s\r\nhome, the harder it is to shake them off."\r\n\r\nHe turned and looked full at her, then exclaimed, "Exactly," and paused,\r\nadding, "I wonder what you want. Has it a form?"\r\n\r\n"Oh yes, I mean to give lectures. I should like to see the world, and\r\nstudy physical science in every place, then tell the next about it. I\r\nread all I can, and I think I shall get consent to give some elementary\r\nlectures at the High School, though Uncle Jasper does not half like it,\r\nbut I must get some more training to do the thing rightly. I thought of\r\nUniversity College. Could you get me any information about it?"\r\n\r\n"Easily; but you\'ll have to conquer the horror of the elders."\r\n\r\n"I know. They think one must learn atheism and all sorts of things\r\nthere."\r\n\r\n"You might go in for physical science at Oxford or Cambridge."\r\n\r\n"I expect that is all my father would allow. In spite of the colonies,\r\nhe has all the old notions about women, and would do nothing Aunt Lily\r\nreally protested against."\r\n\r\n"You are lucky to have a definite plan and notion to work for. Now fate\r\nwas so unkind as to make me a country squire, and not only that, but\r\none bound down, like Gulliver among the Liliputians, with all manner\r\nof cords by all the dear good excellent folks, who look on that old\r\nmediaeval den with a kind of fetish-worship, sprung of their having\r\nbeen kept out of it so long, and it would be an utter smash of all their\r\nhearts if I uttered a profane word against it. I would as soon be an\r\nancient Egyptian drowning a cat as move a stone of it. It is a lovely\r\nsort of ancient Pompeii, good to look at now and then, but not to be\r\nbound down to."\r\n\r\n"Like Beechcroft Court, a fossil. It is very well there are such\r\nplaces."\r\n\r\n"Yes, but not to be the hope of them. It is my luck. If my eldest uncle,\r\nwho had toiled in a bookseller\'s shop all his youth and reigned like a\r\nlittle king, had not gone and got killed in a boating accident, there\r\nhe would be the ruling Sir Roger de Coverley of the county, a pillar of\r\nChurch and State, and I should be a free man."\r\n\r\n"Won\'t they let you go about, and see everything?"\r\n\r\n"Oh yes, I am welcome to do a little globe-trotting. They are no fools;\r\nif they were I should not care half so much; but wherever I went, there\r\nwould be a series of jerks from my string, and not having an integument\r\nof rhinoceros hide, I could not disregard them without a sore more raw\r\nthan I care to carry about. After all, it is only a globe, and one gets\r\nback to the same place again."\r\n\r\n"Men have so many openings."\r\n\r\n"I\'m not rich enough for Parliament, and if I were, maybe it would be\r\nworse for their hearts," he said, with a sigh.\r\n\r\n"There\'s journalism, a great power."\r\n\r\n"Yes, but to put my name to all I could--and long to say--would be an\r\nequal horror to the dear folks."\r\n\r\n"Yet you are helping on this concern."\r\n\r\n"True, but partly pour passer le temps, partly because I really want\r\nto hear \'The Outlaws Isle\' performed, and all under protest that the\r\nwindmill will soon be swept away by the stream."\r\n\r\n"Indeed, yes," cried Dolores. "They hope to regulate the stream. They\r\nmight as well hope to regulate Mississippi."\r\n\r\n"Well-chosen simile! The current is slow and sluggish, but\r\nirresistible."\r\n\r\n"Better than stagnating or sticking fast in the mud."\r\n\r\n"Though the mud may be full of fair blossoms and sweet survivals," said\r\nGerald sadly.\r\n\r\n"Oh yes, people in the old grooves are delightful," said Dolores, "but\r\none can\'t live, like them, with a heart in G. F. S., like my Aunt\r\nJane, really the cleverest of any of us! Or like Mysie, not stupid, but\r\nwrapped up in her classes, just scratching the surface. Now, if I went\r\nin for good works I would go to the bottom--down to the slums."\r\n\r\n"Slums are one\'s chief interest," said Gerald; "but no doubt it will\r\nsoon be the same story over and over, and only make one wish--"\r\n\r\n"What?"\r\n\r\n"That there could be a revolution before I am of age."\r\n\r\n"What\'s that?" cried Primrose, coming up as he spoke. "A revolution?"\r\n\r\n"Yes, guillotines and all, to cut off your head in Rotherwood Park,"\r\nsaid Gerald lightly.\r\n\r\n"Oh! you don\'t really mean it."\r\n\r\n"Not that sort," said Dolores. "Only the coming of the coquecigrues."\r\n\r\n"They are in \'The Water Babies\'," said Primrose, mystified.\r\n\r\nEach of those two liked to talk to the other as a sort of\r\nfellow-captive, solacing themselves with discussions over the \'Censor\'\r\nand its fellows. Love is not often the first thought, even where it\r\nlurks in modern intellectual intercourse between man and maid; and\r\nthough Kitty Varley might giggle, the others thought the idea only\r\nworthy of her. Aunt Jane, however, smelt out the notion, and could not\r\nbut communicate it to her sister, though adding--\r\n\r\n"I don\'t believe in it: Dolores is in love with Physiology, and the\r\nboy with what Jasper calls Socialist maggots, but not with each other,\r\nunless they work round in some queer fashion."\r\n\r\nHowever, Lady Merrifield, feeling herself accountable for Dolores, was\r\nanxious to gather ideas about Gerald from his aunt, with whom she was\r\nbecoming more and more intimate. She was more than twenty years the\r\nsenior, and the thread of connection was very slender, but they suited\r\none another so well that they had become Lilias and Geraldine to one\r\nanother. Lady Merrifield had preserved her youthfulness chiefly from\r\nhaving had a happy home, unbroken by family sorrows or carking\r\ncares, and with a husband who had always taken his full share of\r\nresponsibility.\r\n\r\n"Your nephew\'s production has made a stir," said she, when they found\r\nthemselves alone together.\r\n\r\n"Yes, poor boy." Then answering the tone rather than the words, "I\r\nsuppose it is the lot of one generation to be startled by the next.\r\nThere is a good deal of change in the outlook."\r\n\r\n"Yes," said Lady Merrifield. "The young ones, especially the youngest,\r\nseem to have a set of notions of their own that I cannot always follow."\r\n\r\n"Exactly," said Geraldine eagerly.\r\n\r\n"You feel the same? To begin with, the laws of young\r\nladyhood--maidenliness--are a good deal relaxed--"\r\n\r\n"There I am not much of a judge. I never had any young ladyhood, but I\r\nown that the few times I went out with Anna I have been surprised, and\r\nmore surprised at what I heard from her sister Emily."\r\n\r\n"What we should have thought simply shocking being tolerated now."\r\n\r\n"Just so; and we are viewed as old duennas for not liking it. I should\r\nsay, however, that it is not, or has not, been a personal trouble with\r\nme. Anna\'s passion is for her Uncle Clement, and she has given up the\r\nseason on his account, though Lady Travis Underwood was most anxious to\r\nhave her; and as to Emily, though she is obliged to go out sometimes,\r\nshe hates it, and has a soul set on slums and nursing."\r\n\r\n"You mean that the style of gaieties revolts a nice-minded girl?"\r\n\r\n"Partly. Perhaps such as the Travis Underwoods used to take part in,\r\nrather against their own likings, poor things, are much less restrained\r\nfor the young people than what would come in your daughters\' way."\r\n\r\n"Perhaps; though Lady Rotherwood has once or twice in country-houses had\r\nto protect her daughter, to the great disgust of the other young people.\r\nThat is one development that it is hard to meet, for it is difficult\r\nto know where old-fashioned distaste is the motive, and where the real\r\nprinciple of modesty. Though to me the question is made easy, for\r\nSir Jasper would never hear of cricket for his daughters, scarcely\r\nof hunting, and we have taken away Valetta and Primrose from the\r\ndancing-classes since skirt-dancing has come in; but I fear Val thinks\r\nit hard."\r\n\r\n"Such things puzzle my sisters at Vale Leston. They are part of the\r\nsame spirit of independence that sends girls to hospitals or medical\r\nschools."\r\n\r\n"Or colleges, or lecturing. Dolores is wild to lecture, and I see no\r\nharm in her trying her wings at the High School on some safe subject, if\r\nher father in New Zealand does not object, though I am glad it has not\r\noccurred to any of my own girls."\r\n\r\n"Sir Jasper would not like it?"\r\n\r\n"Certainly not; but if my brother consents he will not mind it for\r\nDolores. She is a good girl in the main, but even mine have very\r\ndifferent ideals from what we had."\r\n\r\n"Please tell me. I see it a little, and I have been thinking about it."\r\n\r\n"Well, perhaps you will laugh, but my ideal work was Sunday-schools."\r\n\r\n"Are not they Miss Mohun\'s ideal still?"\r\n\r\n"Oh yes, infinitely developed, and so they are my cousin\r\nFlorence\'s--Lady Florence Devereux; but the young ones think them\r\nbehind the times. I remember when every girl believed her children the\r\nprettiest and cleverest in nature, showed off her Sunday-school as her\r\npride and treasure, and composed small pink books about them, where the\r\ncatastrophe was either being killed by accident, or going to live in the\r\nclergyman\'s nursery. Now, those that teach do so simply as a duty and\r\nnot a romance."\r\n\r\n"And the difficulty is to find those who will teach," said Geraldine.\r\n"One thing is, that the children really require better teaching."\r\n\r\n"That is quite true. My girls show me their preparation work, and I see\r\nmuch that I should not have thought of teaching the Beechcroft children.\r\nBut all the excitement of the matter has gone off."\r\n\r\n"I know. The Vale Leston girls do it as their needful work, not with\r\ntheir hearts and enthusiasm. I expect an enthusiasm cannot be expected\r\nto last above a generation and perhaps a half."\r\n\r\n"Very likely. A more indifferent thing; you will laugh, but my\r\nenthusiasm was for chivalry, Christian chivalry, half symbolic. History\r\nwas delightful to me for the search for true knights. I had lists of\r\nthem, drawings if possible, but I never could indoctrinate anybody with\r\nmy affection. Either history is only a lesson, or they know a great\r\ndeal too much, and will prove to you that the Cid was a ruffian, and the\r\nBlack Prince not much better."\r\n\r\n"And are you allowed the \'Idylls of the King\'?"\r\n\r\n"Under protest, now that the Mouse-trap has adopted Browning for weekly\r\nreading and discussion. Tennyson is almost put on the same shelf with\r\nScott, whom I love better than ever. Is it progress?"\r\n\r\n"Well, I suppose it is, in a way."\r\n\r\n"But is it the right way?"\r\n\r\n"That\'s what I want to see."\r\n\r\n"Now listen. When our young men, my brothers--especially my very dear\r\nbrother Claude and his contemporaries, Rotherwood is the only one\r\nleft--were at Oxford, they got raised into a higher atmosphere, and came\r\nhome with beautiful plans and hopes for the Church, and drew us up\r\nwith them; but now the University seems just an ordeal for faith to go\r\nthrough."\r\n\r\n"I should think there was less of outward temptation, but more of subtle\r\ntrial. And then the whole system has altered since the times you are\r\nspeaking of, when the old rules prevailed, and the great giants of\r\nChurch renewal were there!" said Geraldine.\r\n\r\n"You belong to the generation whom they trained, and who are now passing\r\naway. My father was one who grew up then."\r\n\r\n"We live on their spirit still."\r\n\r\n"I hope so. I never knew much about Cambridge till Clement went there,\r\nbut it had the same influence on him. Indeed, all our home had that one\r\nthought ever since I can remember. Clement and Lance grew up in it."\r\n\r\n"But you will forgive me. These younger men either go very, very much\r\nfurther than we older ones dreamt of, or they have flaws in their\r\nfaith, and sometimes--which is the strangest difficulty--the vehement\r\nobservance and ritual with flaws beneath in their faith perhaps, or\r\ntheir loyalty--Socialist fancies."\r\n\r\n"There is impatience," said Geraldine. "The Church progress has not\r\nconquered all the guilt and misery in the world."\r\n\r\n"Who said it would?"\r\n\r\n"None of us; but these younger ones fancy it is the Church\'s fault,\r\ninstead of that of her members\' failures, and so they try to walk in the\r\nlight of the sparks that they have kindled."\r\n\r\n"Altruism as they call it--love of the neighbour without love of God."\r\n\r\n"It may lead that way."\r\n\r\n"Does it?"\r\n\r\n"Perhaps we are the impatient ones now," said Geraldine, "in disliking\r\nthe young ones\' experiments, and wanting to bind them to our own views."\r\n\r\n"Then you look on with toleration but with distrust."\r\n\r\n"Distrust of myself as well as of the young ones, and trying not to\r\nforget that \'one good custom may corrupt the world,\' so it may be as\r\nwell that the pendulum should swing."\r\n\r\n"The pendulum, but not its axis--faith!"\r\n\r\n"No; and of my boy\'s mainspring of faith I _do_ feel sure, and of his\r\nreal upright steadiness."\r\n\r\nLady Merrifield asked no more, but could wait.\r\n\r\nBut is not each generation a terra incognita to the last? A question\r\nwhich those feel most decidedly who stand on the border-land of both,\r\nwith love and sympathy divided between the old and the new, clinging to\r\nthe one, and fearing to alienate the other.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIV. -- BUTTERFLY\'S NECTAR\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     If you heed my warning\r\n     It will save you much.--A. A. PROCTOR.\r\n\r\n\r\nClement Underwood was so much better as to be arrived at taking solitary\r\nrides and walks, these suiting him better than having companions, as he\r\nliked to go his own pace, and preferred silence. His sister had become\r\nmuch engrossed with her painting, and saw likewise that in this matter\r\nof exercise it was better to let him go his own way, and he declared\r\nthat this time of thought and reading was an immense help to him,\r\nrestoring that balance of life which he seemed to himself to have lost\r\nin the whirl of duties at St. Matthew\'s after Felix\'s death.\r\n\r\nThe shore, with the fresh, monotonous plash of the waves, when the tide\r\nserved, was his favourite resort. He could stand still and look out\r\nover the expanse of ripples, or wander on, as he pleased, watching the\r\nsea-gulls float along--\r\n\r\n\r\n          "As though life\'s only call and care\r\n             Were graceful motion."\r\n\r\n\r\nThere had been a somewhat noisy luncheon, for Edward Harewood, a\r\nmidshipman in the Channel Fleet, which was hovering in the offing, had\r\ncome over on a day\'s leave with Horner, a messmate whose parents lived\r\nin the town. He was a big lad, a year older than Gerald, and as soon as\r\na little awe of Uncle Clement and Aunt Cherry had worn off, he showed\r\nhimself of the original Harewood type, directing himself chiefly to what\r\nhe meant to be teasing Gerald about Vale Leston and Penbeacon.\r\n\r\n"All the grouse there were on the bit of moor are snapped up."\r\n\r\n"Very likely," said Gerald coolly.\r\n\r\n"Those precious surveyors and engineers that Walsh brings down can give\r\nan account of them! As soon as you come of age, you\'ll have to double\r\nyour staff of keepers, I can tell you."\r\n\r\n"Guardians of ferae naturae," said Gerald.\r\n\r\n"I thought your father did all that was required in that line," said\r\nClement.\r\n\r\n"Not since duffers and land-lubbers have been marauding over\r\nPenbeacon--aye, and elsewhere. What would you say to an engineer\r\npoaching away one of the august house of Vanderkist?"\r\n\r\n"The awful cad! I\'d soon show him what I thought of his cheek," cried\r\nAdrian, with a flourish of his knife.\r\n\r\n"Ha, ha! I bet that he will be shooting over Ironbeam Park long before\r\nyou are of age."\r\n\r\n"I shall shoot him, then," cried Adrian.\r\n\r\n"Not improbably there will be nothing else to shoot by that time,"\r\nquietly said Gerald.\r\n\r\n"I shall have a keeper in every lodge, and bring up four or five hundred\r\npheasants every year," boasted the little baronet, quite alive to the\r\npride of possession, though he had never seen Ironbeam in his life.\r\n\r\nEdward laughed a "Don\'t you wish you may get it," and the others, who\r\nknew very well the futility of the poor boy\'s expectations, even\r\nif Gerald\'s augury were not fulfilled, hastened to turn away the\r\nconversation to plans for the afternoon. Anna asked the visitor if he\r\nwould ride out with her and Gerald to Clipstone or to the moor, and was\r\nrelieved when he declined, saying he had promised to meet Horner.\r\n\r\n"You will come in to tea at five?" said his aunt, "and bring him if you\r\nlike."\r\n\r\n"Thanks awfully, but we hardly can. We have to start from the quay at\r\nsix sharp."\r\n\r\nAll had gone their several ways, and Clement, after the heat of the day,\r\nwas pacing towards a secluded cove out of an inner bay which lay nearer\r\nthan Anscombe Cove, but was not much frequented. However, he smelt\r\ntobacco, and heard sounds of boyish glee, and presently saw Adrian and\r\nFergus Merrifield, bare-legged, digging in the mud.\r\n\r\n"Ha! youngsters! Do you know the tide has turned? I thought you had had\r\nenough of that."\r\n\r\n"I thought I might find my aralia!" sighed Fergus. "The tide was almost\r\nas low."\r\n\r\nJust then there resounded from behind a projecting rock a peal of\r\nundesirable singing, a shout of laughter, and an oath, with--\r\n\r\n"Holloa, those little beasts of teetotallers have hooked it."\r\n\r\nThere were confused cries--"Haul \'em back! Drench \'em. Give \'em a roll\r\nin the mud!" and Adrian shrank behind his uncle, taking hold of his\r\ncoat, as there burst from behind the rock a party of boys, headed by the\r\ntwo cadets, all shouting loudly, till brought to a sudden standstill by\r\nthe sight of "Parson! By Jove!" as the Horner mid muttered, taking\r\nout his pipe, while Edward Harewood mumbled something about "Horner\'s\r\nbrother\'s tuck-out." One or two other boys were picking up the remains\r\nof the feast, which had been on lobsters, jam tarts, clotted cream,\r\nand the like delicacies dear to the juvenile mind. The two biggest\r\nschool-boys came forward, one voluble and thick of speech about Horner\'s\r\ntuck-out, and "I assure you, sir, it is nothing--not a taste. Never\r\nthought of such--" Just then the other lad, staggering about, had almost\r\nlurched over into the deepening channel; but Clement caught him by the\r\ncollar and held him fast, demanding in a low voice, very terrible to his\r\nhearers--\r\n\r\n"Where does this poor boy live?"\r\n\r\nIt was Adrian who answered.\r\n\r\n"Devereux Buildings."\r\n\r\n"You two, Adrian and Fergus, run to the quay and fetch a cab as near\r\nthis place as it can come," said Clement. "You little fellows, you had\r\nbetter run home at once. I hope you will take warning by the shame and\r\ndisgrace of this spectacle."\r\n\r\nThe boys were glad enough to disperse, being terrified by the condition\r\nof the prisoner, as well as by the detection; but the two who were\r\nencumbered with the baskets containing the bottles, jam-pots, and tin\r\nof cream remained, and so did the two young sailors, Horner saying\r\ncivilly--\r\n\r\n"You\'ll not be hard on the kids, sir, for just a spree carried a little\r\ntoo far."\r\n\r\n"I certainly shall not be hard on the children, whom you seem to have\r\ntempted," was the answer as they moved along; and as the younger Horner\r\nturned towards a little shop near the end of the steps to restore the\r\ngoods, he asked--"Were you supplied from hence?"\r\n\r\n"Yes," said Horner, who was perhaps hardly sober enough for caution.\r\n"Mother Butterfly is a jolly old soul."\r\n\r\nLooking up. Clement saw no licence to sell spirituous liquors under the\r\nname of Sarah Schnetterling, tobacconist. The window had the placard\r\n\'Ici on parle Francais\', and was adorned in a tasteful manner with\r\nornamental pipes, fishing-rods and flies, jars of sweets, sheets of\r\nforeign stamps, pictorial advertisements of innocuous beverages. A woman\r\nwith black grizzling hair, fashionably dressed, flashing dark eyes, long\r\ngold ear-rings, gold beads and gaudy attire, came out to reclaim her\r\nproperty. A word or two passed about payment, during which Clement had\r\na strange thrill of puzzled recollection. The bottles bore the labels of\r\nraspberry vinegar and lemonade, but he had seen too much not to say--\r\n\r\n"You drive a dangerous trade."\r\n\r\n"Ah, sir, young people will be gourmands," she said, with a foreign\r\naccent. "Ah, that poor young gentleman is very ill. Will he not come in\r\nand lie down to recover?"\r\n\r\n"No, thank you," said Clement. "A carriage is coming to take him home."\r\n\r\nSomething about the fat in the fire was passing between the cadets, and\r\nthe younger of them began to repeat that he had come for his brother\'s\r\nbirthday, and that he feared they had brought the youngsters into a\r\nscrape by carrying the joke too far.\r\n\r\n"I have nothing to say to you, sir," said the Vicar of St. Matthew\'s,\r\nlooking very majestic, "except that it is time you were returning to\r\nyour ship. As to you," turning to Edward Harewood, "I can only say that\r\nif you are aware of the peculiar circumstances of Adrian Vanderkist,\r\nyour conduct can only be called fiendish."\r\n\r\nFergus and Adrian came running up with tidings that the cab was waiting.\r\nEdward Harewood stood sullen, but the other lad said--\r\n\r\n"Unlucky. We are sorry to have got the little fellows into trouble."\r\n\r\nHe held out his hand, and Clement did not refuse it, as he did that of\r\nhis own nephew. Still, there was a certain satisfaction at his heart as\r\nhe beheld the clear, honest young faces of the other two boys, and he\r\nbade Adrian run home and wait for him, saying to Fergus--\r\n\r\n\r\n"You seem to have been a good friend to my little nephew. Thank you."\r\n\r\nFergus coloured up, speechless between pleasure at the warm tone of\r\ncommendation and the obligations of school-boy honour, nor, with young\r\nCampbell on their hands, was there space for questions. That youth\r\nsubsided into a heavy doze in the cab, and so continued till the arrival\r\nat No. 7, Devereux Buildings, where a capable-looking maid-servant\r\nopened the door, and he was deposited into her hands, the Vicar leaving\r\nhis card with his present address, but feeling equal to nothing more,\r\nand hardly able to speak.\r\n\r\nHe drove home, finding his nephew in the doorway. Signing to the maid to\r\npay the driver, and to the boy to follow him, he reached his study, and\r\nsank into his easy-chair, Adrian opening frightened eyes and saying--\r\n\r\n"I\'ll call Sibby."\r\n\r\n"No--that bottle--drop to there," signing to the mark on the glass with\r\nhis nail.\r\n\r\nAfter a pause, while he held fast the boy, so to speak, with his eyes,\r\nhe said--\r\n\r\n"Thank you, dear lad."\r\n\r\n"Uncle Clement," said Adrian then, "we weren\'t doing anything.\r\nMerrifield thought his old bit of auralia, or whatever he calls it, was\r\nthere."\r\n\r\n"I saw--I saw, my boy. To find you--as you were, made me most thankful.\r\nYou must have resisted. Tell me, were you of this party, or did you come\r\non them by accident?"\r\n\r\n"Horner asked me," said Adrian, twisting from one leg to another.\r\n\r\nClement saw the crisis was come which he had long expected, and rejoiced\r\nat the form it had taken, though he knew he should suffer from pursuing\r\nthe subject.\r\n\r\n"Adrian," he said, "I am much pleased with you. I don\'t want to get you\r\ninto a row, but I should be much obliged if you would tell me how all\r\nthis happened."\r\n\r\n"It wouldn\'t," returned Adrian, "but for that Ted and the other chap."\r\n\r\n"Do you mean that there would have been none of this--drinking--but for\r\nthem? Don\'t be afraid to tell me all. Was the stuff all got from that\r\nMrs. Schnetter--?"\r\n\r\n"Mother Butterfly\'s? Oh yes. She keeps bottles of grog with those\r\nlabels, and it is such a lark for her to be even with the gangers that\r\nour fellows generally get some after cricket, or for a tuck-out."\r\n\r\n"Not Fergus Merrifield?"\r\n\r\n"Oh no; he\'s captain, you know, but he is two years younger than\r\nCampbell and Horner, and they can\'t bear him, and when he made a jaw\r\nabout it--he can jaw awfully, you know--and he is stuck up, and Horner\r\nmajor swore he would make him know his bearings--"\r\n\r\n"I wonder he was there at all."\r\n\r\n"Well, Horner asked him, and he can\'t get those fossils that were lost\r\nout of his head, and he thought they might be washed up. He said too, he\r\nknew they would be up to something if he wasn\'t there."\r\n\r\n"Oh!" said Clement, with an odd recollection, "but I suppose he did not\r\nknow about these cadets?"\r\n\r\n"No, the big Horner sent up to Mother Butterfly\'s for some more stuff,\r\nnot so mild, and then Ted set upon me, and said it was all because of me\r\nthat Vale Leston had to live like a boiling of teetotal frogs and toads,\r\njust to please the little baronet\'s lady mamma, but I was a Dutchman\r\nall the same, and should sell them yet--I sucked it in so well, and they\r\ntalked of seeing how much I could stand. Something about my governor,\r\nand here--that word in the Catechism."\r\n\r\n"Ah!" gasped Clement, fairly clutching his arm, "and what spared you?"\r\n\r\n"Horner came down, and Sweetie Bob, that\'s the errand-boy, and there was\r\na bother about the money, for Bob wasn\'t to leave anything without being\r\npaid, and while they were jawing about that, Merry laid hold of me and\r\nsaid, \'Come and look for the aralia.\' They got to shouting and singing,\r\nand I don\'t think they saw what was doing. They were nasty songs, and\r\nMerry touched me and said, \'Let us go after the aralia.\' We got away\r\nwithout their missing us at first, but they ran after us when they found\r\nit out, and if you had not been there, Uncle Clem--"\r\n\r\n"Thank God I was! Now, Adrian, first tell me, did you taste this stuff?\r\nYou said you sucked it in."\r\n\r\n"Well, I did, a little. You know, uncle, one cannot always be made a\r\nbaby. Women don\'t understand, you know, and don\'t know what a fool it\r\nmakes a man to have them always after him, and have everything put out\r\nof his way like a precious infant, and people drinking it on the sly\r\nlike Gerald, or--"\r\n\r\n"Or me, eh, Adrian? I can tell you that I never tasted it for thirty\r\nyears, and now only as a medicine. Lance, never."\r\n\r\n"But they did not treat you like a baby, and never let you see so much\r\nas a glass of beer."\r\n\r\n"Well, I am going to treat you like a man, but it is a sorrowful history\r\nthat I have to tell you. You know that your mother and Aunt Wilmet are\r\ntwin sisters?"\r\n\r\n"Oh yes, though Aunt Wilmet is stout and jolly, and mother ever so much\r\nprettier and more delicate and nice."\r\n\r\n"Yes, from ill-health. She is never free from suffering."\r\n\r\n"I know. Old Dr. May said there was no help for it."\r\n\r\n"Do you know what caused that ill-health? My boy, they spoke of your\r\nfather to-day--brutes that they were," he could not help muttering.\r\n\r\n"Yes, he died when I was a week old."\r\n\r\n"He had ruined himself when quite a young man, body, soul, and\r\nestate--and you too, beforehand, in estate, and broken your mother\'s\r\nheart and health by being given up to that miserable habit from which we\r\nwant to save you."\r\n\r\n"I thought it was only poor men that got drunk and beat their wives"\r\n(more knowledge, by the bye, than he was supposed to possess). "He did\r\nnot beat her?"\r\n\r\n"Oh no, no," said Clement, "but he as surely destroyed all her\r\nhappiness, and made you and your sisters very poor for your station in\r\nlife, so that it is really hard to educate you, and you will have to\r\nwork for yourself and them. And at only thirty-six years old his life\r\nwas cut off."\r\n\r\n"Was that what D. T. meant? I heard Ted whisper something about that."\r\n\r\n"It was well," thought Clement, "that he had grace enough to whisper.\r\nYes, my poor boy, it is only too true. I was sent for to find your\r\nfather dying of delirium tremens--you just born, your mother nearly\r\ndead, the desolation of your sisters unspeakable. He was only\r\nthirty-six, and that vice, together with racing, had devoured him and\r\nall the property that should have come to his children. I think he tried\r\nto repent at the very last, but there was little time, little power,\r\nonly he put you and your sisters in my charge, and begged me to save you\r\nfrom being like him."\r\n\r\n"Did they mean that I was sure to be like that? Like a pointer puppy,\r\npointing."\r\n\r\n"They meant it. And, Adrian, it is so far true that there is an\r\ninheritance--with some more, with some less--of our forefathers\' nature.\r\nSome have tendencies harder to repress than others. But, my dear boy,\r\nyou know that we all have had a force given us wherewith to repress and\r\nconquer those tendencies, and that we can."\r\n\r\n"When we were baptized, God the Holy Spirit," said Adrian, under his\r\nbreath.\r\n\r\n"You know it, you can believe now. Your uncle Lance and I prayed that\r\nthe old nature might be put down, the new raised up. We pray, your\r\nmother and sisters have prayed ever since, that so it may be, that you\r\nmay conquer any evil tendencies that may be in you; but, Adrian, no one\r\ncan save you from the outside if you do not strive yourself. Now you see\r\nwhy your poor mother has been so anxious to keep all temptation out of\r\nyour reach."\r\n\r\n"But I\'m growing a man now. I can\'t always go on so."\r\n\r\n"No, you can\'t. You shall be treated as a man while you are with me.\r\nBut I do very seriously advise you--nay, I entreat of you, not to begin\r\ntaking any kind of liquor, for it would incite the taste to grow upon\r\nyou, till it might become uncontrollable, and be your tyrant. If you\r\nhave reason to think the pledge would be a protection to you, come to\r\nme, or to Uncle Bill."\r\n\r\nHe was interrupted by Sibby coming in with his cup of tea, and--\r\n\r\n"Now, Mr. Clement, whatever have you been after now? Up to your\r\nantics the minute Miss Cherry is out of the way. Aye, ye needn\'t go\r\nto palavering me. I hear it in your breath," and she darted at the\r\nstimulant.\r\n\r\n"I\'ve had some, Sibby, since I came in."\r\n\r\n"More reason you should have it now. Get off with you, Sir Adrian, don\'t\r\nbe worriting him. Now, drink that, sir, and don\'t speak another word."\r\n\r\nHe was glad to obey. He wanted to think, in much thankfulness for the\r\npresent, and in faith and love which brought hope for the future.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XV. -- A POOR FOREIGN WIDOW\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     Art thou a magistrate?  Then be severe.--GEORGE HERBERT.\r\n\r\n\r\nEarly in the day General Mohun received a note from Clement Underwood,\r\nbegging him to look in at St. Andrew\'s Rock as soon as might be\r\nconvenient.\r\n\r\n"Ah," said his sister, "I strongly suspect something wrong about the\r\nboys. Fergus was very odd and silent last night when I asked him about\r\nJem Horner\'s picnic, and he said something about that Harewood cousin\r\nbeing an unmitigated brute."\r\n\r\n"I hope Fergus was not in a scrape."\r\n\r\n"Oh no, it is not his way. His geology is a great safeguard. If it had\r\nbeen Wilfred I might have been afraid."\r\n\r\n"His head is full--at least as much room as the lost aralia leaves--of\r\nthe examination for the Winchester College election."\r\n\r\n"Yes, you know Jasper has actually promised Gillian that if either\r\nof her brothers gets a scholarship, she may be allowed a year at Lady\r\nMargaret Hall."\r\n\r\n"Yes, it incited her to worry Wilfred beyond sufferance in his holidays.\r\nI know if you or Lily had been always at me I should have kicked as hard\r\nas he does."\r\n\r\n"Lily herself can hardly cram him with his holiday task; but Fergus is a\r\ngood little fellow."\r\n\r\n"You have kept him at it in a more judgmatical way. But won\'t Armytage\r\ncome in between the damsel and her college?"\r\n\r\n"Poor Mr. Armytage--Captain, I believe, for he has got his\r\ncommandership. Gill snubs him desperately. I believe she is afraid of\r\nherself and her heart."\r\n\r\n"I hope she won\'t be a goose. Jasper told me that he is an excellent\r\nfellow, and it will be an absolute misfortune if the girl is besotted\r\nenough to refuse him."\r\n\r\n"Girls have set up a foolish prejudice against matrimony."\r\n\r\n"Well, I am off. Clement Underwood is a reasonable man, and would not\r\nsend for me without cause."\r\n\r\nGeneral Mohun came to that opinion when he heard of the scene on the\r\nbeach, and of the absolute certainty that the contraband goods had been\r\nprocured at Mrs. Schnetterling\'s. Before his visit was over, a note came\r\ndown on gold-edged, cyphered pink paper, informing the Reverend E. C.\r\nUnderwood that Mrs. Campbell was much obliged to him for his attention\r\nto her son, who was very unwell, entirely from the effects of clotted\r\ncream. And while they were still laughing over the scored words, Anna\r\nknocked at the door with a message from her aunt, to ask whether they\r\ncould come and speak to poor Mrs. Edgar, who was in a dreadful state.\r\n\r\n"It is not about Adrian, I hope?" said she.\r\n\r\n"Oh no, no, my dear; Adrian is all right, thanks to Fergus again," said\r\nher uncle. "He is the boy\'s great protector; I only wish they could be\r\nalways together."\r\n\r\nPoor Mrs. Edgar! Rumours had not been slow in reaching her of the\r\ncondition in which her scholars had been found, very odd rumours too.\r\nOne that James Campbell had been brought home insensible, and the two\r\nsailors carried on board in the like state; and an opposite report, that\r\nthe poor dear boys had only made themselves sick with dainties out of\r\nMrs. Schnetterling\'s, and it was all a cruel notion of that teetotal\r\nritualist clergyman. Some boys would not speak, others were vague and\r\ncontradictory, and many knew nothing, Horner and Campbell were absent.\r\nClement much relieved her by giving an account of the matter, and\r\ndeclaring that he feared his own elder nephew was the cause of all the\r\nscandal, though he believed that some of her bigger pupils were guilty\r\nof obtaining a smaller quantity, knowingly, of the Schnetterling\'s\r\nillicit wares, chiefly so far for the fun of doing something\r\nforbidden--"Stolen waters are sweet."\r\n\r\n"A wicked woman! Surely she should not be allowed to go on."\r\n\r\n"I am going, on the spot, to see what can be done," said General Mohun;\r\n"but indeed I should have thought young Campbell rather too old for your\r\nprecincts."\r\n\r\n"Ah! yes. He is troublesome, but he is so backward, and is so delicate,\r\nthat his mother has implored me to keep him on, that he may have\r\nsea-bathing. But this shall be the final stroke!"\r\n\r\n"It will be the ruin of your school otherwise," said the General.\r\n\r\n"Ah! it might. And yet Mrs. Campbell will never be persuaded of the\r\nfact! And she is a person of much influence! However, I cannot have my\r\npoor dear little fellows led astray."\r\n\r\nThen, with some decided praises of dear little Sir Adrian, and regrets\r\nat losing Fergus Merrifield, whom she declared, on the authority of her\r\ngentleman assistant, to be certain of success, she departed; and Clement\r\nresumed his task of writing letters, which he believed to be useless,\r\nbut which he felt to be right--one a grave warning to Edward Harewood,\r\nand one to his father, whose indulgence he could not but hold\r\naccountable.\r\n\r\nReginald Mohun meanwhile went his way to the officer of Inland Revenue,\r\nwho already had his suspicions as to Mrs. Schnetterling, and was glad\r\nof positive evidence. He returned with the General to hear from Mr.\r\nUnderwood the condition in which he had found the boys, and the cause he\r\nhad for attributing it to the supplies from Mother Butterfly, and this\r\nwas thought sufficient evidence to authorize the sending a constable\r\nwith a search-warrant to the shop. The two gentlemen were glad that the\r\ndetection should be possible without either sending a spy, or forcing\r\nevidence from the boys, who had much better be kept out of the matter\r\naltogether. No lack of illicit stores was found when the policemen made\r\ntheir descent, and a summons was accordingly served on its mistress to\r\nappear at the next Petty Sessions.\r\n\r\nReginald Mohun, used to the justice of county magistrates, and the\r\nunflinching dealings of courts-martial, was determined to see the affair\r\nthrough, so he went to the magistrates\' meeting, and returned with the\r\ntidings that the possession of smuggled tobacco ready for sale had been\r\nproved against Mrs. Schnetterling, and she had been fined twenty-five\r\npounds, to be paid at the next Petty Sessions. Otherwise goods would be\r\nseized to that value, or she would have a short term of imprisonment.\r\nThere was no doubt that contraband spirits were also found, but it was\r\nnot thought expedient to press this charge.\r\n\r\nHe said the poor woman had been in a great passion of despair, wringing\r\nher hands and weeping demonstratively.\r\n\r\n"Quite theatrical," he said. "I am sure she has been an actress."\r\n\r\n"It did not prejudice your hard-headed town-councillors in her favour,"\r\nsaid Gerald.\r\n\r\n"Far from it! In fact old Simmonds observed that she was a painted\r\nforeign Jezebel."\r\n\r\n"Not to her face!" said Gerald.\r\n\r\n"We are not quite brutes, whatever you may think us, my boy," said the\r\nGeneral good-humouredly.\r\n\r\n"Well," said Gerald, in the same tone, "how could I tell how it might be\r\nwhen the Philistines conspired to hunt down a poor foreign widow trying\r\nto pick up a scanty livelihood?"\r\n\r\n"If the poor foreign widow had been content without corrupting the\r\nboys," said Clement, "she would have been let alone."\r\n\r\n"It was not for corrupting the boys. That was done--or not done--by my\r\namiable cousin Ted. What harm did her \'baccy do to living soul?"\r\n\r\n"It is a risky thing, to say the least of it, for a living soul to\r\ndefraud the revenue," said Clement.\r\n\r\n"Of which probably she never heard."\r\n\r\n"She must have seen the terms of her licence," said the General.\r\n\r\n"Aye, a way of increasing the revenue by burthens on the chief solace of\r\npoverty," said Gerald hotly.\r\n\r\n"You\'ll come to your senses by and by, young man," imperturbably\r\nanswered the General.\r\n\r\n"Is she likely to be able to pay?" asked Gerald in return.\r\n\r\n"Oh yes, the policeman said she drove a very thriving trade, both with\r\nthe boys and with the sailors, and that there was no doubt that she\r\ncould pay."\r\n\r\nClement was very glad to hear it, for it not only obviated any sense\r\nof harshness in his mind, but he thought Gerald, in his present mood\r\nof compassion--or opposition, whichever it was--capable of offering to\r\nundertake to pay the fine for her.\r\n\r\nPoor little Ludmilla was found the next day by Mrs. Henderson, crying\r\nsoftly over her work at the mosaic department--work which was only\r\nthe mechanical arrangement from patterns provided, for she had no\r\noriginality, and would never attain to any promotion in the profession.\r\n\r\nMrs. Henderson took the poor girl to her own little office, to try to\r\ncomfort her, and bring her into condition for the rehearsal of the scene\r\nwith Ferdinand, which she was to go through in Mr. Flight\'s parlour\r\nchaperoned by his mother. She was so choked with sobs that it did not\r\nseem probable that she would have any voice; for she had been struggling\r\nwith her tears all day, and now, in the presence of her friend, she\r\ngave them a free course. She thought it so cruel--so very cruel of the\r\ngentlemen; how could they do such a thing to a poor helpless stranger?\r\nAnd that tall one--to be a clergyman--how could he?\r\n\r\nMrs. Henderson tried to represent that, having accepted the licence on\r\ncertain terms, it was wrong to break them; and that the gentlemen must\r\nbe right to hinder harm to their nephews.\r\n\r\nIt seemed all past the poor girl\'s understanding, since the nephews had\r\ntaken no harm; and indeed the other boys had only touched the spirits\r\nby way of joke and doing something forbidden: it had all come of those\r\nhorrid young midshipmen, who had come down and worried and bothered her\r\nmother into giving them the bottles of spirits which had not been mixed.\r\nIt was very hard.\r\n\r\n"Ah, Lydia, one sin leads no one knows where! Those little boys, think\r\nof their first learning the taste for alcohol in secret!"\r\n\r\nLydia did see this, but after all, she said, it was not the spirits, but\r\nthe tobacco, which the Dutch and American sailors were glad enough to\r\nexchange for her mother\'s commodities. She had never perceived any harm\r\nin the arrangement, and hardly comprehended when the saying, "Custom to\r\nwhom custom," was pointed out to her.\r\n\r\nKalliope asked whether the fine would fall heavily on her mother.\r\n\r\n"Oh, that is worst of all. Mother is gone to Avoncester to raise the\r\nmoney. She won\'t tell me how. And I do believe O\'Leary\'s circus is\r\nthere."\r\n\r\nThen came another sobbing fit.\r\n\r\n"But how--what do you mean, my dear?"\r\n\r\n"O\'Leary was our clown when my father--my dear father--was alive. He was\r\na coarse horrid man, as cruel to the poor dear horses as he dared. And\r\nnow he has set up for himself, and has been going about all over the\r\ncounty. Mother has been quite different ever since she met him one day\r\nin Avoncester, and I fear--oh, I fear he will advance her this money,\r\nand make her give me up to him; and my dear father made her promise that\r\nI would never be on the boards."\r\n\r\nThis was in an agony of crying, and it appeared that Schnetterling had\r\nreally been a very decent, amiable person, who had been passionately\r\nfond of his little daughter. Her recollection dated from the time when\r\nthe family had come from America, and he had become partner in a circus,\r\nintending to collect means enough to retire to a home in Germany, but he\r\nhad died five years ago, at Avoncester, of fever, and his wife had used\r\nhis savings to set up this little shop at Rockquay, choosing that place\r\nbecause it was the resort of foreign trading-vessels, with whom her\r\nknowledge of languages would be available. She had suffered from the\r\nsame illness, and her voice had been affected at the time, and she was\r\naltogether subdued and altered, and had allowed her daughter to receive\r\na good National school training; but with the recovery of health,\r\nactivity, and voice, a new temper, or rather the old one renewed,\r\nhad seized her, and since she had met her former companion, Ludmilla\r\nforeboded that the impulse of wandering had come upon her, and that if\r\nthe interference of the authorities pressed upon her and endangered her\r\ntraffic, she would throw it up altogether, and drag her daughter into\r\nthe profession so dreadful to all the poor child\'s feelings.\r\n\r\nNo wonder that the girl cried till she had no voice, and took but\r\npartial comfort from repeated assurances that her friends would do their\r\nutmost on her behalf. Mrs. Henderson tried to compose and cheer her,\r\nwalking with her herself to St. Kenelm\'s Parsonage, and trying to keep\r\nup her earnest desire to please Mr. Flight, the special object of her\r\nveneration. But wishes were ineffectual to prevent her from breaking\r\ndown in the first line of her first song, and when Mr. Flight\r\nblamed, and Lady Flight turned round on the music-stool to say\r\nseverely--"Command yourself, Lydia," she became almost hysterical.\r\n\r\n"Wait a minute," said Gerald. "Give her a glass of wine, and she will be\r\nbetter."\r\n\r\n"Oh no, no; please, I\'m temp--" and a sob.\r\n\r\nThe five o\'clock tea was still standing on a little table, and Gerald\r\npoured out a cup and took it to her, then set her down in an arm-chair,\r\nand said--\r\n\r\n"I\'ll go through Angus\' part, and she will be better," and as she tried\r\nto say "Thank you," and "So kind," he held up his hand, and told her to\r\nbe silent. In fact, his encouragement, and the little delay he had made,\r\nenabled her to recover herself enough to get through her part, though\r\nnothing like as well as would have been expected of her.\r\n\r\n"Never mind," said Gerald, "she will be all right when my uncle comes.\r\nWon\'t you, Mona?"\r\n\r\n"I should have expected--" began Lady Flight.\r\n\r\nGerald held up his hand in entreaty.\r\n\r\n"People\'s voices can\'t be always the same," he said cheerily. "I know\r\nour Mona will do us credit yet! Won\'t you, Mona? You know how to pity me\r\nwith my logs!"\r\n\r\n"You had better go and have some tea in the kitchen, Lydia," said Lady\r\nFlight repressively; and Ludmilla curtsied herself off, with a look of\r\ngratitude out of her swollen eyelids at Gerald.\r\n\r\n"Poor little mortal," he said, as she went. "I am afraid that in her\r\ncase summum jus was summa injuria."\r\n\r\n"It was quite right to prosecute that mischievous woman," said Mr.\r\nFlight.\r\n\r\n"Maybe," said Gerald; "but wheat will grow alongside of tares."\r\n\r\n"I hope the girl is wheat," half ironically and severely said the lady.\r\n\r\nGerald shrugged his shoulders and took his leave.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVI. -- "SEE, THE CONQUERING HERO COMES"\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     And with trumpets and with banners\r\n     As becomes gintale good manners.--THACKERAY.\r\n\r\n\r\nA telegram from Sir Jasper brought the good news that Fergus\'s name was\r\nhigh on the Winchester roll, and that he was sure of entering college\r\nafter the holidays. Gillian alone was allowed to go up to the station\r\nwith her uncle Reginald to meet the travellers, lest the whole family\r\nshould be too demonstrative in their welcome. And at the same time there\r\nemerged from the train not only Captain Armytage, but also Lancelot\r\nUnderwood and his little boy. All the rest of his family were gone to\r\nStoneborough to delight the hearts of Dr. May and his daughter Ethel.\r\n\r\nGillian was in such training that she durst not embrace her brother when\r\nhe tumbled out of the carriage, though she could hardly keep her feet\r\nfrom dancing, but she only demurely said--\r\n\r\n"Mamma and all of them are at Aunt Jane\'s."\r\n\r\n"Come then," said Sir Jasper to Captain Armytage, for which Gillian was\r\nnot grateful, or thought herself not, for she made a wry face.\r\n\r\nThere was a good deal of luggage--theatrical appliances to be sent to\r\nthe pavilion.\r\n\r\n"This may as well go too," said Captain Armytage.\r\n\r\n"Oh! oh! It is the buccaneer\'s sword!" cried little Felix. "How lovely!\r\nLast time we only had Uncle Jack\'s, and this is ever so much longer!"\r\n\r\n"Do let me draw it!" cried Fergus.\r\n\r\n"Not here, my boy, or they would think a conspiracy was breaking out.\r\nHa!" as a sudden blare of trumpets broke out as they reached the station\r\ngate.\r\n\r\n"Oh, is it for him?" cried Felix, who had been instructed in Fergus\'s\r\ntriumph.\r\n\r\n\r\n         "See, the conquering hero comes,\r\n          Sound the trumpets, beat the drums!"\r\n\r\n\r\nsaid the General.\r\n\r\nFergus actually coloured crimson, but the colour was deepened as he\r\nmuttered "Bosh!" while two piebald ponies, drawing the drummers and\r\ntrumpeters in fantastic raiment, preceded an elephant shrouded in\r\nscarlet and gold trappings, with two or three figures making contortions\r\non his back, and followed by a crowned and sceptred dame in blue, white,\r\nand gold, perched aloft on a car drawn by four steeds in glittering\r\ncaparisons.\r\n\r\n"Will you mount it, Fergus?" asked his uncle. "You did not expect such a\r\ndemonstration."\r\n\r\nFergus bit his lip. It was hard to be teased instead of exalted; but\r\nFely and he were absorbed in the pink broadsides that the lady in the\r\ncar was scattering.\r\n\r\n\r\n            CIRCUS--THIS NIGHT--ROTHERWOOD PARK.\r\n\r\n                   The Sepoy\'s Revenge!\r\n                   Thrilling Incidents!\r\n                    Sagacious Elephant!\r\n                  Dance of Arab Coursers!!\r\n                 Acrobatic Feats!! &c., &c.\r\n\r\n\r\n"Oh, daddy! daddy! do take me to see it!"\r\n\r\n"Father, I should like to see it very much indeed," were the\r\nexclamations of the two little boys. "You know I have never seen any\r\nacrobatic feats."\r\n\r\n"A long word enough to please you," said Uncle Reginald. "He deserves\r\nsomething. I\'ll take you, master."\r\n\r\n"I should think this was not of the first quality," said Sir Jasper.\r\n\r\n"Never mind. Novelty is the charm that one can have only once in one\'s\r\nlife," said the General.\r\n\r\n"Some of those van fellows are very decent folk," said Lancelot. "I have\r\nseen a great deal of them at Bexley Fair times. You would be astonished\r\nto know how grateful they are for a little treatment as if they were not\r\nout of humanity\'s reach."\r\n\r\nGillian was trying to make Fergus tell her what his questions had been,\r\nand how he had answered them.\r\n\r\n"I declare, Gill, you are as bad as some of the boys\' horrid governors.\r\nThere was one whose father walked him up and down and wouldn\'t let him\r\nplay cricket, and went over all the old questions with him. I should\r\nnever have got in, if papa hadn\'t had more sense than to badger me out\r\nof my life."\r\n\r\nAt the gate between the copper beeches the Underwoods and Merrifields\r\nparted, with an engagement to meet at the circus on the part of the boys\r\nand their conductors.\r\n\r\nFergus was greeted with open-mouthed, open-armed delight by all the\r\nassembled multitude, very little checked by the presence of Captain\r\nArmytage. Only Lady Merrifield did not say much, but there was a dew in\r\nher eyes as she held fast the little active fingers, and whispered--\r\n\r\n"My good industrious boy."\r\n\r\nSir Jasper, in his grand and gracious manner, turned to his\r\nsister-in-law, saying--\r\n\r\n"We could not but come first to you, Jane, for it is to you that he is\r\nindebted, as we all are, primarily for his success."\r\n\r\n"That is the greatest compliment I ever had, Jasper," she answered,\r\nsmiling but almost tearful, and laughing it off. "I feel ready to mount\r\nyonder elephant lady\'s triumphal car."\r\n\r\nThe General refrained from any more teasing of Fergus on his first\r\nimpression; and at seven that evening the younger Merrifield boys with\r\ntheir uncle, and the two from St. Andrew\'s Rock with Lance, set off in\r\nhigh spirits.\r\n\r\nThey re-appeared much sooner than they were expected at Beechcroft\r\nCottage, where the Underwoods were spending the long twilight evening.\r\n\r\n"A low concern!" was the General\'s verdict.\r\n\r\n"We fled simultaneously from the concluding ballet," said Lance. "There\r\nhad been quite as much as we could bear for ingenuous youth."\r\n\r\n"We stood the Sepoy\'s Death Song," said the General, "but the poster of\r\nthe Bleeding Bride was enough for us."\r\n\r\n"They had only one elephant!" cried Adrian.\r\n\r\n"A regular swindle," said Wilfred.\r\n\r\n"No lions!" added Fely, "nothing to see but that poor old elephant!
