I\r\nwish he would have turned round and spouted water at them, as that one\r\ndid to the tailor."\r\n\r\n"Water would be uncommonly good for them," said the General, laughing,\r\n"they are not much acquainted therewith."\r\n\r\n"And such an atmosphere!" said Lance.\r\n\r\n"I see it on your forehead, poor boy," said Geraldine.\r\n\r\n"I should like to set on the Society against cruelty to animals," said\r\nthe General; "I saw galls on the horses\' necks, and they were all half\r\nstarved."\r\n\r\n"Then to see the poor old elephant pretend to be drunk!" added Fergus,\r\n"stagger about, and led off by the policeman, drunk and disorderly!"\r\n\r\n"Was that being drunk?" asked Adrian, with wide-open eyes. "It was like\r\nCampbell that day." Everybody laughed.\r\n\r\nWilfred did so now.\r\n\r\n"You green kid, you."\r\n\r\n"Happy verdure," said the General, "to be unaware that some people can\r\nlaugh when they ought to weep."\r\n\r\n"Weep!" exclaimed Wilfred, "every time one sees a fellow screwy in the\r\nstreet."\r\n\r\n"Perhaps the angels do," murmured Clement.\r\n\r\n"Come, Master Wilfred, you have expressed your opinions sufficiently\r\nto-night," said the General. "Suppose you and Fergus walk home together.\r\nA nasty low place as ever I saw. I have a mind to tell the Mayor about\r\nit."\r\n\r\nGerald said--\r\n\r\n"Is not that making yourself very unpopular?"\r\n\r\n"That is no great matter," said the General, rather surprised.\r\n\r\n"I should have thought it better to refine the people\'s tastes than to\r\nthwart their present ones."\r\n\r\n"The improper must be stopped before the taste for the proper can be\r\npromoted," said Clement.\r\n\r\n"With all the opposition and ill-blood that you cause?" said Gerald.\r\n"Why, if I were an errand-boy, the suppression would send me direct to\r\nthe circus. Would it not do the same by you, Uncle Lance?"\r\n\r\n"Discouragement might, prohibition would prevent wholly, and I should be\r\nthankful," said Lance.\r\n\r\n"Ah! you are of the old loyal nature," said Gerald. "You of the old\r\nschool can never see things by modern lights."\r\n\r\n"I am thankful to say--not," responded Reginald Mohun, in a tone that\r\nmade some laugh, and Gerald sigh in Anna\'s ear--\r\n\r\n"Happy those who see only one side of a question."\r\n\r\nThere was another great day for the boys, namely, the speech or closing\r\nday at the school, when Fergus was the undoubted hero, and was so\r\nexalted that his parents thought it would be very bad for him, and were\r\nchiefly consoled by his strong and genuine dislike to having to declaim\r\nwith Clement Varley the quarrel of Brutus and Cassius. He insisted on\r\nalways calling the former "Old Brute," and all the efforts of mother and\r\naunt never got him beyond the dogged repetition of a lesson learnt by\r\nheart, whereas little Varley threw himself into the part with spirit\r\nthat gained all the applause. Fergus carried off a pile of prizes too,\r\nbut despised them. "Stupid old poetry!" said he, "what should I do with\r\nthat? Do let me change it, father, for the Handbook of Paleontology, or\r\nsomething worth having."\r\n\r\nAdrian had three prizes too, filling Anna with infinite delight. He was\r\nnot to go home immediately on the break-up of the school, but was to\r\nwait for his sisters, who were coming in a few days more with Lady\r\nTravis Underwood to the bazaar and masque, so that he would go home with\r\nthem.\r\n\r\nNeither the prospect nor the company of little Fely greatly reconciled\r\nhim to the delay, but his mother could not believe that her darling\r\ncould travel alone, and his only satisfaction was in helping Fergus to\r\narrange his spare specimens for sale.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVII. -- EXCLUDED\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n   But I needn\'t tell you what to do, only do it out of hand,\r\n   And charge whatever you like to charge, my lady won\'t make a stand.\r\n                                                        --T. HOOD.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe ladies\' committee could not but meet over and over again, wandering\r\nabout the gardens, which were now trimmed into order, to place the\r\nstalls and decide on what should and should not be.\r\n\r\nThere was to be an art stall, over which Mrs. Henderson was to preside.\r\nHere were to be the very graceful and beautiful articles of sculpture\r\nand Italian bijouterie that the Whites had sent home, and that were\r\nspared from the marble works; also Mrs. Grinstead\'s drawings, Captain\r\nHenderson\'s, those of others, screens and scrap-books and photographs.\r\nJasper and a coadjutor or two undertook to photograph any one who wished\r\nit; and there too were displayed the Mouse-traps. Mrs. Henderson, sure\r\nto look beautiful, quite Madonna-like in her costume, would have the\r\ncharge of the stall, with Gillian and two other girls, in Italian\r\npeasant-dresses, sent home by Aunt Ada.\r\n\r\nGillian was resolved on standing by her. "Kalliope wants some one\r\nto give her courage," she said. "Besides, I am the mother of the\r\nMouse-trap, and I must see how it goes off."\r\n\r\nLady Flight and a bevy of young ladies of her selection were to\r\npreside over the flowers; Mrs. Yarley undertook the refreshments; Lady\r\nMerrifield the more ordinary bazaar stall. Her name was prized, and\r\nAnna was glad to shelter herself under her wing. The care of Valetta and\r\nPrimrose, to say nothing of Dolores, was enough inducement to overcome\r\nany reluctance, and she was glad to be on the committee when vexed\r\nquestions came on, such as Miss Pettifer\'s offer of a skirt-dance, which\r\ncould not be so summarily dismissed as it had been at Beechcroft, for\r\nLady Flight and Mrs. Varley wished for it, and even Mrs. Harper was\r\nready to endure anything to raise the much-needed money, and almost\r\nthought Lady Merrifield too particular when she discontinued the\r\ndancing-class for Valetta and Primrose.\r\n\r\n"That speaks for itself," said Mrs. Grinstead.\r\n\r\n"I can fancy seeing no harm in it for little girls," said Lady\r\nMerrifield, "but I don\'t like giving them a talent the use of which\r\nseems to be to enable them to show off."\r\n\r\n"And I know that Lady Rotherwood would not approve," said Miss Mohun,\r\naware that this settled the matter. "And here\'s another outsider, Miss\r\nPenfeather, who offers to interpret handwriting at two-and-sixpence a\r\nhead."\r\n\r\n"By all means," was the cry. "We will build her a bower somewhere near\r\nthe photography."\r\n\r\n"I am only afraid," added Jane, "of her offering to do palmistry. Do you\r\nknow, I dabbled a little in that once, and I came to the conclusion that\r\nit was not a safe study for oneself or any one else."\r\n\r\n"Quite right," said Geraldine.\r\n\r\n"Do you believe in it then?"\r\n\r\n"Not so as to practise it, or accept it so far as the future is\r\nconcerned, and to play at it as a parody of fortune-telling seems to me\r\nutterly inadmissible."\r\n\r\n"And to be squashed with Lord Rotherwood\'s mighty name," said her\r\nsister, laughing.\r\n\r\nLady Rotherwood would do so effectively. Wherewith came on the question\r\nof raffles, an inexhaustible one, since some maintained that they were\r\ncontrary to English law, and were absolutely immoral, while others held\r\nthat it was the only way of disposing of really expensive articles.\r\nThese were two statues sent by Mrs. White, and an exquisite little\r\npicture by Mrs. Grinstead, worth more than any one could be expected\r\nto give. It was one that she had nearly finished at the time of Mr.\r\nGrinstead\'s illness--John Inglesant arriving in his armour of light on\r\nhis wedding morning--and the associations were so painful that she said\r\nshe never wished to see it again.\r\n\r\nThere were likewise a good many charming sketches of figures and\r\nscenery, over which Gerald and Anna grieved, though she had let them\r\nkeep all they could show cause for; but drawing had become as much her\r\nresource as in the good old days. She was always throwing off little\r\noutlines, and she had even begun a grand study, which she called "Safe\r\nHome," a vessel showing signs of storm and struggle just at the verge of\r\na harbour lost in golden light.\r\n\r\nAnd the helmsman\'s face?\r\n\r\nClement and Lance neither of them said in words whose it was, as they\r\nboth stood looking at it, and owned to themselves the steadfast face of\r\ntheir eldest brother, but Clement said, with a sigh--\r\n\r\n"Ah! we are a long way as yet from that."\r\n\r\n"I\'m very glad to hear you say so," exclaimed Lance; then laughing at\r\nhimself, "You are ever so much better."\r\n\r\n"Oh yes, I suppose I am to start again, going softly all my days,\r\nperhaps, and it is well, for I don\'t think the young generation can\r\nspare me yet."\r\n\r\n"Nor Cherry."\r\n\r\n"How thankful I am to have Cherry restored to me I cannot say, and I do\r\nnot feel convinced that there may not be care at hand with Gerald. The\r\nboy is in a reserved mood, very civil and amiable, but clearly holding\r\nback from confidence."\r\n\r\n"Does she see it?"\r\n\r\n"Yes; but she fancies he bestows his confidence on Dolores Mohun, the\r\ngirl from New Zealand, and resigns herself to be set aside. It is pretty\r\nwell time that we went to meet her."\r\n\r\nFor there was to be a dress rehearsal in the pavilion, to which certain\r\nspectators were to be admitted, chiefly as critics.\r\n\r\n"Do you walk up the hill, Clem?"\r\n\r\n"Yes, as long as I don\'t go too fast. Go on if you are wanted, and I\r\nwill follow. Cherry has sent the carriage for an invalid who cannot\r\nventure to be there all the day."\r\n\r\n"Let them wait. A walk with you is not to be wasted. Run on, Fely, tell\r\nthem we are coming," he added to his little Ariel, who had got lost in\r\nJungle Beasts.\r\n\r\nAs they went up the hill together, Clement not sorry to lean on his\r\nbrother\'s arm, a dark woman of striking figure and countenance, though\r\nfar from young, came up with them, accompanied by a stout, over-dressed\r\nman.\r\n\r\n"That\'s the cigar-shop woman," said Lance, "the mother of our pretty\r\nlittle Miranda."\r\n\r\n"I wonder she chooses to show herself after her conviction," said\r\nClement.\r\n\r\n"And if I am not much mistaken, that is the villain of The Sepoy\'s\r\nRevenge," said Lance. "Poor little Butterfly, it is a bad omen for her\r\nfuture fate."\r\n\r\nAs they reached the doors of the great hotel, they found the pair in\r\naltercation with the porter before the iron gate that gave admittance to\r\nthe gardens. "Mother Butterfly" was pleading that she was the mother of\r\nMiss Schnetterling, who was singing, and the porter replying that his\r\norders were strict.\r\n\r\n"No, not on any consideration," he repeated, as the man was evidently\r\nshowing him the glance of silver, and a policeman, who was marching\r\nabout, showed signs of meaning to interfere.\r\n\r\nAt the same moment Gerald\'s quick steps came up from the inside.\r\n\r\n"That\'s right, Lance; every one is crying out for you. Vicar, Cherie is\r\nkeeping a capital place for you."\r\n\r\nThe gate opened to admit them, and therewith Mrs. Schnetterling, trying\r\nto push in, made a vehement appeal--\r\n\r\n"Mr. Underwood, sir, surely the prima donna\'s own mother should not be\r\nexcluded."\r\n\r\n"Her mother!" said Gerald. "Well, perhaps so, but hardly this--person,"\r\nas his native fastidiousness rose at the sight.\r\n\r\n"No, sir," said the porter. "Captain Henderson and Mr. Simmonds, they\r\nhave specially cautioned me who I lets in."\r\n\r\nThe man grumbled something about swells and insolence, and Lance, with\r\nhis usual instinct of courtesy, lingered to say--\r\n\r\n"This is quite a private rehearsal--only the persons concerned!"\r\n\r\n"And if I\'m come on business," said the man confidentially. "You are\r\nsomething in our line."\r\n\r\n"Scarcely," said Lance, rather amused. "At any rate, I don\'t make the\r\nregulations."\r\n\r\nHe sped away at the summons of his impatient son and Gerald.\r\n\r\nThey met Captain Henderson on the way, and after a hasty greeting, he\r\nsaid--\r\n\r\n"So you have let in the Schnetterling woman?"\r\n\r\n"One could not well keep out the mother," returned Lance.\r\n\r\n"Well, no, but did she bring a man with her? My wife says the poor\r\nlittle Mona is in mortal terror lest he is come to inspect her for a\r\ncircus company."\r\n\r\n"Quite according to his looks," said Lance. "Poor child, it may be her\r\nfate, but she ought to be in safe hands, but I suppose the woman wants\r\nto sacrifice her to present gain."\r\n\r\nThey went on their way, and Lance and Gerald were soon absorbed in their\r\ncares of arrangement, while Clement was conducted to the seat reserved\r\nfor him between his sister and Lady Merrifield. The pavilion had been\r\nfitted with stages of seats on the inner side, but the back--behind\r\nthe stage--was so contrived that in case of favourable weather the real\r\nsea-view could be let in upon occasion, though the curtain and adjuncts,\r\nwhich had been painted by some of the deft fingers at Vale Leston,\r\nrepresented the cavern; also there was a first scene, with a real sail\r\nand mast.\r\n\r\nIt was a kind of semi-dress rehearsal, beginning with pirate songs by\r\nthe school-master and choir, who had little difficulty in arranging\r\nthemselves as buccaneers. The sail was agitated, then reefed, stormy\r\nsongs were heard, where Captain Armytage did his part fairly well; the\r\nboatswain was gratified by roaring out his part characteristically, and\r\nthe curtain fell on "We split, we split, we split."\r\n\r\nThen came a song of Prospero, not much disguised by a plaid and Highland\r\nbonnet, interrupted by the pretty, graceful Miranda, very shy and\r\nill-assured at first, but gathering strength from his gentle encouraging\r\nways, while he told what was needful in the recitative that he alone\r\ncould undertake. Then the elves and fairies, led by little Felix, in\r\na charming cap like Puck, danced on and sang, making the prettiest of\r\ntableaux, lulling Miranda to sleep, and then Ariel conversing in a most\r\ndainty manner with Prospero.\r\n\r\nNext Ferdinand and Miranda had their scene, almost all songs and duets.\r\nBoth sang very sweetly, and she had evidently gained in courage, and\r\nthrew herself into her part.\r\n\r\nThe shipwrecked party then came on the scene, performed their songs,\r\nand were led about Puck-fashion by the fairies, and put to sleep by the\r\nlament over Ferdinand. The buccaneers in like manner were deluded by\r\nmore mischievous songs and antics, till bogged and crying out behind the\r\nscenes.\r\n\r\nTheir intended victims were then awakened, to find themselves in the\r\npresence of Prospero; sing themselves into the reconciliation, then\r\nmourn for Ferdinand, until the disclosure of the two lovers, and the\r\nfinal release of Ariel and the sprites, all singing Jacobite songs.\r\n\r\nTo those who were not au fait with the \'Tempest\' and felt no indignation\r\nor jealousy at the travesty, it was charming; and though the audience at\r\nthe rehearsal numbered few of these, the refined sweetness and power\r\nof the performers made it delightful and memorable. Every one was in\r\nraptures with the fairies, who had been beautifully drilled, and above\r\nall with their graceful little leader, with his twinkling feet and arch\r\nlively manner, especially in the parts with his father.\r\n\r\nFerdinand and Miranda--or rather Angus and Mona--were quite ideal in\r\nlooks, voices, and gestures.\r\n\r\n"Almost dangerously so," said Jane Mohun; "and the odd thing is that\r\nthey are just alike enough for first cousins, as they are here, though\r\nShakespeare was not guilty of making them such."\r\n\r\n"The odd thing is," said Geraldine, as she drove home with Clement,\r\n"that this brought me back so strangely to that wonderful concert\r\nat home, with all of you standing up in a row, and the choir from\r\nMinsterham, and poor Edgar\'s star."\r\n\r\n"An evil star!" sighed Clement.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVIII. -- THE EVIL STAR\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                              Lancelot said,\r\n     That were against me, what I can I will;\r\n     And there that day remained.--TENNYSON.\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was on the night before the final bustle and fury, so to speak, of\r\npreparation were to set in, when arrivals were expected, and the sellers\r\nwere in commotion, and he had been all day putting the singers one by\r\none through their parts, that as he went to his room at night, there was\r\na knock at Lancelot\'s door, and Gerald came in, looking deadly white.\r\nHe had been silent and effaced all the evening, and his aunt had thought\r\nhim tired, but he had rather petulantly eluded inquiry, and now he came\r\nin with--\r\n\r\n"Lance, I must have it out with some one."\r\n\r\n"An Oxford scrape?" said Lance.\r\n\r\n"Oh no, I wish it was only that." Then a silence, while Lance looked\r\nat him, thinking, "What trouble could it be?" He had been very kind and\r\ngentle with the little Miranda, but the manner had not struck Lance as\r\nlover-like.\r\n\r\nThere was a gasp again--\r\n\r\n"That person, that woman at the gate, do you remember?"\r\n\r\nTherewith a flash came over Lance.\r\n\r\n"My poor boy! You don\'t mean to say--"\r\n\r\nNeither could bring himself to say the word so sacred to Lancelot, and\r\nwhich might have been so sacred to his nephew.\r\n\r\n"How did you guess?" said Gerald, lifting up the face that he had hidden\r\non the table.\r\n\r\n"I saw the likeness between you and the girl. She reminded me of some\r\none I had once seen."\r\n\r\n"Had you seen her?"\r\n\r\n"Once, at a concert, twenty odd years ago. Your aunt, too, was strangely\r\ncarried back to that scene, by the girl\'s voice, I suppose."\r\n\r\n"Poor child!" said Gerald, still laying down his head and seeming\r\nterribly oppressed, as Lance felt he well might be.\r\n\r\n"It is a sad business for you," said the uncle, with a kind hand on his\r\nshoulder. "How was it she did not claim you before?--not that she has\r\nany real claim."\r\n\r\n"She did not know my real name. My father called himself Wood. I never\r\nknew the rest of it till after I came home. That fellow bribed the\r\ngardener, got in over the wall, or somehow, and when she saw you, and\r\nheard you and me and all three of us, it gave her the clue."\r\n\r\n"Well, Gerald, I do not think she can dare to--"\r\n\r\n"Oh!" interrupted Gerald, "there\'s worse to come."\r\n\r\n"What?" said Lance, aghast.\r\n\r\n"She says," and a sort of dry sob cut him short, "she says she had a\r\nhusband when she married my father," and down went his head again.\r\n\r\n"Impossible," was Lance\'s first cry; "your father\'s first care was to\r\ntell Travis all was right with you. Travis has the certificates."\r\n\r\n"Oh yes, it was no fault of my father--my father, my dear father--no,\r\nbut she deceived him, and I am an impostor--nobody."\r\n\r\n"Gently, gently, Gerald. We have no certainty that this is true. Your\r\nfather had known her for years. Tell me, how did it come out--what\r\nevidence did she adduce?"\r\n\r\nGerald nerved himself to sit up and speak collectedly.\r\n\r\n"I believe it is half that circus fellow\'s doing. I think she is going\r\nto marry him, if she hasn\'t already. She followed me, and just at the\r\nturn down this road, as I was bidding the Mona girl goodnight, she came\r\nup with me, and said I little thought that the child was my sister, and\r\nhow delightful it was to see us acting together. Well then, I can\'t say\r\nbut a horror came over me. I couldn\'t for the life of me do anything but\r\ndraw back, there was something so intolerable in the look of her eyes,\r\nand her caressing manner," and he shuddered, glad of his uncle\'s kind\r\nhand on his shoulder. "Somehow, I let her get me out upon the high\r\nground, and there she said, \'So you are too great a swell to have word\r\nor look for your mother. No wonder, you always were un vilain petit\r\nmiserable; but I won\'t trouble you--I wouldn\'t be bound to live your\r\ndull ennuyant ladies\' life for millions. I\'ll bargain to keep out of\r\nyour way; but O\'Leary and I want a couple of hundred pounds, and you\'ll\r\nnot grudge it to us.\' I had no notion of being blackmailed, besides I\r\nhaven\'t got it, and I told her she might know that I am not of age,\r\nand had no such sum ready to hand. She was urgent, and I began to think\r\nwhether I could do anything to save that poor little sister, when she\r\nevidently got some fresh impulse from the man, and began to ask me how\r\nI should like to have it all disclosed to my nobs of friends. Well, I\r\nwasn\'t going to be bullied, and I answered that my friends knew already,\r\nand she might do her worst. \'Oh, may I?\' she said; \'you wouldn\'t like,\r\nmy fine young squire, to have it come out that I never was your father\'s\r\nwife at all, and that you are no more than that gutter-child.\' I could\r\nnot understand her at first, and said I would not be threatened, but\r\nthat made her worse, and that rascal O\'Leary came to her help. They\r\nraised their demands somehow to five hundred, and declared if they had\r\nnot it paid down, they should tell the whole story and turn me out. Of\r\ncourse I said they were welcome. Either I am my father\'s lawful son, or\r\nI am not, and if not, the sooner it is all up with me the better, for\r\nwhatever I am, I am no thief and robber. So I set off and came down\r\nthe hill; but the brute kept pace with me to this very door, trying\r\nto wheedle me, I believe. And now what\'s to be done? I would go off at\r\nonce, and let Uncle Clem come into his rights, only I don\'t want to be\r\nthe death of him and Cherie."\r\n\r\n"No," said Lance, "my dear fellow! You have stood it wisely and bravely\r\nso far, go on to do so. I don\'t feel the least certain that this is not\r\nmere bullying. She did not tell you any particulars?"\r\n\r\n"No, certainly not."\r\n\r\n"Not the name of this supposed predecessor of Edgar\'s? Where she may\r\nhave been married, or how? How she parted from him, or how she knows he\r\nwas alive? It sounds to me a bogus notion, got up to put the screw on\r\nyou, by surprise. I\'ll tell you what I\'ll do. I\'ll go down to the shop\r\ntomorrow morning, see the woman, and extract the truth if possible, and\r\nI fully expect that the story will shrink up to nothing."\r\n\r\n"\'Tis not the estate I care for," said Gerald, looking somewhat cheered.\r\n"It is my father\'s honour and name. If that can be cleared--"\r\n\r\n"Do not I care?" said Lance. "My dear brother Edgar, my model of all\r\nthat was noble and brilliant--whom Felix loved above all! Nay, and you,\r\nGerald, our hope! I would give anything and everything to free you from\r\nthis stain, though I trust it will prove only mud that will not stick.\r\nAnyway you have shown your true, faithful Underwood blood. Now go to bed\r\nand sleep if you can. Don\'t say a word, nor look more like a ghost than\r\nyou can help--or we shall have to rouge ourselves for our parts. My boy,\r\nmy boy! You are Edgar\'s boy, anyway."\r\n\r\nAnd Lancelot kissed the young pale cheek as he had done when the little\r\nwounded orphan clung to him fourteen years ago, or as he kissed his own\r\nFelix.\r\n\r\nWhatever the night was to Gerald, long was the night, and long the light\r\nhours of the morning to the ever sleepless Lance before he could rise\r\nand make his way to the shop with any hope of gaining admission, and\r\nmany were the sighs and prayers that this tale might be confuted, and\r\nthat the matter might be to the blessing of the youth to whom he felt\r\nmore warmly now than since those winning baby days had given place to\r\nmore ordinary boyhood. He had a long time to pace up and down watching\r\nthe sparkling water, and feeling the fresh wind on the brow, which was\r\nas capable as ever of aching over trouble and perplexity, and dreading\r\nabove all the effect on the sister, whose consolation and darling Gerald\r\nhad always been. How little he had thought, when he had stood staunch\r\nagainst his brother Edgar\'s persuasions, that Zoraya was to be the bane\r\nof that life which had begun so gaily!\r\n\r\nWhen at last the door was unfastened, and, as before, by Ludmilla, he\r\ngreeted her kindly, and as she evidently expected some fresh idea about\r\nthe masque, he gave her his card, and asked her to beg her mother to\r\ncome and speak to him. She started at the name and said--\r\n\r\n"Oh, sir, you will do nothing to hurt him--Mr. Underwood?"\r\n\r\n"It is the last thing I wish," he said earnestly, and Ludmilla showed\r\nhim into a little parlour, full of the fumes of tobacco, and sped away,\r\nbut he had a long time to wait, for probably Mother Butterfly\'s entire\r\ntoilette had to be taken in hand.\r\n\r\nBefore she appeared Lancelot heard a man\'s voice, somewhere in the\r\nentry, saying--\r\n\r\n"Oh! the young ass has been fool enough to let it out, has he? I suppose\r\nthis is the chap that will profit? You\'ll have your wits about you."\r\n\r\nLance was still his old self enough to receive the lady with--\r\n\r\n"I beg to observe that I am not the \'chap who will profit\' if this\r\nmiserable allegation holds water. I am come to understand the truth."\r\n\r\nThe woman looked frightened, and the man came to her rescue, having\r\nevidently heard, and this Lance preferred, for he always liked to deal\r\nwith mankind rather than womankind. Having gone so far there was not\r\nroom for reticence, and the man took up the word.\r\n\r\n"Madame cannot be expected to disclose anything to the prejudice of her\r\nson and herself, unless it was made worth her while."\r\n\r\n"Perhaps not," said Lance, as he looked her over in irony, and drew\r\nthe conclusion that the marriage was a fact accomplished; "but she has\r\ndemanded two hundred pounds from her son, on peril of exposure, and if\r\nthe facts are not substantiated, there is such a thing as an action for\r\nconspiracy, and obtaining money on false pretences."\r\n\r\n"Nothing has been obtained!" said the woman, beginning to cry. "He was\r\nvery hard on his poor mother."\r\n\r\n"Who forsook him as an infant, cast off his father, and only claims him\r\nin order to keep a disgraceful, ruinous secret hanging over his life for\r\never, in order to extort money."\r\n\r\n"Come now, this is tall talk, sir," said O\'Leary; "the long and short\r\nof it is, what will the cove, yourself, or whoever it is that you speak\r\nfor, come down for one way or another?"\r\n\r\n"Nothing," responded Lance.\r\n\r\nNeither of the estimable couple spoke or moved under an announcement so\r\nincredible to them, and he went on--\r\n\r\n"Gerald Underwood would rather lose everything than give hush-money to\r\nenable him to be a robber, and my elder brother would certainly give no\r\nreward for what would be the greatest grief in his life."\r\n\r\nO\'Leary grinned as if he wanted to say, "Have you asked him?"\r\n\r\n"The priest," she muttered.\r\n\r\n"Ay, the meddling parson who has done for you! He would have to come\r\ndown pretty handsomely."\r\n\r\nLancelot went on as if he had not heard these asides.\r\n\r\n"I am a magistrate; I can give you in charge at once to the police, and\r\nhave you brought before the Mayor for conspiracy, when you will have to\r\nprove your words, or confess them to be a lie."\r\n\r\nHe was not in the least certain that where there was no threatening\r\nletter, this could succeed, but he knew that the preliminaries would be\r\nalarming enough to elicit something, and accordingly Mrs. O\'Leary began\r\nto sob out--\r\n\r\n"It was when I was a mere child, a bambina, and he used me so cruelly."\r\n\r\nThere was the first thread, and on the whole, the couple were angry\r\nenough with Gerald, his refined appearance and air of careless\r\nprosperity, to be willing that he should have a fall, and Lance thus\r\nextracted that the "he" who had been cruel was a Neapolitan impresario\r\nin a small way, who had detected that Zoraya, when a very little child,\r\nhad a charming voice, of which indeed she still spoke with pride, saying\r\nLida would never equal it. Her parents were semi-gipsies, Hungarian, and\r\nhad wandered all over the Austrian empire, acting, singing, and bringing\r\nup their children to the like. They had actually sold her to the\r\nimpresario, who had sealed the compact, and hoped to secure the valuable\r\ncommodity by making her his wife. In his security he had trained her\r\nin the severest mode, and visited the smallest want of success with\r\nviolence and harshness, so that her life was utterly miserable, and on\r\nmeeting her brother, who had become a member of a German band, she had\r\ncontrived to make her escape with him, and having really considerable\r\nproficiency, the brother and sister had prospered, and through sundry\r\nvicissitudes had arrived at being "stars" in Allen\'s troupe, where Edgar\r\nUnderwood, or, as he was there known, Tom Wood, had unfortunately joined\r\nthem; and the sequel was known to Lancelot, but he could not but listen\r\nand gather up the details, disgusted as he was--how the prima donna had\r\naccepted his attention as her right, till her jealousy was excited by\r\nhis evident attraction to "the little English doll, for whom he killed\r\nhis man"; how she resolved to win him, and how scandalous reports at\r\nlast had brought him to offer marriage, unknowing, it was plain, of her\r\npast. It was not possible to guess how much she was still keeping back,\r\nspeaking under terror and compulsion as she did. But she declared\r\nthat he had never loved her, and was always wanting her to be like ces\r\nAnglaises fades, and as to her child, he so tormented her about it,\r\nand the ways of his absurd mother and sisters, and so expected her to\r\nsacrifice her art and her prospects to the little wretch, that she was\r\nready to strangle it! "Maternal love, bah! she was not going to be like\r\na bird or a beast," she said, with a strange wild glance in her eyes\r\nthat made Lance shudder, and think how much more he respected the bird\r\nor beast. Then at Chicago, when Wood\'s own folly and imprudence had\r\nbrought on an illness that destroyed his voice, and she knew there would\r\nbe only starvation, or she should have to toil for the whole of them,\r\nSchnetterling, manager of a circus, fell in love with her, and made\r\nher good offers to sing in Canada, and Chicago was a place where few\r\nquestions were asked, so she freed herself.\r\n\r\nShe had made her rounds with Schnetterling, a prudent German, and in\r\nprocess of time had come to England, where, at Avoncester, both had been\r\nattacked by influenza; he died, and she only recovered with a total loss\r\nof voice; but he had been prudent and frugal enough to save a sufficient\r\nsum to set her up at Rockquay with the tobacco-shop. She had chosen that\r\nplace on account of American trading-vessels putting in there, as\r\nwell as those of various foreign nations, with whom her knowledge of\r\nlanguages was available, and no doubt there were some opportunities of\r\ndealing in smuggled goods. Just, however, as the smuggling was beginning\r\nto be suspected, the circus of O\'Leary came in her way, and the old\r\ninstincts were renewed. Then came the detection and prosecution, and the\r\nneed of raising the fine. She had recourse to O\'Leary, who had before\r\nbeen Schnetterling\'s underling, and now was a partner in Jellicoe\'s\r\ncircus, who knew her capabilities as a manager and actress, and\r\nperceived the probabilities of poor little Lida\'s powers. The discovery\r\nthat the deserted baby that she had left at Chicago was a young handsome\r\nsquire, well connected, and, in her eyes, of unlimited means, had of\r\ncourse incited both to make the utmost profit of him. That he should not\r\nwish to hush the suspicion up, but should go straight to his uncles, was\r\nto them a quite unexpected contingency.\r\n\r\nAll this was not exactly told to Lancelot, but he extracted it, or\r\ngathered it before he was able to arrive at what was really important,\r\nthe name of Zoraya\'s first husband, where she was married, and by\r\nwhom, and where she had parted from him. She was so unwilling to give\r\nparticulars that he began almost to hope to make her confess that the\r\nwhole was a myth, but at last she owned that the man\'s name was Giovanni\r\nBenista, and that the marriage had taken place at Messina; she knew not\r\nin what church, nor in what year, only it was before the end of the old\r\nregime, for she recollected the uniforms of the Bomba soldiers, though\r\nshe could not remember the name of the priest. Benista was old, very\r\nold--the tyrant and assassin that he was, no doubt he was dead.\r\nShe often thought he would have killed her--and the history of his\r\nill-treatment had to be gone through before it appeared that she had\r\nfled from him at Trieste with her brother, in an English trading-vessel,\r\nwhere their dexterity and brilliancy gained them concealment and a\r\npassage. This was certainly in the summer of 1865. Of Benista she knew\r\nnothing since, but she believed him to have come from Piedmont.\r\n\r\nLance found Gerald walking up and down anxiously watching for him, and\r\nreceiving him with a "Well!" that had in it volumes of suspense.\r\n\r\n"Well, Gerald, I do not think there can be any blame attached to your\r\nfather, whatever comes of it. He was deceived as much as any one else,\r\nand his attachment to you seems to have been his great offence."\r\n\r\n"Thank Heaven! Then he was deceived?"\r\n\r\n"I am afraid there was some previous ceremony. But stay, Gerald! There\r\nis no certainty that it was valid in the first place, and in the next,\r\nnothing is known of Benista since 1865, when he was an old man, so that\r\nthere is a full chance that he was dead before--"\r\n\r\n"Before April 1868. I say, Uncle Lance, they want to make no end of a\r\nbear-fight for my coming of age. I must be out of the way first."\r\n\r\n"Don\'t cry out too soon. Even if the worst came to the worst, as the\r\nproperty was left to you by will individually, I doubt whether this\r\ndiscovery, if real, would make any difference in law. I do not know."\r\n\r\n"But would I take it on those terms? It would be simply defrauding\r\nClement, and all of you--"\r\n\r\n"Perhaps, long before, we may be satisfied," said Lance. "For the\r\npresent, I think nothing can be done but endeavour to ascertain the\r\nfacts."\r\n\r\n"One comfort is," said Gerald, "I have gained a sister. I have walked\r\nwith her to the corner of her place--the marble works, you know--and\r\nshe really is a jolly little thing, quite innocent of all her mother\'s\r\ntricks, thinking Mrs. Henderson the first of human beings, except\r\nperhaps Flight, the aesthetic parson. I should not have selected him,\r\nyou know, but between them they have kept her quite a white sheet--a\r\nMiranda any Ferdinand might be glad to find, and dreading nothing so\r\nmuch as falling into the hands of that awful brute. Caliban himself\r\ncouldn\'t have been worse! I have promised her to do what I can to save\r\nher--buy her off--anything."\r\n\r\n"Poor child," said Lance. "But, Gerald, nothing of this must be said\r\nthese next few days. We can\'t put ourselves out of condition for this\r\nsame raree-show."\r\n\r\n"I\'m sure it\'s a mere abomination to me," said Gerald disconsolately. "I\r\ncan\'t think why we should be dragged into all this nuisance for what is\r\nnot even our own concern."\r\n\r\n"I\'m sure I thought you the rope that dragged me! At any rate much\r\nhigher up on it."\r\n\r\n"Well, I never thought you would respond--you, who have enough on your\r\nhands at Bexley."\r\n\r\n"One stroke even on the outskirts is a stroke for all the cause."\r\n\r\n"The cause! I don\'t believe in the cause, whatever it is. What a\r\nconcatenation now, that you and I should make fools of ourselves in\r\norder to stave off the establishment of national education, as if we\r\ncould, or as if it was worth doing."\r\n\r\n"Then why did you undertake it?"\r\n\r\n"Oh, ah! Why, one wants something to do down here, and the Merrifield\r\nlot are gone upon it; and I did want to go through the thing again, but\r\nnow it seems all rot."\r\n\r\n"Nevertheless, having pledged ourselves to the performance, we cannot\r\ncry off, and the present duty is to pack dull care away, put all\r\nthis out of our heads, and regard it as a mere mare\'s nest as long as\r\npossible, and above all not upset Cherry. Remember, let this turn out\r\nas it will, you are yourself still, and her own boy, beloved for your\r\nfather\'s sake, the joy of our dear brother, and her great comforter. A\r\nwretched mistake can never change that."\r\n\r\nLance\'s voice was quivering, and Gerald\'s face worked. Lance gave his\r\nhand a squeeze, and found voice to say--\r\n\r\n"\'Hold thee still in the Lord, and abide patiently upon Him.\' And\r\nmeantime be a man over it. It can be done. I have often had to forget."\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIX. -- SHOP-DRESSING\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     But I can\'t conceive, in this very hot weather,\r\n     How I\'m ever to bring all these people together.\r\n                                                 T. HOOD.\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was not a day when any one could afford to be upset. It was chiefly\r\nspent in welcoming arrivals or in rushing about: on the part of Lance\r\nand Gerald in freshly rehearsing each performer, in superintending their\r\nstage arrangements, reviewing the dresses, and preparing for one grand\r\nfinal rehearsal; and in the multifarious occupations and anxieties, and\r\nabove all in the music, Gerald did really forget, or only now and then\r\nrecollect, that a nightmare was hanging on him, and that his little Mona\r\nneed not shrink from him in maidenly shyness, but that he might well\r\nreturn her pretty appealing look of confidence.\r\n\r\nThe only quiet place in the town apparently was Clement Underwood\'s\r\nroom, for even Cherry had been whirled off, at first to arrange her\r\nown pictures and drawings; and then her wonderful touch made such a\r\ndifference in the whole appearance of the stall, and her dainty devices\r\nwere so graceful and effective, that Gillian and Mysie implored her to\r\ncome and tell them what to do with theirs, where they were struggling\r\nwith cushions, shawls, and bags, with the somewhat futile assistance of\r\nMr. Armine Brownlow and Captain Armytage, whenever the latter could be\r\nspared from the theatrical arrangements, where, as he said, it was\r\na case of parmi les borgnes--for his small experience with the\r\nWills-of-the-Wisp made him valuable.\r\n\r\nThe stalls were each in what was supposed to represent by turns a\r\nHighland bothie or a cave. The art stall was a cave, that the back\r\n(really a tool-house) might serve the photographers, and the front was\r\ndecorated with handsome bits of rock and spar, even ammonites. Poor\r\nFergus could not recover his horror and contempt when his collection of\r\nspecimens, named and arranged, was very nearly seized upon to fill\r\nup interstices, and he was infinitely indebted to Mrs. Grinstead for\r\nfinding a place where their scientific merits could be appreciated\r\nwithout letting his dirty stones, as Valetta called them, disturb the\r\ngeneral effect.\r\n\r\n"And my fern-gardens! Oh, Mrs. Grinstead," cried Mysie, "please don\'t\r\nsend them away to the flower place which Miss Simmonds and the gardeners\r\nare making like a nursery garden! They\'ll snub my poor dear pterises."\r\n\r\n"Certainly we\'ll make the most of your pterises. Look here. There\'s\r\nan elegant doll, let her lead the family party to survey them. That\'s\r\nright. Oh no, not that giantess! There\'s a dainty little Dutch lady."\r\n\r\n"Charming. Oh! and here\'s her boy in a sailor\'s dress."\r\n\r\n"He is big enough to be her husband, my dear. You had better observe\r\nproportions, and put that family nearer the eye."\r\n\r\n"Those dolls!" cried Valetta, "they were our despair."\r\n\r\n"Make them tell a story, don\'t you see. Where\'s that fat red cushion?"\r\n\r\n"Oh, that cushion! I put it out of sight because it is such a monster."\r\n\r\n"Yes; it is just like brick-dust enlivened by half-boiled cauliflowers!\r\nNever mind, it will be all the better background. Now, I saw a majestic\r\nlady reposing somewhere. There, let her sit against it. Oh, she mustn\'t\r\nflop over. Here, that match-box, is it? I pity the person deluded enough\r\nto use it! Prop her up with it. Now then, let us have a presentation\r\nof ladies--she\'s a governor\'s wife in the colonies, you see. Never mind\r\ncostumes, they may be queer. All that will stand or kneel--that\'s right.\r\nThose that can only sit must hide behind, like poor Marie Antoinette\'s\r\nladies on the giggling occasion."\r\n\r\nSo she went on, full of fun, which made the work doubly delightful\r\nto the girls, who darted about while she put the finishing touches,\r\ntransforming the draperies from the aspect of a rag-and-bone shop, as\r\nJasper had called it, to a wonderful quaint and pretty fairy bower,\r\nbacked by the Indian scenes sent by Mr. and Mrs. Bernard Underwood, and\r\nthat other lovely one of Primrose\'s pasture. There the merry musical\r\nlaugh of her youth was to be heard, as General Mohun came out with\r\nLancelot to make a raid, order the whole party to come and eat luncheon\r\nat Beechcroft Cottage, and not let Mrs. Grinstead come out again.\r\n\r\n"Oh, but I must finish up Bernard\'s clay costume figures. Look at\r\nthe expression of that delightful dollie! I\'m sure he is watching the\r\nkhitmutgars.\r\n\r\n\r\n          \'Above on tallest trees remote\r\n             Green Ayahs perched alone;\r\n           And all night long the Mussah moaned\r\n             In melancholy tone.\'\r\n\r\n\r\nOh, don\'t you know Lear\'s poem? Can\'t we illustrate it?"\r\n\r\n"Cherry, Cherry, you\'ll be half dead to-morrow."\r\n\r\n"Well, if I am, this is the real fun. I shan\'t see the destruction."\r\n\r\nLance had her arm in his grip to take her over the bridge over the wall,\r\nwhen up rushed Kitty Varley.\r\n\r\n"Oh, if Mrs. Grinstead would come and look at our stall and set it\r\nright! Miss Vanderkist gave us hopes."\r\n\r\n"Perhaps--"\r\n\r\n"Now, Cherry, don\'t you know that you are not to be knocked up! There\r\nare the Travises going to bring unlimited Vanderkists."\r\n\r\n"Oh yes, I know; but there\'s renovation in breaths from Vale Leston, and\r\nI really am of some use here." Her voice really had a gay ring in it.\r\n"It is such fun too! Where\'s Gerald?"\r\n\r\n"Having a smoke with the buccaneer captain. Oh, Miss Mohun, here\'s my\r\nsister, so enamoured of the bazaar I could hardly get her in."\r\n\r\n"And oh! she is so clever and delightful. She has made our stall the\r\nmost enchanting place," cried Primrose, dancing round. "Mamma, you must\r\ncome and have it all explained to you."\r\n\r\n"The very sight is supposed to be worth a shilling extra," said General\r\nMohun, while Lady Merrifield and Miss Mohun, taking possession of her,\r\nhoped she was not tired; and Gillian, who had been wont to consider\r\nher as her private property, began to reprove her sisters for having\r\nengrossed her while she herself was occupied in helping the Hendersons\r\nwith their art stall.\r\n\r\n"The truth is," said Lance, "that this is my sister\'s first bazaar, and\r\nso dear is the work to the female mind, that she can\'t help being sucked\r\ninto the vortex."\r\n\r\n"Is it really?" demanded Mysie, in a voice that made Mrs. Grinstead\r\nlaugh and say--\r\n\r\n"Such is my woeful lack of experience."\r\n\r\n"We have fallen on a bazaar wherever we went," said Lady Merrifield.\r\n\r\n"But this is our first grown-up one, mamma," said Valetta. "There was\r\nonly a sale of work before."\r\n\r\nThey all laughed, and Lance said--\r\n\r\n"To Stoneborough they seem like revenues--at least sales of work, for I\r\ncan\'t say I understand the distinction."\r\n\r\n"Recurring brigandages," said General Mohun.\r\n\r\n"Ah! Uncle Reggie has never forgotten his getting a Noah\'s ark in a\r\nraffle," said Mysie.\r\n\r\nSo went the merry talk, while one and another came in at Miss Mohun\'s\r\nverandah windows to be sustained with food and rest, and then darted\r\nforth again to renew their labours until the evening, Miss Mohun flying\r\nabout everywhere on all sorts of needs, and her brother the General\r\nwaiting by the dining-room to do the duties of hospitality to the strays\r\nof the families who dropped in, chattering and laughing, and exhausted.\r\n\r\nLady Merrifield was authorized to detain Mrs. Grinstead to the last\r\nmoment possible to either, and they fell into a talk on the morality\r\nof bazaars, which, as Lady Merrifield said, had been a worry to her\r\neverywhere, while Geraldine had been out of their reach; since the\r\nUnderwoods had done everything without begging, and Clement disapproved\r\nof them without the most urgent need; but, as Lance had said, his\r\nwife had grown up to them, and had gone through all the stages from\r\ndelighting, acquiescing, and being bored, and they had so advanced\r\nsince their early days, from being simply sales to the grand period of\r\nornaments, costumes, and anything to attract.\r\n\r\n"Clement consents," said Geraldine; "as, first, it is not a church,\r\nand then, though it does seem absurd to think that singing through the\r\nmurdered Tempest should be aiding the cause of the Church, yet anything\r\nto keep our children to learning faith and truth is worthy work."\r\n\r\n"Alas, it is working against the stream! How things are changed when\r\nschool was our romance and our domain."\r\n\r\n"Yes, you should hear Lance tell the story of his sister-in-law Ethel,\r\nhow she began at Cocksmoor, with seven children and fifteen shillings,\r\nand thought her fortune made when she got ten pounds a year for the\r\nschool-mistress; and now it is all Mrs. Rivers can do to keep out the\r\nSchool-board, because they had not a separate room for the hat-pegs!"\r\n\r\n"We never had those struggles. We had enough to do to live at all in our\r\ndear old home days, except that my brother always taught Sunday classes.\r\nBut anyway, this is very amusing. Those young people\'s characters come\r\nout so much. Ah, Gerald, what is it?"\r\n\r\nFor Gerald was coming up to the verandah with a very pretty, dark-eyed,\r\nmodest-looking girl in a sailor hat, who shrank back as he said--\r\n\r\n"I am come to ask for some luncheon for my--my Mona. She has had nothing\r\nto eat all day, and we still have the grand recognition scene to come."\r\n\r\nAt which the girl blushed so furiously that the notion crossed Geraldine\r\nthat he must have been flirting with the poor little tobacconist\'s\r\ndaughter; but Lady Merrifield was exclaiming that he too had had nothing\r\nto eat, and General Mohun came forth to draw them into the dining-room,\r\nwhere he helped Ludmilla to cold lamb, salad, etc., and she sat down\r\nat Gerald\'s signal, very timidly, so that she gave the idea of only\r\npartaking because she was afraid to refuse.\r\n\r\nGerald ate hurriedly and nervously, and drank claret cup. He said they\r\nwere getting on famously, his uncle\'s chief strength being expended\r\nin drawing out the voice of the buccaneer captain, and mitigating the\r\nboatswain. Where were the little boys? Happily disposed of. Little Felix\r\nhad gone through his part, and then Fergus had carried him and\r\nAdrian off together to Clipstone to see his animals, antediluvian and\r\notherwise.\r\n\r\nThen in rushed Gillian, followed by Dolores.\r\n\r\n"Oh, mother!" cried Gillian, "there\'s a fresh instalment of pots and\r\npans come in, such horrid things some of them! There\'s a statue in\r\nterra-cotta, half as large as life, of the Dirty Boy. Geraldine, do pray\r\ncome and see what can be done with him. Kalliope is in utter despair,\r\nfor they come from Craydon\'s, and to offend them would be fatal."\r\n\r\n"Kalliope and the Dirty Boy," said Mrs. Grinstead, laughing. "A dreadful\r\nconjunction; I must go and see if it is possible to establish the line\r\nbetween the sublime and the ridiculous."\r\n\r\n"Shall I ask your nephew\'s leave to let you go," said Lady Merrifield,\r\n"after all the orders I have received?"\r\n\r\n"Oh, no--" she began, but Gerald had jumped up.\r\n\r\n"I\'ll steer you over the drawbridge, Cherie, if go you must. Yes,"--to\r\nthe young ladies--"I appreciate your needs. Nobody has the same faculty\r\nin her fingers as this aunt of mine. Come along, Mona, it is Mrs.\r\nHenderson\'s stall, you know."\r\n\r\nLudmilla came, chiefly because she was afraid to be left, and Lady\r\nMerrifield could not but come too, meeting on the way Anna, come to\r\nimplore help in arranging the Dirty Boy, before Captain Henderson\r\nknocked his head off, as he was much disposed to do.\r\n\r\nGillian had bounded on before with a handful of sandwiches, but Dolores\r\ntarried behind, having let the General help her to the leg of a chicken,\r\nwhich she seemed in no haste to dissect. Her uncle went off on some\r\nother call before she had finished, eating and drinking with the bitter\r\nsauce of reflection on the fleeting nature of young men\'s attentions and\r\neven confidences, and how easily everything was overthrown at sight of\r\na pretty face, especially in the half-and-half class. She had only just\r\ncome out into the verandah, wearily to return to the preparations, which\r\nhad lost whatever taste they had for her, when she saw Gerald Underwood\r\nspringing over the partition wall. Her impulse was to escape him, but it\r\nwas too late; he came eagerly up to her, saying--\r\n\r\n"She is safe with Mrs. Henderson. I am to go back for her when our duet\r\ncomes on."\r\n\r\nDolores did not want to lower herself by showing jealousy or offence,\r\nbut she could not help turning decidedly away, saying--\r\n\r\n"I am wanted."\r\n\r\n"Are you? I wanted to tell you why I am so interested in her. Dolores,\r\ncan you hear me now?--she is my sister."\r\n\r\n"Your sister!" in utter amaze.\r\n\r\n"Every one says they see it in the colour of our eyes."\r\n\r\n"Every one"--she seemed able to do nothing but repeat his words.\r\n\r\n"Well, my uncle Lancelot, and--and my mother. No one else knows yet.\r\nThey want to spare my aunt till this concern is over."\r\n\r\n"But how can it be?"\r\n\r\n"It is a horrid business altogether!" he said, taking her down to the\r\nunfrequented parts of the lower end of the garden, where they could walk\r\nup and down hidden by the bushes and shrubs. "You knew that my father\r\nwas an artist and musician, who fled from over patronage."\r\n\r\n"I think I have heard so."\r\n\r\n"He married a singing-woman, and she grew tired of him, and of me,\r\ndeserted and divorced him in Chicago, when I was ten months old. He was\r\nthe dearest, most devoted of fathers, till he and I were devoured by the\r\nIndians. If they had completed their operations on my scalp, it would\r\nhave been all the better for me. Instead of which Travis picked me\r\nup, brought me home, and they made me as much of an heir of all the\r\ntraditions as nature would permit, all ignoring that not only was my\r\nfather Bohemian ingrain, but that my mother was--in short--one of the\r\ngipsies of civilization. They never expected to hear of her again, but\r\nbehold, the rapturous discovery has taken place. She recognised Lance,\r\nthe only one of the family she had ever seen before, and then the voice\r\nof blood--more truly the voice of L s. d.--exerted itself."\r\n\r\n"How was it she did not find you out before?"\r\n\r\n"My father seems to have concealed his full name; I remember his being\r\ncalled Tom Wood. She married in her own line after casting him off, and\r\nthis pretty little thing is her child--the only tolerable part of it."\r\n\r\n"But she cannot have any claim on you," said Dolores, with a more\r\nshocked look and tone than the words conveyed.\r\n\r\n"Not she--in reason; but the worst of it is, Dolores, that the wretched\r\nwoman avers that she deceived my father, and had an old rascally tyrant\r\nof an Italian husband, who might have been alive when she married."\r\n\r\n"Gerald!"\r\n\r\nDolores stood still and looked at him with her eyes opened in horror.\r\n\r\n"Yes, you may well say Gerald. \'Tis the only name I have a right to if\r\nthis is true."\r\n\r\n"But you are still yourself," and she held out her hand.\r\n\r\nHe did not take it, however, only saying--\r\n\r\n"You know what this means?"\r\n\r\n"Of course I do, but that does not alter you--yourself in yourself."\r\n\r\n"If you say that, Dolores, it will only alter me to make me--more--more\r\nmyself."\r\n\r\nShe held out her hand again, and this time he did take it and press it,\r\nbut he started, dropped it, and said--\r\n\r\n"It is not fair."\r\n\r\n"Oh yes, it is. I know what it means," she repeated, "and it makes no\r\ndifference," and this time it was she who took his hand.\r\n\r\n"It means that unless this marriage is disproved, or the man\'s death\r\nproved, I am an outcast, dependent on myself, instead of the curled\r\ndarling the Grinsteads--blessings on them!--have brought me up."\r\n\r\n"I don\'t know whether I don\'t like you better so," exclaimed she,\r\nlooking into his clear eyes and fine open face, full of resolution, not\r\nof shame.\r\n\r\n"While you say so--" He broke off. "Yes, thus I can bear it better. The\r\nestate is almost an oppression to me. The Bohemian nature is in me, I\r\nsuppose. I had rather carve out life for myself than have the landlord\r\nbusiness loaded on my shoulders. Clement and Lance will make the model\r\nparson and squire far better than I. \'The Inspector\'s Tour\' was a\r\nsuccess--between that and the Underwood music there\'s no fear but I\r\nshall get an independent career."\r\n\r\n"Oh! that is noble! You will be much more than your old self--as you\r\nsaid."\r\n\r\n"The breaking of Cherie\'s heart is all that I care about," said he. "To\r\nher I was comfort, almost compensation for those brothers. I don\'t\r\nknow how--" He paused. "We\'ll let her alone till all this is over; so,\r\nDolores, not one word to any one."\r\n\r\n"No, no, no!" she exclaimed. "I will--I will be true to you through\r\neverything, Gerald; I will wait till you have seen your way, and be\r\nproud of you through all."\r\n\r\n"Then I can bear it--I have my incentive," he said. "First, you see, I\r\nmust try to rescue my sister. I do not think it will be hard, for the\r\nmaternal heart seems to be denied to that woman. Then proofs must be\r\nsought, and according as they are found or not--"\r\n\r\nLoud calls of "Gerald" and "Mr. Underwood" began to resound. He\r\nfinished--\r\n\r\n"Must be _the_ future."\r\n\r\n"_Our_ future," repeated Dolores.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XX. -- FRENCH LEAVE\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     She came, she is gone, we have met,\r\n     And meet perhaps never again.--COWPER.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe evening of that day was a scene of welcomes, dinners, and confusion.\r\nThe Rotherwoods had arrived that evening at the Cliff Hotel just in time\r\nfor dinner, of which they considerately partook where they were, to save\r\nJane Mohun trouble; but all four of the party came the instant it was\r\nover to hear and see all that was going on, and were fervently received\r\nby Gillian and Mysie, who were sleeping at their aunt\'s to be ready for\r\nthe morrow, and in spite of all fatigue, had legs wherewith to walk Lord\r\nIvinghoe and Lady Phyllis round the stalls, now closed up by canvas and\r\nguarded by police. Phyllis was only mournful not to have assisted in the\r\npreparations, and heard all the fun that Mrs. Grinstead had made.\r\nBut over the wall of Carrara a sight was seen for which no one was\r\nprepared--no other than Maura White\'s pretty classical face!\r\n\r\n"Yes," she said, "how could I be away from such an occasion? I made\r\nUncle White bring me to London--he had business there, you know--and\r\nthen I descended on Kalliope, and wasn\'t she surprised! But I have a\r\nlovely Italian dress!"\r\n\r\nKalliope Henderson looked more alarmed than gratified on the whole. She\r\nknew that there had been no idea of Maura\'s coming till after it had\r\nbeen known that the Rotherwoods were to open the bazaar, and "made Uncle\r\nWhite" was so unlike their former relations that all were startled,\r\nGillian asking in a tone of reproof how Aunt Adeline spared Maura.\r\n\r\n"Oh, we shall be back at Gastein in less than a week. I could not miss\r\nsuch an occasion."\r\n\r\n"I only had her telegram half-an-hour ago," said Kalliope, in an\r\napologetic tone; and Lord Ivinghoe was to be dimly seen handing Maura\r\nover the fence. Moonlight gardens and moonlight sea! What was to be\r\ndone? And Ivinghoe, who had begun life by being as exclusive as the\r\nMarchioness herself! "People take the bit between their teeth nowadays,"\r\nas Jane observed to Lady Rotherwood when the news reached her, and\r\nneither said, though each felt, that Adeline would not have promoted\r\nthis expedition, even for the child whom she and Mr. White had conspired\r\nto spoil. Each was secretly afraid of the attraction for Ivinghoe.\r\n\r\nAt St. Andrew\'s Rock there was a glad meeting with the Travis\r\nUnderwoods, who had disposed of themselves at the Marine Hotel, while\r\nthey came up with a select party of three Vanderkists to spend the\r\nevening with Clement, Geraldine, and Lancelot, not to mention Adrian,\r\nwho had been allowed to sit up to dinner to see his sisters, and was\r\nalmost devoured by them. His growth, and the improved looks of both\r\nhis uncle and aunt, so delighted Marilda, that Lancelot declared the\r\nRockquay people would do well to have them photographed "Then" and\r\n"Now," as an advertisement of the place! But he was not without dread of\r\nthe effect of the disclosure that had yet to be made, though Gerald had\r\napparently forgotten all about it as he sat chaffing Emilia Vanderkist\r\nabout the hospital, whither she was really going for a year; Sophy about\r\nthe engineer who had surveyed the Penbeacon intended works, and Francie\r\nabout her Miranda-Mona in strange hands.\r\n\r\nThe Vanderkists all began life as very pretty little girls, but showed\r\nmore or less of the Hollander ancestry as they grow up. Only Franceska,\r\ncontent with her Dutch name, had shot up into a beautiful figure,\r\ntogether with the fine features and complexion of the Underwood twins,\r\nand the profuse golden flax hair of her aunt Angela, so that she took\r\nthem all by surprise in the pretty dress presented by Cousin Marilda,\r\nand chosen by Emilia. Sophy was round and short, as nearly plain as one\r\nwith the family likeness could be, but bright and joyous, and very proud\r\nof her young sister. It was a merry evening.\r\n\r\nIn fact, Lance himself was so much carried away by the spirit of the\r\nthing, and so anxious about the performance, that he made all the\r\nrest, including Clement, join in singing Autolycus\'s song, which was\r\nto precede the procession, to a new setting of his own, before they\r\ndispersed.\r\n\r\nBut Lance was beginning to dress in the morning when a knock came to his\r\ndoor.\r\n\r\n"A note from Mr. Flight, please, sir."\r\n\r\nThe note was--"Circus and Schnetterlings gone off in the night! Shop\r\nclosed! Must performance be given up?"\r\n\r\nThe town was all over red and blue posters! But Lance felt a wild hope\r\nfor the future, and a not ill-founded one for the present. He rushed\r\ninto his clothes, first pencilling a note--\r\n\r\n                 "Never say die.  L. 0. U."\r\n\r\nThen he hurried off, and sent up a message to Miss Franceska Vanderkist,\r\nto come and speak to him, and he walked up and down the sitting-room\r\nwhere breakfast was being spread, like a panther, humming Prospero\'s\r\nsongs, or murmuring vituperations, till Franceska appeared, a perfect\r\npicture of loveliness in her morning youthful freshness.\r\n\r\n"Francie, there\'s no help for it. You must take Mona! She has\r\nabsconded!"\r\n\r\n"Uncle Lance!"\r\n\r\n"Yes, gone off in the night; left us lamenting."\r\n\r\n"The horrible girl!"\r\n\r\n"Probably not her fault, poor thing! But that\'s neither here nor there.\r\nI wish it was!"\r\n\r\n"But I thought--"\r\n\r\n"It is past thinking now, my dear. Here we are, pledged. Can\'t draw\r\nback, and you are the only being who can save us! You know the part."\r\n\r\n"Yes, in a way."\r\n\r\n"You did it with me at home."\r\n\r\n"Oh yes; but, Uncle Lance, it would be too dreadful before all these\r\npeople."\r\n\r\n"Never mind the people. Be Mona, and only think of Alaster and Angus."\r\n\r\n"But what would mamma say, or Aunt Wilmet? And Uncle Clem?" each in a\r\nmore awe-stricken voice.\r\n\r\n"I\'ll tackle them."\r\n\r\n"I know I shall be frightened and fail, and that will be worse."\r\n\r\n"No, it won\'t, and you won\'t. Look here, Francie, this is not a\r\nself-willed freak for our own amusement. The keeping up the Church\r\nschools here depends upon what we can raise. I hate bazaars. I hate\r\nto have to obtain help for the Church through these people\'s idle\r\namusement, but you and I have not two or three thousands to give away to\r\na strange place in a lump; but we have our voices. \'Such as I have give\r\nI thee,\' and this ridiculous entertainment may bring in fifty or maybe\r\na hundred. I don\'t feel it right to let it collapse for the sake of our\r\nown dislikes."\r\n\r\n"Very well, Uncle Lance, I\'ll do as you tell me."\r\n\r\n"That\'s the way to do it, my dear. At least, when you make ready,\r\nrecollect, not that you are facing a multitude, but that you are saving\r\na child\'s Christian faith; when you prepare, that you have to do with\r\nnobody but Gerald and me; when it comes to \'One, two, three, and away,\'\r\nmind nothing but your music and your cue."\r\n\r\n"But the dress, uncle?"\r\n\r\n"The dress is all safe at the pavilion. You must come up and rehearse as\r\nsoon as you have eaten your breakfast. Oh, you don\'t know where. Well,\r\none of us will come and fetch you. Good girl, Francie! Keep up your\r\nheart. By the bye, which is Fernan\'s dressing-room? I must prepare him."\r\n\r\nThat question was answered, for Sir Ferdinand\'s door into the corridor\r\nwas opened.\r\n\r\n"Lance! I thought I heard your voice."\r\n\r\n"Yes, here\'s a pretty kettle of fish! Our Miranda has absconded, poor\r\nchild. Happy thing you brought down Francie; nobody else could take the\r\npart at such short notice. You must pacify Marilda, silence scruples,\r\nsay it is her duty to Church, country, and family. Can\'t stop!"\r\n\r\n"Lance, explain--do! Music-mad as usual!" cried Sir Ferdinand, pursuing\r\nhim down-stairs in despair.\r\n\r\n"I _must_ be music-mad; the only chance of keeping sane just now.\r\nThere\'s an awful predicament! Can\'t go into it now, but you shall hear\r\nall when this is over."\r\n\r\nWherewith Lance was lost to view, and presently burst into St. Kenelm\'s\r\nVicarage, to the relief of poor Mr. Flight, who had tried to solace\r\nhimself with those three words as best he might.\r\n\r\n"All right. My niece, Franceska Vanderkist, who took the part before,\r\nand who has a very good soprano, will do it better as to voice, if not\r\nso well as to acting, as the Little Butterfly."\r\n\r\n"Is she here?"\r\n\r\n"Yes, by good luck. I shall have her up to the pavilion to rehearse her\r\nfor the afternoon."\r\n\r\n"Mr. Underwood, no words can say what we owe you. You are the saving of\r\nour Church education."\r\n\r\nLance laughed at the magniloquent thanks, and asked how the intimation\r\nhad been received.\r\n\r\nIt appeared that on the previous evening O\'Leary had come to him, and,\r\nin swaggering fashion, had demanded twenty pounds as payment for his\r\nstep-daughter\'s performance at the masque. Mr. Flight had replied that\r\nshe had freely promised her services gratuitously for the benefit of the\r\nobject in view. At this the man had scoffed, talked big about her value\r\nand the meanness of parsons, and threatened to withdraw her. Rather\r\nweakly the clergyman had said the question should be considered,\r\nbut that he could do nothing without the committee, and O\'Leary had\r\ndeparted, uttering abuse.\r\n\r\nThis morning "Sweetie Bob," the errand-boy, had arrived crying, with\r\ntidings that the shop and house were shut up; nobody answered his knock;\r\nMother Butterfly had "cut" in the night, gone off, he believed, with\r\nthe circus, and Miss Lydia too; and there was two-and-ninepence owing to\r\nhim, besides his--his--his character!\r\n\r\nHe knew that Mother Butterfly had gone to the magistrates\' meeting\r\nthe day before, and paid her fine of twenty-five pounds, and he\r\nalso believed that she had paid up her rent, and sold her shop to a\r\nneighbouring pastry-cook, but he had never expected her to depart\r\nin this sudden way, and then he began to shed fresh tears over his\r\ntwo-and-ninepence and his character.\r\n\r\nMr. Flight began to reassure him, with promises to speak for him as\r\nan honest lad, while Lance bethought himself of the old organist\'s\r\ndescription of that wandering star, "Without home, without country,\r\nwithout morals, without religion, without anything," and recollected\r\nwith a shudder that turning-point in his life when Edgar had made him\r\nshow off his musical talent, and when Felix had been sharp with him, and\r\nthe office of the \'Pursuivant\' looked shabby, dull, and dreary.\r\n\r\nNothing more could be done, except to make bold assurances to Mr. Flight\r\nthat Mona\'s place should be supplied, and then to hurry home, meeting\r\non his way a policeman, who told him that the circus was certainly gone\r\naway, and promised to let him know whither.\r\n\r\nHe was glad to find that Gerald had not come down-stairs, having\r\noverslept himself in the morning after a wakeful night. He was dressing\r\nwhen his uncle knocked at his door.\r\n\r\n"Here is a shock, Gerald! I hope it is chiefly to our masque. These\r\npeople have absconded, and carried off our poor little Mona."\r\n\r\n"What? Absconded? My sister! I must be after them instantly," cried\r\nGerald, wildly snatching at his coat.\r\n\r\n"What good would that do? you can\'t carry her off vi et armis."\r\n\r\n"Send the police."\r\n\r\n"No possibility. The fine is paid, the rent and all. They have gone, it\r\nseems, with the circus."\r\n\r\n"Ah! Depend upon it that fellow has paid the fine, and bought the poor\r\nchild into slavery with it. Carried her off in spite of our demurring,\r\nand the Vicar\'s prosecution. I must save her. I\'ll go after and outbid."\r\n\r\n"No hurry, Gerald. A circus is not such a microscopical object but that\r\nit can be easily traced. A policeman has promised to find out where, and\r\nmeanwhile we must attend to our present undertaking."\r\n\r\nGerald strode up and down the room in a fiery fit of impatience and\r\nindignation, muttering furious things, quite transformed from the\r\nlistless, ironical youth hitherto known to his family.\r\n\r\n"Come," Lancelot said, "our first duty is to do justice to our part;\r\nFrancie Vanderkist will take Mona."\r\n\r\n"Hang Mona! you care for nothing on earth but your fiddling and songs."\r\n\r\n"I do not see that being frantic will make any difference to the\r\nsituation. All in our power is being done. Meanwhile, we must attend to\r\nwhat we have undertaken."\r\n\r\nGerald rushed about a little more, but finally listened to his uncle\'s\r\nrepresentation that the engrossing employment was good to prevent the\r\nperil of disturbing the two whom they were so anxious to spare. Fely\r\ncame running up with a message that Aunt Cherie and Anna had been sent\r\nfor to see about the decorations of the art stall, and that they would\r\nhave to eat their breakfast without them.\r\n\r\nAppetite for breakfast was lacking, but Lance forced himself to swallow,\r\nas one aware of the consequences of fasting for agitation\'s sake, and he\r\nnearly crammed Gerald; so that Adrian and Fely laughed, and he excused\r\nhimself by declaring that he wanted his turkey-cock to gobble and not\r\npipe. For which bit of pleasantry he encountered a glare from Gerald\'s\r\nHungarian eyes. He was afraid on one side to lose sight of his nephew,\r\non the other he did not feel equal to encounter a scolding from Marilda,\r\nso he sent Adrian and Fely down to the Marine Hotel to fetch Franceska,\r\nwhile he stole a moment or two for greeting Clement, who was much\r\nbetter, and only wanted more conversation than he durst give him.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXI. -- THE MASQUE\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     Your honour\'s players, hearing your amendment,\r\n     Are come to play a pleasant comedy.\r\n                                  Taming of the Shrew.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nPoor Franceska! First she encountered Cousin Marilda\'s wonder and\r\ndispleasure, and the declaration that Uncle Lance went absolutely crazy\r\nover his musical mania. She had seen it before in poor Edgar, and knew\r\nwhat it came to. She wanted to telegraph at once to Alda to ask her\r\nconsent or refusal to Franceska\'s appearance; but Sir Ferdinand stopped\r\nthis on the ground that the circumstances could not be explained, and\r\ntold her to content herself with Clement\'s opinion.\r\n\r\nThis she sent Sophy and Emilia to ascertain, before she would let them\r\nand the boys escort Francie to her destination. Clement, not yet up, had\r\nto hold a lit de justice, and pronounce that Uncle Lance was to be fully\r\ntrusted to ask nothing unbecoming or unnecessary, and that Francie would\r\nhave nothing to do with any one except him and Gerald.\r\n\r\n"Besides," said Emilia, as they walked up, "nobody will find it out. The\r\nposters are all over the town, \'Mona, Miss Ludmilla Schnetterling.\'"\r\n\r\nSo the sisters were received with a murmur on their delay. The pretty\r\ndress prepared for Mona was found to be too small for the tall shapely\r\nFranceska, and Sophy undertook to alter it, while poor Francie\'s\r\ntroubles began.\r\n\r\nWhether it was that Uncle Lance and Gerald were in a secret state of\r\nturmoil, or that their requirements were a good deal higher than for the\r\nVale Leston audience, or perhaps that she had no inheritance of actress\r\ntraditions, they certainly were a great deal sharper with her than they\r\nhad been ever before or with Ludmilla.\r\n\r\nGerald derided her efforts sarcastically, and Uncle Lance found fault\r\ngood-humouredly but seriously, and she was nearly in tears by eleven\r\no\'clock, when the procession was to take place. She was quite surprised\r\nwhen Lance turned to her and said--\r\n\r\n"Thank you, my dear, you are doing capitally. I shall be proud of my\r\ndaughter Mona."\r\n\r\nQuite in spirits again, she was sewn by Sophy into her still unfinished\r\ndress, her beautiful light golden flax tresses were snooded, her\r\nHighland scarf pinned on her shoulder, and she hurried to her uncle, now\r\nbe-robed and be-wigged, with Gerald in full Highland garb, looking very\r\nmuch disgusted, especially when her uncle said--\r\n\r\n"Well done, Francie. You\'ll cut that poor little thing out in looks and\r\nvoice, if not in acting."\r\n\r\n"Oh, uncle, I sang so horridly."\r\n\r\n"You can do better if you try; I wish there was time to train you.\r\nWe\'ll do the \'logs duet\' once more after this tomfoolery. Ha! Captain\r\nArmytage. You are an awful pirate, and no mistake. Where did you get\r\nthat splendid horse-pistol?"\r\n\r\n"From my native home, as well as my sword; but I wrote to Willingham for\r\nthe rest. This will be an uncommonly pretty march-past. The girls look\r\nso well, and all out of doors too."\r\n\r\nThis was decidedly a great advantage, the trees, grass, and blue sky\r\nlending a great grace to the scene. The procession started from the\r\ngarden entrance of the hotel, headed by the town band in uniform, and\r\nthe fire brigade likewise, very proud of themselves, especially the\r\nlittle terrier whom nothing would detach from one of the firemen. Then\r\ncame the four seasons belonging to the flower stall, appropriately\r\ndecked with flowers, the Italian peasants with flat veils, bright\r\naprons, and white sleeves, Maura White\'s beauty conspicuous in the\r\nmidst, but with unnecessary nods and becks. Then came the "mediaeval"\r\ndamsels in ruffs and high hats, the Highland maidens, with Valetta and\r\nPrimrose giggling unmanageably; and Aunt Jane\'s troop of the various\r\ncostumes of charity children, from the green frocks, long mittens, and\r\ntall white caps, and the Jemima Placid flat hats and long waists, down\r\nto the red cloaks, poke straw bonnets, and blue frocks of the Lady\r\nBountiful age. These were followed by the merry fairies and elves; then\r\nby the buccaneers and the captive prisoners; and the rear was brought up\r\nby MacProspero, as Lord Rotherwood called him, with his niece on his arm\r\nand his nephew by his side.\r\n\r\nWhen the central stall, or bothie, in the Carrara grounds was reached,\r\nafter passing in full state and order over two of the bridges, the\r\nprocession halted before a group of the Rotherwood family, Sir Jasper\r\nand Lady Merrifield, Lady Flight, and other local grandees, with the\r\nclergy, who had declined to walk in procession. There the performers\r\nspread themselves out, singing Autolycus\'s song, led of course by\r\nMacProspero; Lady Rotherwood, with as much dignity as the occasion\r\npermitted, declared the bazaar open, and the Marquis hoped every one was\r\ngoing to ruin themselves in the cause of Christian education.\r\n\r\nThe first idea of "every one" was luncheon, except that Lance laid hands\r\non his unfortunate Angus and Mona for their duet, in the midst of which\r\nLord Rotherwood made a raid on them.\r\n\r\n"There! I\'m sure Prospero never was so cruel as to starve\r\nwhat\'s-his-name! Come in and have some food--it is just by."\r\n\r\nThey found themselves in a dining-room, in the presence of Lady\r\nRotherwood, her son and daughter, and a sprinkling of Merrifields and\r\nactors, in full swing of joyous chatter; Mysie and Lady Phyllis telling\r\nall that was specially to be admired, and Lord Rotherwood teasing them\r\nabout the prices, and their wicked extortions in the name of goodness,\r\nGillian snubbing poor Captain Armytage in his splendid buccaneer\r\ndress, Ivinghoe making himself agreeable to Franceska, whose heightened\r\ncarnation tints made her doubly lovely through her shyness. Gerald and\r\nDolores in the less lively vicinity of the Marchioness carrying on a\r\nlow-toned conversation, which, however, enabled Gerald to sustain nature\r\nwith food better than he had done at breakfast.\r\n\r\nIt did not last long. The sellers had to rush off to relieve those\r\nwho had begun the sale, and the performance was to commence at three\r\no\'clock, so that the final preparations had to be hurried through.\r\n\r\nGeraldine had made the tour of the stalls on the arm of Anna, to admire\r\nthem in their first freshness, and put finishing touches wherever\r\nsolicited. The Rocca Marina conservatories were in rare glory, orchids\r\nin weird beauty, lovely lilies of all hues, fabulously exquisite\r\nipomoeas, all that heart could wish. Before them a fountain played in\r\nthe midst of blue, pink, and white lotus lilies, and in a flower-decked\r\nhouse the Seasons dispensed pot-flowers, bouquets, and button-holes;\r\nthe Miss Simmondses and their friends with simpering graces, that\r\nmade Geraldine glad to escape and leave them to the young men who\r\nwere strolling up. At Carrara was the stall in which she was chiefly\r\ninterested, and which had been arranged with a certain likeness to\r\nItalian gardens, the statues and other devices disposed among flowers;\r\nthe Dirty Boy judiciously veiled by the Puzzle Monkey, and the front\r\nof the summer-house prolonged by pillars, sham but artistic. Jasper was\r\nzealously photographing group after group, handing his performances over\r\nto his assistant for printing off. Kalliope looked in her costume most\r\nbeautiful and dignified. Her sister, grown to almost equal beauty, was\r\nhurrying off to see the masque, flushed and eager, while Gillian and one\r\nor two others were assisting in sales that would be rather slack\r\ntill after the performance. Here Geraldine purchased only a couple\r\nof Mouse-traps, leaving further choice to be made after the stranger\r\npurchasers. Here Sir Jasper and General Mohun came up, and gave her\r\na good deal of curious information about Bernard\'s bevy of figures\r\nin Indian costumes; and having the offer of such a strong arm as the\r\nGeneral\'s, she dispensed with Anna, who was really wanted to help with\r\nthe very popular photographs.\r\n\r\nThey passed the refreshments, at present chiefly haunted by Mrs. Edgar\'s\r\nboys, ready to eat at any time of day; they looked civilly at the Varley\r\nElizabethans, and found Lady Merrifield in the midst of her bothie, made\r\ncharming with fresh green branches and purple heather, imported by the\r\nVanderkists.\r\n\r\n"That\'s Penbeacon ling. I know that red tint in the mauve," said\r\nGeraldine; "I\'ll give you half-a-crown, if your decorations can spare\r\nthat spiring spray!" And she put it in her bosom, after touching it with\r\nher lips. "You have a bower for the Lady of the Lake," she added.\r\n\r\n"I\'m afraid I\'m only Roderick Dhu\'s mother," laughed Lady Merrifield;\r\n"but I shall have more ladies when the masque is done. Now I have only\r\nMysie."\r\n\r\n"And oh!" cried Mysie, "please set up the nurse in the nursery gardens\r\nright. Wilfred knocked her over, and she won\'t stand right for me."\r\n\r\n"Perverse woman. There! No, I shall not buy anything now, I shall\r\nwait for Primrose and the refuse. How pretty it does all look! Ah, Mr.\r\nBrownlow," as she shook hands with the curate.\r\n\r\n"I left my brother John at your house," he said; "I persuaded him to run\r\ndown this morning with my mother and see our doings, and he was glad of\r\nthe opportunity of looking in upon the Vicar."\r\n\r\n"How very kind of him. We were wishing to know what he thought!"\r\n\r\n"No doubt he will be here presently. My mother is at the masque. There\r\nwas not a seat for us, so I took him down to St. Andrew\'s Rock."\r\n\r\n"Not a seat! The five-shilling seats?"\r\n\r\n"Not the fraction of one. Numbers standing outside! Pity there can\'t be\r\na second performance."\r\n\r\n"Four hundred seats! That\'s a hundred pounds! We shall beat the\r\nSchool-board yet!"\r\n\r\nSo, with the General politely expressing that there was no saying what\r\nRockquay owed to the hearty co-operation of such birds of passage as\r\nherself and her brothers, she travelled on to the charity stall, which\r\nMiss Mohun had quaintly dressed in the likeness of an old-fashioned\r\nschool, with big alphabet and samplers, flourished copies, and a stuffed\r\nfigure of a \'cont-rare-y\' naughty boy, with a magnificent fool\'s\r\ncap. She herself sat behind it, the very image of the Shenstone\r\nschool-mistress, with wide white cap, black poke-bonnet, crossed\r\nkerchief, red cloak, and formidable rod; and her myrmidons were\r\nin costume to match. It was very attractive, and took every one by\r\nsurprise, but Geraldine had had enough by this time, and listened\r\nto Miss Mohun\'s invitation and entreaty that she would preside over\r\ntea-cups for the weary, in the drawing-room.
