The privacy of the houses\r\nhad been secured by ropes extending from the stalls to the rails of the\r\ngarden, and Geraldine was conducted by her two generals to the verandah,\r\nwhere they installed her, and lingered, as was usual with her squires,\r\nalways won by her spirited talk, till messages came to each of them from\r\nbelow that some grandee was come, who must be talked to and entertained.\r\n\r\nAlready, however, Armine Brownlow had brought up his brother, the\r\ndoctor--John or Jock, an old friend--over, first Clement\'s district and\r\nthen his bed.\r\n\r\n"Well, Mrs. Grinstead, I can compliment you much on your brother. He is\r\nvery materially better, and his heart is recovering tone."\r\n\r\n"I am very glad and thankful! I only wish you had seen him last week. He\r\nwas better then, but he had a worry about our little nephew, which threw\r\nhim back."\r\n\r\n"So he told me. The more quiescent and amused you can keep him, the more\r\nchance of a fair recovery there will be. I am glad he thinks of dining\r\nwith the party to-night."\r\n\r\n"I am glad he still thinks. I had to come away early, when he had still\r\nleft it doubtful."\r\n\r\n"I encouraged the idea with all my might."\r\n\r\n"Do you think he will be able to go back to his parish?"\r\n\r\n"Most assuredly not while every worry tells on him in this manner. You\r\nmust, if possible, take him abroad for the winter, before he begins to\r\nthink about it."\r\n\r\n"He has leave of absence for a year."\r\n\r\n"Dating from Easter, I think. Keep him in warm climates as long as you\r\ncan. Find some country to interest him without over-fatigue, and you\r\nwill, I hope, be able to bring him home fit to consider the matter."\r\n\r\n"That is all you promise?"\r\n\r\n"All I dare--not even to promise--but to let you hope for."\r\n\r\nAn interruption came; one of the young ladies had had her skirt trodden\r\non, and wanted it to be stitched up. Then came Jane Mohun to deposit\r\na handkerchief which some one had dropped. "I can stay a moment," she\r\nsaid; "no one will come to buy till the masque is ended. Oh, this red\r\ncloak will be the death of me!"\r\n\r\n"You look highly respectable without it."\r\n\r\n"I shall only put it on for the coup d\'oeil at first. Oh, Geraldine,\r\nwhat is to be done with that horrid little Maura?"\r\n\r\n"The pretty little Greek girl--Mrs. Henderson\'s sister?"\r\n\r\n"Oh! it is not Mrs. Henderson\'s fault, nor my sister Ada\'s either,\r\nexcept that the little wretch must have come round her. I know Ada meant\r\nto stay away on that very account."\r\n\r\n"What account?"\r\n\r\n"Ivinghoe\'s, to be sure! Oh! I forgot. You are so much one of us that\r\nI did not remember that you did not know how the foolish boy was\r\nattracted--no, that\'s too strong a word--but she thought he was, when\r\nthey were here to open Rotherwood Park. He did flirt, and Victoria--his\r\nmother, I mean--did not like it at all. She would never have come this\r\ntime, but that I assured her that Maura was safe at Gastein!"\r\n\r\n"Is it so very undesirable?"\r\n\r\n"My dear! Their father was old White\'s brother, a stone-mason. He was\r\nraised from the ranks, but his wife was a Greek peasant--and if you had\r\nseen her, when the Merrifield children called her the Queen of the White\r\nAnts! Ivinghoe is naturally as stiff and formal as his mother, I am not\r\nmuch afraid for him, except that no one knows what that fever will make\r\nof a young man, and I don\'t want him to get his father into a scrape.\r\nThere, I have exhaled it to you, and there is a crowd as if the masque\r\nwas done with."\r\n\r\nIt was, and the four hundred auditors were beginning to throng about the\r\nstalls, strays coming up from time to time, and reporting with absolute\r\nenthusiasm on the music and acting. Marilda was one of these.\r\n\r\n"Well, Cherry, I saw no great harm in it after all, and Francie looked\r\nsweetly pretty, just as poor Alda did when she first came to us. Lance\r\nmust make his own excuses to Alda. But Gerald looked horridly ill!\r\nHe sang very well, but he had such red spots on his cheeks! I\'d get\r\nClement\'s doctor to sound him. Lord Rotherwood was quite complimentary.\r\nNow I must go and buy something--I hear there is the Dirty Boy--I think\r\nI shall get it for Fernan\'s new baths and wash-houses. Then isn\'t there\r\nsomething of yours, Cherry?"\r\n\r\n"Not to compete with the Dirty Boy."\r\n\r\n"Ah! now you are laughing at me, Cherry. Quite right, I am glad to hear\r\nyou do it again."\r\n\r\nThe next visitor was Lance.\r\n\r\n"Oh, Cherry, how cool you look! Give me a cup of tea--not\r\nrefreshment-stall tea. That\'s right. Little Francie is a perfect\r\ngem--looks and voice--not acting--no time for that. Heigh-ho!"\r\n\r\n"Where\'s Gerald?"\r\n\r\n"Somewhere about after that Merrifield niece with the doleful name, I\r\nfancy. He did very well when it came to the scratch."\r\n\r\n"Have you seen Dr. Brownlow? He has been to see Clement."\r\n\r\n"That\'s first-rate! Where shall I find him?"\r\n\r\n"Somewhere about, according to your lucid direction, I suppose."\r\n\r\n"What does he think of old Tina?"\r\n\r\nGeraldine told him, and was rather surprised, when he whistled as though\r\nperplexed, and as Fergus rushed in, glorious with the news that Sir\r\nFerdinand had bought his collection of specimens for the Bexley museum,\r\nhe rose up, looking perturbed, to find Dr. Brownlow.\r\n\r\nNext came Gillian with news that the Dirty Boy was sold to Lady Travis\r\nUnderwood.\r\n\r\n"And mayn\'t I stay a moment or two?" said she. "Now the masque is over,\r\nthat Captain Armytage is besetting me again."\r\n\r\n"Poor Captain Armytage."\r\n\r\n"Why do you pity him? He is going to join his ship, the Sparrow Hawk,\r\nnext week, and that ought to content him."\r\n\r\n"Ships do not always fill a man\'s heart."\r\n\r\n"Then they ought. I don\'t like it," she added, in a petulant tone. "I\r\nhave so much to learn and to do, I don\'t want to be tormented about a\r\ntiresome man."\r\n\r\n"Well, he will be out of your way to-morrow."\r\n\r\n"Geraldine, that is a horrid tone."\r\n\r\n"If you choose to put meaning in it, I cannot help it."\r\n\r\n"And that horrid little Maura! She is in the most awful flutter,\r\nstanding on tiptoe, and craning out her foolish little neck. I know it\r\nis all after Ivinghoe, and he never has come to our counter! Kalliope\r\nhas been trying to keep her in order, but I\'m sure the Queen of the\r\nWhite Ants must have been just like that when she got poor Captain White\r\nto marry her. Kalliope is so much vexed, I can see. She never meant to\r\nhave her here. And Aunt Ada stayed away on purpose."\r\n\r\n"Has she seen much of him?"\r\n\r\n"Hardly anything; but he did admire her, and she never was like\r\nKalliope. But what would Aunt Ada do? Oh dear! there\'s that man! He has\r\nno business at Aunt Jane\'s charity stall. I shall go and tell him so."\r\n\r\nGeraldine had her little private laugh before Adrian came up to her with\r\na great ship in his arms--\r\n\r\n"Take care of this, Aunt Cherry. She is going to sail on the Ewe. I\r\nbought her with the sovereign Uncle Fernan gave me."\r\n\r\nGeraldine gave the ship her due admiration, and asked after the masque.\r\n\r\n"Oh, that went off pretty well. I wouldn\'t have been Fely! All the\r\nladies went and said \'Pretty dear!\' when he sang his song about the\r\nbat\'s back. Disgusting! But then he has not been a fellow at school, so\r\nhe made his bow and looked as if he didn\'t mind it."\r\n\r\n"And Francie?"\r\n\r\n"Francie looked perfectly stunning. Everybody said so, and she\r\nsang--well, she sang better than she did at home; but she was in an\r\nawful funk, though I kept on looking at her, and shouting bravo to\r\nencourage her; and she must have heard my voice, for I was just in\r\nfront."\r\n\r\n"I hope she was encouraged."\r\n\r\n"But she is very stupid. I wanted to take her round to all the stalls,\r\nand show her what to buy with the five Jubilee sovereigns Uncle Fernan\r\ngave her, for you know she has never been anywhere, or seen anything. I\r\nthought she would like it, and besides, all our fellows say they never\r\nsaw such an awfully pretty girl, and they can\'t believe all that hair is\r\nher own--she had it all down her back, you know--so I told them I would\r\nlet them have a pull to try."\r\n\r\n"Poor Francie! She declined, I suppose?"\r\n\r\n"Well, there was that ridiculous swell, Fergus\'s cousin, Ivinghoe, and\r\nhe has taken her off to see the stupid flowers in the conservatory. I\r\ntold Sophy I wondered she permitted such flirting, but of course Francie\r\nknew no better."\r\n\r\n"Oh! and you couldn\'t stop it?"\r\n\r\n"Not I, though I called her over and over again to look at things, but\r\nLord Ivinghoe always hung about and gave one no peace. So I just told\r\nSophy to look after her, and came off to tell you. Oh my! here is old\r\nMiss Mohun coming up. I shall be off. I want some chocolate creams. Mrs.\r\nSimmonds has got some splendid ones."\r\n\r\nMiss Mohun was coming, in fact.\r\n\r\n"Well, Geraldine, the masque was a great success. People beg to have it\r\nrepeated, so many could not get in. And it is worth at least a hundred\r\npounds to us. People whose opinion is worth having were quite struck.\r\nThey say your brother really ought to have been a great composer and\r\nsinger."\r\n\r\n"I think he might have been if he had not given up his real passion to\r\ncome to the help of my dear eldest brother. And he is really happier as\r\nhe is."\r\n\r\n"I knew there was conquest in his face. And that dear little elf of a\r\nboy--what a voice! So bright and so arch too. Then the Miranda--she took\r\nall by surprise. I believe half the spectators took her for the Little\r\nButterfly."\r\n\r\n"Ah, the poor Little Butterfly is flown. There was nothing for it but to\r\nmake Francie act, as she had taken the part once before."\r\n\r\n"Her acting was no great things, they say--ladylike, but frightened. Her\r\nvoice is lovely, and as to her looks--people rave about them. Tell me,\r\nshe is not Lady Travis Underwood\'s daughter?"\r\n\r\n"Oh no; she is Anna\'s sister, Adrian\'s sister."\r\n\r\n"So I told Lady Rotherwood, I was sure it was so."\r\n\r\n"The Travis Underwoods have no children, but they adopted Emilia when\r\nI took Anna, and they have brought three Vanderkists to this affair.\r\nFrancie has never been from home before, it is all quite new to her."\r\nThen recollecting what Adrian had repeated, she thought it fair to add,\r\n"My sister was left very badly off, and all these eight girls will have\r\nnothing of their own."\r\n\r\n"Well, I don\'t suppose anything will come of it. I hope it will put no\r\nfolly into her head; but at any rate it effaces that poor silly little\r\nMaura. I hope too, as you say your niece is so innocent, it will do her\r\nno harm."\r\n\r\n"I don\'t suppose any possibilities have occurred to the child."\r\n\r\nLord Rotherwood here came on the scene.\r\n\r\n"Jenny, there\'s an offer for your boy in the fool\'s cap, and Mysie\r\ndoubts if she ought to let him go. Well, Mrs. Grinstead, I think you\r\nhave the best of it. Lookers on, etc."\r\n\r\n"Looking on has always been my trade."\r\n\r\n"You heard the rehearsal of the masque, I believe, but you did not hear\r\nthat charming Mona?"\r\n\r\n"No; she had to take the part suddenly. Her uncle had to tyrannize over\r\nher, to save the whole thing."\r\n\r\n"We are much indebted to him, and to her," said Lord Rotherwood\r\ncourteously. "She looked as if she hated it all in the first scene,\r\nthough she warmed up afterwards. I must say I liked her the better for\r\nher shyness."\r\n\r\n"Her little brother thinks she recovered in consequence of his\r\napplause," said Geraldine, smiling.\r\n\r\n"Ah! I saw him. And heard. A little square fellow--very sturdy."\r\n\r\n"Yes, the Dutchman comes out in him, and he has droll similitudes,\r\nvery curious in one who never saw his father, nor any but his Underwood\r\nrelations."\r\n\r\n"So much the better for him perhaps; I have, and ought to have, great\r\nfaith in uncles\' breeding. I am glad to meet Sir Ferdinand Travis\r\nUnderwood. I have often come across him about London good works."\r\n\r\n"Yes, he is an excellent man."\r\n\r\n"Not wholly English is he, judging by the depth of colour in those\r\neyes?"\r\n\r\n"No; his mother was a Mexican, partly Indian. We used to call him the\r\nCacique;" and Geraldine had the pleasure of telling his story to an\r\nearnest listener, but interruption came in the shape of Sir Ferdinand\r\nhimself who announced that he had hired a steam-yacht wherein to view\r\nthe regatta, and begged Lord Rotherwood to join the party.\r\n\r\nThis was impossible, as the Marquis was due at an agricultural dinner at\r\nClarebridge, but in return, in the openness of his heart, he invited the\r\nTravis Underwoods to their dinner that evening at the hotel, where the\r\nMerrifields and the Underwoods were already engaged, little boys and\r\nall.\r\n\r\n"Thank you, my lord, but we are too large a party. We have three\r\nVanderkist girls with us, and Anna and her brother are to join them to\r\nbe with their sister."\r\n\r\n"Never mind, never mind. The great hall will have room for all."\r\n\r\nStill Fernan demurred, knowing that Marilda had ordered dinner at the\r\nQuay Hotel, and that even liberal payment would not atone for missing\r\nthe feasting of the millionaires; so the matter was compounded by his\r\npromise to bring all his party, who were not ready for bed, up to spend\r\nthe evening.\r\n\r\nAnd Geraldine perceived from Lady Rotherwood\'s ceremonious politeness\r\nthat she did not like it at all, though she never said so even to Lady\r\nMerrifield.\r\n\r\nHowever, it was a very bright evening. Gerald had sung himself into\r\nspirits, and then found Dolores, and retreated into the depths of the\r\ngarden with her, explaining to her all about his sister, and declaring\r\nthat his first object must be to rescue her; and then, unless his name\r\nwas cleared, and he had to resume all his obligations, the new life\r\nwould be open to him, and he had no fear of not succeeding as a\r\njournalist, or if not, a musical career was possible to him, as Dolores\r\nhad now the opportunity of fully perceiving. His sweet voice had indeed\r\nfilled her with double enthusiasm. She had her plan for lecturing, and\r\nthat very morning she had received from her father permission to enter\r\na ladies\' college, and the wherewithal. She would qualify herself for\r\nlecturing by the time he had fixed his career; and they built their airy\r\ncastles, not on earth, but on railroads and cycles, and revelled on\r\nthem as happily as is common to lovers, whether in castle or in cottage.\r\nCertainly if the prospect held out to her had been Vale Leston Priory,\r\nit would not have had the same zest; and when in the evening they\r\njoined the dinner-party, there was a wonderful look of purpose and of\r\nbrightness on both their faces. And Emilia, who had been looking for\r\nhim all the afternoon to tell him, "Gerald, I am really going to be a\r\nnurse," only got for answer an absent "Indeed!"\r\n\r\n"Yes, at St. Roque\'s."\r\n\r\n"I hope I shall never be a patient there," he said, in his half-mocking\r\ntone. "You\'ll look jolly in the cap and apron."\r\n\r\n"I\'m to be there all the time they are in America, and--"\r\n\r\n"Well, I wonder you don\'t go and study the institutions."\r\n\r\n"But, Gerald--"\r\n\r\nHis eye was wandering, and he sprang forward to give Dolores a flower\r\nthat she had dropped.\r\n\r\nLancelot, knowing what was before Gerald, and having always regarded\r\nVale Leston with something of the honours of Paradise, could not\r\nunderstand that joyous look of life, so unlike Gerald\'s usual weary,\r\npassive expression. He himself felt something of the depression that was\r\napt to follow on musical enjoyment; he saw all the failures decidedly\r\nenough not to be gratified with the compliments he met on all sides, and\r\n"he bitterly thought on the morrow," when he saw how Clement was getting\r\nanimated over a discussion on Church matters, and how Geraldine was\r\nenjoying herself. And as to that pretty Franceska, who had blossomed\r\ninto the flower of the flock, he foresaw heart-break for her when\r\nhe watched the Marchioness\'s countenance on hearing that her son had\r\naccepted Sir Ferdinand\'s invitation to cruise to-morrow in the yacht.\r\n\r\nVainly was Ivinghoe reminded of the agricultural dinner. He was only too\r\nglad to escape it, and besides, he thought he could be there in time.\r\n\r\nNevertheless, the present was delightful, and after dinner the young\r\npeople all went off to the great assembly-room, whence Anna came back to\r\ncoax Uncle Lance to play for them. All the elders jumped up from their\r\nseveral discussions. Even Lady Rotherwood moved on, looking as benign\r\nas her feelings would permit. Jane squeezed Geraldine\'s arm, exceedingly\r\namused. Lance struck up, by request, an old-fashioned country dance;\r\nLord Rotherwood insisted that "Lily" should dance with him, as the\r\nremnant of forty good years ago or more, and with Sir Roger de Coverley\r\nthe day ended.\r\n\r\nPoor little Maura, making an excuse to wander about the gardens in the\r\nmoonlight, saw the golden locks shining through the open windows, and\r\nLord Ivinghoe standing over them, went home, and cried herself to sleep\r\nover the fickleness of the nobility, when she had better have cried over\r\nher own unjustified romance, excited by a few kindly speeches and a cup\r\nof tea.\r\n\r\nAnd Emilia! What was Gerald\'s one laughing turn with her, compared with\r\nhis long talk with Dolores in the moonlight?\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXII. -- THE REGATTA\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     She saw a forget-me-not in the grass,\r\n     Gilly-flower, gentle rosemary,\r\n     Ah! why did the lady that little flower pass,\r\n     While the dews fell over the mulberry-tree?\r\n                                            KENEALY.\r\n\r\n\r\nSuch of the party as were not wanted for the second day of the bazaar,\r\nand were not afraid of mal de mer, had accepted the yachting invitation,\r\nexcept the three elders at St. Andrew\'s Rock. Even Adrian and Felix\r\nwere suffered to go, under Sophy\'s charge, on the promise to go nowhere\r\nwithout express permission, and not to be troublesome to any one.\r\n\r\n"Sophy can say, \'Now, boys,\' as effectively as Wilmet," said Geraldine,\r\nwhen she met Lance, who had been to the quay to see them off.\r\n\r\n"She did not say so to much advantage with her own boys," said Clement.\r\n\r\n"We weren\'t Harewoods," returned Lance, "and John never could bear to\r\nsee a tight hand over them; but there\'s good in them that will come out\r\nsome day."\r\n\r\nClement gave an emphatic "Humph!" as he sat down to the second breakfast\r\nafter Anna had gone to the cliff to resume her toils.\r\n\r\n"Who are gone?" asked Geraldine.\r\n\r\n"Poor Marilda, smilingly declaring she shall be in misery in the cabin\r\nall the time, Fernan, and four Vanderkists, General Mohun, Sir Jasper,\r\nand some of his progeny; but others stay to help Miss Mohun finish up\r\nthe sales."\r\n\r\n"Does Lord Ivinghoe go?"\r\n\r\n"Oh yes, he came rushing down just in time. Francie was looking like a\r\nmorning rose off the cloister at Vale Leston."\r\n\r\n"I am sorry they have another day of it. I don\'t see how it can come to\r\ngood," said Geraldine.\r\n\r\n"Perhaps her roses may fade at sea," said Clement, "and disenchantment\r\nmay ensue."\r\n\r\n"At least I hope Alda may not hear of it, or she will be in an agony of\r\nexpectation as long as hope lasts. Gerald is gone, of course?"\r\n\r\n"Oh yes!" said Lance, who had had a farewell from him with the words,\r\n"Get it over while I am out of the way, and tell them I don\'t mind."\r\n\r\nCursory and incomprehensible, but conclusive; and Lance, who minded\r\nenough to have lost sleep and gained a headache, marvelled over young\r\nmen\'s lightness and buoyancy. He had seen Dr. Brownlow, and arranged\r\nthat there should be a call, as a friend, in due time after the\r\ncommunication, in case it should hurt Clement, and when Geraldine\r\nobserved merrily that now they were quit of all the young ones they\r\ncould feel like old times, he was quite grieved to disturb her pleasure.\r\n\r\nClement, however, began by taking out a letter and saying--\r\n\r\n"Here is a remarkable missive left for me yesterday--\'If the Rev.\r\nUnderwood wishes to hear of something to his advantage, he should\r\ncommunicate with Mr. O\'L., care of Mr. John Bast, van proprietor,\r\nWhitechapel.\' An impostor?" said he.\r\n\r\n"I am afraid not," said Lance. "Clement, I fear there is no doubt that\r\nshe is that singing Hungarian woman who was the ruin of Edgar\'s life."\r\n\r\n"Gerald\'s mother!" exclaimed Geraldine.\r\n\r\n"Even so."\r\n\r\n"But she is gone! She gave up all rights. She can\'t claim anything. Has\r\nshe worried him?"\r\n\r\n"Yes, poor boy! She has declared that she had actually a living husband\r\nat the time she married our poor Edgar."\r\n\r\nOf course both broke out into exclamations that it was impossible,\r\nand Lance had to tell them of his interview with the woman at Gerald\'s\r\nentreaty. They were neither of them so overcome by the disclosure as he\r\nhad feared during his long delay.\r\n\r\n"I believe it is only an attempt at extortion," said Clement.\r\n\r\n"Very cruel," said Geraldine. "How--how did my poor boy bear it all this\r\ntime?"\r\n\r\n"He was very much knocked down at first, quite overwhelmed, but less by\r\nthe loss than by the shame, and the imputation on his father."\r\n\r\n"It was no fault of dear Edgar\'s."\r\n\r\n"No, indeed. I am glad Fernan is here to go over again what Edgar told\r\nhim. We may be quite satisfied so far."\r\n\r\n"And is it needful to take it up?" asked Geraldine wistfully. "If we\r\ndon\'t believe it, the horrid story would get quashed."\r\n\r\n"No, Cherry," said Clement. "If you think it over you will see that we\r\nmust investigate. I should be relieved indeed to let it alone, but it\r\nwould not be fair towards Lance there and his boys."\r\n\r\nLance made a strange noise of horror and deprecation, then added--\r\n\r\n"I don\'t believe Gerald would consent to let it alone."\r\n\r\n"No, now he knows, of course. He is a right-minded, generous boy," said\r\nGeraldine. "I was wrong. Did you say he was very much upset?"\r\n\r\n"Just at first, when he came to me at night. I was obliged to dragoon\r\nhim, and myself too, to throw it off enough to be able to get through\r\nour performance yesterday. How thankful I am to the regatta that it is\r\nnot our duty to the country to go through it again to-day! However, he\r\nseems to have rebounded a good deal. He was about all the latter part of\r\nthe day with Miss Mohun."\r\n\r\n"I saw him dancing and laughing with some of them."\r\n\r\n"And he parted from me very cheerfully, telling me to assure you \'he did\r\nnot mind,\' whatever that may mean."\r\n\r\n"He knows that nothing can disturb our love for him, Edgar\'s little\r\ncomfort, passed on to bear us up," said Cherry tearfully. "Oh yes, I\r\nknow what he meant--Felix\'s delight, my darling always."\r\n\r\n"It strikes me," said Lance, "that if he can save his sister--"\r\n\r\nGeraldine started.\r\n\r\n"Oh, the cigar-girl! Only by that mother\'s side."\r\n\r\n"That is true, but she is his half-sister, and he is evidently much\r\ndrawn towards her. She is a nice little thing, and I believe he\r\nmade much of her on the rehearsal day. I saw they got on much better\r\ntogether, and I think she was aware of the relationship."\r\n\r\n"Yes, it is quite right of him," said Geraldine, "but she will be a\r\ndrag on him all his life. Now what ought we to do? Shall you answer this\r\nletter to the care of the van-man, Clem?"\r\n\r\n"I shall think, and wait till I have seen Gerald and Travis. This letter\r\nis evidently written simply in the hope of raising money from me, not in\r\nany friendly spirit."\r\n\r\n"Certainly not," said Lance. "Having failed to black-mail Gerald, and\r\ndiscovered that you are the heir, they begin on you, but not from any\r\ngratitude to you. Sweetie Bob, as they call the ex-errand-boy, gives a\r\nfine account of their denunciations of the tall parson who brought the\r\nbobbies down on them."\r\n\r\nLance felt much reassured by Clement\'s tone, and all the more when he\r\nhad seen Dr. Brownlow, who made a thorough examination, and came to the\r\nconclusion that Clement had recovered tone, so that the shock, whatever\r\nit was, that his brother dreaded had done no present damage, but that he\r\nwas by no means fit for any strain of work or exertion, should be kept\r\nfrom anxiety as much as possible, and had better spend the winter in a\r\nwarm climate. It was not likely--Jock Brownlow said it with grief\r\nand pain--that he would ever be able to return to the charge of St.\r\nMatthew\'s, but as he had a year\'s holiday, there was no need to enter on\r\nthat subject yet, and in a quiet country place, with a curate, he might\r\nlive to the age of man in tolerable health if he took care of himself,\r\nor his sister took care of him for some time to come.\r\n\r\nSo much relieved was Lance that he recollected that he had laid in no\r\nstock of presents for those at home, and went up to profit by the second\r\nday\'s reductions, when he secured Geraldine\'s portrait of Davy Blake\r\nfor his wife, and a statuette of St. Cecilia for Dr. May, some charming\r\nwater-colours for Robina and Ethel, besides various lesser delights for\r\nthe small fry, his own and the flock at Vale Leston, besides a cushion\r\nfor Alda\'s sofa. John Inglesant had been bought by a connoisseur by\r\nspecial commission. He heard at every stall triumphant accounts of the\r\ngrand outlay of the Travis Underwoods and Rotherwoods, and just the\r\ncontrary of Mrs. Pettifer, whom he encountered going about in search of\r\nbargains, and heard haggling for a handsome table-cover, because it was\r\nquite aesthetic, and would not do except in a large house, so of course\r\nit had not sold.\r\n\r\nThe Mouse-traps had been a great success, and there were very few left\r\nof them. They really owed as much to Lance as did the play, for he had\r\nnot only printed them at as small a cost as possible, but had edited,\r\npruned, and got them into shape more than any of the young lady authors\r\nsuspected. The interpretation of handwriting had likewise succeeded in\r\nobtaining many clients, and a large pile of silver coins. Anna, who was\r\nhovering near, was delighted to show him that her sister Sophy\'s\r\nwriting had been declared to indicate homely tastes, an affectionate\r\ndisposition, great perspicuity of perception, much force of character;\r\nand Franceska\'s, scarcely yet formed, showed that she was affectionate,\r\nromantic, and, of all things in the world, fond of horses and of\r\nboating. Emilia\'s was held as a great blunder, for she was said to\r\nhave an eye devoted to temporal advantages, also volatile, yet of great\r\ndetermination, triumphing over every obstacle, and in much danger of\r\nself-deception.\r\n\r\n"The triumph at least is true," said Anna, "now she has her way about\r\nthe nursing."\r\n\r\n"Has she? I did not know it."\r\n\r\n"Yes, she is to try it for a year, while Cousins Fernan and Marilda go\r\nout to their farm in the Rocky Mountains."\r\n\r\nJust then there was a little commotion, and a report came up that a boat\r\nhad been run down and some one drowned. Somebody said, "One of those\r\nacting last night--a buccaneer." Somebody else, "A naval man." Then it\r\nwas "The Buccaneer Captain," and Mrs. Pettifer was exclaiming, "Poor\r\nCaptain Armytage! He was in our theatricals, I remember, but they\r\nthought him rather high. But he was a fine young man! Poor Captain\r\nArmytage!"\r\n\r\nLance had sufficient interests in those at sea to be anxious, and turned\r\nhis steps to the gates to ascertain the facts, when he was overtaken by\r\nGillian, with a hat hastily thrown over her snooded hair and Highland\r\ngarb, hurrying along, and looking very white.\r\n\r\n"Mr. Underwood! Oh! did you hear who it was?"\r\n\r\n"No certainty. I was going down to find out. You," as he saw her\r\npurpose, "had better not come. There will be a great crowd. I will come\r\nback and tell you."\r\n\r\n"Oh no, I must. This is the short way."\r\n\r\nHer hands trembled so that she could hardly undo the private fastening\r\nof Miss Mohun\'s garden, and she began to dash down the cliff steps. Just\r\nat the turn, where the stair-way was narrowest, Lance heard her exclaim,\r\nand saw that she had met face to face no other than Captain Armytage\r\nhimself.\r\n\r\n"Oh! is it?" and she so tottered on the rocky step that the hand he had\r\nput out in greeting became a support, and a tender one, as Lance said\r\n(perhaps with a little _malice_)--\r\n\r\n"We heard that the Buccaneer Captain had come to grief."\r\n\r\n"I?" he laughed; and Gillian shook herself up, asking--\r\n\r\n"Weren\'t you run down?" seeing even as she spoke that not a drop of wet\r\nwas traceable.\r\n\r\n"Me! What! did you think I was going to peril my life in a \'longshore\r\nconcern like this?" said he, with a merry laugh, betraying infinite\r\npleasure.\r\n\r\n"But did nothing happen? Nobody drowned?" she asked, half disappointed.\r\n\r\n"Not a mouse! A little chap, one of the fairies yesterday, tumbled off\r\nthe sea-wall where he had no business to be, but he swam like a cork. We\r\nthrew him a rope and hauled him up."\r\n\r\nWherewith he gave his arm to Gillian, who was still trembling, and\r\nclasped it so warmly that Lance thought it expedient to pass them as\r\nsoon as possible and continue his journey on the staircase, giving a low\r\nwhistle of amusement, and pausing to look out on the beautiful blue\r\nbay, crowded with the white sails of yachts and pleasure-boats, with\r\nbrilliant festoons of little flags, and here and there the feather of\r\nsteam from a launch. He could look, for he was feeling lighter of heart\r\nnow that the communication was over.\r\n\r\nPerhaps Lance would have been edified could he have heard the colloquy--\r\n\r\n"Gillian! you do care for me after all?"\r\n\r\nGillian tried to take her arm away and to say, "Common humanity," but\r\nshe did not get the words out.\r\n\r\n"No, no!" he said. "Confess that if it had been that fisher-boy, you\r\nwould not be here now!" and he kept tight the arm that she was going to\r\ntake away. Her face was in a flame.\r\n\r\n"Well, well; and if--if it wasn\'t, you need not make such a fuss about\r\nit."\r\n\r\n"Not when it is the first ray of hope you have afforded me, for the only\r\njoy of my life?"\r\n\r\n"I never meant to afford--"\r\n\r\n"But you could not help."\r\n\r\n"Oh, don\'t! I never meant it. Oh dear! I never meant to be worried about\r\ntroublesome things like this till I had got older, and learnt a\r\ngreat deal more; and now you want to upset it all. It is very--very\r\ndisagreeable."\r\n\r\n"But you need not be upset!" poor Ernley Armytage pleaded. "Remember, I\r\nam going away for three years. May I not take hope with me?"\r\n\r\nGillian paused.\r\n\r\n"Well," again she said, "I do like you--I mean, I don\'t mind you as much\r\nas most people; you have done something, and you have some sense."\r\n\r\nHis look of rapture at these very moderate words quite overpowered her,\r\nand the tears welled up into her eyes, while she made a sudden change of\r\ntone.\r\n\r\n"There, there--of course it is all right. I\'m a nasty creature, and if\r\nyou like me, it is more than I deserve, only, whatever you do, don\'t\r\nmake me cry. I\'ve got all the horrid dolls and pen-wipers, and bags and\r\nrags to get rid of."\r\n\r\n"May I talk to your mother?"\r\n\r\n"Oh yes, if you can catch her. She will be ever so much more good to you\r\nthan I; and I only hope she will warn you what a Tartar I am."\r\n\r\nWherewith Gillian threw off her hat, swung open the gate, and dashed\r\nlike a hunted hare up to her mother\'s stall, where in truth she had been\r\nwanted, since only two helpers had remained to assist in the cheapening\r\nand final disposal of the remnants. Lady Merrifield read something in\r\nthose wild eyes and cheeks burning, but the exigencies of the moment\r\nobliged her to hold her peace, and apply herself to estimating the\r\nhalf-price of the cushions and table-cloths she rejoiced to see\r\ndeparting, as well as to preserve wits enough not to let Gillian sell\r\nthe Indian screen for two shillings and sixpence, under the impression\r\nthat this was the half of five pounds. Mysie was the only one who kept\r\nher senses fairly undisturbed, and could balance between her duty to the\r\nschools and her desire to gratify a child, happy in that she never saw\r\nmore than one thing at a time. Valetta and Primrose were yachting, so\r\nthat the distraction was less, and Captain Armytage lingered round,\r\ntaking messages, and looking in wistful earnestness for some one to\r\nbe disengaged. Yet there was something in his eyes that spoke of the\r\ncalmness of an attained object, and Miss Mohun, who had sold off all\r\nher remaining frocks and pinafores at a valuation to Marilda for some\r\ninstitution, and was free to help her sister, saw in a moment that his\r\nmind was settled.\r\n\r\nYet speech was scarcely possible till the clearance was finally\r\neffected by a Dutch auction, when Captain Armytage distinguished himself\r\nunexpectedly as auctioneer, and made an end even of the last sachet,\r\nthough it smelt so strongly of lip-salve that he declared that a bearer\r\nmust be paid to take it away. But the purchaser was a big sailor, who\r\nevidently thought it an elegant gift for his sweetheart.\r\n\r\nBy the time it was gone the yachters had come home. Captain Armytage\r\nseized on Sir Jasper, who already know his purpose, and wished him\r\nsuccess, though withheld from saying a word to urge the suit by Lady\r\nMerrifield\'s assurances, that to hurry Gillian\'s decision would be fatal\r\nto success, and that a reproof for petulance would be worse. She did\r\nnot know whether to wish for the engagement or not; Gillian was her very\r\ndear and sufficient companion, more completely so than Mysie, who was\r\nfar less clever; and she had sometimes doubted whether common domestic\r\nlife beginning early was for the girl\'s happiness and full development;\r\nbut she knew that her husband would scout these doubts as nonsense, and\r\nboth really liked Ernley Armytage, and had heard nothing but what was\r\nto his advantage in every way, when they had been in his own county,\r\nand had seen his neighbours and his family. However, she could only keep\r\nquiet, and let her heart rise in a continual aspiration at every silent\r\nmoment for her child\'s guidance.\r\n\r\nBefore she had had her moment of speech with either, she heard her\r\nhusband calling Gillian, and she knew that he was the one person with\r\nwhom his daughter never hid her true self in petulance or sarcasm. So\r\nGillian met him in the General\'s sitting-room, gasping as she turned the\r\nhandle of the door. He set a chair for her, and spoke gravely.\r\n\r\n"My dear," he said, "I find you have gained the heart of a good man."\r\n\r\n"I am sure I never meant it," half whispered Gillian.\r\n\r\n"What is that--you never meant it? I never supposed you capable of such\r\nan unladylike design. You mean that you were taken by surprise?"\r\n\r\n"No; I did see what he was at," and she hung her head.\r\n\r\n"You guessed his intentions?"\r\n\r\n"Yes, papa; but I didn\'t want--"\r\n\r\n"Try to explain yourself," said Sir Jasper as she broke off.\r\n\r\n"I--I did wish to go on improving myself and being useful. Surely it was\r\nnot wrong, papa. Don\'t you see, I did not want to let myself be\r\nworried into letting myself go out, and spoiling all my happiness and\r\nimprovement and work, and getting to care for somebody else?"\r\n\r\n"But you have consented."\r\n\r\n"Well, when I was frightened for him I found I did care, and he got hold\r\nof me, and made me allow that I did; and now I suppose nobody will give\r\nme any peace."\r\n\r\n"Stay, Gillian--keep yourself from this impatient mood. I think I\r\nunderstand your unwillingness to overthrow old associations and admit a\r\nnew overmastering feeling."\r\n\r\n"That\'s just it, papa," said Gillian, looking up. "I can\'t bear that\r\novermastering feeling, nor the being told every one must come to it. It\r\nseems such folly."\r\n\r\n"Folly that Eve was given to be a helpmeet, and as the bride, the Church\r\nto her Bridegroom? Look high enough, Gillian, and the popular chatter\r\nwill not confuse your mind. You own that you really love him."\r\n\r\n"Oh, papa, not half so much as mamma, or Mysie, or Jasper, but--but I\r\nthink I might."\r\n\r\n"Is that all, Gillian? No one would coerce you. Shall I send him away,\r\nand tell him not to think of it? Remember, it is a serious thing--nay,\r\nan unworthy thing to trifle with a right-minded man."\r\n\r\nGillian sat clasping the elbow of her chair, her dark eyes fixed. At\r\nlast she said--\r\n\r\n"Papa, I do feel a sort of trust in him, a sort of feeling as if my life\r\nand all goodness and all that would be safe with him; and I couldn\'t\r\nbear him to go quite away and hear no more of him, only I do wish it\r\nwouldn\'t happen now; and if there is a fuss about it, I shall get cross\r\nand savage, and be as nasty as possible, I know I shall."\r\n\r\n"You can\'t exercise enough self-command to remember what is due--I would\r\nsay kind and considerate--to a man who has loved you through all your\r\npetulance and discouragement, and now is going to a life not without\r\nperil for three years? Suppose a mishap, Gillian--how would you feel as\r\nto your treatment of him on this last evening?"\r\n\r\n"Oh, papa! if you talk in that way I must, I must," and she burst into\r\ntears.\r\n\r\nSir Jasper bent over her and gave her a kiss--a kiss that from him was\r\nsomething to remember. It was late, and summonses to a hurried meal were\r\nringing through Beechcroft Cottage, where the Clipstone party waited to\r\nsee the illuminations.\r\n\r\nTalk was eager between the sellers and the sailors as Valetta described\r\nthe two parties, the fate of the Indian screen, and the misconduct of\r\nCockneys in their launches were discussed by many a voice, but Gillian\r\nwas unwontedly silent. Her mother had no time for more than a kiss\r\nbefore the shouts of Wilfred, Fergus, and Primrose warned them that the\r\nilluminations were beginning. She could only catch Mysie, and beg her\r\nto keep the younger ones away from Gillian and the Captain. Mysie opened\r\nher brown eyes wide and said--\r\n\r\n"Oh!" Then, "Is it really?"\r\n\r\n"Really, my dear, and remember that it is his last evening!"\r\n\r\n"Oh!" said Mysie again. "I never thought it of Gill! May I tell\r\nValetta?"\r\n\r\n"Better not, my dear, if it can be helped."\r\n\r\nA screaming for Gill was heard, and Mysie hastened to answer it. Lady\r\nMerrifield was too much tired to do anything but sit in the garden\r\nwith Miss Mohun and look out at the ships, glittering with festoons of\r\ncoloured lamps, reflected in the sea, but the young people went further\r\nafield, out on the cliff path to Rotherwood Park. The populace were\r\nmainly collected on the quay, and this formed a more select promenade,\r\nthough by no means absolute solitude. Sir Jasper really did keep guard\r\nover the path along which Gillian allowed her Captain to conduct her,\r\nnot exactly knowing which way they were going, and quite away from the\r\nbay and all its attractions.\r\n\r\nShe heard him out without any of the sharp, impatient answers in which\r\nher maiden coyness was wont to disguise itself, as he told her of his\r\nhopes and plans for the time when his three years of the Mediterranean\r\nshould be over.\r\n\r\n"And you see you can go on studying all the time, if you must be so\r\nclever."\r\n\r\n"I think one ought to make the most of oneself, just as you want to rise\r\nin your profession! No, indeed, I could not bear you if you wanted me to\r\nsit down and idle, or to dawdle yourself."\r\n\r\n"Don\'t grow too clever for me."\r\n\r\n"Mother always says that a real man has stuff in him that is quite\r\ndifferent from cleverness, and yet I could not bear to give that up. I\r\nam so glad you don\'t mind."\r\n\r\n"Mind! I mind nothing but to know you are caring for me. And you will\r\nwrite to me?"\r\n\r\n"I shan\'t know what to say. You will tell of volcanoes, and Athens, and\r\nConstantinople, and Egypt, and the Holy Land, and I shall have nothing\r\nto say but who lectures in college."\r\n\r\n"Little you know what that will be to me."\r\n\r\nIt was a curious sensation all the time to Gillian, with a dawning sense\r\nthat was hardly yet love--she was afraid of that--but of something\r\ngood and brave and worthy that had become hers. She had felt something\r\nanalogous when the big deer-hound at Stokesley came and put his head\r\nupon her lap. But the hound showed himself grateful for caresses, and so\r\ndid her present giant when the road grew rough, and she let him draw her\r\narm into his and talk to her.\r\n\r\nIt was the parting, for he had to go to London and to his own family the\r\nnext day early. Gillian spoke not a word all through the dark drive to\r\nClipstone, but when the party emerged into the light her eyes were full\r\nof tears. Lady Merrifield followed her to her room, and her words half\r\nchoked were--\r\n\r\n"Mamma, I never knew what a great, solemn, holy thing _it_ is. Will you\r\nlook me out a prayer to help me to get worthy?"\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIII. -- ILLUMINATIONS\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     \'Twas in the summer-time so sweet,\r\n        When hearts and flowers are both in season,\r\n      That who, of all the world should meet,\r\n        In "twilight eve," but Love and Reason.\r\n                                         T. MOORE.\r\n\r\n\r\nThat moon and sparkling lights did not shine alone for Gerald and\r\nDolores. There were multitudes on the cliffs and the beach, and Sir\r\nFerdinand and Lady Travis Underwood with their party had come to an\r\nirregular sort of dinner-supper at St. Andrew\'s Rock. With them, or\r\nrather before them, came Mr. Bramshaw, the engineer, who sent in\r\nhis card to Mr. Clement Underwood, and entered with a leathern bag,\r\nbetraying the designs on Penbeacon.\r\n\r\nNot that these were more than an introduction. Indeed, under the present\r\ncircumstances, a definite answer was impossible; but there was another\r\nquestion, namely, that which regarded Sophia Vanderkist. She had indeed\r\nlong been of age, but of course her suitor could not but look to her\r\nformer guardian for consent and influence. He was a very bearded man,\r\npleasant-spoken and gentlemanlike, and Lancelot had prepared his brother\r\nby saying that he knew all about the family, and they were highly\r\nrespectable solicitors at Minsterham, one son a master in the school at\r\nStoneborough. So Clement listened favourably, liked the young man, and\r\nthough his fortunes at present depended on his work, and Lady Vanderkist\r\nwas no friend to his suit, gave him fair encouragement, and invited him\r\nto join the meal, though the party was already likely to be too numerous\r\nfor the dining-room.\r\n\r\nThat mattered the less when all the young and noisy ones could be\r\nplaced, to their great delight, under the verandah outside, where they\r\ncould talk and laugh to their utmost content, without incommoding Uncle\r\nClement, or being awed by Cousin Fernan\'s black beard and Cacique-like\r\ngravity. How they discussed and made fun over the humours of the bazaar;\r\nnor was Gerald\'s wit the slackest, nor his mirth the most lagging. He\r\nwas very far from depressed now that the first shock was over. He knew\r\nhimself to be as much loved or better than ever by those whose affection\r\nhe valued, and he was sure of Dolores\' heart as he had never yet been.\r\nThe latent Bohemianism in his nature woke with the prospect of having\r\nhis own way to make, and being free from the responsibilities of an\r\nestate, and his chivalry was excited by the pleasure of protecting his\r\nlittle half-sister, in pursuit of whom he intended to go.\r\n\r\nSo, light-hearted enough to amaze the elders who knew the secret, he\r\njumped up to go with the rest of the party to the cliff walk, where the\r\nbrilliant ships could best be seen. Lance, though his headache was,\r\nas Geraldine said, visible on his brow, declared that night air and\r\nsea-breeze were the best remedy, and went in charge of the two boys,\r\nlest his dainty Ariel should make an excursion over the rocks; and the\r\nfour young ladies were escorted by Gerald and the engineer.\r\n\r\nThe elders were much too tired for further adventures, and Geraldine\r\nand Marilda were too intimate to feel bound to talk. Only a few words\r\ndropped now and then about Emilia and her hospital, where she was to\r\nbe left for a year, while Fernan with Marilda visited his American\r\nestablishments, and on their return would decide whether she would\r\nreturn, or whether they would take Franceska, or a younger one, in her\r\nstead. The desertion put Marilda out of heart, and she sighed what a\r\npity it was that the girl would not listen to young Brown.\r\n\r\nMeanwhile, Clement was making Ferdinand go over with him Edgar\'s words\r\nabout his marriage. They had all been written down immediately after his\r\ndeath, and had been given to Felix with the certificates of the marriage\r\nand birth and of the divorce, and they were now no doubt with other\r\ndocuments and deeds in the strong-box at Vale Leston Priory. Fernan\r\ncould only repeat the words which had been burnt in on his memory,\r\nand promise to hunt up the evidence of the form and manner of the\r\ndissolution of the marriage at Chicago. Like Clement himself, he\r\nvery much doubted whether the allegation would not break down in some\r\nimportant point, but he wished Gerald to be assured that if the worst\r\ncame to the worst, he would never be left destitute, since that\r\nfirst meeting--the baptism, and the receiving him from the dying\r\nfather--amounted to an adoption sacred in his eyes.\r\n\r\nThen, seeing how worn-out Clement looked, he abetted Sibby and\r\nGeraldine, in shutting their patient safe up in his bedroom, not to be\r\n"mislested" any more that night, said Sibby. So he missed the rush of\r\nthe return. First came the two sober sisters, Anna and Emilia, only\r\nsorry that Aunt Cherry had not seen the lovely sea, the exquisite\r\ntwinkle of silvered waves as the moon rose, and then the outburst of\r\ncoloured lights, taking many forms, and the brilliant fireworks darting\r\nto and fro, describing curves, bursting and scattering their sparks.\r\nEmilia had, however, begun by the anxious question--\r\n\r\n"Nan, what is it with Gerald?"\r\n\r\n"I don\'t quite know. I suspect Dolores has somehow teased him, though it\r\nis not like her."\r\n\r\n"Then there is something in it?"\r\n\r\n"I can\'t help believing so, but I don\'t believe it has come to\r\nanything."\r\n\r\n"And is she not a most disagreeable girl! Those black eyebrows do look\r\nso sullen and thunderous."\r\n\r\n"Oh no, Emmie, I thought so at first, but she can\'t help her eyebrows;\r\nand when you come to know her there is a vast deal in her--thought, and\r\noriginality, and purpose. I am sure it has been good for Gerald. He has\r\nseemed more definite and in earnest lately, less as if he were playing\r\nwith everything, with all views all round."\r\n\r\n"But his spirits are so odd!--so merry and then so grave."\r\n\r\n"That is only during these last few days, and I fancy there must be some\r\nhitch--perhaps about Dolores\' father, and we are all in such haste."\r\n\r\nEmilia did not pursue the subject. She had never indulged in the folly\r\nof expecting any signs of actual love from her cousin. She had always\r\nknown that the family regarded any closer bond as impossible; but she\r\nhad been always used to be his chief confidante, and she missed his\r\nattention, but she would not own this even to herself, go she talked\r\nof her hospital schemes with much zest, and how she should spend her\r\noutings at a favourite sisterhood.\r\n\r\n"For," said she, "I am tired of luxury."\r\n\r\nIt had been a delightful walk to Anna, with her companion sister,\r\ndiscussing Adrian, or Emily\'s plans, or Sophy\'s prospects. They had come\r\nhome the sooner, for Emily had to pack, as she was to spend a little\r\nwhile with her mother at Vale Leston. Where was Franceska? They were\r\nsomewhat dismayed not to find her, but it was one of the nights when\r\neverybody loses everybody, and no doubt she was with Uncle Lance, or\r\nwith Sophy, or Gerald.\r\n\r\nNo such thing. Here was Uncle Lance with his two boys in varying kinds\r\nof delight, Adrian pronouncing that "it was very jolly, the most ripping\r\nsight he ever saw," then eating voraciously, with his eyes half shut,\r\nand tumbling off to bed "like a veritable Dutchman," said Lance, who\r\nhad his own son in a very different mood, with glowing cheeks, sparkling\r\neyes, appetite gone for very excitement, as he sprang about and waved\r\nhis hands to describe the beautiful course of the rockets, and the fall\r\nof the stars from the Roman candles.\r\n\r\n"Oh, such as I never--never saw! How shall I get Pearl and Audrey to get\r\neven a notion of it? Grandpapa will guess in a moment! Oh, and the sea,\r\nall shine with a path of--of glory! Oh, daddy, there are things more\r\nbeautiful than anybody could ever dream of!"\r\n\r\n"Go and dream then, my sprite. Try to be as still as you can, even if\r\nyou do go on feeling the yacht, and seeing the sparks when you shut your\r\neyes. For you see my head is bad, and I do want a chance of sleep."\r\n\r\n"Poor daddy! I\'ll try, even if the music goes on in my head.\r\nGood-night."\r\n\r\n"That will keep him quieter than anything," said Lance; "but I would not\r\ngive much for the chance of his not seeing the dawn."\r\n\r\n"Or you either, I fear," said Geraldine. "Have you slept since the\r\ndiscovery?"\r\n\r\n"I shall make my sleep up at home, now I have had the whole out. Who\r\ncomes now?"\r\n\r\nIt was Sophy, with her look of\r\n\r\n\r\n          "Gentle wishes long subdued,\r\n           Subdued and cherished long."\r\n\r\n\r\nMr. Bramshaw had brought her to the door, and no doubt she and he had\r\nhad a quiet, restful time of patient planning; but the not finding\r\nFrancie soon filled her with great alarm and self-reproach for having\r\nlet herself be drawn away from the party, when all had stood together\r\non Miss Mohun\'s lawn. She wanted to start off at once in search of her\r\nsister, and was hardly pacified by finding that Gerald was still to\r\ncome. Then, however, Gerald did come, and alone. He said he had just\r\nseen the Clipstone party off. No, he had not seen Francie there; but he\r\nadded, rather as if recovering from a bewilderment, as Sophy was asking\r\nhim to come out with her again, "Oh, never fear. Lord Ivinghoe was there\r\nsomewhere!"\r\n\r\n"I thought he was gone."\r\n\r\n"No, he said the yacht got in too late for the train. Never mind, Sophy,\r\ndepend upon it she is all right."\r\n\r\nNone of the ladies present felt equally pleased, but in a minute or two\r\nmore in came a creature, bright, lovely, and flushed, with two starry\r\neyes, gleaming like the blue lights on the ships.\r\n\r\n"Oh, Cousin Marilda, have I kept you waiting? I am so sorry!"\r\n\r\n"Where have you been?"\r\n\r\n"Only on the cliff walk. Lord Ivinghoe took me to see the place where\r\nhis father had the accident, and we watched the fireworks from there.\r\nOh, it was so nice, and still more beautiful when the strange lights\r\nwere out and the people gone, and only the lovely quiet moon shining on\r\nthe sea, and a path of light from Venus."\r\n\r\n"I should think so," muttered Gerald, and Marilda began--\r\n\r\n"Pretty well, miss."\r\n\r\n"I am very sorry to bo so late," began Francie, and Geraldine caught an\r\nopportunity while shawling Marilda to say--\r\n\r\n"Dear, good Marilda, I implore you to say nothing to put it into her\r\nhead or Alda\'s. I don\'t think any harm is done yet, but it can\'t be\r\nanything. It can\'t come to good, and it would only be unhappiness to\r\nthem all."\r\n\r\n"Oh, ah! well, I\'ll try. But what a chance it would be, and how happy it\r\nwould make poor Alda!"\r\n\r\n"It can\'t be. The boy\'s mother would never let him look at her! Don\'t,\r\ndon\'t, don\'t!"\r\n\r\n"Well, I\'ll try not." She kissed her fondly.\r\n\r\nGerald\'s walk had been with Dolores of course, a quiet, grave, earnest\r\ntalk and walk, making them feel how much they belonged to one another,\r\nand building schemes in which they were to learn the nature of the poor\r\nand hard-worked, by veritably belonging to them, and being thus able\r\nto be of real benefit. In truth, neither of them, in their brave\r\nyouthfulness, really regretted Vale Leston, and the responsibilities;\r\nand, as Gerald declared, he would give it up tomorrow gladly if he could\r\nsave his name and his father\'s from shame, but, alas! the things went\r\ntogether.\r\n\r\nDolores wished to write fully to her father, and that Gerald should\r\ndo the same, but she did not wish to have the matter discussed in\r\nthe family at once, before his answer came, and Gerald had agreed to\r\nsilence, as indeed they would not call themselves engaged till that\r\ntime. Indeed, Dolores said there was so much excitement about Captain\r\nArmytage that no one was thinking of her.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIV. -- COUNSELS OF PATIENCE\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     He either fears his fate too much,\r\n       Or his deserts are small,\r\n     Who fears to put it to the touch,\r\n       To win or lose it all.\r\n\r\n\r\nIf Sibby hoped to keep her "long boy" from being "mislested," she was\r\nmistaken. He knew too well what was to come, and when she knocked at\r\nhis door with his cup of tea, he came to it half dressed, to her extreme\r\nindignation, calling for his shaving water.\r\n\r\n"Now, Master Clem, if you would only be insinsed enough to keep to your\r\nbed, you might have Miss Sophy to speak to you there, if nothing else\r\nwill serve you."\r\n\r\n"Is she there?"\r\n\r\n"In coorse, and Miss Francie too. What should they do else, after\r\ncolloguing with their young men all night? Ah, \'tis a proud woman poor\r\nMiss Alda would be if she could have seen the young lord! And the real\r\nbeauty is Miss Francie, such as my own babbies were before her, bless\r\nthem!"\r\n\r\n"Stop," cried Clement in consternation. "It is only a bit of passing\r\nadmiration. Don\'t say a word about it to the others."\r\n\r\n"As if I would demane myself to the like of them! Me that has been\r\nforty-seven years with you and yours, and had every one of you in my\r\narms the first thing, except the blessed eldest that is gone to a better\r\nplace."\r\n\r\n"Would that he were here now!" sighed Clement, almost as he had sighed\r\nthat first morning of his loss. "Where are those girls?"\r\n\r\n"Rampaging over the house with Sir Adrian, and his packing of all his\r\nrubbish, enough to break the heart of a coal-heaver! I\'d not let them in\r\nto bother their aunt, and Mr. Gerald is asleep like a blessed baby."\r\n\r\n"And Lance?"\r\n\r\n"Oh! it is down to the sea he is with that child that looks as if he\r\nwas made of air, and lived on live larks! And Master Lance, he\'s no\r\nbetter--eats like a sparrow, and sits up half the night writing for his\r\npaper."\r\n\r\nClement got rid of Sibby at last, but he was hardly out of his room\r\nbefore Sophy descended on him, anxious and blushing, though he could\r\ngive her much sympathy and kindly hope of his influence, only he had\r\nto preach patience. It had been no hasty fancy, but there had long been\r\ngrowing esteem and affection, and he could assure her of all the aid\r\nthe family could give with her mother, though Penbeacon works would be a\r\nvery insecure foundation for hope.\r\n\r\n"I think Gerald would consent," said Sophy, "and he will soon be of\r\nage."\r\n\r\nClement could only say "Humph!"\r\n\r\n"One thing I hope is not wrong," said Sophy, "but I do trust that no one\r\nwill tell mother about Lord Ivinghoe. It is not jealousy, I hope, but I\r\ncannot see that there is anything in it, only the very sound would set\r\nmother more against Philip than ever."\r\n\r\n"You do not suppose that Francie is--is touched?"\r\n\r\n"No," said Sophy, gravely as an elder, "she is such a child. She was\r\nvery much pleased and entertained, and went on chattering, till I begged\r\nher to let us say our prayers in peace. We never talk after that, and\r\nshe went to sleep directly, and was smiling when she woke, but I do not\r\nfancy she will dwell on it, or fancy there is more to come, unless some\r\none puts it into her head."\r\n\r\nIt was sagely said, and Clement knew pretty well who was the one person\r\nfrom whom Sophy had fears. Poor Alda, improved and altered as she was,\r\nif such a hope occurred to her, would she be able to help imparting it\r\nto her daughter and looking out for the fulfilment?\r\n\r\nLoud calls for Sophy rang through the house, and Clement had only time\r\nto add--\r\n\r\n"Patience, dear child, and submission. They not only win the day, but\r\nare the best preparation for it when it is won."\r\n\r\nThat family of girls had grown up to be a care to one who had trusted\r\nthat his calling would be a shield from worldly concerns; but he\r\naccepted it as providential, and as a trust imposed on him as certainly\r\nas Felix had felt the headship of the orphaned house.\r\n\r\nHe was rejoiced to find on coming down-stairs that Lance had decided on\r\ngiving another day to family counsels, sending off little Felix with his\r\ncousins, who would drop him at the junction to Stoneborough, whence\r\nhe would be proud to travel alone. Clement took another resolution, in\r\nvirtue of which he knocked at his sister\'s door before she went down.\r\n\r\n"Cherry," said he, "would it be inconvenient to keep Francie here just\r\nfor the present?"\r\n\r\n"Not at all; it would be only too pleasant for Anna now that she loses\r\nher brother. But why?"\r\n\r\n"I want to hinder her from hearing the conclusions that her mother may\r\ndraw from the diversions of yesterday."\r\n\r\n"I see. It might soon be,\r\n\r\n\r\n          \'He cometh not, she said.\'"\r\n\r\n\r\n"And Sophy will keep her counsel as to those moonlight wanderings. When\r\nwere they to go?"\r\n\r\n"By the 11.30 train. Marilda is coming up first."\r\n\r\nSo the plan was propounded. Franceska was only too much charmed to stay\r\nin what had indeed been an enchanted coast to her, and Sophy was\r\nsure that mamma would not mind; so the matter was settled, and the\r\nexplanatory notes written.\r\n\r\nThe party set off, with each little boy hugging a ship in full sail, and\r\nthe two young sisters were disposed of by a walk to Clipstone to talk\r\nover their adventures. Mrs. Grinstead felt certain of the good manners\r\nand reticence prevailing there to prevent any banter about Lord\r\nIvinghoe, and she secured the matter further by a hint to Anna.\r\n\r\nHowever, Miss Mohun was announced almost as they left the house. She\r\ntoo was full of the bazaar, which seemed so long ago to her hearers, but\r\nwith the result of which she was exceedingly delighted. The voluntary\r\nschools were secured for the present, and the gratitude of the Church\r\nfolk was unbounded, especially to the Vale Leston family, who had\r\ncontributed so greatly to the success of the whole.\r\n\r\nJane too had watched the evening manoeuvres, and perceived, with her\r\nsharp eyes, all that was avowed and not avowed under that rising moon.\r\nThe pair of whom she had first to speak were "Ivanhoe and Rowena," as\r\nshe called them, and she was glad to find that the "fair Saxon" had\r\ngrown up at Vale Leston, educated by her aunt and sister, and imbibing\r\nno outside habits or impressions.\r\n\r\n"Poor child," said Jane, "she looks like a flower; one is sorry it\r\nshould be meddled with."\r\n\r\n"So did my sister Stella, and there, contrary to all our fears, the\r\ncourse of true love did run smooth."\r\n\r\n"If it depended entirely on Rotherwood himself, I think it would,"\r\nsaid Jane, "but--" She paused and went on, "Ivinghoe is, I fear, really\r\nvolage, and he is the mark of a good many London mammas."\r\n\r\n"Is it true about Mrs. Henderson\'s sister?"\r\n\r\n"There\'s nothing in it. I believe he danced with her a few times, and\r\nthe silly little thing put her own construction on it, but her sister\r\nmade her confess that he had never said a word to her, nor made love in\r\nany sense. Indeed, my sister Adeline would never have consented to her\r\ncoming here if she had believed in it, but Maura has a Greek nature and\r\nturns the Whites round her fingers. Well, I hope all will go well with\r\nyour pretty Franceska. I should not like her lovely bloom to be faded by\r\nIvinghoe. He is Rotherwood\'s own boy, though rather a prig, and a man in\r\nLondon. Oh, you know what that means!"\r\n\r\n"We have done _notre possible_ to keep our interpretation from the poor\r\nchild, or any hint of it from reaching her mother."\r\n\r\n"That\'s right. Poor Rowena, I hope the spark will be blown out, or\r\nremain only a pleasant recollection. As to little Maura, she had her\r\nlesson when she was reduced to hanging on Captain Henderson\'s other arm!\r\nShe is off to-day to meet Mr. White in London. That purpose has been\r\nserved."\r\n\r\n"And have you not a nearer interest?"\r\n\r\n"Oh, Gillian! Well, Captain Armytage did get hold of her, in what we\r\nmust now call the Lover\'s Walk! Yes, she has yielded, to her father\'s\r\ngreat satisfaction and perhaps to her mother\'s, for she will be more\r\ncomfortable in looking forward to a commonplace life for her than in\r\nthe dread of modern aberrations. But Gillian is very funny, very much\r\nashamed of having given in, and perfectly determined to go to her\r\ncollege and finish her education, which she may as well do while the\r\nSparrow Hawk is at sea. He is off to-day, and she says she is very glad\r\nto be rid of him. She sat down at once to her dynamite, as Primrose\r\ncalls it, having bound over Mysie and Valetta never to mention the\r\nsubject! I tell them that to obey in silence is the way to serve the\r\npoor man best."\r\n\r\nMiss Mohun was interrupted by the announcement of Lady Flight and Mr.\r\nFlight, who came equally eager with delight and gratitude to thank the\r\nHouse of Underwood for the triumph. The rest of the clergy of Rockquay\r\nand half the ladies might be expected, and in despair at last of a\r\n"lucid interval," Geraldine ordered the carriage for a long drive into\r\nthe country, so as to escape all visitors. Even then, they could not\r\ngot up the hill without being stopped four or five times to receive the\r\nthanks and compliments which nearly drove Gerald crazy, so much did\r\nhe want to hear what his family had to say to his plans, that he had\r\nactually consented to partake of a dowager-drive in a landau!\r\n\r\nHe and his uncle had discovered from the police in the course of the\r\nmorning that Ludmilla and her mother had not gone with the circus, but\r\nhad been seen embarking in the Alice Jane, a vessel bound for London.\r\nHis idea had been to hurry thither and endeavour to search out his\r\nhalf-sister, and rescue her; but Lance had assured him not only that\r\nit would probably be a vain quest, but that there would be full time to\r\nmeet the Alice Jane by land before she could get there by sea.\r\n\r\nTo this he had yielded, but not so readily to the representation that\r\nthe wisest way would be to keep out of sight; but to let Lance, as\r\na less interested party, go and interview the van proprietor, whose\r\ndirection had been sent to Clement, try to see O\'Leary, and do his best\r\nto bargain for Ludmilla\'s release, a matter on which all were decided,\r\nwhatever might be the upshot of the question respecting Gerald. To leave\r\na poor girl to circus training, even if there were no interest in her,\r\nwould have been shocking to right-minded people; but when it was such\r\na circus as O\'Leary\'s, and the maiden was so good, sweet, and modest\r\nas Lida, the thought would have been intolerable even without the\r\nconnection with Gerald, who had been much taken with all he had seen of\r\nher.\r\n\r\n"That is fixed, even if we have to bid high for our Mona," said Lance.\r\n\r\n"By all means," said Geraldine. "It will be another question what will\r\nbe good for her when we have got her."\r\n\r\n"I will take care of that!" said Gerald.\r\n\r\n"Next," Lance went on, "we must see what proofs, or if there be any, of\r\nthis person\'s story. I expect one of you will have to pay well for them,\r\nbut I had better take a lawyer with me."\r\n\r\nClement named the solicitor who had the charge of the Vanderkist\r\naffairs.\r\n\r\n"Better than Staples, or Bramshaw & Anderson. Yes, it would be best\r\nto have no previous knowledge of the family, and no neighbourly\r\nacquaintance. Moreover, I am not exactly an interested party, so I may\r\nbe better attended to."\r\n\r\n"Still I very much doubt, even if you do get any statement from the\r\nwoman, whether it can be depended upon without verification," said\r\nClement.\r\n\r\n"From the registers, if there are any at these places?"\r\n\r\n"Exactly, and there must be personal inquiry. The first husband, Gian\r\nBenista, will have to be hunted down, dead or alive."\r\n\r\n"Yes; and another thing," said Lance, "if the Italian marriage were\r\nbefore the revolution in Sicily, I expect the ecclesiastical ceremony\r\nwould be valid, but after that, the civil marriage would be required."\r\n\r\n"Oh!" groaned Gerald, "if you would let me throw it all up without these\r\nwretched quibbles."\r\n\r\n"Not your father\'s honour," said his aunt.\r\n\r\n"Nor our honesty," said Clement. "It is galling enough to have your\r\nwhole position in life depend on the word of a worthless woman, but\r\nthere are things that must be taken patiently, as the will of One who\r\nknows."\r\n\r\n"It is so hard to accept it as God\'s will when it comes of human sin,"\r\nsaid Geraldine.\r\n\r\n"Human thoughtlessness," said Clement; "but as long as it is not by our\r\nown fault we can take it as providential, and above all, guard against\r\nimpatience, the real ruin and destruction."\r\n\r\n"Yes," said Lance, "sit on a horse\'s head when he is down to keep him\r\nfrom kicking."\r\n\r\n"So you all are sitting on my head," said Gerald; "I shall get out and\r\nwalk--a good rush on the moors."\r\n\r\n"Wait at least to allow your head to take in my scheme," said Clement.\r\n\r\n"Provided it is not sitting still," said Gerald.\r\n\r\n"Far from it. Only it partly depends on my lady and mistress here--"\r\n\r\n"I guess," said Geraldine. "You know I am disposed that way by Dr.\r\nBrownlow\'s verdict."\r\n\r\n"And \'that way\' is that we go ourselves to try to trace out this strange\r\nallegation--you coming too, Gerald, so that we shall not quite be\r\nsitting on your head."\r\n\r\n"But my sister?"\r\n\r\n"We will see when we have recovered her," said Mrs. Grinstead.\r\n\r\n"I would begin with a visit to Stella and her husband," said\r\nClement; "Charlie could put us in the way of dealing with consuls and\r\nvice-consuls."\r\n\r\n"Excellent," cried his sister; "Anna goes of course, and I should like\r\nto take Francie. It would be such an education for her."\r\n\r\n"Well, why not?"\r\n\r\n"And what is to become of Adrian?"\r\n\r\n"Well, we should not have been here more than six months of course."\r\n\r\n"I could take him," said Lance, "unless Alda holds poor old Froggatt &\r\nUnderwood beneath his dignity."\r\n\r\n"That can be considered," said Clement; "it approves itself best to me,\r\nexcept that he is getting on so well here that I don\'t like to disturb\r\nhim."\r\n\r\n"And when can you come up to town with me?" demanded Gerald; "tomorrow?"\r\n\r\n"To-morrow being Saturday, it would be of little use to go. No, if you\r\nwill not kick, master, I must go home to-morrow, and look up poor \'Pur,\'\r\nalso the organ on Sunday. Come with me, and renew your acquaintance. We\r\nwill make an appointment with your attorney, Clem, and run up on Monday\r\nevening, see him on Tuesday."\r\n\r\nGerald sighed, submitting perforce, and they let him out to exhale as\r\nmuch impatience as he could in a tramp over the hills, while they sat\r\nand pitied him from their very hearts.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXV. -- DESDICHADO\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     \'Perish wealth and power and pride,\r\n        Mortal boons by mortals given;\r\n      But let constancy abide--\r\n        Constancy\'s the gift of Heaven.--SCOTT.\r\n\r\n\r\nLancelot and Gerald did not obtain much by their journey to London.\r\nGerald wanted to begin with Mr. Bast, van proprietor, but Lance insisted\r\non having the lawyer\'s counsel first, and the advice amounted to\r\nexhortations not to commit themselves, or to make offers such as to\r\nexcite cupidity, especially in the matter of Ludmilla, but to dwell\r\non the fact of her being so close to the age of emancipation, and the\r\nillegality of tyrannical training.\r\n\r\nThis, however, proved to be wasted advice. Mr. Bast was impervious. He\r\nundertook to forward a letter to Mr. O\'Leary, but would not tell where,\r\nnor whether wife and daughter were with him. The letter was written, and\r\nin due time was answered, but with an intimation that the information\r\ndesired could only be given upon the terms already mentioned; and\r\nrefusing all transactions respecting the young lady mentioned, who was\r\nwith her natural guardians and in no need of intervention.\r\n\r\nThey were baffled at all points, and the lawyer did not encourage\r\nany idea of holding out a lure for information, which might easily be\r\ntrumped up. Since Lancelot had discovered so much as that the first\r\nmarriage had taken place at Messina, and the desertion at Trieste, as\r\nwell as that the husband was said to have been a native of Piedmont, he\r\nmuch recommended personal investigation at all these points, especially\r\nas Mr. Underwood could obtain the assistance and interest of consuls. It\r\nwas likely that if neither uncle nor nephew made further demonstration,\r\nthe O\'Learys would attempt further communication, which he and Lance\r\ncould follow up. This might be a clue to finding "the young lady"--to\r\nhim a secondary matter, to Gerald a vital one, but for the present\r\nnothing could be done for her, poor child.\r\n\r\nSo they could only return to Rockquay to make immediate preparations for\r\nthe journey. Matters were simplified by Miss Mohun, who, hearing that\r\nClement\'s doctors ordered him abroad for the winter, came to the rescue,\r\nsaying that she should miss Fergus and his lessons greatly, and she\r\nthought it would be a pity for Mrs. Edgar to lose their little baronet,\r\njust after having given offence to certain inhabitants by a modified\r\nexpulsion of Campbell and Horner, and therefore volunteering to take\r\nAdrian for a few terms, look after his health, his morals, and his\r\nlessons, and treat him in fact like a nephew, "to keep her hand in," she\r\nsaid, "till the infants began to appear from India."\r\n\r\nThis was gratefully accepted, and Alda liked the plan better than\r\nplacing him at Bexley, which she continued to regard as an unwholesome\r\nplace. The proposal to take Franceska was likewise welcome, and the\r\ndamsel herself was in transports of delight. Various arrangements had to\r\nbe made, and it was far on in August that the farewells were exchanged\r\nwith Clipstone and Beechcroft Cottage, where each member of the party\r\nfelt that a real friend had been acquired. The elders, ladies who had\r\ngrown up in an enthusiastic age, were even more devoted to one another\r\nthan were Anna and Mysie. Gillian stood a little aloof, resolved against\r\n"foolish" confidences, and devoting herself to studies for college life,\r\nin which she tried to swallow up all the feelings excited by those ship\r\nletters.\r\n\r\nDolores had her secret, which was to be no longer a secret when she\r\nhad heard from her father, and in the meantime, with Gerald\'s full\r\nconcurrence, she was about to work hard to qualify herself for lecturing\r\nor giving lessons on physical science. She could not enter the college\r\nthat she wished for till the winter term, and meant to spend the autumn\r\nin severe study.\r\n\r\n"We will work," was the substance of those last words between them, and\r\ntheir parting tokens were characteristic, each giving the other a little\r\ncase of mathematical instruments, "We will work, and we will hope."\r\n\r\n"And what for?" said Dolores.\r\n\r\n"I should say for toil, if it could be with untarnished name," said\r\nGerald.\r\n\r\n"Name and fame are our own to make," said Dolores, with sparkling eyes.\r\n\r\nThis was their parting. Indeed they expected to meet at Christmas or\r\nbefore it, so soon as Mr. Maurice Mohun should have written. Gerald\r\nwas, by the unanimous wish of his uncles, to finish his terms at Oxford.\r\nWhatever might be his fate, a degree would help him in life.\r\n\r\nHe had accepted the decision, though he had rather have employed the\r\ntime in a restless search for his mother and sister; but after vainly\r\npursuing two or three entertainments at fairs, he became amenable to the\r\nconviction that they were more likely to hear something if they gave up\r\nthe search and kept quiet, and both Dolores and Mrs. Henderson promised\r\nto be on the watch.\r\n\r\nThe state of suspense proved an admirable tonic to the whole being of\r\nthe young man. His listlessness had departed, and he did everything with\r\nan energy he had never shown before. Only nothing would induce him to\r\ngo near Vale Leston, and he made it understood that his twenty-first\r\nbirthday was to be unnoticed. Not a word passed between Gerald and his\r\naunt as to the cause of the journey, and the doubt that hung over him,\r\nbut nothing could be more assiduous and tender than his whole conduct to\r\nher and his uncle throughout the journey, as though he had no object in\r\nlife but to save them trouble and make them comfortable.\r\n\r\nThe party started in August, travelled very slowly, and he was the\r\nkindest squire to the two girls, taking them to see everything, and\r\nbeing altogether, as Geraldine said, the most admirable courier in the\r\nworld, with a wonderful intuition as to what she individually would\r\nlike to see, and how she could see it without fatigue. Moreover, on the\r\nSunday that occurred at a little German town, it was the greatest joy to\r\nher that he sought no outside gaiety, but rather seemed to cling to his\r\nuncle\'s home ministrations, and even to her readings of hymns. They\r\nhad a quiet walk together, and it was a day of peace when his gentle\r\nkindness put her in mind of his father, yet with a regretful depth she\r\nhad always missed in Edgar.\r\n\r\nNor was there any of that old dreary, half-contemptuous tone and manner\r\nwhich had often made her think he was only conforming to please her, and\r\nshrinking from coming to close quarters, where he might confess opinions\r\nthat would grieve her. He was manifestly in earnest, listening and\r\njoining in the services as if they had a new force to him. Perhaps they\r\nhad the more from the very absence of the ordinary externals, and with\r\nnothing to disturb the individual personality of Clement\'s low, earnest,\r\nand reverent tones. There were tears on his eyelashes as he rose up,\r\nbent over, and kissed his Cherie. And that evening, while Clement and\r\nthe two nieces walked farther, and listened to the Benediction in the\r\nlittle Austrian church, Gerald sat under a linden-tree with his aunt,\r\nand in the fullness of his heart told her how things stood between him\r\nand Dolores.\r\n\r\nGeraldine had never been as much attracted by Dolores as by Gillian\r\nand Mysie, but she was greatly touched by hearing that the meeting and\r\nopening of affection had been on the discovery that Gerald was probably\r\nnameless and landless, and that the maiden was bent on casting in her\r\nlot with him whatever his fate might be.\r\n\r\nHe murmured to himself the old lines, with a slight alteration--\r\n\r\n\r\n          "I could not love thee, dear, so much,\r\n             Loved I not justice more."\r\n\r\n\r\n"Yes, indeed, Cherie, our affection is a very different and better\r\nthing than it would be if I were only the rich young squire sure of my\r\nposition."\r\n\r\n"I am sure it is, my dear. I honour and love her for being my boy\'s\r\nbrave comforter--comforter in the true sense. I see now what has helped\r\nyou to be so brave and cheery. But what will her father say?"\r\n\r\n"He will probably be startled, and--and will object, but it would be a\r\nmatter of waiting anyway, the patience that the Vicar preaches, and we\r\nhave made up our minds. I\'ll fight my own way; she to prepare by her\r\nCambridge course to come and work with me, as we can do so much better\r\namong the people--among them in reality, and by no pretence."\r\n\r\n"Ah! don\'t speak as if you gave up your cause."\r\n\r\n"Well, I won\'t, if you don\'t like to hear it, Cherie," he said, smiling;\r\n"but anyway you will be good to Dolores."\r\n\r\n"Indeed I will do my best, my dear. I am sure you and she, whatever\r\nhappens, have the earnest purpose and soul to do all the good you can,\r\nwhether from above or on the same level, and that makes the oneness of\r\nlove."\r\n\r\n"Thank you, Cherie carissima. You see the secret of our true bond."\r\n\r\n"One bond to make it deeper must be there. The love of God beneath the\r\nlove of man."\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVI. -- THE SILENT STAR\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     Then the traveller in the dark\r\n     Thanks you for your tiny spark;\r\n     He would not know which way to go\r\n     If you did not twinkle so.--JANE TAYLOR.\r\n\r\n\r\nAnd so they came to Buda, where Charles Audley represented English\r\ndiplomatic interests on the banks of the Danube. When the quaint old\r\nsemi-oriental-looking city came in sight and the train stopped, the neat\r\nEnglish-looking carriage, with gay Hungarian postillions, could be seen\r\ndrawn up to meet them outside the station.\r\n\r\nCharles and his father, now Sir Robert, were receiving them with\r\noutstretched hands and joyous words, and in a few seconds more they were\r\nwith their little Stella! Yes, their little Stella still, as Clement and\r\nCherry had time to see, when Gerald and the two girls had insisted on\r\nwalking, however far it might be, with the two Audleys, though Charlie\r\ntold them that no one ever walked in Hungary who could help it, and that\r\nhe should be stared at for bringing such strange animals.\r\n\r\nGeraldine had stayed with Stella once before, and Clement had made one\r\nhurried and distressful rush in the trouble about Angela; but that\r\nwas at Munich, and nearly nine years ago, before the many changes and\r\nchances of life had come to them.
