To Stella those years had brought two\r\nlittle boys, whose appearance in the world had been delayed till the\r\nAudley family had begun to get anxious for an heir, but while the\r\nUnderwoods thought it was well that their parents, especially their\r\nfather, should have time to grow a little older.\r\n\r\nAnd Stella looked as daintily, delicately pretty as ever, at first sight\r\nlike a china shepherdess to be put under a glass shade, but on a second\r\nview, with a thoughtful sweetness and depth in her face that made her\r\nnot merely pretty but lovely. How happy she was, gazing at her brother\r\nand sister, and now and then putting a question to bring out the\r\noverflow of home news, so dear to her. For she was still their silent\r\nstar, making very few words evince her intense interest and sympathy.\r\n\r\nEven when they were at home, in the house that looked outside like a\r\ncastle in a romance, but which was so truly English within, and the two\r\nlittle fellows of four and three came toddling to meet her, shrinking\r\ninto her skirts at sight of the new uncle and aunt, there was a quiet\r\ngentle firmness--all the old Stella--in her dealings with them, as she\r\ndrew them to kiss and greet the strangers. Robbie and Theodore were\r\nsturdy, rosy beings, full of life, but perfectly amenable to that\r\nsweet low voice. Their father and grandfather might romp with them to\r\nscreaming pitch, and idolize them almost to spoiling, yet they too were\r\nunder that gentle check which the young wife exercised on all around.\r\n\r\nShe was only thirty-one, and so small, so fair and young in looks, that\r\nto her elder sister her pretty matronly rule _would_ at first seem like\r\nthe management of a dolls\' house, even though her servants, English,\r\nGerman, or Magyar, obeyed her implicitly; and for that matter, as\r\nCharlie and Sir Robert freely and merrily avowed, so did they. The young\r\nsecretary was her bounden slave, and held her as the ideal woman, though\r\nthere came to be a little swerving of his allegiance towards the tall\r\nand beautiful Franceska, who had insensibly improved greatly in grace\r\nand readiness on her travels, and quite dazzled the Hungarians; while\r\nAnna was immensely exultant, and used to come to her aunt\'s room every\r\nnight to talk of her lovely Francie as a safety-valve from discussing\r\nthe matter with Francie herself, who remained perfectly simple and\r\nunconscious of her own charms. Geraldine could not think them quite\r\nequal to the more exquisite and delicately-finished, as well as more\r\nmatured, beauty of little Stella, but that was a matter of taste.\r\n\r\nThe household was more English than Hungarian, or even German, and there\r\nwere curious similitudes to the Vale Leston Priory arrangements, which\r\nkept Stella\'s Underwood heart in mind. There had to be receptions, and\r\nit was plain that when she put Fernan\'s diamonds on, Mrs. Audley was\r\nquite at home and at perfect ease in German and Hungarian society,\r\nspeaking the languages without hesitation when she _did_ speak, while in\r\nher quiet way keeping every one entertained, showing the art de tenir un\r\nsalon, and moreover, preserving Francie from obtrusive admiration in a\r\nway perhaps learnt by experience on that more perilous subject, Angela,\r\nwho had invited what Francie shrank from. The two girls were supremely\r\nhappy, and Francie seemed to have a fountain of joy that diffused a\r\nrose-coloured spray over everything.\r\n\r\nOne of the famous concerts of Hungarian gipsies was given, and in that\r\nClement and Geraldine were alike startled by tones recalling those of\r\nthe memorable concert at Bexley, all the more because they seemed to\r\nhave a curious fascination for Gerald. Moreover, those peculiar eyes and\r\neyelashes, the first link observed between him and the Little Butterfly,\r\nwere so often repeated in the gipsy band that it was plain whence they\r\nwere derived. Charles Audley thought it worth while to find means of\r\ninquiry among the gipsies as to whether anything was known of Zoraya\r\nPrebel or her brother Sebastian; but after some delay and various\r\nexcitements nothing was discovered, but that there had been a family,\r\nwho were esteemed recreants to their race, and had sold their children\r\nto the managers of German or Italian bands of musicians. One brother had\r\ncome back a broken man, who had learnt vices and ruined himself, though\r\nhe talked largely of his wonderful success in company with his sister,\r\nwho had made grand marriages. What had become of her he did not know;\r\nand when Gerald went with Mr. Audley to a little mountain valley to\r\nvisit him, he had been dead for a week or more.\r\n\r\nAll this had made some delay, and it was almost the end of the long\r\nvacation. Charles Audley undertook to go to Trieste with the travellers,\r\nand make inquiries about Zoraya and her first husband. Sir Robert, the\r\nSkipper, as the family still termed him, had written for his yacht to\r\nmeet him there, and be ready for him to convey the party to Sicily.\r\nHe professed that he could not lose sight of Franceska, with whom he\r\ndeclared himself nearly as much smitten as ever he had been with his\r\ndaughter-in-law.\r\n\r\nThey left that pretty creature in her happy home, and arrived at\r\nTrieste, where Charles Audley set various agencies to work, and arrived\r\nat a remembrance of Giovanni Benista, an impresario, having been in a\r\nstate of great fury at his wife, his most able performer, having fled\r\nfrom him just as he had been at the expense of training and making her\r\nvaluable. He tried to have her pursued, but there was reason to think\r\nthat she had been smuggled away in an English or American ship, and\r\nnothing could be done.\r\n\r\nThus much of the story then was confirmed, and Gerald had little or no\r\ndoubt of the rest of it, but he was obliged to leave the pursuit of\r\nthe quest to his uncle and aunt, being somewhat consoled for having to\r\nreturn to England by the expectation of hearing from Mr. Maurice Mohun.\r\n\r\nTwice he returned for his aunt\'s last kiss, nay, even a third time, and\r\nthen with the half-choked words, "My true, my dearest mother!"\r\n\r\nAnd he absolutely bent his knee as he asked for his uncle Clement\'s\r\nblessing.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVII. -- THE RED MANTLE\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     And deemed themselves a shameful part\r\n     Of pageant which they cursed in heart.--SCOTT.\r\n\r\n\r\nDolores was waiting till the Christmas term to go to her college.\r\nThe fame of her volcanic lectures had reached Avoncester, and she was\r\nentreated to repeat them at the High School there. The Mouse-trap had\r\nnaturally been sent to Miss Vincent, the former governess, who had\r\nbecome head-mistress of the High School at Silverton, and she wrote an\r\nurgent request that her pupils might have the advantage of the lectures.\r\nWould Dolores come and give her course there, and stay a few days with\r\nher, reviving old times?\r\n\r\nDolores consented, being always glad of an opportunity of trying her\r\nwings, though she had not the pleasantest recollections connected with\r\nSilverton, but she would be really glad to see Miss Vincent, who had\r\nbeen always kind to her. So she travelled up to Silverton, and found the\r\nhead-mistress living in cheerful rooms, with another of the teachers in\r\nthe same house, all boarding together, but with separate sitting-rooms.\r\n\r\nDolores\' first walk was to see Miss Hackett. It was quite startling to\r\nfind the good old lady looking exactly the same as when she had come to\r\nluncheon at Silverfold, and arranged for G. F. S., and weakly stood up\r\nfor her sister nine years previously, those years which seemed ages long\r\nago to the maiden who had made the round of the world since, while the\r\nlady had only lived in her Casement Cottage, and done almost the same\r\nthings day by day.\r\n\r\nThere was one exception, however, Constance had married a union\r\ndoctor in the neighbourhood. She came into Silverton to see her old\r\nacquaintance, and looked older and more commonplace than Dolores could\r\nhave thought possible, and her talk was no longer of books and romances,\r\nbut of smoking chimneys, cross landlords, and troublesome cooks, and the\r\nwicked neglects of her vicar\'s and her squire\'s wife. As Dolores walked\r\nback to Silverton, she heard drums and trumpets, and was nearly swept\r\naway by a rushing stream of little boys and girls. Then came before her\r\nan elephant, with ornamental housing and howdah, and a train of cars,\r\nmeant to be very fine, but way-worn and battered, with white and piebald\r\nsteeds, and gaudy tinselly drivers, and dames in scarlet and blue,\r\nmuch needing a washing, distributing coloured sheets about the grand\r\nperformance to take place that night at eight o\'clock, of the Sepoy\'s\r\nDeath Song and the Bleeding Bride.\r\n\r\nMiss Vincent had asked Miss Hackett to supper, and prepared herself and\r\nher fellow-teacher, Miss Calton, for a pleasant evening of talk, but\r\nto her great surprise, Dolores expressed her intention of going to the\r\nperformance at the circus.\r\n\r\n"My dear," said Miss Vincent, "this is a very low affair--not Sanger\'s,\r\nnor anything so respectable. They have been here before, and the\r\nlodging-house people went and were quite shocked."\r\n\r\n"Yes," said Dolores, "but that is all the more reason I want to go.\r\nThere is a girl with them in whom we are very much interested. She was\r\nkidnapped from Rockquay at the time this circus was there. At least I am\r\nalmost sure it is the same, and I must see if she is there."\r\n\r\n"But if she is you cannot do anything."\r\n\r\n"Yes, I can; I can let her brother know. It must be done, Miss Vincent.\r\nI have promised, and it is of fearful consequence."\r\n\r\n"Should you know her?"\r\n\r\n"Oh yes. I have often talked to her in Mrs. Henderson\'s class. I could\r\nnot mistake her."\r\n\r\nMiss Hackett was so much horrified at the notion of a G. F. S. "business\r\ngirl" being in bondage to a circus, that she gallantly volunteered to go\r\nwith Miss Mohun, and Miss Vincent could only consent.\r\n\r\nThe place of the circus was an open piece of ground lying between\r\nSilverton and Silverfold, and thither they betook themselves--Miss\r\nHackett in an old bonnet and waterproof that might have belonged to\r\nany woman, and Dolores wearing a certain crimson ulster, which she had\r\nbought in Auckland for her homeward voyage, and which her cousins had\r\nchosen to dub as "the Maori." After a good deal of jostling and much\r\nscent of beer and bad tobacco they achieved an entrance, and sat upon a\r\nhard bench, half stifled with the odours, to which were added those of\r\nhuman and equine nature and of paraffin. As to the performance, Dolores\r\nwas too much absorbed in looking out for Ludmilla, together with the\r\nfear that Miss Hackett might either faint or grow desperate, and come\r\naway, to attend much to it; and she only was aware that there was a\r\ngeneral scurrying, in which the horses and the elephant took their part;\r\nand that men and scantily dressed females put themselves in unnatural\r\npositions; that there was a firing of pistols and singing of vulgar\r\nsongs, and finally the hero and heroine made their bows on the\r\nelephant\'s back.\r\n\r\nMiss Hackett wanted to depart before the Bleeding Bride came on, but\r\nDolores entreated her to stay, and she heroically endured a little\r\nlonger. This seemed, consciously or not, to be a parody of the ballad of\r\nLord Thomas and Fair Annet, but of course it began with an abduction on\r\nhorseback and a wild chase, in which even the elephant did his part,\r\nand plenty more firing. Then the future bride came on, supposed to\r\nbe hawking, during which pastime she sang a song standing upright on\r\nhorseback, and the faithless Lord Thomas appeared and courted her with\r\nthe most remarkable antics of himself and his piebald steed.\r\n\r\nThe forsaken Annet consoled herself with careering about, taking a last\r\nleave of her beloved steed--a mangy-looking pony--and performing various\r\nfreaks with it, then singing a truculent song of revenge, in pursuance\r\nof which she hid herself to await the bridal procession. And as the\r\nbride came on, among her attendants Dolores detected unmistakably those\r\neyes of Gerald\'s! She squeezed Miss Hackett\'s hand, and saw little\r\nmore of the final catastrophe. Somehow the bride was stabbed, and fell\r\nscreaming, while the fair Annet executed a war dance, but what became\r\nof her was uncertain. All Dolores knew was, that Ludmilla was there! She\r\nhad recognized not only the eyes, but the air and figure.\r\n\r\nWhen they got free of the crowd, which was a great distress to poor Miss\r\nHackett, Dolores said--\r\n\r\n"Yes, it is that poor girl! She must be saved!"\r\n\r\n"How? What can you do?"\r\n\r\n"I shall telegraph to her brother. You will help me, Miss Hackett?"\r\n\r\n"But--what--who is her brother?" said Miss Hackett, expecting to hear he\r\nwas a carpenter perhaps, or at least a clerk.\r\n\r\n"Mr. Underwood of Vale Leston--Gerald Underwood," answered Dolores. "His\r\nfather made an unfortunate marriage with a singer. She really is his\r\nhalf-sister, and I promised to do all I could to help him to find her\r\nand save her. He is at Oxford. I shall telegraph to him the first thing\r\nto-morrow."\r\n\r\nThere was nothing in this to object to, and Miss Hackett would not be\r\npersuaded not to see her to the door of Miss Vincent\'s lodgings,\r\nthough lengthening her own walk--alone, a thing more terrible to her\r\nold-fashioned mind than to that of her companion.\r\n\r\nDolores wrote her telegram--\r\n\r\n"Dolores Mohun, Valentia, Silverton, to Gerald Underwood, Trinity\r\nCollege, Oxford. Ludmilla here. Circus. Come."\r\n\r\nShe sent it with the more confidence that she had received a letter\r\nfrom her father with a sort of conditional consent to her engagement to\r\nGerald, so that she could, if needful, avow herself betrothed to him;\r\nthough her usual reticence made her unwilling to put the matter forward\r\nin the present condition of affairs. She went out to the post-office\r\nat the first moment when she could hope to find the telegraph office\r\nat work, and just as she had turned from it, she met a girl in a dark,\r\nlong, ill-fitting jacket and black hat, with a basket in her hand.\r\n\r\n"Lydia!" exclaimed Dolores, using the old Rockquay name.\r\n\r\n"Miss Dolores!" she cried.\r\n\r\n"Yes, yes. You are here! I saw you last night."\r\n\r\n"Me! Me! Oh, I am ashamed that you did. Don\'t tell Mr. Flight."\r\n\r\nThere were tears starting to her eyes.\r\n\r\n"Can I do anything for you?"\r\n\r\n"No--no. Oh, if you could! But they have apprenticed me."\r\n\r\n"Who have?"\r\n\r\n"My mother and Mr. O\'Leary."\r\n\r\n"Are they here?"\r\n\r\n"Yes. They wanted money--apprenticed me to this Jellicoe! I must make\r\nhaste. They sent me out to take something to the wash, and buy some\r\nfresh butter. They must not guess that I have met any one."\r\n\r\n"I will walk with you. I have been telegraphing to your brother that I\r\nhave found you."\r\n\r\n"Oh, he was so good to me! And Mr. Flight, I was so grieved to fail him.\r\nThey made me get up and dress in the night, and before I knew what I\r\nwas about I was on the quay--carried out to the ship. I had no paper--no\r\nmeans of writing; I was watched. And now it is too dreadful! Oh, Miss\r\nDolores! if Mrs. Henderson could see the cruel positions they try to\r\nforce on me, the ways they handle me--they hurt so; and what is worse,\r\nno modest girl could bear the way they go on, and want me to do the\r\nsame. I could when I was little, but I am stiffer now, and oh! ashamed.\r\nIf I can\'t--they starve me--yes, and beat me, and hurt me with their\r\nthings. It is bondage like the Israelites, and I don\'t want to get to\r\nlike it, as they say I shall, for then--then there are those terrible\r\nsongs to be sung, and that shocking dress to be shown off in. My mother\r\nwill not help. She says it is what she went through, and all have to\r\ndo, and that I shall soon leave off minding; but oh, I often think I\r\nhad rather die than grow like--like Miss Bellamour. I hope I shall (they\r\noften frighten me with that horse), only somehow I can\'t wish to be\r\nkilled at the moment, and try to save myself. And once I thought I would\r\nlet myself fall, rather than go on with it, but I thought it would be\r\nwicked, and I couldn\'t. But I have prayed to God to help me and spare\r\nme; and now He has heard. And will my brother be able--or will he choose\r\nto help me?"\r\n\r\n"I am sure of it, my poor dear girl. He wishes nothing more."\r\n\r\n"Please turn this way. They must not see me speak to any one."\r\n\r\n"One word more. How long is the circus to be here?"\r\n\r\n"We never know; it depends on the receipts--may go to-morrow. Oh,\r\nthere--"\r\n\r\nShe hurried on without another word, and Dolores slowly returned to Miss\r\nVincent\'s lodgings. Her lecture was to be given at three o\'clock, but\r\nshe knew that she should have to be shown the school and class-rooms in\r\nthe forenoon. Gerald, as she calculated the trains, might arrive either\r\nby half-past twelve or a quarter past four.\r\n\r\nNervously she endured her survey of the school, replying to the comments\r\nas if in a dream, and hurrying it over, so as must have vexed those\r\nwho expected her to be interested. She dashed off to the station, and\r\nreached it just in time to see the train come in. Was it--yes, it was\r\nGerald who sprang out and came towards her.\r\n\r\n"Dolores! My gallant Dolores! You have found her!"\r\n\r\n"Yes, but in cruel slavery--apprenticed."\r\n\r\n"That can be upset. Her mother--is she here?"\r\n\r\n"Yes, and O\'Leary. They sold her, apprenticed her, and these people use\r\nher brutally. She told me this morning. No, I don\'t think you can get at\r\nher now."\r\n\r\n"I will see her mother at any rate. I may be able to buy her off. Where\r\nshall I find you?"\r\n\r\nDolores told him, but advised him to meet her at Miss Hackett\'s, whom\r\nshe thought more able to help, and more willing than Miss Vincent, in\r\ncase he was able to bring Ludmilla away with him.\r\n\r\n"Have you heard from my father?"\r\n\r\n"Yes--what I expected."\r\n\r\n"But it will make no difference in the long run."\r\n\r\n"Dearest, do I not trust your brave words? From Trieste I hear that the\r\nendeavour of Benista to recover his wife is proved. There\'s one step of\r\nthe chain. Is it dragging us down, or setting us free?"\r\n\r\n"Free--free from the perplexities of property," cried Dolores. "Free to\r\ncarve out a life."\r\n\r\n"Certainly I have wished I was a younger son. Only if it could have come\r\nin some other way!"\r\n\r\nDolores had to go to luncheon at Miss Vincent\'s, and then to deliver her\r\nlecture. It was well that she had given it so often as almost to know it\r\nby heart, for the volcano of anxiety was surging high within her.\r\n\r\nAs she went out she saw Gerald waiting for her, and his whole mien spoke\r\nof failure.\r\n\r\n"Failed! Yes," he said. "The poor child is regularly bound to that\r\nJellicoe, the master of the concern, for twenty-five pounds, the fine\r\nthat my uncle brought on the mother, as O\'Leary said with a grin, and\r\nshe is still under sixteen."\r\n\r\n"Is there no hope till then?"\r\n\r\n"He and O\'Leary declare there would be breach of contract if she left\r\nthem even then. I don\'t know whether they are right, but any amount of\r\nmischief might be done before her birthday. They talk of sending her to\r\nBelgium to be trained, and that is fatal."\r\n\r\n"Can\'t she be bought off?"\r\n\r\n"Of course I tried, but I can\'t raise more than seventy pounds at the\r\nutmost just now."\r\n\r\n"I could help. I have twenty-three pounds. I could give up my term."\r\n\r\n"No use. They know that I shall not be of age till January, besides\r\nthe other matter. I assured them that however that might end, my uncles\r\nwould honour any order I might give for the sake of rescuing her, but\r\nthey laughed the idea to scorn. O\'Leary had the impudence to intimate,\r\nhowever, that if I chose to accept the terms expressed, \'his wife might\r\nbe amenable.\'"\r\n\r\n"They are?"\r\n\r\n"Five hundred for evidence on the previous marriage in my favour; but\r\nI am past believing a word that she says, at least under O\'Leary\'s\r\ndictation. She might produce a forgery. So I told him that my uncle was\r\ninvestigating the matter with the consul in Sicily; and the intolerable\r\nbrutes sneered more than over at the idea of the question being in\r\nthe hands of the interested party, when they could upset that meddling\r\nparson in a moment."\r\n\r\n"Can nothing be done?"\r\n\r\n"I thought of asking one of your old ladies whether there is a lawyer or\r\nPrevention of Cruelty man who could tell me whether the agreement holds,\r\nbut I am afraid she is too old. You saw no mark of ill-usage?"\r\n\r\n"Oh no. They would be too cunning."\r\n\r\n"If we could help her to escape what a lark it would be!"\r\n\r\n"I do believe we could" cried Dolores. "If I could only get a note to\r\nher! And this red ulster! I wonder if Miss Hackett would help!"\r\n\r\nDolores waited for Miss Hackett, who had lingered behind, and told her\r\nas much of the facts as was expedient. There was a spice of romance in\r\nthe Hackett soul, and the idea of a poor girl, a G. F. S. maiden, in the\r\nhands of these cruel and unscrupulous people was so dreadful that she\r\nwas actually persuaded to bethink herself of means of assistance.\r\n\r\n"Where did you meet the girl?" she said. Dolores told her the street.\r\n\r\n"Ah! depend upon it the things were with Mrs. Crachett, who I know\r\nhas done washing for people about on fair-days, when they can\'t do it\r\nthemselves. She has a daughter in my G. F. S. class; I wonder if we\r\ncould get any help from her."\r\n\r\nIt was a very odd device for a respectable associate and member of G. F.\r\nS. to undertake, but if ever the end might justify the means it was on\r\nthe present occasion. Fortune favoured them, for Melinda Crachett was\r\nalone in the house, ironing out some pale pink garments.\r\n\r\n"Are you washing for those people on the common, Melinda?" asked Miss\r\nHackett.\r\n\r\n"Yes, Miss Hackett. They want them by seven o\'clock to-night very\r\nparticular, and they promised me a seat to see the performance, miss, if\r\nI brought them in good time, and I wondered, miss, if you would object."\r\n\r\n"Only tell me, Melinda, whom you saw."\r\n\r\n"I saw the lady herself, ma\'am, the old lady, when I took the things."\r\n\r\n"No young person?"\r\n\r\n"Yes, ma\'am. It was a very nice young lady indeed that brought me down\r\nthis pink tunic, because it got stained last night, and she said her\r\norders was to promise me a ticket if it came in time; but, oh my! ma\'am,\r\nshe looked as if she wanted to tell me not to come."\r\n\r\n"Poor girl! She is a G. F. S. member, Melinda, and I do believe you\r\nwould be doing a very good deed if you could help us to get her away\r\nfrom those people."\r\n\r\nMelinda\'s eyes grew round with eagerness. She had no doubts respecting\r\nwhat Miss Hackett advised her to do, and there was nothing for it but\r\nto take the risk. Then and there Dolores sat down and pencilled a note,\r\ndirecting Ludmilla to put on the red ulster after her performance, if\r\npossible, when people were going away, and slip out among them, joining\r\nMelinda, who would convey her to Miss Hackett\'s. This was safer than for\r\nGerald to be nearer, since he was liable to be recognized. Still it was\r\na desperate risk, and Dolores had great doubts whether she should ever\r\nsee her red Maori again.\r\n\r\nSo in intense anxiety the two waited in Miss Hackett\'s parlour, where\r\nthe good lady left them, as she said, to attend to her accounts, but\r\nreally with an inkling or more of the state of affairs between them.\r\nEach had heard from New Zealand, and knew that Maurice Mohun was\r\nsuspending his consent till he had heard farther from home, both as\r\nto Gerald\'s character and prospects, and there was no such absolute\r\nrefusal, even in view of his overthrow of the young man\'s position, as\r\nto make it incumbent on them to break off intercourse. Colonial habits\r\nmodified opinion, and to know that the loss was neither the youth\'s own\r\nfault nor that of his father, would make the acceptance a question of\r\nonly prudence, provided his personal character were satisfactory. Thus\r\nthey felt free to hold themselves engaged, though Gerald had further to\r\ntell that his letters from Messina purported that an old priest had been\r\ntraced out who had married the impresario, Giovanni Benista, a native\r\nof Piedmont, to Zoraya Prebel, Hungarian, in the year 1859, when\r\necclesiastical marriages were still valid without the civil ceremony.\r\n\r\n"Another step in my descent," said Gerald. "Still, it does not prove\r\nwhether this first husband was alive. No; and Piedmont, though a small\r\ncountry, is a wide field in which to seek one who may have cut all\r\nconnection with it. However, these undaunted people of mine are resolved\r\nto pursue their quest, and, as perhaps you have heard, are invited to\r\nstay at Rocca Marina for the purpose."\r\n\r\n"I should think that was a good measure; Mr. White gets quarry-men\r\nfrom all the country round, and would be able to find out about the\r\nvillages."\r\n\r\n"But how unlikely it is that one of these wanderers would have kept\r\nup intercourse with his family! They may do their best to satisfy the\r\ngeneral conscience, but I see no end to it."\r\n\r\n"And a more immediate question--what are we to do with your sister if\r\nshe escapes to-night? Shall I take her to Mrs. Henderson?"\r\n\r\n"She would not be safe there. No, I must carry her straight to America,\r\nthe only way to choke off pursuit."\r\n\r\n"You! Your term!"\r\n\r\n"Never mind that. I shall write to the Warden pleading urgent private\r\nbusiness. I have enough in hand for our passage, and the \'Censor\' will\r\ntake my articles and give me an introduction. I shall be able to keep\r\nmyself and her. I have a real longing to see Fiddler\'s Ranch."\r\n\r\n"But can you rough it?" asked Dolores, anxiously looking at his delicate\r\ngirlish complexion and slight figure.\r\n\r\n"Oh yes! I was born to it. I know what it was when Fiddler\'s Ranch was\r\nfar from the civilization of Violinia, as they call it now. I don\'t mean\r\nto make a secret of it, and grieve your heart or Cherie\'s. She has had\r\nenough of that, but I must make the plunge to save my sister, and if\r\nthings come round it will be all the better to have some practical\r\nknowledge of the masses and the social problems by living among them."\r\n\r\n"Oh that I could make the experiment with you!"\r\n\r\n"You will be my inspiration and encouragement, and come to me in due\r\ntime."\r\n\r\nHe came round to her, and she let him give her his first kiss.\r\n\r\n"God will help us," she said reverently; "it is the cause of uprightness\r\nand deliverance from cruel bondage."\r\n\r\nThe plans had been settled; Gerald had arranged with a cab which was\r\nto take him and his sister to a house five miles out in the country, of\r\nwhich Miss Hackett had given the name, so that they might seem to have\r\nbeen spending the evening with her. Thence it was but a step to the\r\nstation of a different railway from that which went through Silverton,\r\nand they would go by the mail train to London, where Ludmilla could\r\nbe deposited at Mrs. Grinstead\'s house at Brompton, where Martha could\r\nprovide her with an outfit, while Gerald saw the editor of the \'Censor\',\r\ngot some money from the bank, telegraphed to Oxford for his baggage,\r\nand made ready to start the next morning for Liverpool, whither he had\r\ntelegraphed to secure a second-class passage to New York for G. F. Wood\r\nand Lydia Wood, the names which he meant to be called by.\r\n\r\n"The first name I knew," he said, "the name of Tom Wood, is far more\r\nreal to me or my father than Edgar Underwood ever could be."\r\n\r\nHe promised that Dolores should have a telegram at Clipstone by the time\r\nshe reached it, for she had to give her second lecture the next day,\r\nand was to return afterwards. All this had been discussed over and over\r\nagain, and there had been many quakings and declarations that the scheme\r\nhad failed, and that neither girl could have had courage, nor perhaps\r\nadroitness, and that the poor prisoner had been re-captured. Gerald had\r\nmade more than one expedition into the little garden to listen, and\r\nhad filled the house with cold air before he returned, sat down in a\r\nresigned fashion, and declared--\r\n\r\n"It is all up! That comes of trusting to fools of girls."\r\n\r\n"Hark!"\r\n\r\nHe sprang up and out into the vestibule. Miss Hackett opened the\r\ndoor into the back passage. There stood the "red mantle" and Melinda\r\nCrachett. Gerald took the trembling figure in his arms with a brotherly\r\nkiss.\r\n\r\n"My little sister," he said, "look to me," then gave her to Dolores, who\r\nled her into the drawing-room, and put her into an arm-chair.\r\n\r\nShe could hardly stand, but tried to jump up as Miss Hackett entered.\r\n\r\n"No, no, my poor child," she said, "sit still! Rest. Were you followed?"\r\n\r\n"No; I don\'t think they had missed me."\r\n\r\nShe was so breathless that Miss Hackett would have given her a glass of\r\nwine, but she shook her head,\r\n\r\n"Oh no, thank you! I\'ve kept the pledge."\r\n\r\nThe tea-things were there, waiting for her arrival. Dolores would have\r\nhelped her take off the red garment, but she shrank from it. She had\r\nonly her gaudy theatrical dress beneath. How was she to go to London\r\nin it? However, Miss Hackett devised that she should borrow the little\r\nmaid-servant\'s clothes, and Gerald undertook to send them back when\r\nMartha should have fitted her out at Brompton. The theatrical costume\r\nMiss Hackett would return by a messenger without implicating Melinda\r\nCrachett. They took the girl up-stairs to effect the change, and restore\r\nher as much as they could, and she came down with her rouge washed off,\r\nand very pale, but looking like herself, as, poor thing, she always\r\ndid look more or less frightened, and now with tears about her eyelids,\r\ntears that broke forth as Gerald went up to her, took her by the hand,\r\nand said--\r\n\r\n"Brighten up, little sister; you have given yourself to me, and I must\r\ntake care of you now."\r\n\r\n"Ah, I do beg your pardon, but my poor mother--I didn\'t know--"\r\n\r\n"You don\'t want to go back?"\r\n\r\n"Oh no, no," and she shuddered again; "but I am sorry for her. She has\r\nsuch a hard master, and she used to be good to me."\r\n\r\nMiss Hackett had come opportunely to make her drink some tea, and then\r\nmade both take food enough to sustain them through the night journey.\r\nThen, and afterwards, they gathered what had been Ludmilla\'s sad little\r\nstory. Her father, in spite of his marriage, which was according to the\r\nlax notions of German Protestants, had been a fairly respectable man,\r\nvery fond of his little daughter, and exceedingly careful of her, though\r\neven as a tiny child he had made her useful, trained her to singing and\r\ndancing, and brought her forward as a charming little fairy, when it was\r\nall play to her.\r\n\r\n"Oh, we were so happy in those days," she said tearfully.\r\n\r\nWhen he died it was with an injunction to his wife not to bring up\r\nLudmilla to the stage now that he was not there to take care of her.\r\nWith the means he had left she had set up her shop at Rockquay, and\r\nthough she had never been an affectionate mother, Ludmilla had been\r\nfairly happy, and had been a favourite with Mr. Flight and the school\r\nauthorities, and had been thoroughly imbued with their spirit. A\r\nchange had, however, come over her mother ever since an expedition to\r\nAvoncester, when she had met O\'Leary. She had probably always contrived\r\na certain amount of illicit trade in tobacco and spirits by means of\r\nthe sailors in the foreign traders who put into the little harbour of\r\nRockquay; but her daughter was scarcely cognizant of this, and would not\r\nhave understood the evil if she had done so, nor did it affect her life.\r\nO\'Leary had, however, been the clown in Mr. Schnetterling\'s troupe, and\r\nhad become partner with Jellicoe. The sight of him revived all Zoraya\'s\r\nBohemian inclinations, and on his side he knew her to have still great\r\ncapabilities, and recollected enough of her little daughter to be sure\r\nthat she would be a valuable possession. Moreover, Mrs. Schnetterling\r\nhad carried her contraband traffic a little too far, especially where\r\nthe boys of the preparatory school were concerned. She began to fear the\r\ngauger and the policeman, and she had consented to marry O\'Leary at the\r\nAvoncester register office, meaning to keep the matter a secret until\r\nshe could wind up her affairs at Rockquay. Even her daughter was kept in\r\nignorance.\r\n\r\nTwo occurrences had, however, precipitated matters. One was the stir\r\nthat Clement had made about the school-boys\' festival, ending in\r\nthe fine being imposed; the other, the discovery that the graceful,\r\nwell-endowed young esquire was the child who had been left to probable\r\nbeggary with a dying father twenty years previously.\r\n\r\nJellicoe, the principal owner of the circus, advanced the money for the\r\nfine, on condition of the girl and her mother becoming attached to the\r\ncircus; and the object of O\'Leary was to make as much profit as possible\r\nout of the mystery that hung over the young heir of Vale Leston. His\r\nrefusal to attend to the claim on him, together with spite at his\r\nuncle, as having brought about the prosecution, and to Mr. Flight for\r\nhesitating to remunerate the girl for the performance that was to have\r\nbeen free; perhaps too certain debts and difficulties, all conspired to\r\noccasion the midnight flitting in such a manner as to prevent the circus\r\nfrom being pursued.\r\n\r\nThenceforth poor Lida\'s life had been hopeless misery, with all her\r\nwomanly and religious instincts outraged, and the probability of worse\r\nin future. Jellicoe, his wife, and O\'Leary had no pity, and her mother\r\nvery little, and no principle; and she had no hope, except that release\r\nmight come by some crippling accident. Workhouse or hospital would be\r\ndeliverance, since thence she could write to Mrs. Henderson.\r\n\r\nShe shook and trembled still lest she should be pursued, though Miss\r\nHackett assured her that this was the last place to be suspected, and it\r\nwas not easy to make her eat. Presently Gerald stood ready to take her\r\nto the cab.\r\n\r\nDolores came to the gate with them. There was only space for a fervent\r\nembrace and "God bless you!" and then she stood watching as they went\r\naway into the night.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVIII. -- ROCCA MARINA\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     There was of course in Adeline\r\n       A calm patrician polish in the address,\r\n     Which ne\'er can pass the equinoctial line\r\n       Of anything which nature could express.--BYRON.\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was a late autumn or winter day, according to the calendar, when The\r\nMorning Star steamed up to the quay of Rocca Marina, but it was hard to\r\nbelieve it, for all the slope of one of the Maritime Alps lay stretched\r\nout basking in the noonday sunshine, green and lovely, wherever not\r\nbroken by the houses below, or the rocks quarried out on the mountain\r\nside. Some snow lay on the further heights, enough to mark their forms,\r\nand contrast with the soft sweetness of the lap of the hills and the\r\nglorious Mediterranean blue.\r\n\r\nAnna and Franceska stood watching and exclaiming in a trance of delight,\r\nas one beauty after another revealed itself--the castellated remnant of\r\nthe old tower, the gabled house with stone balconies and terraces, with\r\nparapets and vases below, the little white spire of the church tower of\r\nthe English colony, looking out of the chestnut and olive groves above,\r\nand the three noble stone pines that sheltered the approach.\r\n\r\nMr. White, in his launch, came out with exulting and hearty welcome to\r\nbring them ashore, through the crowd of feluccas, fishing-vessels, and\r\none or two steamers that filled the tiny bay, and on landing, the party\r\nfound an English wagonette drawn by four stout mules waiting to receive\r\nthem--mules, as being better for the heights than horses.\r\n\r\nAnna and Franceska insisted on walking with Mr. White and Sir Robert,\r\nand they fairly frisked in the delicious air of sea and mountain after\r\nbeing so long cramped on board ship, stopping continually with screams\r\nof delight over violets or anemones, or the views that unfolded\r\nthemselves as they went higher and higher. The path Mr. White chose\r\nwas a good deal steeper than the winding carriage road cut out of the\r\nmountain side, and they arrived before the mules with Mrs. Grinstead and\r\nher brother, at the Italian garden, with a succession of broad terraces\r\nprotected and adorned with open balustrades, with vases of late blooming\r\nflowers at intervals, and broad stone steps, guarded by carved figures,\r\nleading from one to another.\r\n\r\n"It is like Beauty\'s palace," sighed out in delight Francie to her\r\nsister.\r\n\r\n"There\'s Beauty," laughed Anna, as at the open window upon the highest\r\nverandah-shaded balcony appeared the darkly handsome Maura and Mrs.\r\nWhite, her small features as pretty as ever, but her figure a good deal\r\nmore embonpoint than in Rockquay times.\r\n\r\nHers was a very warm welcome to the two sisters and their friend, and\r\nto the others who reached the front door a few minutes later. Such an\r\narrival was very pleasant to her, for it must be confessed that, save\r\nfor the English visitors, who were always gladly received, the life at\r\nRocca Marina was a dull one, in spite of its being near enough to San\r\nRemo by the railway for expeditions for a day.\r\n\r\nWithin, the dwelling was a combination of the old Italian palace with\r\nEnglish comforts. Mr. White, in his joy at possessing his graceful\r\nlady wife, had spared no expense in making it a meet bower for her, and\r\nGeraldine was as much amused as fascinated by the exquisiteness of all\r\naround her; as she sat, in a most luxurious chair, looking out through\r\nthe open window at the blue sea, yet with a lively wood fire burning\r\nunder a beauteous mantelpiece; statues, pictures, all that was recherche\r\naround, while they drank their English tea out of almost transparently\r\ndelicate cups, filled by Maura out of a beautifully chased service of\r\nplate on a marble mosaic table.\r\n\r\n"And now you must let me show you your rooms," said Mrs. White. "I\r\nthought you would like to have them en suite, for I am such a poor\r\ncreature that I cannot breakfast down-stairs, and Mr. White is obliged\r\nto be out early."\r\n\r\nSo she led the way through a marble hall, pillared in different colours,\r\nrich and rare, with portraits of ancient Contes and Contessas on the\r\nwalls, up a magnificent stone stair with a carved balustrade, to a\r\nsuite indeed, where, at the entrance, Sibby was found very happy at her\r\nwelcome from Mrs. Mount, who was equally glad to receive a countrywoman.\r\n\r\nThere was a sitting-room with a balcony looking out on the bay, a study\r\nand bedroom beyond for Clement on one side, and on the other charmingly\r\nfitted rooms for Geraldine, for her nieces, and her maid; and Mrs. White\r\nleft them, telling them the dinner hour, and begging them to call freely\r\nand without scruple for all and everything they could wish for. Nothing\r\nwould be any trouble.\r\n\r\n"We have even an English doctor below there," she said, pointing to\r\nthe roofs of the village. "There are so many accidents that Mr. White\r\nthought it better to be provided, so we have a little hospital with a\r\ntrained nurse."\r\n\r\nIt was all very good, very kind, yet the very family likeness to Lilias\r\nMerrifield and Jane Mohun made Geraldine think how much more simple in\r\nmanner one of them would have been without that nouveau riche tone of\r\nexultation.\r\n\r\n"Here is a whole packet of letters," ended Mrs. White, "that came for\r\nyou these last two or three days."\r\n\r\nShe pointed to a writing-table and went away, while the first letters so\r\namazed Geraldine that she could think of nothing else, and hastened to\r\nsummon Clement.\r\n\r\nIt was from Gerald, posted by the pilot from on board the steamer, very\r\nshort, and only saying--\r\n\r\n\r\n"DEAREST CHERIE,\r\n\r\n"I know you will forgive me, or rather see that I do not need pardon for\r\nrescuing my sister. Anywhere in England she would be in danger of being\r\nreclaimed to worse than death. Dolores will tell you all the situation,\r\nand I will send a letter as soon as we arrive at New York. No time for\r\nmore, except that I am as much as ever\r\n\r\n"Your own, my Cherie\'s own,\r\n\r\n"GERALD."\r\n\r\n\r\nThere followed directions how to send letters to him through the office\r\nof the \'Censor\'.\r\n\r\nThen she opened, written on the same day, a letter from Dolores Mohun,\r\nsent in obedience to his telegram, when he found that time for details\r\nfailed him. It began--\r\n\r\n\r\n"DEAR MRS. GRINSTEAD,\r\n\r\n"I know you will be shocked and grieved at the step that your nephew has\r\ntaken, but when you understand the circumstances, I think you will see\r\nthat it was unavoidable for one of so generous and self-sacrificing a\r\nnature. I may add, that my aunt Lily is much touched, and thoroughly\r\napproves, and my uncle Jasper says imprudence is better than\r\nselfishness."\r\n\r\n\r\nAfter this little preamble ensued a full and sensible account of\r\nLudmilla\'s situation and sufferings at the circus, and the history of\r\nher escape, demonstrating (to the writer\'s own satisfaction) that there\r\nwas no other means of securing the poor child.\r\n\r\nOf course the blow to Geraldine was a terrible one.\r\n\r\n"We have lost him," she said.\r\n\r\n"That does not follow," said Clement. "It is quite plain that he does\r\nnot mean to cut himself off from us, and America is not out of reach."\r\n\r\n"It is just the restless impatience that you warned him against. As if\r\nhe could not have taken her to the Hendersons."\r\n\r\n"She would not have been safe there, unless acts of cruelty could have\r\nbeen proved."\r\n\r\n"Or to us, out here."\r\n\r\n"My dear Cherry, imagine his sudden arrival with such an appendage! I\r\nreally think the boy has acted for the best."\r\n\r\n"Giving up Oxford too!"\r\n\r\n"That can be resumed."\r\n\r\n"And most likely that wretched little girl will run off in a month\'s\r\ntime. It is in the blood."\r\n\r\n"Come, come, Cherry. I can\'t have you in this uncharitable mood."\r\n\r\n"Then I mustn\'t say what I think of that Dolores abetting him."\r\n\r\n"No, I like her letter."\r\n\r\nIt fell hard upon Geraldine to keep all to herself, while entertained in\r\nfull state by her hosts. Perhaps Adeline would have liked something on a\r\nsmaller scale, for she knew what was ostentatious; but though Mr. White\r\nhad once lived in a corner of the castle, almost like an artisan; since\r\nhe had married, it had become his pride to treat his guests on the\r\ngrandest London scale, and the presence of Sir Robert Audley for one\r\nnight evoked all his splendours. He made excuses for having no one to\r\nmeet the party but the chaplain and his wife and the young doctor, who\r\nhe patronizingly assured them was "quite the gentleman," and Theodore\r\nWhite--"Just to fill up a corner and amuse the young ladies." Theodore\r\nhad been lately sent out, now a clerk, soon to be a partner; but he was\r\nvery shy, and did not amuse the young ladies at all! Indeed, he was soon\r\nso smitten with admiration for Franceska, that he could do nothing but\r\nsit rapt, looking at her under his eyelids.\r\n\r\nThe chaplain had received an offer of preferment in England, and was\r\nanxious to go home as soon as possible. Clement was now so well, that\r\nafter assisting the next day in the week\'s duties among the people, and\r\nat the pretty little church that Mr. White had built, he ventured to\r\naccept the proposal of becoming a substitute until the decision was made\r\nor another chaplain found. He was very happy to be employed once more in\r\nhis vocation.\r\n\r\nThe climate suited him exactly, and the loan of the chaplain\'s house\r\nwould relieve him and Geraldine from the rather oppressive hospitality\r\nof the castle. The search for Benista\'s antecedents would of course go\r\non with the assistance of Mr. White and his Italian foreman, but both\r\nassured him that the inquiry might be protracted, as winter was likely\r\nto cut off the communications with many parts of the interior, and many\r\nof the men would be at their distant homes till the spring advanced.\r\n\r\nMeantime, Geraldine and her nieces had a home life, reading, studying\r\nItalian, drawing with endless pleasure, and the young ones walking about\r\nthe chestnut-covered slopes. She sat in the gardens or drove with Mrs.\r\nWhite in her donkey-chaise, and would have been full of enjoyment but\r\nfor the abiding anxiety about Gerald. It was rather a relief not to\r\nbe living in the same house with the Whites, whose hospitality and\r\nmagnificence were rather oppressive. Mr. White wanted to have everything\r\nadmired, and its cost appreciated; and Adeline, though clever enough,\r\nhad provoking similarities and dissimilarities to her sisters. The same\r\nmight be said of Maura, to whom Francie at first took a great fancy, but\r\nAnna, who had seen more of the world, had a sense of distrust.\r\n\r\n"There\'s something fawning about her ways," said she, "and I don\'t know\r\nwhether she is quite sincere."\r\n\r\n"Perhaps it is only being half Greek," said Geraldine.\r\n\r\nHowever, the two families met every day, and Mrs. White called their\r\nintercourse "such a boon, such a charming friendship," all unaware that\r\nthere was no real confidence or affection.\r\n\r\nThey had not long been seated when the little Italian messenger boy\r\nbrought them a budget of letters. Of course the first that Geraldine\r\nopened was in her nephew\'s writing. It had been written at intervals\r\nthroughout the voyage, and finished on landing at New York.\r\n\r\nPassing over the expressions of unabated affection, and explanation of\r\nthe need of removing Ludmilla out of reach of her natural guardians,\r\nwith the date on the second day of the voyage, the diary continued:\r\n\r\n\r\n"Whom, as the fates would have it, should I have encountered but the\r\nCacique! Yes, old Fernan and Marilda have the stateliest of staterooms\r\nin this same liner, and he was as much taken aback as I was when we ran\r\nagainst one another over a destitute and disconsolate Irish family in\r\nthe steerage. Marilda is as yet invisible, as is my poor little Lida. It\r\nis unlucky, for the good man is profuse in his offers of patronage, and\r\nI don\'t mean to be patronized."\r\n\r\n\r\nThen, after some clever descriptions of the fellow second-class\r\npassengers in his own lively vein, perhaps a little forced, so as not\r\nto betray more than he intended, that he felt them uncongenial, there\r\ncame--\r\n\r\n\r\n"Lida is up again; she is a sweet little patient person, and I cannot\r\nwithstand Fernan\'s wish to present her to his wife, who remains\r\nprostrate at present, and will till we get out of the present stiff\r\nbreeze and its influences.\r\n\r\n"12th.--The presentation is over, and it has ended in Lida devoting\r\nherself to the succour of Marilda, and likewise of her maid, who is a\r\ngood deal worse than herself.\r\n\r\n       *            *            *            *            *\r\n\r\n"16th.--These amiable folks want to take Lida off with them, not to say\r\nmyself, to their \'Underwood\' in the Rockies; but I don\'t intend her to\r\nbe semi-lady\'s-maid, semi-companion, as she is becoming, but to let\r\nher stand on her own legs, or mine, and put her to a good school at New\r\nYork. I have finished an article on \'Transatlantic Travellers\' for the\r\n\'Censor\', also some reviews, and another paper that may pave my way to\r\nwork in New York or elsewhere. My craving is for the work of hard hands,\r\nbut I look at mine, and fear I run more to the brain than the hands. My\r\nfather must have been of finer physique than the Sioux bullet left to\r\nme; but I have no fears."\r\n\r\n"No, indeed," sighed Geraldine; "he has not the fine athletic strength\r\nof his dear father, but still--still I think there is that in him which\r\nEdgar had not."\r\n\r\n"Force of character," said Clement, "even if he is wrong-headed. Here is\r\nFernan\'s letter--\r\n\r\n\r\n"\'Imagine my amazement at finding Gerald on board with us. He tells me\r\nthat you are aware of his escapade, so I need not explain it. He is\r\nnot very gracious to either of us, and absolutely refuses all offers of\r\nassistance either for himself or his sister. However, I hope to be able\r\nto keep a certain watch over him without offending him, and to obviate\r\nsome of the difficulties in his way, perhaps unknown to him. Marilda\r\nhas, as usual, suffered greatly on the voyage, but the little Lida, as\r\nhe calls her, has been most attentive and useful both to her and her\r\nmaid, who was quite helpless, and much the worst of the two. My wife was\r\nmuch prejudiced against Lida at first, but has become very fond of her,\r\nand is sure that she is a thoroughly good girl--worth the sacrifice\r\nGerald has made for her. In his independent mood, he will not hear\r\nof our offering a home to the poor child; but if, as I hope, your\r\nresearches turn out in his favour, he may consent to let us find\r\nsuitable education for her. At any rate, I promise Geraldine not to\r\nleave these two young things to their fate, though I may have to act\r\nsecretly. I can never forget how I took him from his father\'s side, and\r\nthe baptism almost in blood. We go to New Orleans first, and after the\r\ncold weather home, but letters to the Bank will find us.\'"\r\n\r\n\r\n"Good, dear old Fernan and Marilda!" cried Geraldine, "I can see their\r\nkindness, and how, with all their goodness, it must jar on Gerald\'s\r\nnerves."\r\n\r\n"I hope he won\'t be an ass," returned Clement. "Such patient goodness\r\nought not to be snubbed by--" He caught his sister\'s eye, and made his\r\nlast words "youthful theorists."\r\n\r\nMrs. Henderson too forwarded a letter from Lida, being sure that it\r\nwould be a great pleasure to Mrs. Grinstead. It went into many more\r\nparticulars about the miseries of the circus training than had been\r\nknown before, and the fears and hints which made it plain that it had\r\nbeen quite right to avail herself of the means of escape; after which\r\nwas added--\r\n\r\n\r\n"I never thought to be so happy as I am here. My brother is the noblest,\r\nmost generous, most kind of creatures, and that he should do all this\r\nfor me, after all the harm he has suffered from my poor mother! It quite\r\noverpowers me when I think of it. I see a tear has dropped, but it is\r\nsuch a happy one. Please tell Mr. Flight what peace and joy this is to\r\nme, after all my prayers and trying to mind what he said. There are such\r\na gentleman and lady here, cousins to my brother, Sir Ferdinand and Lady\r\nTravis Underwood. She has been more or less ill all through the voyage,\r\nand her maid worse, and she has let me do what I could for her, and\r\nhas been kindness itself. They were at the bazaar. Did you see Sir\r\nFerdinand? He is the very grandest and handsomest man I ever did see,\r\nand so good to all the poor emigrants in the steerage. He is very kind\r\nto me; but I see that my brother will not have me presume. They have\r\nbidden me write to them in any need. I never thought there could be so\r\nmany good people out of Rockquay. Please give my duty to Mr. Flight and\r\nLady Flight, good Miss Mohun, and dear Miss Dolores. I wear her ulster,\r\nand bless the thought of her."\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIX. -- ROWENA AND HER RIVAL\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     And yet if each the other\'s name\r\n       In some unguarded moment heard,\r\n     The heart that once you thought so tame\r\n       Would flutter like a wounded bird.--ANON.\r\n\r\n\r\nLetters continued to come with fair regularity; and it was understood\r\nthat Gerald, with Lida, had taken up his quarters in an\r\n"inexpensive" boarding-house at New York, where he had sent Lida to a\r\nhighly-recommended day-school, and he was looking out for employment.\r\nHis articles had been accepted, he said; but the accounts of his\r\nadventures and of his fellow-inmates gave the sense that there was more\r\nhumour in the retrospect than in the society, and that they were better\r\nto write about than to live with. He never confessed it, but to his\r\naunt, who understood him, it was plain that he found it a different\r\nthing to talk philanthropic socialism, or even to work among the poor,\r\nand to live in the society of the unrefined equals.\r\n\r\nThen he wrote that Lida had come one day and told him that one of the\r\ngirls, with whom she had made friends, had a bad attack of cough and\r\nbronchitis, and could not fulfil an engagement that she had made to come\r\nand sing for a person who was giving lectures upon national music. "\'I\r\nlooked at some of her songs,\' little Lida said in her humble way, \'and\r\nI know them. Don\'t you think, brother, I might take her part?\' Well, not\r\nto put too fine a point upon it, it was not an unwelcome notion, for my\r\narticles, though accepted, don\'t bring in the speedy remuneration with\r\nwhich fiction beguiles the aspirant. Only one of them, which I send you,\r\nhas seen the light, and the \'Censor\' is slow, though sure, so dollars\r\nfor immediate expenses run short. I called on the fellow, Mr. Gracchus\r\nB. Van Tromp, to see whether he were fit company for my sister, and I\r\nfound him much superior to his name--gentlemanlike and intelligent, not\r\nill-read, and pretty safe, like most Yankees, to know how to behave to a\r\nyoung girl. When he found I could accompany my sister on piano or violin\r\nhe was transported. Moreover, he could endure to be enlightened by a\r\nBritisher on such little facts as the true history of Auld Robin Gray\r\nand the Wacht am Rhein. The lecture was a marked success. We have\r\nanother tonight, 16th. It has resulted in a proposal to these two\r\ninteresting performers to accompany the great Gracchus on a tour through\r\nthe leading \'cities,\' lecturing by turns with him and assisting. He\r\nhas hitherto picked up as he could \'local talent,\' but is glad of less\r\nuncertain help, and so far as appears, he is superior to jealousy,\r\nthough he sees that I\'m better read, \'and of the cut that takes the\r\nladies.\' It is no harm for Lida; she was not learning much, and I can\r\ncultivate her better when I have her to myself, and get her not to\r\nregard me so much like a lion, to be honoured with distant respect and\r\nobedience. We shall get dollars enough to keep us going till my talents\r\nbreak upon the world, and obtain stunning experiences for the \'Censor\'.\r\nMy father\'s dear old violin is coming to the front. Our first start will\r\nbe at Boston; but continue to write to Gerald F. Wood, care of Editor of\r\n\'Cole\'s Weekly\'."\r\n\r\n"How like his father!" was the natural exclamation; but the details that\r\nfollowed in another week were fairly satisfactory, and the spirit of\r\nindependence was a sound one, which had stood harder proofs than perhaps\r\nhis home was allowed to know, though these were early days.\r\n\r\nFebruary was beginning to open the buds and to fill the slopes with\r\ndelicate anemones, as well as to bring back Mr. White\'s workmen, among\r\nwhom Clement could make inquiries. One young man knew the name of\r\nBenista as belonging to a family in a valley beyond his own, but it was\r\nnot an easily accessible one, and a fresh fall of snow had choked the\r\nravine, and would do so for weeks to come.\r\n\r\nYet all was lovely on the coast, and Mr. White having occasion to go to\r\nSan Remo, offered to take the three girls with him.\r\n\r\n"Young ladies always have a turn for shops," said he.\r\n\r\n"I want to see the coast," said Franceska, with a little dignity.\r\n\r\n"But I do want some gloves--and some blue embroidery silk, thank you,\r\nMr. White," said Anna, more courteously.\r\n\r\n"And I want some handkerchiefs, if Mr. White will take me too!" returned\r\nUncle Clement in the same tone.\r\n\r\n"I know so well what you mean, dear," observed Maura, sotto voce to\r\nFrancie. "It is so trying to be supposed mere common-place, when one\'s\r\nthoughts are on the beautiful and romantic."\r\n\r\nIt was just one of the sayings that had begun to go against Francie\'s\r\ntaste, and she answered--\r\n\r\n"Mr. White is very good-natured."\r\n\r\n"Ah, yes, but so--so--you know."\r\n\r\nFrancie was called, and left Mr. White\'s description to be unutterable.\r\n\r\nThe two elder ladies spent the day together, and Mrs. Grinstead then\r\nheard that Jane Mohun had written, that both Lord Ivinghoe and Lady\r\nPhyllis Devereux were recovering from the influenza, and that Lord\r\nRotherwood had had a slight touch of the complaint.\r\n\r\n"It is a very serious thing in our family," said Adeline, with all the\r\nsatisfaction of having a family, especially with a complaint, and she\r\nbegan to enumerate the victims of the Devereux house and her own, only\r\nbreaking off to exclaim, "I really shall write at once to beg them all\r\nto come here for the rest of the winter, March winds and all. My cousin\r\nRotherwood has never been here, and they might be quite quiet among\r\nrelations. So unlike a common health resort."\r\n\r\nMrs. White\'s hospitable anticipations were forestalled. The party came\r\nhome from San Remo in high spirits. They had met Lord Rotherwood and\r\nhis son in the street, they had been greeted most warmly, and brought\r\nto luncheon at the villa, where they found not only Lady Rotherwood and\r\nPhyllis, but Mysie Merrifield.\r\n\r\nIt was explained that their London doctor had strongly advised immediate\r\ntransplantation before there was time to catch fresh colds, and a friend\r\nof the Marchioness, who permanently possessed a charming house at San\r\nRemo, had offered it just as it was for the spring. The journey had been\r\nmade at once, with one deviation on Lord Rotherwood\'s part, to beg for\r\nMysie, as an essential requisite to his "Fly\'s" perfect recovery. A\r\nvisit had been due before, only deferred by the general illness, and no\r\ndifficulty was made in letting it be paid in these new and delightful\r\nscenes. Phyllis had been there before. She was weak and languid, and\r\nwould much rather have stayed at home, except for seeing Mysie\'s delight\r\nin the mountains and the blue Mediterranean, which she dimly remembered\r\nfrom her infancy at Malta. Only she made it a point of honour not to\r\nallow that the sea was bluer than the bay of Rockquay.\r\n\r\nIvinghoe was looking ill and disgusted, but brightened up at the sight\r\nof the visitors, and his mother, who thought Monte Carlo too near,\r\nthough she had kept as far from it as possible, accepted the more\r\nwillingly Mr. White\'s cordial invitation to come and spend a day or two\r\nat Rocca Marina. Trifles were so much out of the good lady\'s focus of\r\nvision that the possible dangers in that quarter never occurred to her,\r\nthough Maura was demurely bridling, and Francie, all unawakened, but\r\nprettier than ever, was actually wearing a scarlet anemone that Ivinghoe\r\nhad given to her.\r\n\r\nIn the intervening days, Rocca Marina was in a wonderful state of\r\npreparation. The master of it was genuinely and honestly kindly and\r\nsimple-hearted, and had entertained noble travellers before, who had\r\nbeen attracted by his extensive and artistic works; but no words can\r\ndescribe the satisfaction of his wife. In part there was the heartfelt\r\npleasure of receiving the cousin who had been like one of her brothers\r\nin the home of her childhood; but to this was added the glory of knowing\r\nthat this same cousin was a marquis, and that the society of San Remo,\r\nnay of all the Riviera and the Italian papers to boot, would know that\r\nshe was a good deal more than the quarry-owner\'s wife. Moreover, like\r\nall her family, there was a sense of Lady Rotherwood\'s coming from a\r\ndifferent sphere, and treating them with condescension. Jane and Lily\r\nmight laugh, but to Adeline it was matter of a sort of aggressive awe,\r\nhalf as asserting herself as "Victoria\'s" equal and relation, half as\r\nprotecting her from inferior people.\r\n\r\nGeraldine perceived and was secretly amused. Of course all the party\r\ndined at the castle on Saturday night, and heard some lamentations that\r\nthere was no one else to meet the distinguished guests, for the young\r\ndoctor was not thought worthy.\r\n\r\n"But I knew you would like a family party best, and the Underwoods\r\nare--almost connections, though--"\r\n\r\nIn that "though" was conveyed their vast inferiority to the house of\r\nMohun.\r\n\r\n"I always understood that it was a very good old family," said Lady\r\nRotherwood.\r\n\r\n"Clement Underwood is one of the most valuable clergy in London," said\r\nher lord; "I am glad he is recovering. I shall be delighted to hear him\r\nagain."\r\n\r\nMaura was standing under the pergola with Lord Ivinghoe.\r\n\r\n"And is not it sad for poor Franceska Vanderkist?--Oh! you know about\r\npoor Mr. Gerald Underwood?" said Maura, blushing a little at the awkward\r\nsubject.\r\n\r\n"Of course," said Ivinghoe impatiently. "He is in America, is he not?\r\nBut what has she to do with it?"\r\n\r\n"Oh, you know, after being his Mona, and all. It can\'t go any further\r\ntill it is cleared up."\r\n\r\nPhyllis and Mysie came up, asking Maura to tell them the name of a\r\nmountain peak with a white cap. The party came up to dinner, which was\r\nas genial and easy as the host and Lord Rotherwood could make it, and as\r\nstiff and grand as the hostess could accomplish, aided by the deftness\r\nand grace of her Italian servants. In the evening Theodore came up to\r\nassist in the singing of glees, and Clement\'s voice was a delightful and\r\nwelcome sound in his sister\'s ears. Ivinghoe stood among the circle at\r\nthe piano, and enjoyed. He and his sister were not particularly musical,\r\nbut enough to enjoy those remarkable Underwood voices. After that Maura\r\nnever promoted musical evenings.\r\n\r\nAn odd little Sunday-school for the children of the English workmen had\r\nbeen instituted at Rocca Marina, where Maura had always assisted the\r\nchaplain\'s wife, and Anna and Francie shared the work. Mysie heard of it\r\nwith enthusiasm, for, as Ivinghoe told her, she was pining for a breath\r\nof the atmosphere, but she came down to enjoy the delights thereof\r\nalone, taking Maura\'s small class. Maura was supposed to be doing the\r\npolite to Lady Phyllis, but in point of fact Phyllis was lying down\r\nin the balcony of her mother\'s dressing-room, and Maura was gracefully\r\nfanning herself under a great cork tree, while Lord Ivinghoe was lying\r\non the grass.\r\n\r\nFrancie looked languid, and said it was getting dreadfully hot, but\r\nMrs. Grinstead took no notice, trusting that the cessation of attentions\r\nwould hinder any feeling from going deeper, so that--as she could not\r\nhelp saying to herself--she might not have brought the poor child out of\r\nthe frying-pan into the fire--not an elegant proverb, but expressing her\r\nfeeling!\r\n\r\nMore especially did it do so, when she found that Lord Rotherwood was\r\nso much delighted with the beauty and variety of the marbles of Rocca\r\nMarina as to order a font to be made of them for the church that was\r\nbeing restored at Clarebridge, and he, and still more his son, found\r\nconstant diversion in running over by train from San Remo to superintend\r\nthe design, and to select the different colours and patterns of the\r\nstones as they were quarried out and bits polished so as to show their\r\nbeauty. Their ladies often accompanied them, and these expeditions\r\ngenerally involved luncheon at the castle, and often tea at the\r\nparsonage, but it might be gradually observed, as time went on, that\r\nthere was a shade of annoyance on the part of the great house at the\r\npreference sometimes unconsciously shown for the society of the smaller\r\none.\r\n\r\nMysie openly claimed Anna as her own friend of some standing, and\r\nboth she and Phyllis had books to discuss, botanical or geological\r\ndiscoveries to communicate or puzzle out, with Mrs. Grinstead or her\r\nnieces. Lord Rotherwood had many more interests in common with Clement\r\nUnderwood than with Mr. White, and even the Marchioness, though more\r\nimpartial and on her guard, was sensible to Mrs. Grinstead\'s charm of\r\nmanner and depth of comprehension. She patronized Adeline, but respected\r\nMrs. Grinstead as incapable of and insensible to patronage.\r\n\r\nThat her gentlemen should have found such safe and absorbing occupation\r\nin the opposite direction to Monte Carlo was an abiding satisfaction\r\nto her, and she did not analyze the charms of the place as regarded her\r\nson. She had seen him amused by other young ladies, as he certainly was\r\nnow by that Miss White, who was very handsome and very obliging.\r\n\r\nShe knew and he knew all the antecedents too well for alarm, till one\r\nday she saw Maura\'s face, as she made him pull down a spray of banksia\r\nfrom the side of a stone wall, and watched the air of gallant courtesy\r\nwith which he presented it.\r\n\r\nFrancie watched it too, as she had watched the like before, and said\r\nnothing, but there was an odd, dull sense of disappointment, and the\r\nglory had faded away from sea and sky, spring though it was. Yet there\r\nwere pressures of the hand in greeting and parting, and kind, wistful\r\nlooks, as if of sympathy, little services and little attentions, that\r\nset her foolish little heart bounding, in a way she was much ashamed\r\nto feel, and would have been more utterly ashamed to speak of, or to\r\nsuppose observed. She only avowed to Anna that it was very warm, weary\r\nweather, and that she was tired of absence, and felt homesick, but Aunt\r\nCherry was so kind that she must not be told.\r\n\r\nLady Rotherwood proposed moving away, but her husband and son would not\r\nhear of it till their font was finished.\r\n\r\nIt was not unwelcome to any one of the elder ladies that the young\r\nofficer\'s leave would be over in another week. Geraldine was glad that\r\nFrancie should be freed from the trial of seeing attention absorbed by\r\nMaura, and herself so often left in the lurch, so far as that young lady\r\ncould contrive it, for though not a word was said, the brightened eye\r\nand glowing cheek, whenever Lord Ivinghoe brought her forward, or paid\r\nher any deference or civility, were dangerous symptoms. Peace of mind\r\nin so modest and innocent a maiden would probably come back when the\r\nexcitement was once over.\r\n\r\nAs to Adeline, there was nothing she dreaded so much as the commotion\r\nthat would be excited if Ivinghoe\'s flirtation came to any crisis. His\r\nmother would never forgive her, his father would hardly do so; she would\r\nfeel like a traitor to the whole family, and all her attempts to put a\r\ncheck on endeavours on Maura\'s part to draw him on--an endeavour that\r\nbegan to be visible to her--were met by apparent unconsciousness or by\r\ntears. And when she ventured a word to her husband, he gruffly answered\r\nthat his niece\'s father had been an officer in the army, and he could\r\nmake it worth any one\'s while to take her! Young lords were glad enough\r\nin these days to have something to put into their pockets.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXX. -- DREAMS AND NIGHTINGALES\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     Then in that time and place I spoke to her.--TENNYSON.\r\n\r\n\r\n"Office of \'Lacustrian Intelligencer,\'\r\n\r\n"Jonesville, Ohio,\r\n\r\n"March 20.\r\n\r\n"DEAREST CHERIE,\r\n\r\n"I told you in my last that the chief boss in the office at New York had\r\nwritten to me that he had been asked to send an intelligent young man\r\nto sub-edit the Lacustrian Intelligencer at Jonesville, a rising city on\r\nLake Erie. I thought it would be worth while to look at it, especially\r\nas we were booked to give a lecture at Sandusky, and moreover our\r\nrelations to Gracchus have been growing rather strained, and I do not\r\nthink this wandering life good for Lida in the long run; nor are my\r\narticles paid enough for to be a dependence. So after holding forth\r\nat Sandusky, we took our passage in a little steamer which crosses the\r\nlittle bay in the Lake to Jonesville--one of those steamers just like a\r\nNoah\'s Ark.\r\n\r\n"Presently Lida came up and touched me, saying in her little awestruck\r\nwhisper (which has never been conquered), \'Brother, I am sure I saw one\r\nof mother\'s cigarettes.\' I said \'Bosh!\' thinking it an utter delusion;\r\nbut she was so decided and so frightened, that I told her to go into the\r\nsaloon, and went forward. A woman was going about the deck, offering the\r\npassengers a basket of candies, lights, cigarettes, and cigars. Saving\r\nfor Lida\'s words, I never should have recognized her; she was thin\r\nto the last degree, haggard, yellow, excessively shabby and\r\nforlorn-looking, and with a hollow cough; but as her eyes met mine\r\n(those eyes that you say are our water-mark) both of us made a sort of\r\nleap as if to go overboard, and I went up to her at once, and would have\r\nspoken, but she cried out, \'What have you done with Lida?\' I answered\r\nthat she was safe, and demanded in my turn where were O\'Leary and\r\nJellicoe. \'Drowned, drowned,\' she said, \'in the wreck of the Sirius.\r\nThey\'ll never trouble you more. But Lida!\' I thought that it was safe to\r\ntake her into the saloon to see Lida, when they fell into each other\'s\r\narms, and afforded the spectators a romantic spectacle. Don\'t think I\r\nam making a joke of it, for it was tragic enough in the result of the\r\nagitation. Blood was choking the poor woman. We could only lay her\r\ndown on the couch, and happily there were lemons on board. There was\r\na good-natured Irishman who gave me all the help he could, even to the\r\ncarrying her to his house, where his wife was equally kind. He fetched\r\nthe priest, a French Canadian, and the doctor, and Lida has been\r\nwatching over her most tenderly; poor things--they seem really to have\r\ncared for one another, and Lida will be the happier for having done\r\nthese last duties.\r\n\r\n"21st. She is a little better. So far as we have gathered from one who\r\nmust not talk nor be agitated, the circus had got into difficulties and\r\ndebt to Bast, the van proprietor. I believe Lida\'s voice was their last\r\nhope, and they had some ghastly scheme of disposing of her in Belgium.\r\nWhen they lost her, their chances were over, and with the proceeds of\r\ntheir last exhibition, Jellicoe and the O\'Leary pair left the elephant,\r\netc., to take care of themselves and make their excuses to Mr. Bast, and\r\nstarted for Liverpool and the U. S. in the Sirius. Storms overtook\r\nthem, the women were put into the first boat, those which followed were\r\nswamped. Poor fellows, I own I can\'t sing a pious dirge for them. There\r\nwere three days of hunger and exposure before the boat was picked\r\nup, and she was finally landed at Quebec, where she was laid up with\r\npleurisy in the hospital. And there was a subscription for the wrecked\r\nwhen she came out, which enabled her to set up this reminiscence of her\r\nold trade, drifting from one pier or boat to another till she came to\r\nthis one, but all the time with this awful cough. The doctor thinks it\r\nher knell; her lungs are far gone, but she may probably rally in some\r\ndegree for the summer, though hardly so as to be moved.\r\n\r\n"That being the case, I have been to the Lacustrian office, and engaged\r\nmyself to be its hack, since I must have some fixed pay while she lives.\r\nPerhaps I shall be able to do a little extra writing and lecturing,\r\nespecially if she gets better, enough to spare Lida to help me. Her\r\nvoice really is a lovely soprano, and draws wonderfully, but I don\'t\r\nwant it to be strained too early. Our good Irishwoman, Mrs. Macbride, is\r\nwilling to let us have her two rooms, left empty by her sons going west,\r\nand her daughter marrying, on fair terms, Lida promising to be a sort\r\nof help and to teach the children. We shall eat with them. I shall be at\r\nthe office all day and half the night, so I don\'t need a sitting-room.\r\nDon\'t be anxious, dear old Cherie. We shall do very well, and it is only\r\nfor a time. Lida is like a little angel, and as thankful for a smile\r\nfrom her mother as if she had been the reprobate runaway.\r\n\r\n"Your ever-loving\r\n\r\n"GERALD."\r\n\r\n\r\nThis was the letter that came to Mrs. Grinstead, and one with similar\r\ninformation went to Dolores Mohun at her college at Cambridge. Dolores,\r\nwho had found Mysie much more sympathetic than Gillian, could not but\r\nwrite the intelligence to her, and Mysie was so much struck with the\r\nbeauty of the much-injured brother and sister devoting themselves\r\nto their mother, that she could not help telling the family party at\r\nbreakfast.\r\n\r\n"That\'s right," said Lord Rotherwood. "The mother can clear up the doubt\r\nif any one can. Is there nothing about it?"\r\n\r\n"No," replied Mysie; "I should think the poor woman was too ill to be\r\nasked."\r\n\r\n"They must not let her slip through their fingers without telling,"\r\nadded Ivinghoe.\r\n\r\n"I have a mind to run over to Rocca Marina and see what more they have\r\nheard there," said Lord Rotherwood. "I suppose your letter is from one\r\nof the girls there?"\r\n\r\n"Oh no, it is from Dolores."\r\n\r\n"Dolores! She is at Cambridge. Then this news must have been round by\r\nClipstone! They must have known it for days past at Rocca!" exclaimed\r\nLord Rotherwood.\r\n\r\n"No," said Mysie, "this came direct to Dolores from Gerald Underwood\r\nhimself.--Oh, didn\'t you know? I forgot, nobody was to know till Uncle\r\nMaurice gave his consent."\r\n\r\n"Consent to what?" exclaimed Ivinghoe.\r\n\r\n"To Dolores and Gerald! Oh dear, mamma said so much to me about not\r\ntelling, but I did think Cousin Rotherwood knew everything. Please--"\r\n\r\nWhatever she was going to ask was cut short by Ivinghoe\'s suddenly\r\nstriking on the table so as to make all the cups and saucers ring as he\r\nexclaimed--\r\n\r\n"If ever there lived a treacherous Greek minx!" Then, "I beg your\r\npardon, mother."\r\n\r\nHe was off: they saw him dash out of the house. There was a train due\r\nnearly at this time, as all recollected.\r\n\r\n"Papa, had not you better go with him?" said Lady Rotherwood.\r\n\r\n"He will get on much better by himself, my dear," and Lord Rotherwood\r\nthrew himself back in his chair and laughed heartily and merrily, to the\r\namazement and mystification of the two girls. "You will have a beauty on\r\nyour hands, my lady."\r\n\r\n"Well, as long as it is not that horrid White girl--" said her ladyship,\r\nbreaking off there.\r\n\r\n"A very sorry Rebecca," said her lord, laughing the more.\r\n\r\nBut the Marchioness rose up, and the two cousins had to accept the\r\nsignal.\r\n\r\nThe train, after the leisurely fashion of continental railways,\r\nkept Ivinghoe fuming at the station, and rattled along so as to give\r\ntravellers a full view of the coast, more delightful to them than to\r\nthe youth, who had rushed off with intentions, he scarce knew what, of\r\nsetting right the consequences of Maura\'s--was it deception, or only a\r\nthought, of which the wish was father?\r\n\r\nHe reached the station that led to the works at Rocca Marina. The sun\r\nwas high, the heat of the day coming on, and as he strode along, the\r\nworkmen were leaving off to take their siesta at noontide. On he went,\r\nacross the private walks in the terraced garden, not up the broad stone\r\nsteps that led to the house, but to a little group of olive trees which\r\ncut off the chaplain\'s house from the castle gardens, and where stood\r\na great cork tree, to whose branches a hammock had been fastened, and\r\nseats placed under it. As he opened the gate a little dog\'s bark was\r\nheard, and he was aware of a broad hat under the tree. Simultaneously a\r\nsmall Maltese dog sprang forward, and Francie\'s head rose from leaning\r\nover the little table with a start, her cheeks deeper rose than usual,\r\nhaving evidently gone to sleep over the thin book and big dictionary\r\nthat lay before her.\r\n\r\n"Oh!" she said, "it is you. Was I dreaming?"\r\n\r\n"I am afraid I startled you."\r\n\r\n"No--only"--she still seemed only half awake--"it seemed to come out of\r\nmy dream."\r\n\r\n"Then you were dreaming of me?"\r\n\r\n"Oh no. At, least I don\'t know," she said, the colour flushing into her\r\nface, as she sat upright, now quite awake and alive to the question,\r\nbetween truthfulness and maidenly modesty.\r\n\r\n"You were--you were; you don\'t deny it!" And as she hung her head and\r\ngrew more distressfully redder and redder, "You know what that means."\r\n\r\n"Indeed--indeed--I couldn\'t help--I never meant! Oh--"\r\n\r\nIt was an exclamation indeed, for Uncle Clement\'s head appeared above\r\nthe hammock, where he too had been dozing over his book, with the\r\nwords--\r\n\r\n"Halloo, young people, I\'m here!"\r\n\r\nFranceska would have fled, but Ivinghoe held her hand so tight that she\r\ncould not wrench it away. He held it, while Clement struggled to the\r\nground, and then said--\r\n\r\n"Sir, there is no reason you or all the world should not know how I love\r\nthis dearest, loveliest one. I came here this morning hoping that she\r\nmay grant me leave to try to win her to be my own."\r\n\r\nHe looked at Francie. Her head drooped, but she had not taken her hand\r\naway, and the look on her face was not all embarrassment, but there was\r\na rosy sunrise dawning on it.\r\n\r\nAll Clement could say was something of "Your father."\r\n\r\n"He knows, he understands; I saw it in his eyes," said Ivinghoe.\r\n\r\nTo Clement the surprise was far greater than it would have been to his\r\nsister, and the experience was almost new to him, but he could read\r\nFrancie\'s face well enough to say--\r\n\r\n"My dear, I think we had better let you run in and compose yourself, or\r\ngo to your aunt, while I talk to Lord Ivinghoe."\r\n\r\nTrembling, frightened, Francie was really glad to be released, as her\r\nlover with one pressure said--\r\n\r\n"I shall see you again, sweetest."\r\n\r\nShe darted away, and Clement signed to Ivinghoe to sit down with him on\r\nthe bench under the tree.\r\n\r\n"I should like this better if you had brought your father\'s full\r\nassent," he said.\r\n\r\n"There was no time.
