I only read his face; he will come to-morrow."\r\n\r\n"No time?"\r\n\r\n"Yes, to catch the train. I hurried away the moment I learnt that--that\r\nher affections were not otherwise engaged. I never saw any one like her.\r\nShe has haunted me ever since those days at Rockquay; but--but I was\r\ntold that she cared for your nephew, and I could not take advantage of\r\nhim in his absence. And now I have but three days more."\r\n\r\n"Whoever told you was under a great error," said Clement gravely, "and\r\nyou have shown very generous self-command; but the advantages of this\r\naffair are so much the greatest on one side, that you cannot wonder if\r\nthere is hesitation on our part, till we explicitly know that our poor\r\nlittle girl would not be unwelcome to your parents."\r\n\r\n"I know that no one can compare with her for--for everything and\r\nanything," stammered Ivinghoe, breaking from his mother\'s language into\r\nhis father\'s, "and my father admires her as much as I do--almost."\r\n\r\n"But what will he and your mother say to her being absolutely\r\npenniless?"\r\n\r\n"Pish!"\r\n\r\n"And worse--child to a spendthrift, a man of no connection, except on\r\nhis mother\'s side."\r\n\r\n"She is your niece, your family have bred her up, made her so much more\r\nthan exquisitely lovely."\r\n\r\n"She is a good little girl," said Clement, "but what are we? No,\r\nIvinghoe, I do not blame you for speaking out, and she will be the\r\nhappier for the knowledge of your affection, but it will not be right of\r\nus to give free consent, without being fully assured of that of Lord and\r\nLady Rotherwood."\r\n\r\nIvinghoe could only protest, but Clement rose to walk to the house,\r\nwhere his sister was sitting under the pergola in the agitation of\r\nanswering Gerald\'s letter, and had only seen Francie flit by, calling\r\nto her sister in a voice that now struck her as having been strange and\r\nsuppressed.\r\n\r\nClement trusted a good deal to his sister\'s quicker perceptions and\r\nhabit of observation to guide his opinion in the affair that had burst\r\non him, and was relieved that when Ivinghoe, like the well-bred young\r\nman that he was, went up to her, and taking her hand said, "I have been\r\nventuring to put my fate into the hands of your niece," she did not seem\r\nastonished or overwhelmed, but said--\r\n\r\n"She is a dear good girl; I do hope it will be for her happiness--for\r\nboth."\r\n\r\n"Thank you," he said fervently. "It will be the most earnest desire of\r\nmy life."\r\n\r\nGeraldine thought it best to go in quest of Francie, whom she found with\r\nAnna, incoherent and happy in the glory of the certainty that she was\r\nloved, after the long trial of suppressed, unacknowledged suspense. No\r\nfears of parents, no thought of inequalities had occurred to trouble\r\nher--everything was absorbed in the one thought--"he really did love\r\nher." How should she thank God enough, or pray enough to be worthy of\r\nsuch joy? There was no room for vexation or wonder at the delay, nor\r\nthe attentions paid to Maura. She hushed Anna, who was inclined to be\r\nindignant, and who was obliged afterwards to pour out to her aunt all\r\nher wonder, though she allowed that on his side there was nothing to be\r\nreally called flirtation, it was all Maura--"she was sure Maura was at\r\nthe bottom of it."\r\n\r\n"My dear, don\'t let us be uncharitable; there is no need to think about\r\nit. Let us try to be like Francie, and swallow all up in gladness. Your\r\nmother--"\r\n\r\n"Oh, I can\'t think what she will do for joy. It will almost make her\r\nwell again."\r\n\r\n"But remember, we don\'t know what his parents will say."\r\n\r\nAnd with that sobering thought they had to go down to luncheon, where\r\nFrancie sat blushing and entranced, too happy to speak, and Ivinghoe\r\napparently contented to look at her. Afterwards he was allowed to take\r\npossession of her for the afternoon, so as to be able to tease her about\r\nwhat she was dreaming about him. After all it had probably been evoked\r\nby the dog\'s bark and his step; for she had thought a wolf was pursuing\r\nher, and that he had come to save her. It was quite enough to be food\r\nfor a lover.\r\n\r\nClement would have wished to keep all to themselves, at least till the\r\npaternal visit was over, but Ivinghoe\'s days were few, and he made sure\r\nof bringing his parents on the morrow. An expedition had been arranged\r\nto the valley where some of the Benista family were reported to live,\r\nsince the snows had departed enough for safety; but this must needs be\r\ndeferred, and there was no doubt that the "reason why" would be sought\r\nout.\r\n\r\nIndeed, so close was the great house, and so minute a watch was kept,\r\nthat the fact of Lord Ivinghoe\'s spending the whole day at the parsonage\r\nwas known, and conclusions were arrived at. Maura stole down in the late\r\nevening among the olive trees, ostensibly to ask Anna and Francie to\r\ncome and listen to the nightingales.\r\n\r\nBut thereby she was witness to a scene that showed that there was\r\nanother nightingale for Franceska than the one who was singing with such\r\nenergy among the olive boughs. In fact, she saw the evening farewell,\r\nand had not the discretion, like Anna, to withdraw herself and her eyes,\r\nbut beheld, what had ever been sacred to both those young things, the\r\nfirst kiss.\r\n\r\nPoor Maura, she had none of the reticent pride and shame of an English\r\ngentlewoman. She believed herself cruelly treated, and rushing away,\r\nfell on Anna, who was hovering near, watching to prevent any arrival\r\nsuch as was always probable.\r\n\r\nIt would not be well to relate the angry, foolish words that Anna had to\r\nhear, nor how Maura betrayed herself and her own manoeuvre. It is\r\nenough to say that she went home, weeping demonstratively, perhaps\r\nuncontrollably; and that Anna, after her trying scene, was able to exalt\r\nmore than ever Ivinghoe\'s generosity towards the absent Gerald, and\r\nforbearance towards Franceska. If he had ever passed the line, it was\r\nmore Maura\'s doing than his own.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXI. -- THE COLD SHOULDER\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     Loath to depose the child, your brother\'s son.--SHAKESPEARE.\r\n\r\n\r\nA telegram early the next day announced that the Rotherwood family were\r\non their way, and they came in due time, the kind embrace that Francie\r\nreceived from each in turn being such as to set doubts at rest.\r\n\r\nIn fact, the dread, first of Monte Carlo, and secondly of Maura White,\r\nhad done much to prepare the way with Lady Rotherwood. If she had\r\nfirst heard of her son\'s attachment to the pretty child who acted Mona,\r\ndaughter to the upstart Vanderkists, and with a ruined father of no good\r\nrepute, she would have held it a foolish delusion to be crushed without\r\ndelay; but when this same attachment had lasted eight or nine months,\r\nand had only found avowal on the removal of a supposed rival; when,\r\nmoreover, her darling had been ill, had revived at the aspect of the\r\nyoung lady, and had conducted himself in a place of temptation so as to\r\ncalm an anxious mother\'s heart, she could see with his eyes, not only\r\nthat Franceska was really beautiful, graceful, and a true lady, but\r\nlikely to develop still more under favourable circumstances; that she\r\nhad improved in looks, air, and manner on her travels, also that she had\r\nnever been injured by any contact with undesirable persons, but had been\r\ntrained by the excellent Underwoods, whose gentle blood and breeding\r\nwere undeniable. Nor would "the daughter of the late Sir Adrian\r\nVanderkist, Baronet, of Ironbeam Park," sound much amiss. He was so\r\nlate, that his racing doings might be forgotten.\r\n\r\nIndeed, as the Marchioness looked up to the castle, she felt that she\r\ncould forgive a good deal to the damsel who had saved the family from\r\nthe "sorry Rebecca," who had cried all night, and was still crying,\r\nwhenever any more tears would come, and not getting much pity from any\r\nof her relatives. Mr. White told her that she was a little fool to have\r\nexpected anything from a young swell; her brother said she might have\r\nknown that it was absurd to expect that any one could look at her when\r\nMiss Franceska was by; and Mrs. White observed that it was wonderful to\r\nher to see so little respect shown for maiden dignity, as to endure to\r\nmanifest disappointment. Adeline might speak from ample experience, and\r\ncertainly her words had a salutary effect.\r\n\r\nHowever, the Whites en famille were not quite the same externally. When\r\nLord Rotherwood, after luncheon, went to see old White at the works, and\r\nlook after his font, he met with a reception as stiff and cold as could\r\nwell be paid to a distinguished customer who was not at all in fault;\r\nand for the first time Mr. White was too busy to walk back with him to\r\nthe castle to see Adeline, whom he found, as usual, on a couch on the\r\nterrace in the shade of the house, a pretty picture among the flowers\r\nand vines. She was much more open with him, as became one who understood\r\nmore of his point of view.\r\n\r\n"Well, Rotherwood, I suppose I am to congratulate you, though it is\r\nscarcely a fair match in a worldly point of view."\r\n\r\n"For which I care not a rap. She is a good, simple girl, and a perfect\r\nlady."\r\n\r\n"And Victoria? May I ask, does not she think it a misalliance,\r\nconsidering what these Vanderkists are--and the Underwoods?"\r\n\r\n"There\'s no one I respect more than Lancelot Underwood. As to Victoria,\r\nshe is thankful that it is no worse."\r\n\r\n"Ah! I know what you mean, but you can\'t wonder that my husband should\r\nfeel it hard that there should have been some kind of flirtation. He is\r\nfond of Maura, you know, and he does feel that there must have been some\r\nslyness in some one to cause this affair to have been so suddenly sprung\r\non us."\r\n\r\n"Slyness--aye, I believe there was. Tell me, Ada, had you any notion\r\nthat that lad, Gerald Underwood, was engaged to Dolores Mohun?"\r\n\r\n"No; who told you?"\r\n\r\n"Mysie let it out. She had been warned not to mention it till his\r\nposition was ascertained, Maurice\'s consent and all."\r\n\r\n"I must say Mysie should have spoken. It was not fair towards me to keep\r\nit back."\r\n\r\n"Still less fair of Maura, if that\'s her name, to hint at attachment\r\nbetween Franceska and the boy. That was the embargo upon my poor fellow.\r\nHe rushed off to have it out the moment he saw how matters stood."\r\n\r\n"Well, it was a great shame; but girls are girls, especially with those\r\nantecedents, and Maura did not know to the contrary. You will believe\r\nme, Rotherwood, I never had any desire that she should succeed. I would\r\nhave sent her away if I could; but you can\'t wonder that Mr. White is\r\nvexed, and feels as if there had been underhand dealing."\r\n\r\n"I see he is. But you will not let him make it unpleasant for the\r\nUnderwoods."\r\n\r\n"Oh no, no! They have not much longer to stay. They are in\r\ncorrespondence about a rheumatic clergyman."\r\n\r\nMrs. White, however, determined not to expose Maura to her husband,\r\nthough she reproached her, and was rather shocked by the young lady\'s\r\nself-defence. It was a natural idea, and no one had ever told her to\r\nthe contrary. It was all spite in Mysie Merrifield to proclaim it after\r\nhaving kept it back so long.\r\n\r\nShe really was in such a state of mind that Mrs. White was rather\r\nrelieved that the Rotherwoods had taken Franceska to San Remo to stay\r\ntill Ivinghoe had to depart. Anna was left to send off the little\r\nfelicitous note that she had written to her mother.\r\n\r\nEach and all were writing letters that would be received with\r\nrapture almost incredulous, for no one but Sophia could have had any\r\npreparation.\r\n\r\n"It is pleasant to think of poor Alda\'s delight," said Geraldine, over\r\nher writing-case. "After all her troubles, to have her utmost ambition\r\nfulfilled at last; and yet--and yet it does seem turning that pretty\r\ncreature over to a life of temptation."\r\n\r\n"In good hands," said Clement. "The youth himself is a nice honest\r\nfellow, a mere boy as yet; but it is something to have no harm in him at\r\ntwo-and-twenty and in the Guards; and his parents are evidently ready to\r\nwatch over and guide them."\r\n\r\n"If her head does not get turned," sighed Geraldine.\r\n\r\n"Just as likely in any other station," replied Clement. "The protection\r\nmust come from within, not from the externals; and I do think that\r\nshe--yes, and he too--have that Guard within them."\r\n\r\n"I think the sooner we are away from this place the better," said\r\nGeraldine. "There are such things as cold shoulders, and perhaps\r\ndispleasure is in human nature, though it is not our fault."\r\n\r\n"Which is the worse for us," laughed her brother, "since we can\'t beg\r\npardon."\r\n\r\nThe cold shoulder was manifested by a note of apology the next morning\r\nfrom Mr. White. He was too busy to go with Mr. Underwood to Santa\r\nCarmela on this day, but had sent the young quarry-man to act as\r\nguide, and his foreman as interpreter. So Clement had his long ride on\r\nmule-back mostly in silence, though this he scarcely lamented, for he\r\ncould better enjoy the mountain peaks and the valleys bright with rich\r\ngrass, with anemones of all colours, hyacinths, strange primulas and\r\ngentians, without having to make talk to Mr. White. But his journey was\r\nwithout result. He did find an exceedingly old woman keeping sheep and\r\nspinning wool with a distaff, who owned to the name of Cecca Benista.\r\nShe once had a brother. Yes, Gian was his name, but he went away, as\r\nthey all did. He had a voice bellissima, si bellissima; and some one\r\ntold her long, long ago, that he had made his fortune, and formed a\r\ncompany, but he had never come home--no, no, and was probably dead,\r\nthough she had never heard; and he had sent nothing--no, no!\r\n\r\nThen Clement tried the priest of the curious little church on the\r\nhill-side, a memory of Elijah and the convents on Mount Carmel. The\r\nParrocco was a courteous man, quite a peasant, and too young to know\r\nmuch about the past generation. He gave Clement a refection of white\r\nbread, goats\' milk cheese, and coffee, and held up his hands on the\r\ndeclining of his thin wine. There was a kind of register of baptisms,\r\nand Giovanni Batista Benista was hunted out, and it was found that if\r\nalive he would be over seventy years old. But no more was known, and\r\nthere was no proof that he was dead twenty-two years before!\r\n\r\nThat long day had convinced Geraldine that the pleasantness of\r\nintercourse with the Whites was over, and she was not sorry that a\r\nletter was waiting for Clement to say that the rheumatic clergyman would\r\narrive, if desired, in another week. This was gladly accepted, and the\r\nquestion remained, whither should they go? Clement\'s year of absence\r\nwould be over in June, and he was anxious to get home; besides that,\r\nit was desirable to take Francie to her mother as soon as possible.\r\nThe only cause for delay was the possibility of Gerald\'s extracting\r\nsomething further from his mother, which might lead to further\r\nresearches on the Continent; but as most places were readily accessible\r\nfrom London, this was decided against, and it was determined to go back\r\nto Brompton at the same time as the Rotherwoods returned from San Remo.\r\n\r\nOn the last Sunday Mr. White showed himself much more cordial than he\r\nhad been since the crisis. He waited in the porch to say--\r\n\r\n"Well, sir, you have given us some very excellent sermons, and I am sure\r\nwe are much obliged to you. If I can help you any more in investigating\r\nthat unlucky affair of your nephew, do not hesitate to write to me. I\r\nshall be delighted to assist you in coming to your rights."\r\n\r\n"Thank you; though I sincerely hope they are not my rights."\r\n\r\n"Ah, well. You are not so advanced in life but that if you came into\r\nanything good, you might marry and start on a new lease! You are pounds\r\nbetter than when you came here."\r\n\r\nWhich last clause was so true that Clement could only own it, with\r\nthanks to his good-humoured host, who lingered a little still to say--\r\n\r\n"I am sorry any vexation arose about those foolish young people, but you\r\nsee young women will wish to do the best they can for themselves, and\r\nwill make mischief too if one listens to them. A sensible man won\'t.\r\nThat\'s what I say."\r\n\r\nClement quite agreed, though he was not sensible of having listened to\r\nany of the mischief-making, but he heartily shook hands with Mr. White,\r\nand went away, glad to be at peace.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXII. -- THE TEST OF DAY-DREAMS\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     Faith\'s meanest deed more favour bears,\r\n       Where hearts and wills are weighed,\r\n     Than brightest transports, choicest prayers,\r\n       That bloom their hour and fade.--J. H. NEWMAN.\r\n\r\n\r\nThat return to Brompton was the signal for the numerous worries awaiting\r\nClement. First, the doctors thought him much improved, but declared that\r\na return to full work at St. Matthew\'s would overthrow all the benefit\r\nof his long rest, and would not hear of his going back, even with\r\nanother curate, for an experiment.\r\n\r\nThen all went down to Vale Leston together. Mr. Ed\'dard was welcomed\r\nwith rapture by his old flock. Alda had been almost ill with excitement\r\nand delight, and had not words enough to show her ecstasy over her\r\nbeautiful daughter, nor her gratitude to Geraldine, to whose management\r\nshe insisted on attributing the glorious result. In vain did Geraldine\r\ndisclaim all diplomacy, Lady Vanderkist was sure that all came of\r\nher savoir faire. At any rate, it was really comfortable to be better\r\nbeloved by Alda than ever in the course of her life! Alda even intimated\r\nthat she should be well enough to come to Brompton to assist in the\r\nchoice of the trousseau, and the first annoyance was with Clement for\r\nnot allotting a disproportioned sum for the purpose. He declared that\r\nFrancie ought not to have more spent on her than was reserved for\r\nher sisters, especially as it would be easy for her to supply all\r\ndeficiencies, while Alda could not endure that the future Lady Ivinghoe\r\nshould have an outfit unworthy of her rank, even though both Wilmet and\r\nGeraldine undertook to assist.\r\n\r\nThere were other difficulties, for which the sojourn at Vale Leston was\r\nto be dreaded. Gerald had been of age for two months, and there were\r\nleases to be signed and arrangements made most difficult to determine in\r\nthe present state of things. Major and Mrs. Harewood wanted to wind up\r\ntheir residence in the Priory, and to be able to move as soon as the\r\nwedding was over, since Franceska begged that it might be at the\r\nonly home she remembered, and her elders put aside their painful\r\nrecollections to gratify her; so that it was fixed for early August,\r\njust a year since her unprepared appearance as Mona.\r\n\r\nAfter all, Alda was really too ill to go to London, and Franceska had\r\nto be sent in charge of her aunt Cherry and of her sister Mary.\r\nLady Rotherwood would be in town, and might be trusted to have no\r\nunreasonable expectations.\r\n\r\nPoor Sophy! Penbeacon\'s destiny was one of the affairs that could not be\r\nsettled, and therewith her own, though her mother could not succeed in\r\npenetrating any of the family with the horror of giving Lord Ivinghoe\r\nsuch a brother-in-law.\r\n\r\nIn the midst of the preparations came a letter from Gerald. He did\r\nindeed write every Sunday, but of late his had been hurried letters: he\r\nwas so fully occupied and had so much writing on hand that he could not\r\nindulge in more length.\r\n\r\n\r\n"You have been urging me," he said, "to find out what my mother knows. I\r\nhave not liked to press the subject while she was so ill, as she always\r\nmet every hint of it with tears and agitation. However, at last, Lida\r\nbrought her to it, and we really believe she knows no more than we do\r\nwhat became of her first husband. She never heard of him after she fled\r\nfrom him. She was almost a child, and he had been very cruel to her. But\r\nshe did tell us where we may be nearly certain of finding out, namely\r\nfrom Signor Menotti, Via San Giacomo, Genoa, or his successors, a\r\nman who trained singers and performers, and moreover took charge of\r\nBenista\'s money, and she thinks he had considerable savings. Poor woman,\r\nI believe she had no idea of the harm she might be doing me, though\r\nit was scarcely in human nature to see prosperity look so aggressive\r\nwithout trying to profit thereby; and when she had put herself into\r\nO\'Leary\'s power, the notion was to make an income out of me by private\r\nthreats and holding their tongues. That I should have any objection\r\nto such an arrangement, except on economical principles, never entered\r\ntheir heads, and they tried to make as much as possible out of either me\r\nor Clement, by withholding all the information possible till it was\r\npaid for, and our simultaneous refusal to be blackmailed entirely\r\ndisconcerted them, and made them furious. Lida said the man was violent\r\nwith her mother for letting out even what she did to trousseau, and the\r\nfirst annoyance was with Clement for not allotting a disproportioned sum\r\nfor the purpose. He declared that Francie ought not to have more spent\r\non her than was reserved for her sisters, especially as it would be easy\r\nfor her to supply all deficiencies, while Alda could not endure that the\r\nfuture Lady Ivinghoe should have an outfit unworthy of her rank, even\r\nthough both Wilmet and Geraldine undertook to assist.\r\n\r\nThere were other difficulties, for which the sojourn at Vale Leston was\r\nto be dreaded. Gerald had been of age for two months, and there were\r\nleases to be signed and arrangements made most difficult to determine in\r\nthe present state of things. Major and Mrs. Harewood wanted to wind up\r\ntheir residence in the Priory, and to be able to move as soon as the\r\nwedding was over, since Franceska begged that it might be at the\r\nonly home she remembered, and her elders put aside their painful\r\nrecollections to gratify her; so that it was fixed for early August,\r\njust a year since her unprepared appearance as Mona.\r\n\r\nAfter all, Alda was really too ill to go to London, and Franceska had\r\nto be sent in charge of her aunt Cherry and of her sister Mary.\r\nLady Rotherwood would be in town, and might be trusted to have no\r\nunreasonable expectations.\r\n\r\nPoor Sophy! Penbeacon\'s destiny was one of the affairs that could not be\r\nsettled, and therewith her own, though her mother could not succeed in\r\npenetrating any of the family with the horror of giving Lord Ivinghoe\r\nsuch a brother-in-law.\r\n\r\nIn the midst of the preparations came a letter from Gerald. He did\r\nindeed write every Sunday, but of late his had been hurried letters: he\r\nwas so fully occupied and had so much writing on hand that he could not\r\nindulge in more length.\r\n\r\n\r\n"You have been urging me," he said, "to find out what my mother knows. I\r\nhave not liked to press the subject while she was so ill, as she always\r\nmet every hint of it with tears and agitation. However, at last, Lida\r\nbrought her to it, and we really believe she knows no more than we do\r\nwhat became of her first husband. She never heard of him after she fled\r\nfrom him. She was almost a child, and he had been very cruel to her. But\r\nshe did tell us where we may be nearly certain of finding out, namely\r\nfrom Signor Menotti, Via San Giacomo, Genoa, or his successors, a\r\nman who trained singers and performers, and moreover took charge of\r\nBenista\'s money, and she thinks he had considerable savings. Poor woman,\r\nI believe she had no idea of the harm she might be doing me, though\r\nit was scarcely in human nature to see prosperity look so aggressive\r\nwithout trying to profit thereby; and when she had put herself into\r\nO\'Leary\'s power, the notion was to make an income out of me by private\r\nthreats and holding their tongues. That I should have any objection\r\nto such an arrangement, except on economical principles, never entered\r\ntheir heads, and they tried to make as much as possible out of either me\r\nor Clement, by withholding all the information possible till it was\r\npaid for, and our simultaneous refusal to be blackmailed entirely\r\ndisconcerted them, and made them furious. Lida said the man was violent\r\nwith her mother for letting out even what she did to Lance, and he meant\r\nto put a heavy price even on the final disclosure, in the trust (which I\r\nshare) that it may prove the key to the mystery. She had no notion that\r\nthe doubt was upsetting my position. Poor thing, she never had a chance\r\nin her life--gipsy breeding at first, then Benista\'s tender mercies\r\nand the wandering life. She could not fail to love my father till his\r\nrequirements piqued her, and it was a quarrel, exasperated perhaps by\r\nthe commencement of his illness, over her neglect of my unlucky self,\r\nand her acceptance of Schnetterling\'s attentions, that led to her\r\nabandoning him. I really do not think she ever realized that it was a\r\nsin. That good Pere Duchamps is the first priest of any kind she ever\r\nlistened to, and he has had a great effect upon her. He would like to\r\nextend it to Lida and me, but Lida is staunch to her well-beloved Mr.\r\nFlight as well as to me, and there is a church on the other side the\r\nbay to which I take her when our patient is well enough to spare her to\r\nwalk, or we can afford the crossing. Easter was a comfort there.\r\n\r\n"The warm weather has revived the patient, and she may live some months\r\nlonger, though she is a mere skeleton. Lida tends her in the most\r\naffectionate manner, and is really a little angel in her way. She has\r\ngot some private pupils in music, and is delighted to bring in grist to\r\nthe mill, which grinds hard enough to make me realize the old days you\r\nare so fond of recollecting.\r\n\r\n"Don\'t ask me to send you the Lacustrian. I am ashamed of it, and of my\r\nown articles. Nothing will go down here but the most highly spiced, and\r\nit is matter of life and death to us, as long as my mother lives, to\r\nkeep on the swaying top of the poplar tree of popularity. You would\r\ndespise the need, and talk of Felix, but it is daily bread, and I cannot\r\nlet my mother and sister starve for opinions of mine. One comfort for\r\nyou is that if I ever do come home again to reign at Vale Leston, I\r\nshall have seen the outcome of various theories of last year, and proved\r\nwhat is the effect of having no class to raise a standard or to look up\r\nto. I don\'t think I shall be quite so bumptious, and I am quite sure I\r\nshall value my Cherie\'s tenderness much better than I have ever done,\r\nmore shame for me! Love to the bride and all at Vale Leston. There is\r\nan old age of novelty about these eastern states, quite disgusting in\r\ncomparison with the reverend dignity of such a place as Vale Leston. You\r\nnever thought that I appreciated it! You will find no fault with me on\r\nthat score now. The lake is beautiful enough, but I begin to hate the\r\nsight of it, especially when a Yankee insists on my telling him whether\r\nwe have in all Europe anything better than a duck-pond in comparison.\r\nLittle Lida is my drop of comfort, since she has ceased to be mortally\r\nafraid of \'Brother.\' Love to all and sundry again.\r\n\r\n"Your loving G."\r\n\r\n\r\nThere was a consultation over this letter, which ended in John\r\nHarewood\'s volunteering to go to Genoa, and find out this Menotti or his\r\nrepresentative, returning in time for the wedding, and hoping that the\r\nuncertainty would thus be over in time for the enjoyment of a truly\r\nprosperous event.\r\n\r\nA letter that came before his departure rendered Geraldine doubly\r\nanxious for the decision. Mrs. Henderson sent it to her to read, saying\r\nthat it was by Lady Merrifield\'s advice, since she thought that it\r\nshould be known how it was with Gerald, for even to Dolores he had not\r\ntold half what Ludmilla related.\r\n\r\n\r\n"MY DEAREST MRS. HENDERSON,\r\n\r\n"It is a long time since I received your dearest, kindest of letters,\r\nand if I did not answer it sooner, it was not from want of gratitude,\r\nbut attendance on my poor dear mother and assistance to our landlady\r\noccupies me at every minute that I can spare from giving music lessons\r\nto some private families, and an evening class. I am very thankful to be\r\nable to earn something, so as to take off something of the burthen on my\r\ndear brother\'s shoulders. For, alas! the care and support of my mother\r\nand me weigh very heavily upon him. The proprietor of the Lacustrian has\r\nparted with his other clerk, and my brother has the entire business\r\nof not only writing, extracting for, and editing the paper, but of\r\ncorrecting the press, and he dares not remonstrate or demand better\r\npayment, as we live from week to week, and he could not afford to be\r\ndismissed. He is at the office all day, beginning at six in the morning\r\nto meet the central intelligence, he only rushes home for his meals,\r\nand goes back to work till twelve or one o\'clock at night. Even then he\r\ncannot sleep. I hear him tossing about with the pain in his back that\r\nsitting at his desk brings on, and his hands are so tired by writing,\r\nand with the heat, which has been dreadful for the last few weeks, and\r\nhas taken away all the appetite he ever had. You would be shocked to\r\nsee him, he is so thin and altered; I cannot think how he is to continue\r\nthis, but he will not hear of my writing to Lady Travis Underwood. He\r\nis never angry, except when I try to persuade him, and you never saw\r\nanything like his patience and gentleness to my poor mother. She never\r\ndid either, she cannot understand it at all. At first she thought he\r\nwanted to coax the confession out of her, and when she found that it\r\nmade no difference, she could not recover from her wonder--he, whom she\r\nhad deserted in his babyhood, and so cruelly injured in his manhood, to\r\ndevote himself to toiling for her sake, and never to speak harshly to\r\nher for one moment. She knew I loved her, and she had always been good\r\nto me, except when O\'Leary forced her to be otherwise, but his behaviour\r\nhas done more to touch her heart than anything, and I am sure she is,\r\nas Pere Duchamps says, a sincere penitent. She is revived by the summer\r\nheat, and can sit under the stoop and enjoy the sweet air of the lake;\r\nbut she is very weak, and coughs dreadfully in the morning, just when it\r\nis cooler, and my brother might get some sleep. She tries to be good and\r\npatient with us both, and it really does soothe her when my brother can\r\nsit by her, and talk in his cheerful droll way; but he can stay but a\r\nvery short time. He has to rush back to his horrid stuffy office, and\r\nthen she frets after him and says, \'But what right have I to such a\r\nson?\' and she begins to cry and cough."\r\n\r\n\r\n"Ah!" said Clement, as Geraldine, unable to speak for tears, gave him\r\nthe letter. "This is a furnace of real heroism."\r\n\r\n"Christian heroism, I am sure," said Geraldine. "Oh, my boy, I am proud\r\nof him. He will be all the better for his brave experiment."\r\n\r\n"Yes, he had an instinct that it would be wholesome, besides the\r\nimpelling cause. Real hardship is sound training."\r\n\r\n"If it is not too hard," said she.\r\n\r\n"\'Let not their precious balms break my head,\'" said Clement.\r\n\r\n"I do not like that pain in the back. Remember how he dragged his\r\nlimbs when first we had him at home, and how delicate he was up to\r\nthirteen--only eight years ago!"\r\n\r\n"Probably it will not last long enough to do him much harm."\r\n\r\n"And how nobly uncomplaining he is!"\r\n\r\n"This has brought out all the good we always trusted was in reserve."\r\n\r\n"Better than Emilia\'s experiment," sighed Geraldine.\r\n\r\nFor Emilia Vanderkist, before her year was over, was at home, having\r\nbroken down, and having spent most of her holidays with Mrs. Peter\r\nBrown, the wife of Sir Ferdinand\'s partner. She had come back, not\r\nlooking much the worse for her hospital experience, but with an immense\r\ndeal to say of the tyranny of the matron, the rudeness of the nurses to\r\nprobationers, the hardness and tedium of the work to which she had been\r\nput, and the hatefulness of patients and of doctors.\r\n\r\nAnna sympathized with all the vehemence of her sisterly affection,\r\nand could hardly believe her aunts, who told her that things must have\r\nchanged in a wonderful manner since the time of Angela\'s experiences,\r\nfor she had been very happy in the same place, and made no complaints.\r\n\r\nEmilia had written to her cousin Marilda to express her willingness to\r\nreturn so soon as the Travis Underwoods should come home, and in\r\nthe meantime she remained at Vale Leston, not showing quite as much\r\ntolerance as might be expected of the somewhat narrow way of life of\r\nher sisters. She did not like being a lodger, as it were, in Sophy\'s\r\nbedroom; she found fault with the parlour-maid\'s waiting, complained of\r\nthe noise of the practising of the three little sisters, and altogether\r\nreminded Geraldine of Alda in penance at home.\r\n\r\nMajor Harewood was detained longer than he expected, for on arriving at\r\nGenoa he found that Menotti had migrated, and had to follow him to his\r\nvilla on the Apennines, where, in the first place, he had to overcome\r\nthe old man\'s suspicions that he was come to recover Benista\'s means on\r\nbehalf of his family, and then at last was assured that the man had been\r\ndead long before 1870. Still John Harewood thought it well to obtain\r\npositive evidence, and pursued the quest to Innspruck, where Menotti\r\naverred that the man had been left by his companions dying in the care\r\nof some Sisters of Charity.\r\n\r\nSo it proved. At Innspruck, the record of the burial of Giovanni\r\nBenista, a native of Piedmont, was at length produced, dated the 12th\r\nof February, 1868, happily and incontestably before Zoraya\'s marriage to\r\nEdgar Underwood!\r\n\r\nJohn Harewood made haste to telegraph the tidings to Vale Leston and to\r\nJonesville, and came home exultant, having dispelled the cloud that\r\nhad brooded over the family for nearly a year, and given them freely to\r\nenjoy the wedding.\r\n\r\nWould they do so the more or the less for Emilia\'s announcement that\r\nshe had a letter from Mr. Ferdinand Brown, eldest son of Sir Ferdinand\'s\r\npartner, offering her marriage, and that she had accepted him? He was,\r\nof course, a rich man, but oh! how Emily, Annie, and Gerald had been\r\nwont to make fun of him, and his parents.\r\n\r\n"But, my dear Nan," said she, "I shall be able to do much more good in\r\nthat way."\r\n\r\n"Oh!"\r\n\r\n"And really I cannot go back to those intolerable backgammon evenings at\r\nKensington Palace Gardens."\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXIII. -- A MISSIONARY WEDDING\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     Till the smooth temper of my age might be\r\n     Like the high leaves upon the holly tree.--SOUTHEY.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe neighbourhood said that nothing was ever done at Vale Leston\r\naccording to the conventionalities, and the Devereux wedding was an\r\ninstance.\r\n\r\nLancelot had brought word that Bishop Norman May had actually arrived\r\nfrom New Zealand for a half-year\'s visit, bringing with him the younger\r\nmissionary Leonard Ward, and that Dr. May\'s happiness was unspeakable.\r\n"A renewed youth, if he needed to have it renewed."\r\n\r\nClement and William Harewood went over to see them, and returned greatly\r\nimpressed, and resolved on convoking the neighbourhood to be stirred\r\nin the cause of the Pacific islands. At the same time, one of the many\r\nletters from Lady Rotherwood about arrangements ended with--"My husband\r\nhopes you will be able to arrange for us to be introduced to your\r\nconnections of the May family, the Bishop, Mr. Ward, and the good old\r\ndoctor of whom we have heard so much."\r\n\r\n"We must invite them all to the wedding," said Mrs. Harewood, who, as\r\nstill inhabiting the Priory, would be the hostess.\r\n\r\n"Certainly," returned William Harewood, "but I don\'t think Mr. Ward\r\nwould come. He looks like an ancient hermit."\r\n\r\n"The best way," said Mrs. Grinstead, "would be to finish up the\r\nwedding-day with a missionary garden-party."\r\n\r\n"Geraldine!" said Lady Vanderkist from her sofa, in feeble accents of\r\ndismay; but Mrs. William Harewood hardly heard, and did not notice.\r\n\r\n"It would be the most admirable plan. It would give people something to\r\ndo, and make a reason for having ever so many more."\r\n\r\n"Baits cleverly disposed," said William. "The S.P.G. to attract Ward,\r\nWard to attract the Marquis, and the Marquis to attract the herd."\r\n\r\n"Everybody throngs to the extremest outskirts of a wedding," said\r\nGeraldine.\r\n\r\n"They may have the presents on view in the long room," said Wilmet.\r\n\r\n"Provided they don\'t have the list of them printed," said Geraldine.\r\n"Lance won\'t put them into the \'Pursuivant\'; it is disgusting!"\r\n\r\n"So I have always thought," said Robina; "but you hardly make allowances\r\nfor the old ladies who love to spell them out."\r\n\r\n"The Marquis of Rotherwood--a gold-topped dressing-case; Miss Keren\r\nHappuch Tripp--a pincushion," said Geraldine. "It is the idlest gossip,\r\nand should not be encouraged."\r\n\r\n"And," added Robina, "as we go out through the cloister there will\r\nhappily be no rice. Will has stopped it in the churchyard."\r\n\r\n"And fortunately we have no school-boys to reckon with, except Adrian\r\nand Fely, who will be quite amenable."\r\n\r\nFor Kester Harewood was in India, and Edward on the Mediterranean;\r\nAdrian was at home, doing credit to Miss Mohun, and so vehemently\r\ncollecting stamps, that he was said to wish to banish all his friends to\r\nthe most remote corners of the earth to send them home.\r\n\r\nFrancie\'s elder sisters declined being bridesmaids, so that Phyllis and\r\nMysie were the chief, and the three young sisters, Wilmet, Alda, and\r\nJoan, with two little Underwoods and two small Harewoods, all in white\r\nfrocks and sashes, were to attend and make a half-circle round the\r\nbride.\r\n\r\nAll took effect as had been purposed, each party being equally desirous\r\nthat it should be truly a Christian wedding, such as might be a fit\r\nemblem of the great Marriage Feast, and bring a blessing--joyous and\r\nhappy, yet avoiding the empty pomp and foolish mirth that might destroy\r\nthe higher thoughts.\r\n\r\nHow beautiful Vale Leston church looked, decked with white roses,\r\nlilies, and myrtle! The bride, tall and stately in her flowing veil and\r\nglistening satin train, had her own sweet individuality, not too closely\r\nrecalling the former little bride. She came on her uncle Clement\'s arm,\r\nas most nearly representing a father to her, and the marriage blessing\r\nwas given by the majestic-looking Bishop, with the two chief local\r\nclergy, Mr. William Harewood and Mr. Charles Audley, taking part of the\r\nservice. It was a beautiful and impressive scene, and there was a great\r\npeace on all. It was good to see the intense bliss on Ivinghoe\'s face\r\nas he led his bride down the aisle, and along the cloister; and as they\r\ncame into the drawing-room, after she had received an earnest kiss, and\r\n"my pretty one" from his father, it was to Dr. May that he first led\r\nher. Dr. May, his figure still erect, his face bright and cheery, his\r\nbrow entirely bare, and his soft white locks flowing over his collar.\r\nHe held out his hands, "Ah, young things! You are come for the old man\'s\r\nblessing! Truly you have it, my lady fair. You are fair indeed, as fair\r\nwithin as without. You have a great deal in the power of those little\r\nhands, and you--oh yes, both of you, believe, that a true, faithful,\r\nloving, elevating wife is the blessing of all one\'s days, whether it be\r\nonly for a few years, or, as I trust and pray it may be with you, for a\r\nlong--long, good, and prosperous life together."\r\n\r\nThe two young things bent their heads, and he blessed them with his\r\nblessing of eighty years. Lord Rotherwood\'s eyes were full of tears, as\r\nhe said in a choked voice--\r\n\r\n"Thank you, sir," while Franceska murmured to Mysie--\r\n\r\n"I do like that he should have been the first to call me \'my lady.\'"\r\n\r\nThe luncheon included only the two families, and the actual assistants\r\nat the wedding, and it was really very merry. Lady Rotherwood did\r\ninspire a little awe, but then Alda, sitting near, knew exactly how to\r\ntalk to her, and Alda, who, like Geraldine, had dressed herself in soft\r\ngreys and whites, with her delicate cheeks flushed with pleasure and\r\ntriumph, looked as beautiful as ever, and far outshone her twin, whose\r\ncomplexion and figure both had become those of the portly housewife.\r\n\r\nMeta, otherwise Mrs. Norman May, had eyes as bright and lively as ever,\r\nthough face and form had both grown smaller, and she was more like a\r\nfairy godmother than the Titania she had been in times of old. She had\r\ngot into the middle of all the varieties of children, dragged thither by\r\nGertrude\'s Pearl and Audrey, and was making them happy.\r\n\r\nEthel and Geraldine never could come to the end of what they had to say\r\nto one another, except that Ethel could but be delighted to make her\r\nfriend know the brother of her early youth; and show her the grave,\r\nearnest-looking man who had suffered so much, and whose hair was as\r\nwhite as the doctor\'s, his face showing the sunburn of the tropics; and\r\nthe crow\'s-feet round his eyes, the sailor\'s habit of searching gaze. He\r\ndid not speak much, but watched the merry young groups as if they were a\r\nsort of comedy in his eyes.\r\n\r\nThey were very merry, especially when the doctor had proposed the health\r\nof the bride, and her brother, Sir Adrian, was called on to return\r\nthanks for her.\r\n\r\n"Gentlemen and ladies," he said, "no, I mean ladies and gentlemen, I am\r\nvery much obliged to you all for the honour you have done my sister. I\r\ncan tell Lord Ivinghoe she is a very good girl, and very nice, and all\r\nthat, when she is not cocky, and doesn\'t try to keep one in order."\r\n\r\nThe speech was drowned in laughter, and calls to Ivinghoe to mind what\r\nhe was about, and beware of the "new woman."\r\n\r\nSo the young couple were seen off to spend their honeymoon in Scotland,\r\nand the rest of the party could pair off to enjoy their respective\r\nfriends, except that Mary and Sophy had to exhibit the wedding presents\r\nto all and sundry of the visitors of all degrees who began to flock in.\r\n\r\nSeats were ranged on the lawn, and when every one had had time to wonder\r\nat everything, from Lady Rotherwood\'s set of emeralds, down to the\r\nchoirboys\' carved bracket, the house-bell was rung, and all had to take\r\ntheir places on the lawn, fairly shaded by house, cloister, and cedar\r\ntree, and facing the conservatory, whose steps, with the terrace, formed\r\na kind of platform. It is not needful to go through all, or how John\r\nHarewood, as host, explained that they had thought that it would be\r\nwell to allow their guests to have the advantage of hearing their\r\ndistinguished visitors tell of their experiences. And so they did,\r\nthe Bishop pleading the cause of missions with his wonderful native\r\neloquence, as he stood by the chair where his father sat listening to\r\nhim, as to a strain of sweet music long out of reach. Then Leonard Ward\r\nsimply and bluntly told facts about the Pacific islands and islanders,\r\nthat set hearts throbbing, and impelled more than one young heart to\r\nlong to tread in the like course.\r\n\r\nThen Lord Rotherwood thanked and bungled as usual, so that Gustave\r\nTanneguy would have a hard matter to reduce what he called the\r\n"aristocratic tongue" to plain English, or rather reporter\'s English.\r\nThe listeners were refreshed with tea, coffee, and lemonade, and there\r\nwas a final service in the church, which many gladly attended, and thus\r\nended what had been a true holiday.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXIV. -- RIGHTED\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     Perhaps the cup was broken here,\r\n     That Heaven\'s new wine might show more clear.\r\n                                      E. B. BROWNING.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n"No. 14, Huron St., Jonesville, Ohio,\r\n\r\n"July 19.\r\n\r\n"MY DEAR MADAM,\r\n\r\n"You were so kind as to tell me to write to your ladyship if we were\r\nin any difficulty or distress, and I have often longed to do so, but my\r\nbrother always said that we had no right to trespass on your goodness.\r\nNow, however, things are at such a pass that I think you will hear of us\r\nwith true compassion. I do not know whether he told you that we met\r\nmy poor mother on board a steamer upon this lake. Her husband had been\r\ndrowned in a wreck while crossing, and she was reduced to great poverty,\r\nand had also, from exposure, contracted disease of the lungs, which,\r\nthe doctor said, must terminate fatally in a few months. My brother\r\ntook charge of her, and has supported us ever since, now four months,\r\nby working at the editorship of the Lacustrian Intelligencer, with such\r\nsmall assistance as I could give by music lessons. It involved severe\r\nlabour at desk work and late hours, and his health has latterly given\r\nway, his back and lower limbs being gradually affected, and last\r\nMonday even his hands proved helpless. My poor mother broke another\r\nblood-vessel on Sunday, and died ten minutes later. My brother desired\r\nme to sell his dear violin and his watch to pay the funeral expenses,\r\nbut after that I know not what we can do, as he is quite helpless, and\r\ncan hardly be left even for the sake of my small earnings. Dear Lady\r\nTravis Underwood, pray help us, as I know you and Sir Ferdinand love my\r\npoor dear generous brother, and will not think him ungrateful for having\r\ndeclined your kindness while he could support himself and us. No doubt\r\nwe shall get help from England, but not for some time, so I dare to ask\r\nyou.\r\n\r\n"I remain, your humble servant,\r\n\r\n"LUDMILLA.\r\n\r\n"P.S.--Everybody knows him as Jerry Wood. We are at Mr. MacMahon\'s, 14\r\nHuron Street."\r\n\r\n\r\nThis sad letter, in Lida\'s neat pupil-teacher\'s hand, came enclosed\r\nwithin a longer letter from Marilda.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n"Grand National Hotel, Jonesville.\r\n\r\n"July 23rd.\r\n\r\n"MY DEAR GERALDINE,\r\n\r\n"You will believe that this letter from poor Lydia made Fernan telegraph\r\nat once to her, and hurry off as soon as we could reach the train. We\r\nfound things quite as bad or worse than we expected. The poor children\r\nwere living in two rooms in a wretched little house of an Irish collier,\r\nwho with his wife happily has been very kind to them, and says that\r\nnothing could surpass their goodness to that poor mother of theirs, who,\r\nshe tells me, \'made a real Christian end\' at last. I am sure she had\r\nneed to do so.\r\n\r\n"The burial was happily over, conducted by the French priest, as the\r\nwoman was a Roman Catholic to the last. Gerald was sitting up by the\r\nwindow, so changed that we should not have known him, except for the\r\nwonderful likeness to Felix that has come upon him. It seems that he\r\nhad not only all the writing of that horrid paper to do, but all the\r\ncompositor\'s work, or whatever you call it. The people put upon him when\r\nthey saw how well he could do it, and he could not refuse because his\r\nmother needed comforts, and he durst not get thrown out of employment.\r\nHe went on, first with aching back, then his legs got stiff and\r\nstaggering, but still he went on, and now it has gone into his hands; he\r\ncannot hold a pen, and can hardly lift a tea-cup. But he is so cheerful,\r\nalmost merry. The doctor says it is a paralytic affection, and that\r\noverwork has developed the former disease from the old injury to the\r\nspine, which seemed to have passed off, and there is intermittent fever\r\nabout him too, a not uncommon thing in these low-lying lake districts.\r\nWe have moved him to this Grand National Hotel, a big, half-inhabited\r\nplace, but better than the MacMahons\' house, though the good woman cried\r\nover him and Lydia at their farewells, and said she never should see\r\nsuch a young gentleman and lady again with hearts so like ould Ireland.\r\nShe would hardly take the money that Fernan offered her; she said they\r\nhad brought a blessing on her house with their tender, loving ways.\r\n\r\n"Fernan is gone to Milwaukee to get further advice and more comforts for\r\nGerald, and we mean, as soon as he can be moved, to take him home with\r\nus, since the air of the Rockies will revive him if anything will. This\r\nplace is fearfully hot and oppressive; the bay seems to shut out air\r\nfrom Lake Erie, and I cannot bear to think what that poor boy must have\r\ngone through in that close little den, with the printing-press humming\r\nand stamping away close to him; but he says it is his native element,\r\nand that when he is better he must go to Fiddler\'s Ranch. He sends his\r\nlove, and fears that you have missed his letters, but he could hardly\r\nwrite them, and thought Lydia might alarm you. He is a very dear boy,\r\nand I do hope we make him comfortable; he is so thankful for the little\r\nwe can do for him, and so patient. He tells me to give special love to\r\nFrancie, and say he is glad that Mona\'s game of chess was played out\r\nwith a good substitute for Ferdinand. These are his words, which no\r\ndoubt she will understand. We think of moving next week, but much\r\ndepends on the doctor\'s verdict. My love too to the dear Francie; she\r\nwill be a great lady, quite beyond our sphere. Perhaps she may be able\r\nto give Emily some amusements, though I fear they will only make her\r\nmore discontented with our humdrum ways. I never thought hospital work\r\nwould suit her. Gerald says there is nothing like trying one\'s theories,\r\nand that having to exaggerate his own has made him sick of a good deal\r\nof them, though not of all. Poor dear boy, I hope he will live to show\r\nthe benefits he says he has derived from this sad time. It shall not\r\nbe for want of anything we can do. He is as near our hearts as ever his\r\ndear father was, and Lydia is a dear little girl.\r\n\r\n"Your ever affectionate cousin,\r\n\r\n"MARY ALDA TRAVIS UNDERWOOD."\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was a great shock, though mitigated by hearing that Gerald was in\r\nsuch hands as those of his first friend, and kind Marilda; but there\r\nwas great surprise at no notice being taken of the tidings that secured\r\nGerald\'s position. John Harewood had telegraphed them, but it only now\r\nfully broke on him that he ought to have sent them to Jerry Wood instead\r\nof Gerald Underwood, so that Italian telegrams were not to blame.\r\n\r\nOn one thing Clement ventured, being nearly certain that the reaction of\r\nGerald\'s mind would not include the preventing of all Penbeacon works.\r\nHe encouraged young Bramshaw to set about the plans so as to make the\r\nwashings as innocuous as possible, being persuaded that this was the\r\nonly way to prevent more obnoxious erections on ground just beyond.\r\nMoreover, this gave the lovers hope, and Alda had, under Clement\'s\r\npersuasion and rebukes, withdrawn her opposition to the engagement, so\r\nthat Sophy was free to wander about Penbeacon with her Philip, and help\r\nto set up his theodolite, and hold the end of his measuring-tape.\r\n\r\nHer mother could not well stand out on the score of unequal birth, when\r\nMr. Ferdinand Brown, whose father had swept out the office, came down\r\nand was accepted with calm civility, it could not be called delight,\r\neven by Emilia.\r\n\r\nBut he was a worthy young man, and well educated, and it was for his\r\nsake that Clement and Geraldine had stayed on at the Priory, giving the\r\nHarewoods and their curates holidays in turn; though even this amount of\r\nwork was enough to leave with Clement a dread conviction that his full\r\nshare of St. Matthew\'s would be fatal to him, insomuch that he had\r\nwritten to the patron, the Bishop of Albertstown, seriously to propose\r\nresignation.\r\n\r\nFresh letters arrived from America, the first slightly more cheery, but\r\nthe next was dated from Violinia, to the general surprise, and it was\r\nvery short, from Sir Ferdinand.\r\n\r\n\r\n"DEAR CLEMENT,\r\n\r\n"We have the telegram, a relief to the poor lad\'s mind, but he has\r\nnot spoken much since. It came just as we were starting in an invalid\r\nPullman, fitted with every comfort; but the jars of these lines are\r\nunavoidable and unspeakable, and he suffered so terribly, as well as\r\nso patiently, that we had to give up our intention of taking him to\r\nUnderwood. The one thing he begged for was that we would take him to\r\nFiddler\'s Ranch. You know there is a mission-station here, so we have\r\nhim in the clergyman\'s house, and the place is so advanced that he has\r\nevery comfort. But I doubt whether the dear boy will ever move again. He\r\nis perfectly helpless, but his brain quite clear, and his spirits good.\r\n\r\n\r\n"Ever yours,\r\n\r\n"F. A. TRAVIS UNDERWOOD."\r\n\r\n\r\nThere followed a long letter, dictated by Gerald himself, and partly\r\nwritten by Lida, partly by Marilda, at several different times.\r\n\r\n\r\n"DEAREST, MOST DEAREST CHERIE, AND ALL--\r\n\r\n"I should like to be able to sign my name to my thanks to all, if only\r\nto feel that I have a name, and one so honoured, but these fingers\r\nof mine will not obey me, so you must take the will for the deed, and\r\nbelieve that you have made me very happy, and completed all I could\r\nwish. I fear you never will believe how jolly it is to lie here, the\r\npain all gone, since having done with that terrific train, and the three\r\ntenderest, most watchful of slaves always round me, while my Cherie is\r\nspared the sight of the wreck.--(L.)\r\n\r\n"You know that good old Fernan established a missionary station here,\r\nbuilding a church, and getting the ground consecrated where my father\r\nlies. I can just see the top of the cross, and there he promises that\r\nI shall lie. You will be able to put my name in the cloister under my\r\nfather\'s, as no impostor.\r\n\r\n"Don\'t grieve, my Cherie, it is best as it is; my brains were full of\r\nmore notions than you ever quite guessed, and of which I have seen the\r\nseamy side out here, though there is much that I should feel bound to\r\nwork out, and that might have grieved you. I was not tough enough for\r\nthe discipline that was needed to strike the balance. (He is thinking\r\naloud, dear fellow.--M. A.) I am afraid I have often vexed you in my\r\ncrudeness and conceit, but I know you forgive. I am very thankful for\r\nthis year, and for the way in which my poor mother was given into\r\nmy hands at last. Fernan has helped me to make a short will, to save\r\nconfusion and difficulty.\r\n\r\n"I have left everything to Clement, knowing that you and he will provide\r\nfor all. Fernan and Marilda will care for Lida. (That we will.--M. A.)\r\nI cannot leave her to be a tax on Vale Leston. Give my books and MSS. to\r\nDolores, and please be kind to her. My violin, which Fernan redeemed for\r\nme, the eponym (How do you spell it?--M. A.), by the way, of this place,\r\nmy father\'s own fiddle, give to Lance for his pretty Ariel; Anna, my\r\ngood sister, should have my music, which will be a memory of happy\r\nevenings. Emmie may like the portfolio of drawings that I made for the\r\nmission-house; dear old Sibby the photograph in my room of the \'Ecce\r\nHomo.\' I have it in my eye now.--(M. A.)\r\n\r\n"Everything is such a comfort, Fernan and Marilda are the best of nurses\r\nand helpers, and I mourn for the folly that chaffed about them and\r\nboredom. Tell Emmie so. Fernan has made this place a little oasis round\r\nmy father\'s grave, and his parson, who has a mission among the remains\r\nof the Sioux, is with me every other day, and does all that Clement\r\ncould desire for me. So do--do believe that it is all for the best, dear\r\npeople.--(L.)\r\n\r\n"One thing good is, that I shall not bring any bad blood into the\r\nUnderwood inheritance. By the bye, tell them--(Continued by Marilda)\r\nMr. Gracchus Van ------ suddenly arrived here, greatly shocked at Gerald\'s\r\nstate, and actually wanting to marry Lydia on the spot--which of course\r\nshe declined. But Fernan was pleased with him, and he told him he had\r\nnever met any one to hold a candle to \'Jerry Wood,\' so \'smart\' and\r\n\'chipper,\' as he saw at first, and then cheerful, good-humoured, and\r\nkindly, whatever happened. None of your Britisher\'s airs, but ready to\r\nmake the best of any fixings. I don\'t think dear Gerald meant me to tell\r\nall this, but think of the difference from the fastidious fine gentleman\r\nhe used to be! He is dozing now, I fear he is getting weaker; but he is\r\never so sweet and good, and I quite long to beg his pardon for having\r\ncalled him your spoilt boy. Mr. Fraser, the clergyman here, is very much\r\nstruck with him, and Fernan remembers the time when he baptized him as\r\nhe lay unconscious. Dear Cherry, it will grieve you, but I think there\r\nwill be comfort in the grief.\r\n\r\n"Your affectionate cousin,\r\n\r\n"M. A. T. U."\r\n\r\n\r\nThere were long letters to Dolores, dictated to Lida--all in the same\r\nspirit. One of them said, "Go bravely on, my Dolores; though we do not\r\nlive together in our bicycle-roving castles. You will do good work if\r\nyou uphold the glory of God and the improvement of man, all through\r\ncreation and science. I should like to talk it over with you. Things\r\nare plainer to me than in the days of my inexperience and cocksureness.\r\nShort as the time was, in months, it showed me much more, especially my\r\nown inefficiency to deal with the great problems of these times, perhaps\r\nof all times. Remember this, but go on--if we do but put grains of sand\r\ninto the great Edifice."\r\n\r\nMore was written, but these were the most memorable extracts, before the\r\nletter that told that something like a fresh stroke had come, and taken\r\naway the power of distinct speech, then that the throat had failed, and\r\nthere was only one foreboding more to be told, and soon realized. The\r\nyoung ardent spirit, trained by so short a discipline, had passed away\r\nin peace. And they laid him beside his father, whose better spirit he\r\nhad unconsciously evoked, and whom he had loved so deeply. The doctors\r\nsaid that the real cause of his death had been the Indian bullet,\r\ninflicting injury on the spine, which the elasticity of youth had for\r\nthe time overcome, but which manifested itself again under overstrain.\r\nFerdinand, when he awoke the child back to life, had given him years not\r\nspent in vain for himself or for others.\r\n\r\n\r\nIt would have been utter desolation to the little sister save for the\r\nmotherly tenderness of Marilda, who took her to the home in the Rocky\r\nMountains, and would fain have adopted her, but that Lida, acting\r\nperhaps on advice from her brother, only begged to be so educated as to\r\nfit her to be independent, and to be given a start in life. It would\r\nbe shown in a year or two whether her vocation should be musical or\r\nscholastic.\r\n\r\nGerald had his meed of tears at home, but not bitter ones. Nay, those\r\nthat had the most quality of bitterness were Emilia\'s, shed in secret\r\nlest interpretations should be put on those that had the quality of\r\nremorse, as she recollected the high aspirations that had ended so\r\ndifferently in the two cousins.\r\n\r\nDolores dried hers, to feel a consecration on her studies and her\r\nlabours as she grew forward to the fulfilment of her purpose of being a\r\nleading woman in the instruction and formation of young minds, working\r\nall the better for the inspiriting words and example, and the more\r\ngently and sympathizingly for the love that was laid up in her heart.\r\n\r\nShe and his "Cherie" came to have a great affection and understanding\r\nof each other, and discussed what Dolores called "ethics" with warm\r\ninterest, the elder lady bringing the old and sacred lights to bear on\r\nthe newer theories.\r\n\r\nClement was the undoubted owner of Vale Leston, and the John Harewoods\r\nhad decided on leaving the Priory. Just at the same time, when the\r\nacceptance of Clement\'s resignation of St. Matthew\'s had arrived,\r\nWilliam Harewood was offered a canonry at Minsterham, with the headship\r\nof the theological college. The canonry had been the summit of his\r\nambition when a boy, and there was no one fitter than he for the care of\r\na theological college. He was pre-eminently a scholar, and his fifteen\r\nyears of parish experience made good preparation for training young\r\nclergy.\r\n\r\nSo Clement could decide on presenting himself to the living of Vale\r\nLeston, with a staff of curates, and Geraldine to be his home sister,\r\nmaking the Priory a resting-place for overworked people, whether clergy,\r\ngovernesses, or poor, or mission-folk at home. It was a trust to be kept\r\nfor Lancelot and his boy, who would make the summer home of the family\r\nthere, to Dr. May\'s great content. It was a peaceful home, and to every\r\none\'s surprise, Alda decided to remain at hand, chiefly to keep her\r\nboy under his uncle\'s influence, which thus far was keeping him well in\r\nhand, and as he would go to a public school with little Felix, might be\r\nprolonged.\r\n\r\nIt was a comfort and encouragement to feel that hereditary dangers and\r\ntemperament could be subdued and conquered in Gerald; and if the sins of\r\nparents had their consequence in the children, the scourge might become\r\na palm. When the commemorative brass in the cloister was to be put up,\r\nGeraldine said--\r\n\r\n"I should like to put \'Rejoice with me, for I have found the piece that\r\nwas lost.\'"\r\n\r\n"He never was lost."\r\n\r\n"Oh no, no, my dear boy. But his work was so like the finding the\r\nstained, tarnished piece of silver, cast aside, defaced, dust-marked,\r\nand by simple duty and affection bringing her back."\r\n\r\n"I see! Let us have the inscription in Greek. Then none can apply it\r\nto himself! It was a wonderful work, and it is strange that having\r\nfulfilled it, he who brought the child from his father\'s arms should lay\r\nhim to his rest beside his father."\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE END\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nEnd of the Project Gutenberg EBook of The Long Vacation, by Charlotte M.
